Abstract	  
This	  qualitative	  study	  entitled,	  Concrete	  Roses,	  explored	  the	  literacy	  and	  language	  
practices	  of	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  in	  an	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  street	  literature	  book	  club.	  This	  
qualitative	  study,	  situated	  within	  the	  social	  networking	  site	  Facebook,	  as	  well	  as	  an	  urban	  
public	  library,	  explored	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  make	  meaning	  of	  
culturally	  relevant	  texts.	  The	  focus	  of	  this	  inquiry	  was	  to	  examine	  broadly	  how	  societal,	  
institutional,	  and	  situational	  factors	  influence	  the	  ways	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  make	  
meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts.	  Three	  interconnected	  research	  questions	  drove	  this	  
study:	  (a)	  How	  do	  media	  representations	  of	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  influence	  how	  they	  see	  
themselves?	  (Societal);	  (b)	  How	  do	  black	  adolescents	  girls	  make	  meaning	  of	  street	  
literature	  texts	  in	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  spaces?	  (Institutional);	  and	  (c)	  How	  does	  the	  role	  of	  the	  
researcher	  influence	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  make	  meaning	  of	  street	  
literature	  texts?	  (Situational).	  	  
Three	  theoretical	  frameworks,	  including	  Reader	  Response	  Theory,	  New	  Literacy	  
Studies,	  and	  Hip-­‐hop	  Feminist	  Theory	  (3rd	  Wave)	  drove	  this	  qualitative	  study.	  Several	  
ethnographic	  methods	  were	  employed,	  including	  audiotaped	  transcriptions	  of	  fieldnotes,	  
online	  book	  discussions,	  focus	  group	  interviews,	  participant	  journal	  writing,	  and	  a	  
researcher	  journal.	  Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis	  was	  the	  analytical	  tool	  employed	  to	  
interpret	  data	  (Fairclough,	  1989;	  Rogers,	  2003).	  Concrete	  Roses	  contributes	  to	  the	  current	  
body	  of	  literature	  by	  positioning	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  at	  the	  center	  and	  bringing	  visibility	  
to	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  their	  positionalities	  (raced,	  gendered,	  and	  classed	  identities)	  influence	  
how	  they	  “do”	  literacy.	  By	  doing	  so,	  it	  addresses	  the	  dearth	  of	  research	  in	  LIS	  scholarship	  
focusing	  on	  the	  intersection	  of	  raced,	  gendered,	  and	  classed	  identities.	  Concrete	  Roses	  also	  

contributes	  to	  the	  current	  body	  of	  literature	  by	  addressing	  educational	  approaches	  to	  
urban	  literacies	  and	  highlighting	  pedagogical	  strategies	  designed	  to	  bridge	  black	  
adolescent	  girls’	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  and	  in-­‐school	  literacy	  and	  language	  practices.	  Overall,	  
Concrete	  Roses	  stresses	  the	  importance	  of	  creating	  inclusive	  learning	  environments,	  in	  
order	  to	  effectively	  meet	  the	  literacy	  and	  language	  practices	  of	  diverse	  populations.	  	  
	  

	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
CONCRETE	  ROSES:	  A	  CRITICAL	  EXPLORATION	  OF	  BLACK	  ADOLESCENT	  	  
GIRLS’	  LITERACY	  AND	  LANGUAGE	  PRACTICES	  IN	  AN	  OUT-­‐OF-­‐SCHOOL	  	  
STREET	  LITERATURE	  BOOK	  CLUB	  
	  
	  
By	  
	  
Delicia	  Tiera	  Greene	  
	  
	  
B.S.	  John	  Jay	  College	  of	  Criminal	  Justice	  (CUNY),	  1998	  
M.S.	  Pratt	  Institute,	  2000	  
M.A.	  City	  College	  of	  New	  York	  (CUNY),	  2005	  
C.A.S.	  Hunter	  College	  (CUNY),	  2008

Doctor	  of	  Philosophy	  in	  Information	  Science	  and	  Technology

Theoretical	  Perspectives	  Chart	  ........................................................................................................	  16	  

	  

	  
	  

xiii	  

1	  
Chapter	  One	  
Introduction	  
	  
Statement	  of	  the	  Problem	  
Black	  adolescent	  girls’	  personhood,	  lived	  experiences,	  literacy,	  and	  language	  
practices	  have	  constantly	  been	  scrutinized	  across	  various	  spheres	  of	  society.	  	  Historically	  
positioned	  at	  the	  margins	  being	  both	  black	  and	  female,	  black	  girls	  have	  been	  one	  of	  the	  
most	  oppressed	  groups	  in	  society.	  They	  have	  been	  bombarded	  by	  controlling	  images	  of	  
black	  femininity	  throughout	  various	  spheres	  of	  society,	  including	  music	  videos,	  films,	  
reality	  shows,	  and	  songs	  to	  name	  a	  few.	  	  
In	  2012,	  9-­‐year	  old	  African-­‐American	  actress	  Quvenzhane	  Wallis,	  star	  of	  the	  film	  
Beasts	  of	  the	  Southern	  Wild	  became	  the	  youngest	  person	  ever	  to	  be	  nominated	  for	  best	  
actress	  in	  a	  leading	  role.	  Wallis’	  nomination	  for	  her	  role	  as	  Hushpuppy	  in	  the	  film	  resulted	  
in	  media	  backlash.	  In	  response	  to	  her	  nomination,	  the	  Onion,	  a	  well-­‐known	  online	  news	  
source,	  referred	  to	  Wallis	  as	  a	  “cunt”	  on	  their	  Twitter	  feed.	  The	  label	  “cunt”	  is	  a	  derogatory	  
and	  misogynistic	  obscenity.	  The	  Onion	  tweeted	  “Everyone	  else	  seems	  afraid	  to	  say	  it,	  but	  
that	  Quvenzhane	  Wallis	  is	  kind	  of	  a	  cunt,	  right?	  #Oscars2013”	  (@TheOnion,	  2013).	  The	  
Onion’s	  response	  to	  Wallis’	  nomination	  is	  one	  example	  of	  how	  black	  females	  have	  
historically	  been	  sexualized,	  objectified,	  and	  robbed	  of	  their	  youth	  and	  innocence.	  	  
In	  2013,	  Wallis	  starred	  in	  the	  title	  role	  in	  the	  remake	  of	  the	  Broadway	  musical	  Annie.	  
Wallis	  was	  again	  targeted	  by	  the	  media	  and	  was	  subjected	  to	  racist	  Tweets	  for	  being	  cast	  in	  
a	  role	  many	  felt	  was	  best	  suited	  for	  a	  white	  female	  actress.	  A	  Twitter	  user	  tweeted,	  “I	  don’t	  
like	  this	  remake	  of	  Annie.	  There	  can’t	  be	  a	  black	  Annie”	  (@tayylorhartwell,	  2014).	  Another	  

	  

2	  
Twitter	  user	  tweeted,	  “Annie	  was	  a	  freckled	  face	  redhead,	  not	  a	  nappy	  headed	  parasite	  
infected	  #nigger	  shitbag!	  #niggersruin	  everything”	  (@BumFarto,	  2004).	  @BumFarto	  
(2004)	  further	  tweeted	  ”A	  black	  Annie-­‐really?	  Should	  we	  now	  have	  a	  white	  Aunt	  Jemima?”	  
Racist	  tweets	  about	  Wallis	  starring	  in	  a	  remake	  of	  the	  Broadway	  musical	  Annie	  are	  
examples	  of	  how	  the	  standard	  of	  femininity	  in	  society	  are	  viewed	  as	  both	  white	  and	  female.	  	  
In	  the	  high	  profile	  Trayvon	  Martin	  murder	  case,	  the	  prosecution’s	  key	  witness,	  
Rachel	  Jeantel,	  a	  19-­‐year	  old	  black	  woman,	  was	  the	  target	  of	  public	  shaming	  and	  smearing	  
by	  many	  in	  the	  media.	  Blogger	  and	  feminist	  scholar	  Sherri	  Williams	  posted	  “instead	  of	  
focusing	  on	  her	  testimony	  social	  media	  erupted	  with	  criticism	  about	  her	  speech,	  looks,	  
mannerisms,	  race,	  and	  education”	  (@Sherriwrites,	  2013).	  Many	  of	  the	  racist	  tweets	  and	  
Instagram	  posts	  were	  rooted	  in	  sizism	  and	  colorism	  and	  depicted	  Rachel	  as	  a	  gorilla	  and	  
also	  labeled	  her	  a	  hood	  rat,	  a	  term	  used	  to	  describe	  a	  female	  who	  is	  ghetto	  and	  sexually	  
promiscuous.	  	  
The	  media	  also	  scrutinized	  Jeantel’s	  use	  of	  African	  American	  Vernacular	  English	  
(AAVE),	  or	  what	  is	  commonly	  known	  as	  Black	  English.	  In	  a	  series	  of	  cross	  examinations,	  
defense	  attorney	  Don	  West,	  devalued	  her	  literacy	  and	  language	  practices	  and	  equated	  her	  
linguistic	  style	  to	  her	  inability	  to	  understand	  Standard	  English.	  The	  media	  described	  
Jeantel’s	  linguistic	  style	  as	  “ungrammatical	  English.”	  According	  to	  Dr.	  Brittney	  Cooper	  
(2014),	  co-­‐founder	  of	  the	  Crunk	  Feminist	  Collective,	  despite	  the	  fact	  that	  “Jeantel	  was	  
entrenched	  in	  a	  hostile	  environment	  that	  devalued	  her	  personhood	  and	  her	  traumatic	  
experience,	  and	  publicly	  discredited	  her	  testimony,	  the	  media	  labeled	  her	  combative	  and	  
aggressive	  throughout	  the	  trial	  based	  on	  her	  body	  language.”	  Furthermore,	  the	  media	  often	  

	  

3	  
felt	  that	  Jeantel’s	  raced	  and	  gendered	  identity,	  body	  language,	  level	  of	  education,	  and	  
linguistic	  style	  didn’t	  make	  her	  a	  credible	  witness.	  	  
The	  invisibility	  of	  black	  girls	  has	  also	  been	  prominent	  within	  other	  spheres,	  
including	  hip-­‐hop	  culture.	  The	  commodification	  of	  hip-­‐hop	  culture	  is	  deeply	  entrenched	  in	  
hypermasculinity	  and	  patriarchy.	  In	  many	  aspects	  of	  hip-­‐hop	  culture,	  including	  music	  
videos	  and	  reality	  shows,	  black	  girls	  and	  women	  are	  often	  sexualized	  and	  portrayed	  as	  
video	  vixens,	  scantily	  clad	  and	  using	  their	  sexuality	  for	  financial	  gain	  and	  for	  the	  affection	  of	  
black	  males.	  In	  the	  highly-­‐rated	  reality	  shows,	  such	  as	  Love	  and	  Hip-­‐Hop,	  Basketball	  Wives,	  
The	  Real	  Housewives	  of	  Atlanta,	  and	  Bad	  Girls	  Club,	  black	  girls	  and	  women	  are	  often	  labeled	  
as	  “angry	  black	  women,”	  bitter,	  aggressive	  women	  that	  are	  prone	  to	  engage	  in	  physical	  
altercations.	  These	  oppressive	  images	  across	  various	  media	  spheres	  have	  perpetuated	  the	  
invisibility	  and	  degradation	  of	  black	  girlhood	  and	  womanhood	  through	  twisted	  
constructions	  of	  both	  blackness	  and	  femaleness.	  	  According	  to	  feminist	  columnist	  and	  
author	  Charing	  Ball	  (2013),	  although	  images	  of	  the	  black	  male	  as	  abusers	  and	  sexual	  
exploiters	  in	  reality	  shows	  are	  prevalent	  and	  equally	  as	  damaging,	  it	  is	  the	  black	  males’	  
image	  that	  is	  the	  least	  questionable	  and	  subjected	  to	  critique	  by	  the	  media.	  Charing	  Ball	  
(2013)	  further	  revealed	  that	  the	  media	  is	  often	  quick	  to	  denounce	  stereotypes	  of	  black	  men	  
and	  boys,	  but	  uphold	  stereotypes	  of	  black	  girls	  and	  women.	  	  
According	  to	  Hip-­‐hop	  feminist	  scholar	  Elaine	  Richardson	  (2013),	  
such	  ghettoized	  and	  distorted	  images	  in	  present	  day,	  are	  rooted	  in	  brutal	  patriarchy	  
and	  chattel	  slavery	  of	  the	  past,	  which	  include	  freaks,	  hos,	  and	  sluts	  are	  a	  continuum	  
of	  twisted	  constructions	  of	  femininity	  of	  the	  past,	  which	  include	  wenches,	  mammies,	  
and	  jezebels.	  (p.	  329)	  
	  
It	  is	  quite	  evident	  that	  controlling	  images	  forced	  upon	  blackness	  and	  females	  are	  ever	  
present	  and	  rather	  than	  fading	  over	  time,	  are	  simply	  re-­‐shaped	  to	  fit	  the	  evolving	  society	  
	  

4	  
(Stephens	  and	  Phillips,	  2003).	  “These	  systems	  of	  brutal	  patriarchy	  and	  chattel	  slavery	  have	  
been	  reduced	  and	  metamorphosed	  into	  present	  day	  forms	  of	  structural	  racism,	  sexism,	  and	  
cultural	  hegemony	  and	  still	  powerfully	  influence	  the	  lives	  of	  black	  girls”	  (Richardson,	  2013,	  
p.	  329).	  These	  controlling	  images	  are	  meant	  to	  affirm	  White	  supremacy	  and	  dehumanize	  
black	  girls	  and	  women.	  Such	  forms	  of	  marginalization	  of	  black	  girlhood	  have	  been	  
damaging	  and	  have	  been	  instrumental	  in	  the	  ways	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  see	  themselves	  
and	  construct	  their	  own	  identities	  and	  representations	  of	  self	  (Richardson,	  2013).	  These	  
hegemonic	  ideologies	  have	  also	  transcended	  into	  social	  institutions,	  specifically	  school	  
spaces	  that	  have	  perpetuated	  inequities	  and	  have	  greatly	  influenced	  the	  literacy	  and	  
language	  practices	  that	  are	  valued.	  
In	  February	  of	  2014,	  President	  Obama	  announced	  his	  $200	  million	  dollar	  initiative,	  
“My	  Brother’s	  Keeper,”	  an	  effort	  designed	  to	  help	  black	  males	  succeed	  in	  school	  and	  
aggressively	  disrupt	  the	  school	  to	  prison	  pipeline	  (The	  White	  House,	  Office	  of	  the	  Press	  
Secretary,	  2014).	  President	  Obama’s	  education	  initiative	  acknowledged	  the	  nation’s	  history	  
of	  racial	  disparities,	  however	  absent	  from	  the	  initiative	  were	  black	  females.	  Many	  believe	  
that	  black	  girls	  generally	  fare	  better	  than	  black	  boys,	  however	  this	  creates	  a	  potentially	  
damaging	  narrative	  that	  prevents	  society	  and	  institutions	  from	  empowering	  and	  uplifting	  
black	  girls	  (The	  White	  House,	  Office	  of	  the	  Press	  Secretary,	  2014).	  	  
According	  to	  Fatima	  Goss	  Graves,	  vice	  president	  of	  education	  and	  employment	  at	  
the	  National	  Women’s	  Law	  Center	  in	  Washington,	  DC,	  black	  girls	  face	  both	  academic	  and	  
social	  barriers	  that	  are	  connected	  to	  their	  gendered,	  raced,	  and	  classed	  identities	  (National	  
Women’s	  Law	  Center,	  2014).	  In	  2014,	  the	  United	  States	  Department	  of	  Education	  for	  Civil	  
Rights	  released	  a	  report	  that	  offered	  a	  striking	  glance	  at	  the	  extent	  racial	  inequities	  plague	  

	  

5	  
the	  nation’s	  education	  system	  (U.S.	  Department	  of	  Education,	  2014).	  	  The	  study	  provided	  a	  
snapshot	  of	  the	  experiences	  of	  49	  million	  students	  across	  the	  country	  from	  2011	  through	  
2012	  and	  is	  based	  on	  data	  from	  97,000	  public	  schools	  across	  the	  country	  (U.S.	  Department	  
of	  Education,	  2014).	  The	  study	  highlighted	  how	  racial	  inequities	  and	  discrimination	  in	  
school	  spaces	  disproportionately	  affect	  black	  girls.	  
Although	  students	  of	  color	  were	  significantly	  over-­‐represented	  among	  those	  who	  
experienced	  exclusionary	  discipline,	  such	  as	  out-­‐of	  school	  suspensions,	  expulsions,	  arrests,	  
and	  other	  punishments	  than	  their	  white	  counterparts,	  “black	  girls,	  in	  particular,	  were	  
suspended	  three	  times	  the	  rate	  than	  girls	  of	  any	  other	  race	  or	  ethnicity	  and	  most	  boys”	  
(U.S.	  Department	  of	  Education,	  2014).	  The	  gender	  breakdown	  reveals	  that	  nationally,	  12%	  
of	  black	  girls	  received	  at	  least	  one	  in-­‐school	  suspension,	  whereas	  the	  rate	  for	  white	  girls	  is	  
2%,	  and	  for	  white	  boys	  6%	  (U.S.	  Department	  of	  Education,	  2014).	  These	  disparities	  began	  
as	  early	  as	  preschool,	  continued	  throughout	  their	  education,	  and	  persisted	  even	  when	  
socioeconomic	  status	  and	  other	  demographic	  factors	  were	  controlled	  (U.S.	  Department	  of	  
Education,	  2014).	  
The	  over-­‐representation	  of	  exclusionary	  discipline	  among	  black	  girls	  is	  often	  based	  
upon	  stereotypical	  perceptions	  and	  images.	  According	  to	  Monique	  Morris	  (2014),	  co-­‐
founder	  of	  the	  National	  Black	  Women’s	  Justice	  Institute,	  black	  girls	  are	  more	  likely	  to	  be	  
punished	  for	  being	  “unladylike”	  than	  white	  girls	  and	  are	  seen	  by	  teachers	  as	  	  “loud,	  defiant	  
and	  precocious.”	  Furthermore,	  when	  black	  girls	  speak	  up	  and	  speak	  out	  in	  school	  spaces	  
they	  are	  often	  stereotyped	  as	  “loud	  black	  females,”	  a	  continuum	  of	  the	  “angry	  black	  woman	  
stereotype,”	  which	  is	  reinforced	  in	  mainstream	  media	  portrayals	  of	  black	  girls	  and	  women	  
(Morris,	  2014).	  Stereotypes	  of	  the	  “loud	  black	  female”	  are	  rooted	  in	  widespread	  

	  

6	  
perceptions	  of	  black	  girls	  being	  dangerous,	  hostile,	  and	  uneducable,	  which	  contradicts	  
traditional	  standards	  of	  femininity	  and	  renders	  black	  girls	  having	  trouble	  in	  school	  spaces	  
(Morris,	  2014).	  	  
Despite	  the	  push	  for	  state,	  local,	  and	  federal	  educational	  mandates	  that	  promote	  
multicultural	  literature,	  21st	  century	  literacy	  skills	  and	  inclusive	  learning	  environments	  in	  
school	  spaces,	  traditional	  models	  of	  education	  continue	  to	  be	  prevalent	  in	  school	  spaces.	  
“Schools	  are	  viewed	  as	  the	  dominant	  discourse	  of	  learning,	  which	  continues	  to	  be	  rooted	  in	  
a	  tradition	  that	  privileges	  print,	  canonical	  text,	  and	  individualized	  instruction”	  (King	  &	  
Obrien,	  2002,	  p.	  48).	  The	  traditional	  model	  of	  education	  often	  unfairly	  associates	  deficit-­‐
oriented	  labels	  such	  as	  “at	  risk,”	  “unmotivated,”	  “underperforming,”	  “educationally	  
disadvantaged,”	  “struggling,”	  “educationally	  handicapped”	  to	  describe	  black	  girls	  whose	  
literacy	  and	  language	  practices	  may	  be	  different	  than	  the	  dominant	  discourse	  (King	  &	  
Obrien,	  2002,	  p.	  45).	  The	  use	  of	  such	  deficit	  labeling	  to	  describe	  students	  who	  engage	  in	  
literacy	  and	  language	  practices	  vastly	  different	  from	  those	  prevalent	  in	  the	  traditional	  
model	  of	  education	  clearly	  indicates	  that	  there	  exists	  a	  hierarchy	  of	  domains,	  in	  which	  
school-­‐sanctioned	  texts	  are	  deemed	  appropriate	  and	  serve	  as	  the	  benchmark	  by	  which	  
other	  forms	  of	  literature	  are	  classified	  (Alvermann,	  2001;	  Hughes-­‐Hassell	  &	  Rodge,	  2007;	  
Mahiri	  &	  Sablo,	  1996)	  constructing	  what	  counts	  as	  reading	  and	  who	  counts	  as	  readers.	  This	  
monolithic	  approach	  to	  texts	  often	  results	  in	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  viewing	  school	  spaces	  
as	  sites	  of	  struggle,	  resistance,	  and	  exclusion.	  According	  to	  Moje	  (2000),	  marginalized	  
readers	  are	  	  
those	  who	  are	  not	  engaged	  in	  the	  reading	  and	  writing	  done	  in	  school;	  who	  have	  
language	  or	  cultural	  practices	  different	  from	  those	  valued	  in	  school;	  or	  who	  are	  
outsiders	  to	  the	  dominant	  group	  because	  of	  their	  race,	  class,	  gender,	  and/or	  sexual	  
orientation.	  (p.	  405)	  
	  

7	  
	  
The	  experiences	  are	  important	  to	  consider	  because	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  have	  
historically	  been	  left	  out	  of	  engagements	  with	  literature	  (McHenry,	  2002;	  McHenry	  &	  
Heath,	  1994;	  Richardson,	  2002;	  Sutherland,	  2005,	  as	  cited	  in	  Gibson,	  2010,	  p.	  565).	  With	  a	  
monolithic	  approach	  to	  literacy	  and	  school	  spaces	  privileging	  canonical	  texts,	  black	  
adolescent	  girls	  are	  often	  times	  disengaged	  from	  literacy	  in	  school	  spaces.	  Excluded	  from	  
their	  literacy	  experiences	  in	  school	  spaces	  is	  their	  knowledge	  gained	  from	  their	  family	  and	  
cultural	  background,	  or	  what	  Moll	  and	  Amanti	  (2005)	  describe	  as	  funds	  of	  knowledge.	  
Funds	  of	  knowledge,	  is	  knowledge	  rooted	  in	  their	  experiences	  and	  passions	  nurtured	  in	  
their	  home	  communities.	  Furthermore,	  canonical	  texts	  in	  school	  spaces	  often	  times	  do	  not	  
reflect	  who	  they	  are	  and	  how	  they	  make	  meaning	  of	  the	  world	  as	  black	  adolescent	  girls.	  As	  
a	  result,	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  tend	  to	  not	  engage	  in	  identity	  work	  or	  draw	  upon	  their	  own	  
race	  and	  gendered	  positionalities	  when	  making	  meaning	  of	  texts.	  The	  lack	  of	  culturally	  
conscious	  texts	  has	  resulted	  in	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  silencing	  their	  own	  voices,	  in	  order	  to	  
accommodate	  dominant	  perspectives	  (DeBlase,	  2003).	  This	  has	  resulted	  in	  a	  gap	  between	  
black	  girls’	  in-­‐school	  and	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  literacies	  (Gainer	  &	  Lapp,	  2010).	  
Black	  adolescents	  girls	  who	  are	  often	  disengaged	  with	  more	  traditional	  forms	  of	  
texts	  are	  often	  engaged	  in	  more	  culturally	  conscious	  forms	  of	  texts,	  such	  as	  street	  literature	  
texts.	  Street	  literature	  texts	  tend	  to	  be	  location	  and	  setting	  specific,	  taking	  place	  in	  urban	  
communities	  within	  major	  cities	  (Morris,	  2012).	  This	  sub-­‐genre	  is	  also	  language	  specific,	  
drawing	  upon	  elements	  of	  African-­‐American	  Vernacular	  English	  (Morris,	  2012;	  Harris,	  
1997).	  This	  allows	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  to	  engage	  in	  lived	  experiences	  and	  language	  
practices	  rooted	  in	  their	  own	  cultural	  background	  (Brooks,	  2006;	  Harris,	  1997;	  Sims,	  1983;	  
Davis,	  2000).	  Black	  adolescent	  girls	  are	  typically	  drawn	  to	  street	  literature	  texts	  most	  likely	  
	  

8	  
due	  to	  the	  fact	  that	  the	  protagonists	  tend	  to	  be	  female	  and	  the	  female	  protagonist	  is	  often	  
faced	  with	  life	  experiences	  often	  considered	  taboo	  such	  as	  sexual	  abuse,	  teen	  pregnancy,	  
and	  physical	  abuse	  (Morris	  et	  al.,	  2006).	  This	  creates	  opportunities	  for	  black	  adolescent	  
girls	  to	  draw	  upon	  their	  own	  raced	  and	  gendered	  positionalities	  when	  making	  meaning	  of	  
texts	  and	  in	  doing	  so	  engage	  in	  their	  own	  female	  identity	  formation.	  Street	  literature	  texts	  
are	  also	  appealing	  to	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  because	  of	  its	  popular	  culture	  elements,	  
specifically	  its	  hip-­‐hop	  aesthetics	  (Jocson,	  2005;	  Mahiri,	  2005;	  Morrell,	  2004b,	  2008).	  As	  
members	  of	  a	  historically	  marginalized	  and	  disenfranchised	  group,	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  
are	  also	  drawn	  to	  the	  hip-­‐hop	  aesthetic	  aspect	  of	  street	  literature	  texts,	  as	  well	  as	  the	  social	  
issues	  present	  in	  urban	  communities	  (Jocson,	  2005).	  	  Despite	  black	  girls’	  appeal	  to	  street	  
literature	  texts	  there	  still	  continues	  to	  be	  contention	  between	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  out-­‐of-­‐
school	  and	  in-­‐school	  literacies	  and	  devaluation	  of	  street	  literature	  in	  school	  spaces.	  	  
	  
Purpose	  of	  the	  Study	  
The	  purpose	  of	  this	  qualitative	  study	  was	  to	  examine	  broadly	  how	  domains,	  
specifically	  societal,	  institutional	  and	  situational	  factors,	  influenced	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  black	  
adolescent	  girls	  made	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts.	  Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis	  was	  the	  
analytical	  tool	  used	  to	  interpret	  the	  data	  in	  this	  study	  (Fairclough,	  1989;	  Rogers,	  2003).	  By	  
employing	  Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis	  in	  this	  study,	  I	  acknowledge	  that	  black	  adolescent	  
girls	  draw	  from	  a	  set	  of	  lived	  experiences	  and	  that	  power,	  ideologies,	  and	  positionalities	  are	  
embedded	  within	  their	  literacy	  and	  language	  practices.	  Furthermore,	  as	  members	  of	  a	  
historically	  marginalized	  and	  disenfranchised	  group,	  that	  black	  adolescent	  girls’	  literacy	  
and	  language	  practices	  do	  not	  occur	  within	  a	  vacuum,	  but	  instead	  are	  influenced	  by	  

	  

9	  
external	  factors,	  including	  societal,	  institutional,	  and	  situational	  perspectives.	  Hence,	  
literacy	  and	  language	  are	  never	  neutral,	  but	  instead	  are	  rooted	  in	  social	  practices,	  social	  
institutions,	  and	  the	  lived	  experiences	  of	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  (Street,	  2003).	  	  
Three	  interconnected	  lenses	  drove	  this	  study	  and	  focused	  on	  the	  media	  
representations	  of	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  (societal),	  black	  adolescent	  girls’	  meaning	  making	  
of	  street	  literature	  texts	  in	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  spaces	  (institutional),	  and	  the	  researcher‘s	  
influence	  on	  black	  adolescent	  girls’	  meaning	  making	  of	  street	  literature	  texts	  (situational).	  
Employing	  Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis,	  the	  purpose	  of	  this	  study	  was	  also	  to	  examine	  
specifically	  a	  set	  of	  orders	  of	  discourse	  (genres,	  discourses,	  styles)	  that	  drove	  each	  domain	  
(societal,	  institutional,	  situational).	  Broadly,	  genres	  are	  comprised	  of	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  
black	  girls	  interact	  with	  each	  other,	  discourses	  are	  comprised	  of	  how	  black	  girls	  represent	  
their	  positions	  on	  particular	  issues,	  and	  styles	  are	  comprised	  of	  black	  girls’	  linguistic	  styles	  
(see	  Chapters	  4-­‐6).	  Drawing	  upon	  both	  orders	  of	  discourse	  and	  domains	  provided	  an	  
opportunity	  to	  closely	  examine	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  socio-­‐political	  factors	  influenced	  black	  
adolescent	  girls’	  meaning	  making	  process.	  The	  framing	  method	  in	  this	  study	  was	  to	  
position	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  at	  the	  center	  and	  create	  a	  safe	  space	  for	  them	  to	  tell	  their	  
own	  stories	  in	  their	  own	  voices	  on	  their	  own	  terms.	  Lastly,	  it	  was	  my	  hope	  that	  in	  
highlighting	  black	  adolescent	  girls’	  experiences,	  this	  study	  would	  serve	  to	  legitimize	  their	  
personhood,	  literacy,	  and	  language	  practices	  and	  bridge	  both	  their	  in-­‐school	  literacy	  and	  
language	  practices	  with	  their	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  literacy	  and	  language	  practices.	  

	  

10	  
Research	  Questions	  	  
	  

Three	  interconnected	  research	  questions	  drove	  this	  qualitative	  research	  study	  (see	  

Appendix	  E).	  Drawing	  upon	  Fairclough’s	  Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis,	  each	  research	  
question	  focused	  on	  a	  particular	  domain	  (society,	  institution,	  and	  situation)	  that	  influenced	  
the	  way	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  make	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts.	  
RQ1.	  How	  do	  media	  representations	  of	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  influence	  how	  they	  see	  
themselves?	  (Societal)	  
RQ2.	  How	  do	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  make	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts	  in	  out-­‐
of-­‐school	  spaces?	  (Institutional)	  
RQ3.	  How	  does	  the	  role	  of	  the	  researcher	  influence	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  black	  
adolescent	  girls	  make	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts?	  (Situational)	  
	  
Theoretical	  Perspectives	  
Three	  complementary	  critical	  frameworks	  undergird	  this	  qualitative	  research	  
study:	  Hip-­‐hop	  Feminist	  Theory	  (Morgan,	  1999;	  Pough,	  2002;	  Richardson,	  2007),	  Reader	  
Response	  Theory	  (Rosenblatt,	  1994;	  Sipe,	  2008),	  and	  New	  Literacy	  Studies	  (Gee,	  2001;	  
Luke,	  2000;	  Street,	  2003).	  Hip-­‐hop	  Feminist	  Theory	  accounts	  for	  the	  intersection	  of	  
blackness	  and	  femaleness	  among	  black	  girls	  and	  the	  hegemonic	  narratives	  that	  devalue	  
who	  they	  are	  and	  their	  lived	  experiences.	  Reader	  Response	  Theory	  takes	  into	  account	  the	  
reader’s	  positionality	  and	  how	  their	  experiences	  influence	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  they	  make	  
meaning	  of	  text.	  New	  Literacy	  Studies	  accounts	  for	  the	  ways	  that	  context	  and	  power	  
dynamics	  influence	  participant	  and	  facilitator	  interactions.	  The	  following	  describes	  

	  

11	  
detailed	  features	  of	  each	  theoretical	  perspective	  and	  highlights	  how	  each	  theoretical	  
perspective	  informed	  this	  qualitative	  research	  study.	  
	  
Hip-­‐hop	  feminist	  theory.	  Historically,	  Black	  feminism	  has	  focused	  on	  the	  needs	  of	  
black	  women.	  As	  ‘”double	  minorities’—twice	  removed	  from	  the	  experiences	  of	  mainstream	  
Americans”	  (Delgado	  &	  Stefanic,	  2001,	  p.	  58)	  as	  both	  black	  and	  female,	  black	  women	  have	  
historically	  been	  one	  of	  the	  most	  oppressed	  racial	  groups	  in	  society.	  Although	  the	  black	  
feminist	  movement	  has	  been	  instrumental	  in	  attaining	  more	  privileges,	  social	  freedoms,	  
and	  equality	  for	  black	  women,	  black	  girls	  of	  this	  generation	  continue	  to	  be	  confronted	  with	  
new	  challenges.	  Bombarded	  by	  mass	  media,	  digital	  technologies	  and	  popular	  culture,	  black	  
girls	  need	  a	  version	  of	  feminism	  suited	  to	  address	  the	  new	  issues	  confronting	  them.	  Hip-­‐
hop	  feminism	  is	  a	  culturally	  and	  generationally	  critical	  framework	  that	  focuses	  on	  
contemporary	  systematic	  issues,	  such	  as	  colorism,	  misogyny,	  and	  sexual	  abuse	  that	  
confront	  young	  black	  girls	  (Durham,	  Cooper,	  &	  Morris,	  2013;	  Pough,	  2002;	  Richardson,	  
2007).	  Hip-­‐hop	  feminism,	  situated	  at	  the	  intersection	  of	  several	  conflicting	  constructs,	  
including	  feminist	  ideologies,	  consciousness,	  and	  black	  male-­‐dominated	  hip-­‐hop	  culture,	  
offers	  a	  new	  and	  distinct	  version	  of	  feminism	  that	  addresses	  the	  needs	  of	  young	  black	  girls	  
in	  the	  21st	  Century	  (Durham	  et	  al.,	  2013;	  Henry,	  2010).	  	  
A	  major	  aspect	  of	  hip-­‐hop	  culture	  is	  the	  glorification	  of	  sex,	  violence,	  materialism,	  
and	  misogyny	  (Henry,	  2010).	  “This	  glorification	  is	  rooted	  in	  hegemony	  and	  commodifies	  
blackness	  and	  perpetuates	  the	  status	  quo	  and	  existing	  structures	  of	  inequality”	  (Love,	  
2012,	  p.	  18),	  which	  is	  constant	  conflict	  with	  feminist	  ideologies	  designed	  to	  empower	  black	  
girls.	  According	  to	  Pough	  (2002),	  “it	  is	  the	  objectionable	  elements	  of	  hip-­‐hop	  culture	  that	  

	  

12	  
are	  part	  of	  what	  makes	  it	  valuable	  to	  feminism	  because	  they	  provide	  the	  opportunity	  for	  
youth	  to	  analyze	  and	  hone	  their	  critical	  literacy	  skills”	  (pp.	  92-­‐93).	  One	  of	  the	  central	  
hallmarks	  of	  Hip-­‐hop	  feminism	  is	  the	  multiplicity	  of	  identities	  and	  the	  use	  of	  personal	  
narratives	  to	  illustrate	  racialized	  and	  gendered	  identities	  (Snyder,	  2008),	  in	  order	  to	  speak	  
back	  to	  dominant	  discourses,	  specifically	  media	  representations	  of	  black	  girls.	  “Hip-­‐hop	  
feminism	  highlights	  the	  gaps	  between	  the	  dominant	  discourses	  and	  the	  reality	  of	  the	  lives	  
of	  black	  girls	  and	  exposes	  hegemonic	  narratives	  and	  thus	  works	  to	  undermine	  their	  
legitimacy”	  (Snyder,	  2008,	  pp.	  184-­‐185).	  This	  allows	  black	  girls	  to	  look	  to	  personal	  
experiences	  to	  provide	  knowledge	  about	  how	  the	  world	  works	  and	  disrupt	  dominant	  
narratives	  about	  who	  they	  are.	  
Hip-­‐hop	  Feminist	  Theory	  provides	  participants	  with	  the	  opportunity	  to	  have	  crucial	  
conversations	  about	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  black	  girls	  are	  represented	  across	  a	  variety	  of	  social	  
media	  outlets,	  including	  film,	  television,	  hip-­‐hop	  music,	  and	  literature.	  In	  doing	  so,	  Hip-­‐hop	  
Feminist	  Theory	  also	  provides	  an	  opportunity	  for	  black	  girls	  to	  speak	  back	  to	  controlling	  
images	  of	  black	  girlhood	  and	  provides	  counter	  narratives	  that	  accurately	  represent	  who	  
they	  are.	  	  Hip-­‐hop	  Feminist	  Theory	  also	  takes	  into	  account	  the	  Hip-­‐hop	  aesthetics	  
embedded	  in	  street	  literature	  texts,	  as	  well	  as	  the	  critical	  social	  issues	  that	  female	  
protagonists	  in	  the	  text	  are	  confronted	  with.	  Lastly,	  Hip-­‐hop	  Feminist	  Theory	  informs	  the	  
ways	  in	  which	  participants,	  as	  well	  as	  myself	  (researcher)	  drew	  upon	  our	  positions	  as	  both	  
black	  and	  female	  in	  making	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts	  within	  and	  across	  domains.	  
	  
Reader	  response	  theory.	  Reader	  Response	  Theory,	  a	  transactional	  theory	  of	  
reading	  recognizes	  the	  reading	  process	  as	  a	  dialogue	  between	  the	  reader,	  the	  text,	  and	  the	  

	  

13	  
author	  rather	  than	  as	  a	  unilateral	  interaction	  where	  the	  reader	  is	  uncovering	  the	  authorial	  
intention	  or	  where	  a	  passive	  reader	  is	  acted	  upon	  by	  the	  text	  (Rosenblatt,	  1978).	  According	  
to	  hooks	  (1991),	  a	  reader	  actively	  engages	  in	  the	  simultaneous	  processes	  of	  reading,	  
interpretation,	  and	  criticism.	  Reader	  Response	  Theory	  recognizes	  that	  “reading	  is	  never	  
neutral	  and	  that	  the	  text	  itself	  doesn’t	  contain	  the	  meaning,	  but	  instead	  the	  text	  is	  subjected	  
to	  multiple	  meanings	  that	  are	  made	  possible	  by	  the	  lived	  experiences,	  worldviews,	  morals,	  
social	  codes,	  and	  personality	  traits	  of	  the	  reader”	  (Rosenblatt,	  1978,	  p.	  14).	  A	  reader	  
response	  approach	  to	  literature	  emphasizes	  a	  uniqueness	  of	  readers'	  backgrounds	  and	  
encourages	  them	  to	  develop	  their	  own	  authentic	  responses	  to	  texts	  (Rosenblatt,	  1984;	  Sipe,	  
2000).	  Because	  all	  readers	  create	  their	  own	  reading	  experience,	  all	  interpretations	  are	  
unique.	  Although	  the	  interpretations	  are	  unique,	  “Rosenblatt	  focuses	  attention	  to	  
sociocultural	  context	  and	  believes	  that	  readers	  who	  have	  similar	  social	  and	  cultural	  
backgrounds	  often	  arrive	  at	  similar	  (though	  not	  necessarily	  identical)	  interpretations	  of	  
texts”	  (Sipe,	  2008,	  p.	  59).	  By	  focusing	  on	  the	  positionality	  of	  each	  reader,	  Reader	  Response	  
Theory	  serves	  to	  illustrate	  how	  one	  draws	  upon	  personal	  experiences	  and	  identity	  markers	  
to	  engage	  in	  authentic	  responses	  texts	  (Sipe,	  2008).	  	  
Although	  the	  current	  qualitative	  study	  examines	  meaning	  making	  of	  street	  literature	  
texts	  among	  adolescent	  participants,	  Sipe’s	  (2008)	  theoretical	  model	  of	  children’s	  
understanding	  of	  picture	  storybooks	  has	  informed	  this	  study.	  Sipe’s	  (2008)	  theoretical	  
Model	  of	  Literary	  Understanding	  extends	  the	  work	  of	  Rosenblatt	  and	  provides	  five	  facets	  of	  
literary	  understanding	  including,	  analytical,	  intertextual,	  personal,	  transparent,	  and	  
performative.	  Each	  facet	  of	  literary	  understanding	  suggests	  that	  children	  employ	  different	  
types	  of	  literary	  meaning	  making	  strategies	  resulting	  in	  different	  types	  of	  reader	  responses.	  

	  

14	  
Rosenblatt’s	  (1984;	  1978)	  Reader	  Response	  Theory	  and	  Sipe’s	  (2008)	  Theoretical	  Model	  of	  
Literary	  Understanding	  provided	  a	  framework	  to	  explore	  the	  way	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  
bring	  their	  experiences	  to	  street	  literature	  texts	  and	  take	  experiences	  from	  street	  literature	  
texts.	  Sipe	  (2008)	  draws	  upon	  reader	  stances	  to	  detail	  how	  readers	  position	  themselves	  in	  
relation	  to	  the	  text.	  Sipe	  (2008)	  defines	  stance	  as	  “the	  particular	  set	  of	  assumptions	  and	  
expectations	  a	  particular	  reader	  has	  of	  a	  particular	  text	  at	  a	  particular	  time”	  (p.	  71).	  Reader	  
Response	  Theory	  provided	  a	  window	  into	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  black	  girls	  situate	  themselves	  
in	  relation	  to	  the	  street	  literature	  texts	  and	  how	  their	  reader	  stances	  influence	  how	  they	  
make	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts	  in	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  spaces.	  	  
Sipe’s	  (2008)	  theoretical	  model	  also	  offers	  conceptual	  categories	  for	  
facilitator/adult	  talk,	  a	  prominent	  feature	  that	  influences	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  adolescents	  
make	  meaning	  of	  texts.	  Sipe’s	  (2008)	  theoretical	  model	  offers	  five	  conceptual	  categories	  for	  
adult	  talk,	  including	  readers,	  managers/encouragers,	  clarifiers/probers,	  
wonderers/spectators.	  This	  aspect	  of	  Sipe’s	  theoretical	  model	  accounts	  for	  my	  role	  as	  a	  
researcher	  engaging	  in	  discussions	  around	  texts	  with	  participants	  and	  the	  impact	  that	  
these	  interactions	  have	  on	  participants’	  meaning	  making	  process.	  
	  
New	  literacy	  studies.	  New	  Literacy	  Studies	  draws	  upon	  an	  ideological	  view	  of	  
literacy	  focusing	  on	  shifts	  from	  traditional	  views	  of	  literacy	  as	  skills,	  knowledge,	  and	  
cognitions	  within	  the	  mind	  to	  an	  approach	  to	  literacy	  as	  visible	  social	  practices	  with	  
language,	  text,	  and	  discourse	  (Gee,	  2000;	  Street,	  1995).	  New	  Literacy	  Studies	  refers	  to	  
literacy	  in	  broad	  terms	  and	  challenges	  the	  monolithic	  representations	  of	  literacy.	  New	  
Literacy	  Studies	  positions	  all	  discourse	  within	  a	  political	  and	  social	  framework	  by	  

	  

15	  
attending	  to	  differences	  across	  race,	  class,	  gender,	  and	  sexual	  orientation.	  This	  theoretical	  
framework	  “offers	  a	  more	  culturally	  sensitive	  view	  of	  literacy	  practices	  from	  one	  context	  to	  
the	  next”	  (Street,	  2003,	  p.	  1).	  By	  offering	  a	  more	  culturally	  sensitive	  view	  of	  literacy	  
practices,	  New	  Literacy	  Studies	  challenges	  dominant	  v.	  marginalized	  literacies	  by	  
problematizing	  what	  counts	  as	  literacy	  and	  who	  counts	  as	  readers	  (Street,	  2003).	  In	  doing	  
so,	  New	  Literacy	  Studies	  acknowledges	  that	  there	  are	  multiple	  literacies	  at	  play.	  
Subsequently,	  this	  framework	  also	  views	  literacy	  as	  mediated	  by	  digital	  technologies,	  
situated	  in	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  spaces,	  and	  takes	  into	  account	  the	  social	  and	  political	  contexts	  in	  
which	  literacy	  is	  embedded	  and	  gives	  it	  meaning	  (Alvermann,	  2002;	  Street,	  1995).	  Gee	  
(2000)	  identifies	  these	  literacies	  as	  situated	  since	  they	  only	  make	  sense	  when	  studied	  
within	  a	  particular	  context.	  New	  Literacy	  Studies	  further	  highlights	  that	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  
people	  address	  literacy	  are	  rooted	  in	  their	  identity.	  Since	  one’s	  identity	  is	  shaped	  by	  
particular	  worldviews,	  New	  Literacy	  Studies	  argues	  that	  literacy	  is	  never	  neutral	  and	  
universal.	  This	  framework	  serves	  to	  uplift	  the	  voices	  of	  the	  disenfranchised	  populations	  
whose	  literacy	  practices	  are	  often	  devalued	  in	  favor	  of	  more	  mainstream,	  dominant	  
populations.	  	  
New	  Literacy	  Studies	  provided	  a	  framework	  to	  explore	  how	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  
make	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  from	  a	  critical	  perspective,	  in	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  spaces.	  In	  the	  
current	  study,	  it	  accounted	  for	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  make	  meaning	  of	  
street	  literature	  texts	  across	  digital,	  face-­‐to-­‐face,	  and	  written	  domains.	  In	  the	  current	  study,	  
New	  Literacy	  Studies	  accounted	  for	  the	  social	  practice	  of	  book	  discussions	  and	  the	  power	  
dynamics	  present	  in	  conversations	  between	  participants	  and	  facilitators.	  By	  privileging	  

	  

16	  
black	  adolescent	  girls’	  literacy	  experiences	  and	  literature	  selections,	  New	  Literacy	  Studies	  
highlighted	  ways	  to	  develop	  spaces	  that	  bridge	  in-­‐school	  and	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  literacies.	  
Figure	  1	  (see	  below)	  highlights	  three	  key	  components	  in	  this	  study:	  research	  
questions,	  research	  question	  focus,	  and	  theoretical	  frameworks.	  Figure	  1	  also	  details	  key	  
features	  of	  each	  theoretical	  framework	  and	  how	  each	  theoretical	  framework	  drove	  the	  
research	  questions.

RQ	  3

Role	  of	  the	  Researcher	  	  

	  
Figure	  1.	  Theoretical	  perspectives	  chart.	  
	  
Significance	  of	  the	  Study	  
	  

The	  significance	  of	  this	  qualitative	  study	  is	  multilayered.	  First,	  this	  study	  is	  

significant	  because	  it	  explores	  the	  literacy	  and	  language	  practices	  of	  a	  historically	  
marginalized	  population,	  specifically	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  who	  are	  underrepresented	  in	  

	  

17	  
the	  current	  body	  of	  literature.	  Secondly,	  this	  study	  is	  significant	  because	  it	  draws	  upon	  the	  
intersection	  of	  urban	  literacies	  and	  digital	  technologies	  and	  its	  influence	  on	  the	  ways	  in	  
which	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  make	  meaning	  of	  texts.	  Thirdly,	  this	  study	  is	  significant	  
because	  it	  accounts	  for	  how	  the	  meaning	  making	  of	  street	  literature	  texts	  is	  influenced	  by	  
domains,	  specifically	  society,	  institution,	  and	  situation.	  This	  study	  is	  also	  significant	  
because	  it	  positions	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  at	  the	  center	  and	  brings	  visibility	  to	  the	  ways	  in	  
which	  their	  racialized,	  gendered,	  and	  classed	  identities	  influence	  how	  they	  “do”	  literacy.	  
The	  current	  study	  is	  also	  significant	  because	  it	  explores	  my	  role	  as	  a	  researcher	  and	  
encourages	  a	  more	  reflexive	  approach	  to	  research.	  
In	  addition,	  this	  qualitative	  study	  is	  significant	  because	  it	  addresses	  several	  gaps	  in	  
the	  literature.	  Much	  of	  the	  literature	  on	  book	  clubs	  has	  been	  situated	  within	  the	  middle	  
school	  and	  high	  school	  context	  and	  has	  focused	  predominantly	  on	  quantitative	  measures	  of	  
literacy	  achievement.	  Very	  few	  studies	  have	  focused	  on	  book	  clubs	  situated	  within	  public	  
libraries.	  Of	  the	  few	  studies	  focusing	  on	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  spaces,	  most	  of	  them	  were	  situated	  
either	  within	  a	  community	  center	  or	  an	  afterschool	  space.	  Lastly,	  many	  of	  the	  studies	  that	  
have	  explored	  the	  intersection	  of	  literacy	  and	  digital	  technologies	  have	  not	  focused	  on	  
diverse	  populations,	  specifically	  black	  adolescent	  girls.	  	  
	  

Based	  on	  the	  overwhelming	  appeal	  of	  popular	  culture,	  digital	  technologies	  and	  

street	  literature	  texts	  among	  black	  adolescent	  girls,	  this	  research	  study	  seeks	  to	  provide	  an	  
opportunity	  for	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  to	  tell	  their	  own	  stories	  in	  their	  own	  words	  on	  their	  
own	  term	  in	  the	  hopes	  of	  informing	  the	  work	  of	  education	  policymakers,	  school	  and	  public	  
library	  administrators,	  school	  and	  public	  librarians,	  classroom	  teachers,	  and	  social	  

	  

18	  
workers.	  A	  qualitative	  research	  study	  focusing	  on	  black	  girls’	  literacy	  and	  language	  
practices	  is	  important	  for	  several	  reasons:	  
1. It	  provides	  education	  policymakers	  and	  administrators	  an	  opportunity	  to	  
understand	  that	  literacy	  and	  language	  in	  school	  spaces	  should	  extend	  beyond	  
standardized	  assessments	  and	  that	  literacy	  and	  language	  in	  school	  spaces	  should	  
include	  opportunities	  for	  diverse	  populations	  to	  engage	  in	  identity	  work	  
2. It	  provides	  information	  professionals	  an	  opportunity	  to	  develop	  library	  collections,	  
provide	  reader	  advisory	  services,	  and	  create	  digital	  literacy	  programming	  that	  are	  
inclusive	  and	  meet	  the	  needs	  of	  diverse	  populations	  
3. It	  provides	  classroom	  teachers	  an	  opportunity	  to	  create	  diverse	  thematic	  units,	  as	  
well	  as	  incorporate	  multicultural	  literature	  into	  the	  curricula	  
4. It	  provides	  social	  workers	  an	  opportunity	  to	  use	  street	  literature	  texts	  as	  
therapeutic	  tools	  designed	  to	  support	  diverse	  populations	  confronted	  with	  
traumatic	  experiences	  
	  
Summary	  
This	  qualitative	  study	  was	  designed	  to	  examine	  broadly	  how	  context,	  specifically	  
societal,	  institutional,	  and	  situational	  factors	  influence	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  black	  adolescent	  
girls	  make	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts.	  In	  doing	  so,	  this	  study	  examines	  the	  ways	  	  	  
media	  representations	  influence	  how	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  see	  themselves,	  how	  black	  
adolescent	  girls	  make	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts	  in	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  spaces,	  and	  how	  
the	  role	  of	  the	  researcher	  influences	  their	  meaning	  making.	  Lastly,	  this	  qualitative	  study	  
examines	  how	  black	  adolescent	  girls’	  positionalities	  influence	  how	  they	  “do”	  literacy.	  	  

	  

19	  
Overview	  of	  Chapters	  
	  

Chapter	  2	  outlines	  the	  review	  of	  the	  literature,	  which	  laid	  the	  foundation	  for	  this	  

study.	  Chapter	  3	  describes	  the	  epistemological	  approaches,	  the	  data	  sources,	  and	  the	  
analytic	  procedures	  that	  were	  employed	  in	  this	  study.	  Chapters	  4,	  5,	  and	  6	  highlight	  the	  
findings	  in	  this	  study.	  Each	  of	  the	  data	  chapters	  focuses	  on	  a	  particular	  domain	  perspective.	  
Chapter	  4	  focuses	  on	  how	  media	  representations	  influence	  how	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  see	  
themselves	  (societal).	  Chapter	  5	  focuses	  on	  how	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  make	  meaning	  of	  
street	  literature	  texts	  in	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  spaces	  (institutional).	  Chapter	  6	  focuses	  on	  how	  the	  
role	  of	  the	  researcher	  influences	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  make	  meaning	  of	  
street	  literature	  texts	  (situational).	  Lastly,	  Chapter	  7	  outlines	  the	  implications	  for	  future	  
research,	  implications	  for	  practice,	  and	  limitations	  of	  this	  study.	  	  
	  

	  

20	  
Chapter	  Two	  
Review	  of	  the	  Literature	  
	  

	  

	  

This	  chapter	  provides	  a	  robust	  review	  of	  the	  literature.	  Several	  bodies	  of	  literature	  

lay	  the	  foundation	  for	  this	  qualitative	  study,	  which	  include	  the	  following:	  Black	  Girlhood	  
(Part	  I),	  Black	  Adolescent	  Girls’	  Literacy	  Practices	  &	  Street	  Literature	  Texts	  (Part	  II),	  
Adolescent	  Literacy	  Practices	  in	  Book	  Clubs	  (Part	  III),	  and	  Adolescent	  Literacy	  Practices	  in	  
Digital	  Spaces	  (Part	  IV).	  	  
	  

Part	  I—Black	  Girlhood	  provides	  an	  in	  depth	  account	  of	  the	  marginalization	  of	  black	  

adolescent	  girls	  and	  the	  normalization	  of	  White	  femininity	  in	  the	  media.	  This	  literature	  
strand	  also	  highlights	  the	  stereotypical	  and	  hegemonic	  representations	  of	  black	  girls	  in	  a	  
media-­‐saturated	  environment.	  This	  literature	  strand	  highlights	  the	  educational	  landscape	  
in	  formal	  institutions	  and	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  these	  spaces	  mirror	  societal	  images	  of	  black	  
girls.	  This	  body	  of	  literature	  also	  provides	  an	  account	  of	  black	  adolescent	  girls’	  literacy	  and	  
language	  practices	  and	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  “safe	  spaces”	  provide	  outlets	  for	  black	  adolescent	  
girls.	  
	  

Part	  II—Black	  Adolescent	  Girls’	  Literacy	  Practices	  &	  Street	  Literature	  Texts	  examines	  

how	  black	  adolescent	  female	  readers	  make	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts	  in	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  
environments	  and	  how	  the	  meaning	  making	  process	  shapes	  their	  identities.	  This	  literature	  
strand	  also	  provides	  an	  in	  depth	  account	  of	  the	  literary	  trajectory	  and	  political	  landscape	  of	  
street	  literature	  texts	  and	  the	  need	  to	  bridge	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  and	  in-­‐school	  literacies	  in	  an	  
effort	  to	  create	  inclusive	  literacy	  environments.	  

	  

21	  
	  

Part	  III—Adolescent	  Literacy	  Practices	  in	  Book	  Clubs	  provides	  a	  detailed	  account	  of	  

the	  adolescent	  literacy	  practices	  situated	  both	  in	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  and	  in-­‐school	  spaces.	  This	  
strand	  of	  literature	  also	  highlights	  the	  divergent	  goals	  of	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  and	  in-­‐school	  book	  
clubs.	  This	  strand	  of	  literature	  further	  addresses	  key	  features	  of	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  book	  clubs	  
that	  create	  opportunities	  for	  student-­‐centered	  literacy	  experiences	  in	  school	  spaces.	  	  
	  

Part	  IV—Adolescent	  Literacy	  Practices	  in	  Digital	  Spaces	  provides	  an	  account	  of	  how	  

the	  “new”	  in	  New	  Literacy	  has	  re-­‐conceptualized	  adolescents’	  literacy	  practices.	  In	  doing	  
so,	  it	  also	  highlights	  how	  new	  literacies’	  participatory	  culture	  impacts	  the	  ways	  adolescents	  
communicate	  in	  digital	  spaces.	  The	  literature	  strand	  also	  examines	  how	  participatory	  
culture	  through	  the	  use	  of	  technologies	  has	  contributed	  to	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  adolescents	  
author	  their	  identities	  in	  digital	  spaces.	  	  
	  

This	  body	  of	  literature,	  comprised	  of	  four	  strands	  of	  literature	  Black	  Girlhood	  (Part	  

I),	  Black	  Adolescent	  Girls’	  Literacy	  Practices	  &	  Street	  Literature	  Texts	  (Part	  II),	  Adolescent	  
Literacy	  Practices	  in	  Book	  Clubs	  (Part	  III),	  and	  Adolescent	  Literacy	  Practices	  in	  Digital	  Spaces	  
(Part	  IV)	  informs	  three	  interconnected	  areas	  that	  drive	  this	  study:	  (a)	  Media	  
representations	  of	  black	  girls	  influence	  how	  they	  see	  themselves	  (Societal),	  (b)	  Black	  
adolescent	  girls	  make	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts	  (Institutional),	  and	  (c)	  The	  
researcher’s	  influence	  on	  how	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  make	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  
texts	  (Situational).	  
	  
Part	  I:	  Black	  Girlhood	  
In	  Black	  Girlhood	  Celebrations,	  Hip-­‐hop	  feminist	  Ruth	  Nicole	  Brown	  (2009)	  defines	  
Black	  girlhood	  as	  “the	  representations,	  memories,	  and	  lived	  experiences	  of	  being	  and	  

	  

22	  
becoming	  in	  a	  body	  marked	  as	  youthful,	  Black,	  and	  female”	  (p.	  x).	  Brown	  (2009)	  argues	  
that	  identity	  markers	  such	  as	  “Black,”	  “adolescent,”	  and	  “girl”	  locate	  persons	  in	  a	  larger	  
social	  structure	  as	  members	  of	  a	  marginalized	  groups	  (p.	  3).	  According	  to	  Brown	  (2009,	  p.	  
3,	  citing	  Cohen,	  Jones,	  &	  Tronto,	  1997),	  “the	  citizenship	  of	  Black	  girls	  is	  inherently	  tied	  to	  
their	  social-­‐political	  identity	  as	  members	  of	  a	  historically	  oppressed	  and	  marginalized	  
group.”	  	  
The	  marginalization	  of	  Black	  girls.	  As	  double	  minorities	  being	  both	  Black	  and	  
female,	  black	  girls	  are	  confronted	  with	  forms	  of	  oppression	  that	  work	  in	  conjunction	  with	  
other	  forms	  of	  oppression	  to	  produce	  social	  injustice	  and	  marginalization	  (Collins,	  2000).	  
Evans-­‐Winters	  (2011)	  points	  out	  that	  the	  intersection	  of	  race	  and	  gender	  positions	  Black	  
girls	  at	  the	  lowest	  caste	  within	  society	  resulting	  in	  Black	  girls	  experiences	  in	  particular	  
being	  “left	  out,	  whited	  out	  (subsumed	  under	  white	  girl’s	  experiences),	  blacked	  out	  
(generalized	  within	  the	  Black	  male	  experiences),	  or	  simply	  pathologized”	  (p.	  13).	  Collins	  
(2004)	  further	  states:	  
All	  women	  engage	  in	  an	  ideology	  that	  deems	  middle-­‐class,	  heterosexual,	  White	  
femininity	  as	  normative.	  In	  this	  context,	  Black	  femininity	  as	  a	  subordinated	  gender	  
identity	  becomes	  constructed	  not	  just	  in	  relation	  to	  white	  women,	  but	  also	  in	  
relation	  to	  multiple	  others,	  namely,	  all	  men,	  sexual	  outlaws,	  unmarried	  women,	  and	  
girls.	  These	  benchmarks	  construct	  a	  discourse	  of	  a	  hegemonic	  (White)	  femininity	  
that	  becomes	  a	  normative	  yardstick	  for	  all	  femininities	  in	  which	  Black	  women	  
typically	  are	  relegated	  to	  the	  bottom	  of	  the	  gender	  hierarchy.	  (p.	  193)	  
	  
Black	  girls’	  experiences	  are	  often	  generalized	  with	  the	  experiences	  of	  black	  males	  
and	  White,	  western,	  middle	  class	  girls,	  which	  continues	  to	  leave	  black	  girls	  voiceless	  and	  
their	  experiences	  invisible	  (Collins,	  2000;	  Fordham,	  1993;	  Lorde,	  1984;	  Peoples,	  2008).	  	  
Controlling	  images	  of	  Black	  girls	  and	  self-­‐identity.	  According	  to	  Richardson	  
(2007)	  “hegemonic	  ideologies	  work	  in	  tandem	  with	  economic,	  political,	  and	  social	  

	  

23	  
structures	  to	  maintain	  societal	  order	  and	  control	  how	  people	  view	  and	  conduct	  
themselves”	  (p.	  806).	  Richardson	  (2007)	  further	  argues	  that	  “popular	  culture	  and	  the	  
media	  work	  in	  tandem	  to	  produce	  and	  sustain	  stereotypical	  and	  hegemonic	  
representations	  of	  black	  women	  and	  girls”	  (p.	  790).	  “This	  is	  complicated	  by	  the	  fact	  that	  
black	  girls	  formulate	  their	  identities	  in	  a	  media-­‐saturated	  environment	  fraught	  with	  
stereotypes	  and	  controlling	  images	  of	  black	  girlhood”	  (Richardson,	  2007,	  p.	  790).	  For	  many	  
black	  adolescent	  girls,	  their	  identity	  is	  largely	  shaped	  through	  images	  of	  themselves	  seen	  in	  
a	  various	  media	  outlets—movies,	  television,	  magazines,	  music,	  and	  literature,	  including	  
patriarchal	  hip-­‐hop	  culture.	  
In	  instances,	  in	  which	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  are	  deemed	  visible	  it	  is	  often	  based	  on	  
their	  willingness	  to	  conform	  to	  existing	  ideas	  of	  black	  womanhood	  and	  femininity,	  which	  
often	  resemble	  notions	  of	  controlling	  images	  that	  continue	  to	  perpetuate	  their	  positions	  
within	  society	  (Collins,	  2000;	  Richardson,	  2007).	  Originating	  during	  antebellum	  slavery,	  
traditional	  controlling	  images	  of	  black	  girls	  have	  included	  images	  of	  the	  superwoman,	  the	  
mammy,	  and	  the	  jezebel	  to	  name	  a	  few.	  Many	  Hip-­‐hop	  feminist	  scholars	  contend	  that	  these	  
images	  have	  transformed	  into	  more	  contemporary	  images	  of	  black	  girls	  via	  popular	  culture,	  
including	  the	  video	  vixen,	  the	  baby	  momma,	  welfare	  mother,	  ghetto	  bitch,	  
strongblackwoman,	  and	  the	  hoochie	  momma	  (Morgan	  1999;	  Pough,	  2004;	  Richardson,	  
2007;	  Springer,	  2002).	  	  
Black	  feminist	  scholar	  Patricia	  Hill	  Collins	  (2000)	  suggests	  that	  such	  “controlling	  
images	  of	  black	  women	  are	  fundamental	  to	  Black	  women’s	  oppression	  and	  these	  socially	  
constructed	  images	  are	  designed	  to	  make	  racism,	  sexism,	  and	  poverty,	  social	  hegemony,	  
and	  other	  injustices	  appear	  to	  be	  normal	  within	  society”	  (p.	  69).	  Controlling	  images	  are	  

	  

24	  
deeply	  entrenched	  in	  everyday	  society	  and	  reproduce	  controlling	  images	  designed	  to	  
marginalize	  black	  girls.	  Formal	  education	  institutions	  are	  often	  politically	  driven	  and	  
constitute	  important	  sites	  for	  reproducing	  controlling	  images	  (Brown,	  2009;	  Collins,	  2000;	  
Evans-­‐Winters,	  2011).	  These	  formal	  spaces	  often	  mirror	  societal	  images	  of	  black	  girls	  and	  
stereotype	  black	  girls	  as	  loud,	  sexual,	  disruptive,	  and	  aggressive	  (Brown,	  2009;	  Fordham,	  
1993).	  	  	  
	  

Literacy	  and	  language	  practices.	  In	  response	  to	  the	  stereotypical	  images	  of	  black	  

girls	  that	  are	  embedded	  within	  popular	  culture,	  “black	  girls	  have	  developed	  language	  and	  
literacy	  practices	  to	  counteract	  White	  supremacist	  images	  of	  Black	  women	  that	  convey	  
subhuman	  and	  immoral	  images”	  (Richardson,	  2002,	  p.	  677).	  Black	  linguist	  scholar	  Geneva	  
Smitherman	  (2006,	  1977)	  argues	  that	  black	  girls	  are	  equipped	  with	  language	  practices	  or	  
what	  is	  known	  as	  the	  Mother	  Tongue	  that	  allow	  them	  to	  make	  sense	  of	  the	  world.	  
Smitherman	  (2006,	  1997)	  describes	  the	  Mother	  Tongue	  as	  a	  set	  of	  language	  practices	  that	  
originate	  in	  the	  home	  that	  black	  girls	  are	  conditioned	  to	  use,	  in	  order	  to	  socialize	  with	  
others	  in	  the	  world.	  Hip-­‐hop	  feminist	  Elaine	  Richardson	  (2002)	  asserts	  that	  “the	  
suppression	  of	  the	  mother	  tongue	  further	  subordinates	  African	  Americans	  in	  society	  and	  in	  
educational	  institutions	  and	  is	  unhealthy	  for	  African	  Americans	  who	  are	  members	  of	  the	  
African	  American	  Discourse	  community”	  (p.	  678).	  Often	  times,	  black	  girls	  are	  forced	  to	  
suppress	  their	  Mother	  Tongue	  in	  school	  spaces,	  in	  order	  to	  assimilate	  linguistically	  through	  
the	  use	  of	  Standard	  English.	  This	  practice	  fails	  to	  create	  an	  inclusive	  learning	  environment	  
for	  diverse	  populations	  and	  further	  perpetuates	  the	  invisibility	  of	  black	  girls	  and	  their	  
home	  language	  practices	  (Richardson,	  2002;	  Smitherman,	  2006).	  There	  needs	  to	  be	  more	  
alignment	  between	  black	  girls’	  literacy	  and	  language	  practices	  within	  the	  home	  and	  the	  

	  

25	  
community	  and	  the	  literacy	  and	  language	  practices	  that	  are	  sanctioned	  in	  school	  spaces.	  
This	  requires	  schools	  to	  incorporate	  the	  black	  girls’	  literacy	  and	  language	  practices	  into	  the	  
curricula.	  This	  creates	  school	  spaces	  that	  value	  diversity	  and	  promote	  inclusivity.	  
	  

Safe	  spaces	  and	  coming	  to	  voice.	  With	  hegemonic	  ideologies	  embedded	  in	  both	  

society	  and	  formal	  institutions,	  black	  girls	  often	  seek	  out	  safe	  spaces	  as	  a	  form	  of	  Black	  
female	  resistance	  (Collins,	  2000).	  Historically,	  these	  alternative	  spaces	  were	  “safe”	  because	  
they	  provided	  an	  outlet	  for	  black	  girls	  and	  black	  women	  to	  discuss	  freely	  issues	  that	  were	  
of	  concern	  to	  them	  both	  individually	  and	  collectively	  (McHenry,	  2002).	  This	  provides	  black	  
girls	  a	  level	  of	  consciousness	  in	  order	  to	  resist	  controlling	  images.	  One	  form	  of	  safe	  space	  
involves	  black	  girls	  being	  in	  relationship	  with	  one	  another	  in	  informal	  spaces	  (Collins,	  
2000;	  McHenry,	  2002).	  Black	  girls	  listening	  and	  speaking	  to	  one	  another	  is	  significant,	  
particularly	  given	  their	  marginalized	  positions	  in	  society	  and	  the	  importance	  of	  voice	  in	  
affirming	  spaces	  (McHenry,	  2002;	  Collins,	  2000;	  Lorde,	  1984).	  McElroy-­‐Johnson	  (1993)	  
defines	  voice	  as	  “a	  strong	  sense	  of	  identity	  within	  an	  individual,	  an	  ability	  to	  express	  a	  
personal	  point	  of	  view,	  and	  a	  sense	  of	  personal	  well-­‐being”	  (pp.	  85-­‐86).	  McElroy-­‐Johnson	  
(1993)	  further	  argues	  that	  voice	  creates	  opportunities	  for	  youth	  to	  be	  fully	  engaged	  in	  the	  
learning	  process	  and	  to	  be	  to	  be	  an	  active	  contributor	  to	  the	  learning	  community.	  
Several	  scholars	  explored	  the	  possibilities	  and	  potential	  of	  black	  girls	  and	  the	  
influence	  of	  safe	  spaces	  on	  self-­‐expression.	  In	  a	  qualitative	  study	  situated	  in	  an	  afterschool	  
program,	  Wissman	  (2008)	  documented	  the	  constant	  struggles	  and	  marginalization	  that	  
black	  girls	  are	  confronted	  with	  in	  school	  spaces.	  Wissman	  argued	  that	  school	  spaces,	  
specifically	  urban	  schools,	  are	  often	  plagued	  with	  decontextualized	  curricula,	  constant	  
surveillance,	  and	  silencing	  of	  the	  literacy	  and	  language	  practices	  of	  black	  girls.	  Drawing	  

	  

26	  
upon	  a	  black	  feminist	  framework,	  Wissman	  found	  that	  black	  girls	  used	  poetry	  and	  
photography	  as	  forms	  of	  self-­‐expression.	  She	  also	  found	  that	  black	  girls	  used	  oral	  and	  
written	  language	  to	  resist	  the	  discourses	  and	  institutional	  practices	  in	  school	  spaces.	  	  	  
Conversely,	  Henry	  (1998)	  examined	  black	  girls’	  use	  of	  voice	  in	  a	  reading	  and	  writing	  
program	  in	  a	  middle	  school	  classroom	  and	  found	  that	  participants	  displayed	  difficulty	  
using	  their	  own	  voices	  in	  their	  writing.	  Drawing	  upon	  a	  Black	  Feminist	  framework,	  Henry	  
(1998)	  found	  that	  black	  girls	  often	  conformed	  to	  patriarchal	  norms	  in	  society	  and	  drew	  
upon	  issues	  critical	  to	  their	  own	  lives.	  Similarly,	  in	  a	  qualitative	  study	  Sutherland	  (2005)	  
examined	  societal	  norms	  and	  documented	  the	  literacy	  practices,	  identity,	  and	  social	  
positioning	  of	  black	  girls	  as	  they	  discussed	  Toni	  Morrison’s	  The	  Bluest	  Eye	  in	  their	  high	  
school	  English	  classroom.	  Sutherland	  (2005)	  found	  that	  Eurocentric	  views	  of	  beauty	  served	  
as	  boundaries	  in	  their	  lives.	  Sutherland	  (2005)	  also	  found	  that	  other	  people’s	  assumptions	  
about	  black	  girls	  also	  served	  as	  boundaries.	  The	  participants	  in	  the	  Sutherland	  study	  
viewed	  the	  text	  as	  a	  tool	  for	  understanding,	  questioning,	  and	  positioning	  themselves	  in	  the	  
world.	  Sutherland	  (2005)	  also	  argued	  that	  texts	  with	  characters	  that	  are	  from	  the	  same	  
racial	  and	  gender	  group	  are	  self-­‐affirming	  for	  black	  girls.	  	  Sutherland	  further	  asserts	  that	  
black	  girls	  should	  see	  themselves	  reflected	  in	  the	  literature	  through	  the	  implementation	  of	  
more	  inclusive,	  culturally	  relevant,	  and	  girl-­‐centered	  curricula.	  Similarly,	  DeBlase	  (2003)	  
documented	  black	  girls’	  gendered	  transactions	  with	  both	  canonical	  and	  popular	  text.	  
Drawing	  upon	  a	  Black	  Feminist	  Perspective,	  DeBlase	  (2003)	  found	  that	  black	  girls	  
displayed	  tensions	  between	  tradition	  and	  liberal	  representations	  of	  women.	  
Hip-­‐hop	  feminism.	  The	  black	  feminist	  movement	  has	  been	  pivotal	  in	  attaining	  
more	  privileges,	  social	  freedoms,	  and	  equality	  for	  black	  women;	  however,	  black	  girls	  of	  this	  

	  

27	  
generation	  continue	  to	  be	  confronted	  with	  new	  challenges.	  Bombarded	  by	  mass	  media,	  
digital	  technologies,	  and	  popular	  culture,	  black	  girls	  need	  a	  version	  of	  feminism	  suited	  to	  
address	  the	  new	  issues	  confronting	  them.	  Hip-­‐hop	  feminism	  is	  a	  culturally	  and	  
generationally	  critical	  framework	  that	  focuses	  on	  contemporary	  systematic	  issues,	  such	  as	  
colorism,	  misogyny,	  and	  sexual	  abuse	  that	  confront	  young	  black	  girls	  (Pough,	  2002;	  
Richardson,	  2007).	  Hip-­‐hop	  Feminism,	  situated	  at	  the	  intersection	  of	  several	  antagonistic	  
constructs,	  including	  feminist	  ideologies,	  consciousness,	  and	  hip-­‐hop	  culture	  offers	  a	  new	  
and	  distinct	  version	  of	  feminism	  that	  addresses	  the	  needs	  of	  young	  black	  girls	  in	  the	  21st	  
Century.	  An	  unpalatable	  aspect	  of	  hip-­‐hop	  culture	  is	  the	  “glorification	  of	  sex,	  violence,	  
materialism,	  and	  misogyny”	  (Henry,	  2010,	  p.	  143).	  “This	  glorification	  is	  rooted	  in	  
hegemony	  and	  commodifies	  blackness	  and	  perpetuates	  the	  status	  quo	  and	  existing	  
structures	  of	  inequality”	  (Love,	  2012,	  p.	  18).	  According	  to	  Pough	  (2002),	  “it	  is	  the	  
objectionable	  elements	  of	  hip-­‐hop	  culture	  that	  are	  part	  of	  what	  makes	  it	  valuable	  to	  
feminism	  because	  they	  provide	  the	  opportunity	  for	  youth	  to	  analyze	  and	  hone	  their	  critical	  
literacy	  skills”	  (pp.	  92-­‐93).	  	  
Richardson’s	  (2007)	  qualitative	  study,	  examined	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  black	  girls	  
analyze	  and	  negotiate	  stereotypical	  and	  hegemonic	  representations	  of	  women	  in	  mass	  
media,	  specifically	  hip-­‐hop	  music	  videos.	  	  Richardson	  focused	  on	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  black	  
girls	  demonstrate	  literacy,	  language,	  and	  knowledge-­‐making	  that	  exist	  when	  they	  
participate	  in	  the	  hip-­‐hop	  culture.	  The	  study	  also	  provided	  black	  girls	  with	  an	  opportunity	  
to	  explore	  the	  politics	  of	  gender	  and	  sexuality	  in	  its	  many	  forms	  (Collins,	  2000).	  Drawing	  
upon	  a	  Critical	  Discourse	  framework,	  Richardson	  (2007)	  found	  that	  “black	  girls	  exhibited	  
both	  alignment	  and	  conflict	  between	  black	  and	  dominant	  discourses	  with	  regards	  to	  the	  

	  

28	  
social	  construction	  of	  women”	  (p.	  793).	  Similarly,	  Richardson’s	  (2013)	  qualitative	  study	  
situated	  in	  an	  afterschool	  club,	  examined	  black	  girls’	  discourse	  on	  the	  meaning	  of	  black	  
womanhood	  in	  American	  society.	  Richardson	  focused	  on	  her	  positionality	  as	  a	  researcher	  
through	  the	  use	  of	  self-­‐reflexivity	  and	  reflected	  on	  her	  own	  Black	  respectability	  politics	  and	  
the	  perpetuation	  of	  sexist	  patriarchal	  discourse.	  Drawing	  upon	  Hip-­‐hop	  Feminist	  Literacies,	  
African	  American	  Women’s	  Literacies,	  and	  Critical	  Discourse	  Perspectives,	  Richardson	  
(2013)	  found	  that	  black	  girls	  revealed	  both	  competing	  and	  controlling	  discourses	  of	  Black	  
female	  sexuality.	  Competing	  and	  controlling	  discourses	  of	  Black	  female	  sexuality	  are	  
indications	  of	  black	  girls’	  constant	  identity	  negotiations.	  Black	  girls	  are	  constantly	  in	  flux	  
due	  to	  the	  vast	  images	  of	  Black	  female	  sexuality	  they	  are	  bombarded	  with	  in	  the	  media,	  
specifically	  hip-­‐hop	  culture.	  
Brown	  (2009)	  highlighted	  the	  role	  of	  popular	  culture	  in	  the	  lives	  of	  black	  girls.	  
Brown’s	  ethnographic	  study	  (2009)	  examined	  black	  girls’	  use	  of	  popular	  culture	  to	  
illustrate	  their	  personal	  truths	  and	  lived	  experiences.	  Drawing	  upon	  a	  Hip-­‐hop	  feminist	  and	  
critical	  pedagogy	  framework,	  Brown	  (2009)	  found	  that	  black	  girls	  used	  elements	  of	  Hip-­‐
hop	  to	  construct	  their	  identities	  and	  make	  sense	  of	  the	  world.	  Similarly,	  Love’s	  (2012)	  
qualitative	  study	  situated	  in	  an	  afterschool	  setting	  focused	  on	  Black	  adolescent	  girls’	  
meaning	  making	  of	  popular	  culture	  and	  its	  influence	  on	  their	  racial	  and	  gendered	  identities.	  
Love	  (2012)	  found	  that	  black	  girls	  used	  elements	  of	  hip-­‐hop	  to	  construct	  their	  identities	  
and	  make	  sense	  of	  the	  world.	  Pough	  (2004)	  extended	  the	  Hip-­‐hop	  framework	  and	  explored	  
the	  construction	  of	  female	  characters	  in	  two	  popular	  street	  literature	  texts.	  Pough’s	  
analyses	  showcased	  two	  popular	  street	  literature	  texts,	  which	  included	  Omar	  Tyree’s	  Flyy	  
Girl	  and	  Sister	  Souljah’s	  Coldest	  Winter	  Ever.	  Pough	  (2004)	  found	  that	  the	  male	  author	  

	  

29	  
Omar	  Tyree’s	  views	  of	  female	  protagonist	  Tracy	  in	  Flyy	  Girl	  limited	  societal	  impact	  on	  who	  
she	  was	  and	  how	  she	  behaved,	  while	  Sister	  Souljah’s	  views	  of	  female	  protagonist	  Winter	  in	  
The	  Coldest	  Winter	  Ever	  accounted	  for	  societal	  impact	  on	  her	  life.	  Street	  literature	  texts,	  
entrenched	  in	  elements	  of	  Hip-­‐hop,	  often	  depict	  black	  female	  protagonist	  both	  conforming	  
to	  and	  resisting	  gendered	  social	  norms.	  The	  myriad	  of	  representations	  of	  black	  girlhood	  
often	  informs	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  black	  girls	  negotiate	  and	  construct	  their	  identities.	  
Research	  trends	  on	  Black	  girlhood.	  There	  is	  a	  dearth	  of	  literature	  on	  black	  
girlhood.	  Much	  of	  the	  literature	  on	  the	  literacy	  and	  language	  practices	  of	  black	  girls	  has	  
been	  situated	  in	  both	  the	  field	  of	  education	  and	  rhetorical	  studies	  and	  has	  been	  published	  
in	  empirical	  journals	  in	  both	  fields,	  including	  Equity	  &	  Excellence	  in	  Education	  (Richardson,	  
2013),	  Journal	  of	  Adolescent	  &	  Adult	  Literacy	  (DeBlase,	  2003;	  Wissman,	  2008),	  Journal	  of	  
Literacy	  Research	  (Henry,	  1998;	  Sutherland,	  2005),	  and	  Discourse	  &	  Society	  (Richardson,	  
2007).	  	  There	  has	  been	  a	  dearth	  of	  literature	  on	  the	  literacy	  and	  language	  practices	  of	  black	  
girls	  published	  in	  the	  field	  of	  library	  and	  information	  science.	  	  With	  limited	  research	  on
