black	  girls’	  experiences	  in	  the	  field	  of	  library	  and	  information	  science,	  library	  professionals	  
may	  often	  develop	  programs	  and	  offer	  reader	  advisory	  services	  that	  do	  not	  effectively	  meet	  
the	  needs	  of	  diverse	  populations.	  Within	  the	  dearth	  of	  literature	  on	  black	  girls’	  literacy	  and	  
language	  practices,	  much	  of	  it	  has	  focused	  primarily	  on	  their	  interactions	  in	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  
environments.	  The	  literacy	  and	  language	  practices	  of	  black	  girls	  in	  digital	  spaces	  have	  
remained	  underrepresented	  in	  the	  library	  and	  information	  science	  literature.	  
Much	  of	  the	  literature	  in	  the	  field	  of	  education	  and	  rhetorical	  studies	  that	  has	  
focused	  on	  the	  literacy	  and	  language	  practices	  of	  black	  girls	  has	  been	  situated	  within	  two	  
main	  areas	  of	  research,	  including	  (a)	  stereotypical	  representations	  of	  black	  females	  and	  (b)	  

	  

30	  
black	  girls’	  meaning	  making	  of	  popular	  culture	  texts.	  Two	  critical	  theoretical	  frameworks,	  
Black	  Feminist	  Thought	  and	  Hip-­‐hop	  Feminism	  were	  prominent	  frameworks	  that	  drove	  
several	  empirical	  studies	  on	  black	  girls’	  experiences.	  Black	  Feminist	  Thought	  (DeBlase,	  
2003;	  Henry,	  1998)	  accounted	  for	  hegemonic	  ideologies	  and	  the	  ways	  that	  participants	  
used	  their	  voice	  to	  negotiate	  their	  racialized	  and	  gendered	  identities.	  Hip-­‐hop	  Feminism	  
(Brown,	  2009;	  Love,	  2012;	  Pough,	  2004;	  Richardson,	  2013)	  accounted	  for	  the	  media	  
saturated	  environment	  that	  black	  girls	  are	  confronted	  with	  and	  the	  generationally	  relevant	  
issues,	  including	  misogyny,	  sexuality	  that	  influence	  the	  representation	  of	  black	  girls.	  
The	  current	  study	  addressed	  many	  of	  the	  gaps	  in	  the	  literature	  on	  the	  literacy	  and	  
language	  practices	  of	  black	  girls.	  This	  study	  extended	  the	  current	  body	  of	  literature	  and	  
examined	  the	  ways	  black	  girls	  make	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts	  in	  digitally	  mediated	  
spaces.	  The	  current	  study	  also	  extended	  the	  Hip-­‐hop	  feminist	  framework	  and	  examined	  
street	  literature,	  a	  sub-­‐genre	  rooted	  in	  Hip-­‐hop	  aesthetics.	  The	  current	  study	  also	  focused	  
on	  generationally	  relevant	  issues	  that	  black	  girls	  are	  confronted	  with	  in	  the	  21st	  century,	  
including	  female	  sexuality,	  the	  black	  female	  condition,	  black	  motherhood	  and	  black	  
masculinity.	  The	  current	  study	  also	  extended	  the	  body	  of	  literature	  by	  accounting	  for	  
societal,	  institutional,	  and	  situational	  factors	  that	  influences	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  black	  girls	  
make	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts.	  	  
	  
Part	  II:	  Street	  Literature	  Texts	  
	  

Street	  literature,	  also	  known	  as	  urban	  fiction	  texts,	  is	  a	  collection	  of	  culturally	  

conscious,	  coming	  of	  age	  texts	  that	  graphically	  depict	  the	  ills	  of	  inner	  city	  life	  and	  vividly	  
illustrate	  the	  impact	  of	  African	  Americans	  living	  in	  marginalized	  societies	  (Hill,	  Perez,	  &	  

	  

31	  
Irby,	  2008;	  Morris,	  2007).	  This	  sub-­‐genre	  has	  been	  referred	  to	  as	  street	  literature	  (Morris,	  
Hughes-­‐Hassell,	  &	  Agosto,	  2006),	  hip-­‐hop	  literature	  (Meloni,	  2007;	  Osborne,	  2001),	  Black	  
pulp	  fiction	  (Osborne,	  2001),	  ghetto	  lit	  (Stovall,	  2005),	  urban	  fiction	  (Marshall,	  Staples,	  &	  
Gibson,	  2009),	  gangsta	  lit	  (Venable,	  2004),	  and	  street	  fiction	  (Morris,	  2007;	  Hill	  et	  al.,	  
2008).	  Gibson	  (2010)	  describes	  the	  characteristics	  of	  urban	  fiction	  literature	  as,	  	  
generally	  written	  in	  first	  person,	  from	  the	  perspective	  of	  the	  protagonist.	  The	  text	  is	  
often	  written	  in	  nonstandard	  English	  and	  prominently	  features	  the	  use	  of	  slang	  and	  
profanity.	  This	  genre	  appeals	  to	  disengaged	  populations	  of	  readers	  who	  desire	  to	  
read	  and	  interact	  with	  texts	  and	  issues	  presented	  within	  the	  genre.	  (p.	  567)	  
	  
Street	  literature	  texts	  typically	  take	  place	  in	  major	  cities,	  like	  New	  York,	  
Philadelphia,	  and	  Baltimore,	  to	  name	  a	  few.	  Morris	  (2010)	  describes	  street	  literature	  as	  
location	  and	  setting	  specific.	  Morris	  (2012)	  further	  states	  that	  prominent	  elements	  of	  street	  
literature	  texts,	  such	  as	  backdrop	  within	  an	  inner	  city	  community	  is	  location	  specific,	  which	  
results	  in	  street	  literature	  texts	  found	  in	  various	  formats	  across	  genres,	  such	  as	  picture	  
books,	  graphic	  novels,	  and	  poetry.	  	  
Literary	  features	  of	  street	  literature	  texts.	  In	  addition	  to	  street	  literature	  texts	  
being	  location	  and	  setting	  specific,	  it	  tends	  to	  also	  be	  language	  specific	  drawing	  upon	  
elements	  of	  African-­‐American	  Vernacular	  English	  (AAVE),	  hip-­‐hop	  slang,	  and	  other	  
regional	  dialects.	  According	  to	  Morris	  (2012),	  “the	  deliberate	  use	  of	  African-­‐American	  
Vernacular	  English	  (AAVE),	  hip-­‐hop	  slang,	  and	  other	  regional	  dialects	  does	  not	  suggest	  that	  
the	  texts	  are	  poorly	  written”	  (p.	  7).	  The	  features	  in	  street	  literature	  text	  target	  an	  audience	  
who	  is	  literate	  in	  the	  particular	  language	  style.	  African-­‐American	  Vernacular	  English,	  hip-­‐
hop	  slang,	  and	  other	  regional	  dialects	  serve	  as	  tools	  for	  storytelling	  and	  also	  function	  as	  
forms	  of	  resistance	  from	  more	  mainstream,	  dominant	  language	  practices.	  	  

	  

32	  
Based	  on	  a	  textual	  analysis	  of	  street	  literature	  texts,	  Morris	  (2010)	  identified	  
prominent	  features	  of	  the	  sub-­‐genre:	  
•

Fast	  paced	  stories	  that	  often	  include	  flashback	  sequences;	  

•

Vivid	  depictions	  of	  the	  inner-­‐city	  environment,	  including	  lack	  of	  societal	  
resources	  and	  poverty;	  

•

Female	  and	  male	  identity	  formation	  (via	  relationships,	  often	  romantic	  in	  
nature),	  with	  protagonist	  often	  being	  young	  adults	  (common	  age	  range	  tends	  
to	  be	  between	  nineteen	  and	  twenty	  five);	  

•

Navigation	  of	  interpersonal	  relationships,	  including	  surviving	  sexual	  and/or	  
physical	  abuse,	  crime	  and	  violence,	  drugs,	  incarceration,	  teen	  pregnancy	  and	  
parenthood,	  betrayal	  in	  friendships,	  and	  fantastical	  revenge	  plots;	  

•

Commodification	  of	  particular	  lifestyles;	  

•

Surviving	  street	  life	  and	  overcoming	  street	  lifestyle—the	  challenge	  of	  moving	  
up	  and	  away	  from	  the	  streets.	  (p.	  3)	  

Due	  to	  its	  gritty,	  action	  packed	  stories	  of	  street	  life	  and	  survival,	  and	  the	  use	  of	  what	  
has	  been	  described	  as	  poor	  grammar,	  weak	  sentence	  structure,	  and	  improper	  word	  usage,	  
authors	  of	  street	  literature	  texts	  were	  often	  unable	  to	  secure	  major	  publishing	  deals	  
(Gibson,	  2010;	  Hill	  et	  al.,	  2008).	  This	  resulted	  in	  street	  literature	  texts	  being	  published	  by	  
independent	  presses,	  or	  by	  the	  authors	  themselves	  (Honig,	  2011).	  “Unlike	  most	  successful	  
book	  movements,	  the	  street	  literature	  genre	  has	  thrived	  without	  the	  support	  of	  
mainstream	  publishing	  houses”	  (Hill	  et	  al.,	  2008,	  p.	  77).	  Despite	  the	  lack	  of	  market	  support	  
of	  street	  literature	  by	  major	  publishing	  houses,	  street	  literature	  became	  popular	  through	  
community	  channels,	  word	  of	  mouth	  and	  were	  sold	  through	  independent	  bookstores,	  

	  

33	  
street	  vendors,	  and	  the	  Internet,	  as	  well	  as	  in	  “underground”	  outlets	  (Hill	  et	  al.,	  2008;	  
Morris,	  2012).	  	  
Street	  literature’s	  appeal	  to	  Black	  adolescent	  girls.	  Street	  literature	  was	  
originally	  written	  for	  adults;	  however,	  the	  genre	  experienced	  an	  expansion	  in	  its	  
readership	  among	  young	  adults	  in	  the	  early	  1990s	  (Meloni,	  2007).	  The	  popularity	  of	  street	  
literature	  through	  the	  growth	  of	  its	  readership	  and	  its	  appeal	  among	  urban	  youth	  has	  
catapulted	  it	  into	  the	  mainstream.	  In	  the	  past	  decade,	  street	  literature	  gained	  significant	  
readership	  among	  African	  American	  readers,	  particularly	  women	  between	  the	  ages	  of	  18-­‐
35	  and	  teenagers	  ages	  14	  and	  up	  (Morris	  et	  al.,	  2006;	  Publishers	  Weekly,	  2004;	  Rosen,	  
2004).	  According	  to	  Morris	  et	  al.	  (2006),	  street	  literature	  texts	  typically	  draw	  a	  
predominantly	  female	  audience	  most	  likely	  due	  to	  the	  fact	  that	  the	  protagonist	  tends	  to	  be	  
female.	  
	  

According	  to	  Gibson	  (2010),	  one	  of	  the	  reasons	  that	  street	  literature	  appeals	  to	  

African	  American	  youth	  “is	  the	  fact	  that	  many	  street	  literature	  writers	  are	  African	  
American	  authors	  who	  hail	  from	  the	  neighborhoods	  featured	  within	  their	  texts,	  and	  they	  
employ	  references	  to	  real	  cities	  and	  neighborhoods”	  (p.	  567).	  Authors	  of	  street	  literature	  
texts	  tend	  to	  write	  about	  controversial	  topics	  that	  urban	  adolescents	  may	  face,	  such	  as	  teen	  
pregnancy,	  violence,	  and	  racism.	  Authors	  incorporate	  topics	  many	  view	  as	  taboo	  into	  their	  
stories	  as	  a	  way	  of	  authenticating	  the	  experiences	  of	  urban	  youth	  (Hughes-­‐Hassell	  &	  Guild,	  
2002).	  Osborne	  (2001)	  argues	  that	  it	  is	  these	  “authentic	  renderings”	  that	  urban	  youth	  tend	  
to	  have	  a	  strong	  connection	  to.	  	  
Another	  reason	  that	  street	  literature	  appeals	  to	  African	  American	  youth	  is	  because	  it	  
draws	  upon	  popular	  culture	  elements	  (Jocson,	  2005;	  Mahiri,	  2005;	  Morrell,	  2004b,	  2008).	  

	  

34	  
“Nowhere	  is	  the	  influence	  of	  hip-­‐hop	  more	  apparent	  than	  in	  the	  literary	  genre,	  urban	  
fiction”	  (Morris	  et	  al.,	  2006,	  p.	  17).	  Hip-­‐hop	  has	  had	  a	  profound	  impact	  on	  street	  literature.	  
“Many	  scholars	  argue	  that	  Hip-­‐hop	  music	  is	  the	  representative	  voice	  of	  urban	  youth	  
because	  the	  genre	  was	  created	  by	  and	  for	  urban	  youth”	  (Morrell,	  2002,	  p.	  74).	  “With	  its	  
urban	  and	  political	  roots,	  hip-­‐hop	  has	  given	  voice	  to	  groups	  that	  have	  been	  historically	  
marginalized	  and	  disenfranchised—the	  poor,	  urban,	  and	  young”	  (Kim,	  2011,	  p.	  160).	  In	  
addition	  to	  hip-­‐hop	  being	  an	  expression	  of	  African	  American	  youth	  culture,	  it	  also	  has	  
illuminated	  the	  social	  issues	  present	  in	  urban	  communities	  (Jocson,	  2005).	  
Black	  adolescent	  girls’	  meaning	  making	  of	  street	  literature	  texts.	  Strong	  
connections	  to	  experiences	  and	  elements	  familiar	  to	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  provide	  them	  
with	  an	  opportunity	  to	  make	  sense	  of	  and	  take	  power	  in	  their	  worlds	  through	  their	  literacy	  
practices	  (Bean	  &	  Moni,	  2003;	  Freire,	  2001;	  Moje,	  2000,	  2002;	  Morrell,	  2004b).	  Morris	  
(2007,	  p.	  5,	  citing	  Delpit,	  2006)	  states	  that	  “reading	  is	  essentially	  a	  meaning-­‐making	  
process.”	  Morris	  (2007)	  extends	  Delpit’s	  argument	  and	  asserts	  that,	  
for	  street	  literature	  readers	  this	  “meaning	  making	  process”	  allows	  them	  to	  look	  at	  
life	  critically	  in	  order	  to	  “know	  what’s	  going	  on”	  and	  is	  key	  to	  survival.	  Urban	  fiction	  
plays	  an	  important	  role	  in	  not	  only	  heightening	  their	  resistances	  to	  the	  unsavory	  
people	  and	  locations	  surrounding	  them,	  but	  it	  also	  strengthens	  resiliencies,	  allowing	  
them	  to	  carve	  out	  a	  sober	  space	  within	  their	  neighborhoods.	  (p.	  5)	  
	  
Underlying	  meaning	  making	  is	  Freire’s	  (2001)	  critical	  literacy	  framework,	  which	  is	  
rooted	  in	  reading	  the	  world	  and	  analyzing	  the	  world	  using	  a	  critical	  lens.	  Black	  adolescent	  
girls’	  connections	  to	  street	  literature	  texts	  may	  not	  necessarily	  relate	  directly	  to	  the	  
protagonist,	  however	  they	  seem	  themselves	  “titillated,	  provoked,	  and	  challenged	  by	  the	  
characterizations,	  situations,	  and	  language	  in	  text”	  (Gibson,	  2010,	  p.	  568).	  	  

	  

35	  
Morris	  (2007)	  explored	  the	  emergence	  of	  street	  literature	  among	  urban	  youth	  on	  
the	  streets	  of	  North	  Philadelphia.	  Morris’	  study	  (2007)	  examined	  the	  emergence	  of	  urban	  
fiction	  on	  the	  streets	  of	  Philadelphia	  and	  the	  influence	  of	  hip-­‐hop	  culture	  on	  the	  reading	  
choices	  of	  urban	  youth	  in	  an	  urban	  fiction	  book	  club	  situated	  in	  an	  urban	  public	  library.	  
Employing	  a	  Reader	  Response	  Theory,	  Morris	  found	  that	  there	  is	  a	  connection	  between	  the	  
worlds	  depicted	  in	  street	  literature	  novels	  and	  the	  worlds	  that	  youth	  navigate	  on	  a	  daily	  
basis.	  Morris	  (2007)	  further	  revealed	  that	  urban	  youth	  come	  to	  street	  literature	  with	  
shared	  narratives	  from	  their	  own	  life	  experiences.	  Morris	  (2007)	  concluded	  that	  street	  
literature	  texts’	  influence	  on	  hip-­‐hop	  culture	  encourages	  a	  more	  critical	  read	  of	  urban	  
youths’	  lived	  experiences	  and	  enhances	  their	  critical	  literacies.	  Hence,	  not	  only	  do	  urban	  
youth	  identify	  with	  characters	  and	  experiences	  depicted	  in	  street	  literature,	  they	  also	  turn	  
around	  and	  look	  critically	  at	  their	  own	  lives.	  
	  

Gibson	  (2010)	  extended	  the	  work	  of	  Morris	  (2007)	  and	  specifically	  explored	  black	  

adolescent	  girls’	  meaning	  making	  of	  street	  literature	  texts.	  Employing	  Critical	  Literacy,	  
Reader	  Response	  Theory,	  and	  New	  Literacy	  Studies,	  Gibson	  found	  that	  (a)	  female	  
participants	  drew	  upon	  gendered	  roles	  within	  their	  own	  lives	  to	  make	  meaning	  of	  female	  
and	  male	  relationships	  in	  street	  literature	  texts,	  (b)	  female	  participants	  held	  contradictory	  
views	  about	  the	  representations	  of	  women	  in	  the	  texts	  often	  viewing	  them	  as	  both	  
“powerful	  and	  foolish,”	  and	  (c)	  participants	  associated	  lighter-­‐skinned	  characters	  with	  
voluptuous	  bodies	  as	  a	  standard	  of	  beauty.	  Marshall	  et	  al.	  (2009)	  also	  examined	  gendered	  
roles	  through	  the	  use	  of	  literary	  analysis	  and	  explored	  black	  adolescent	  femininity	  in	  street	  
literature	  texts.	  Employing	  a	  Black	  Feminist	  Thought	  Perspective	  and	  rooted	  in	  two	  
overlapping	  themes-­‐-­‐color	  consciousness	  and	  Black	  femininity,	  Marshall	  et	  al.	  (2009)	  found	  

	  

36	  
that	  (a)	  street	  literature	  texts	  authored	  and	  published	  by	  women	  are	  “sites	  of	  struggle”	  
where	  popular	  ideas	  about	  black	  femininity	  are	  negotiated	  (p.	  29).	  The	  meaning	  making	  
process	  often	  entails	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  interpreting	  conflicting	  images.	  
The	  most	  popular	  street	  literature	  texts	  among	  urban	  youth	  tend	  to	  be	  cautionary	  
tales	  that	  are	  moralistic	  in	  tones	  (Hill	  et	  al.,	  2008;	  Morris,	  2007;	  Morris	  &	  Hughes,	  2006).	  
The	  moral	  values	  that	  are	  stressed	  in	  street	  literature	  texts	  provide	  urban	  youth	  with	  the	  
tools	  necessary	  to	  address	  circumstances	  in	  their	  lives.	  Morris	  et	  al.	  (2006)	  asserts	  that	  
Readers	  of	  the	  genre,	  especially	  teen	  readers	  who	  live	  in	  similar	  settings	  to	  those	  of	  
the	  stories,	  say	  that	  reading	  street	  literature	  teaches	  them	  “what	  not	  to	  do,”	  
confirms	  the	  fact	  that	  these	  streets	  is	  real,	  and	  validates	  that	  “this	  is	  how	  it	  is	  in	  our	  
hood.”	  (p.	  22)	  
	  
Critiques	  of	  street	  literature	  texts.	  Despite	  urban	  youths’	  connections	  to	  street	  
literature,	  critics	  of	  the	  genre	  complain	  that	  it	  lacks	  the	  literary	  quality	  necessary	  to	  be	  
considered	  appropriate	  or	  suitable	  texts	  for	  youth.	  Hence,	  critics	  have	  questioned	  teen	  
readership	  of	  street	  literature	  because	  of	  its	  “melodramatic	  plot	  lines,	  trite	  
characterizations,	  and	  scandalous	  book	  covers”	  (Morris,	  Agosto,	  &	  Hughes-­‐Hassell,	  2010,	  
p.	  53).	  Two	  major	  critiques	  of	  the	  genre	  with	  regard	  to	  formal	  education	  has	  been	  the	  
quality	  of	  writing	  and	  the	  objectionable	  content	  within	  street	  literature	  texts.	  Many	  library	  
and	  subject	  content	  classroom	  professionals	  believe	  that	  the	  sub-­‐genres’	  lack	  of	  conformity	  
to	  standard	  English	  and	  objectionable	  content	  poses	  a	  problem	  for	  those	  who	  are	  
interested	  in	  using	  literature	  that	  model	  traditional	  canonical	  texts	  (Hill	  et	  al.,	  2008).	  
Gibson	  (2010)	  argues	  that	  despite	  the	  critiques,	  educators	  should	  recognize	  the	  merits	  of	  
the	  genre	  and	  its	  relevance	  to	  the	  reading	  interest	  of	  African-­‐American	  adolescents.	  
The	  politics	  of	  literacy	  and	  the	  legitimization	  of	  street	  literature	  texts.	  Despite	  
its	  popularity	  within	  the	  last	  decade,	  street	  literature	  is	  not	  a	  new	  genre.	  According	  to	  Hill	  
	  

37	  
et	  al.	  (2008)	  it	  is	  “connected	  to	  a	  much	  longer	  tradition	  of	  texts	  that	  depict	  the	  Black	  
experience”	  (p.	  77).	  In	  fact,	  street	  literature	  is	  a	  rebirth	  of	  texts	  written	  in	  the	  1960s	  and	  
1970s.	  In	  the	  1970s,	  authors	  Donald	  Goines	  and	  Iceberg	  Slim	  introduced	  readers	  to	  texts,	  
known	  as	  “ghetto	  realism”	  that	  vividly	  depicted	  the	  realities	  of	  living	  in	  inner	  city	  
communities.	  Sister	  Souljah’s	  The	  Coldest	  Winter	  Ever	  (1999)	  has	  also	  been	  credited	  with	  
the	  resurgence	  of	  contemporary	  urban	  fiction	  from	  its	  original	  appearance	  in	  the	  1960s	  
and	  1970s	  	  (Gibson,	  2010).	  
Morris	  (2012)	  extends	  Hill’s	  and	  Gibson’s	  work	  and	  argues	  that	  in	  addition	  to	  street	  
literature	  being	  part	  of	  a	  much	  longer	  tradition	  of	  texts	  that	  depict	  the	  Black	  experience,	  
street	  literature	  “is	  part	  of	  a	  broader	  historical	  [literary]	  continuum”	  (p.	  11).	  In	  the	  Reader’s	  
Advisory	  Guide	  to	  Street	  Literature,	  Morris	  (2012)	  introduces	  a	  new	  framing	  of	  street	  
literature	  and	  outlines	  a	  historical	  timeline	  of	  street	  literature	  that	  dates	  back	  to	  the	  late	  
1800s.	  Morris	  (2012)	  further	  reveals	  that	  despite	  the	  contention	  that	  has	  been	  created	  
between	  urban	  fiction	  (out-­‐of-­‐school	  literacies)	  and	  canonical	  (classical)	  forms	  of	  literature	  
(in-­‐school	  literacies),	  there	  are	  more	  similarities	  than	  differences	  between	  both	  forms	  of	  
literature.	  	  
	  

In	  support	  of	  her	  argument,	  Morris	  (2012)	  references	  “the	  work	  of	  Charles	  Dickens,	  

Stephen	  Crane,	  and	  Upton	  Sinclair	  to	  name	  a	  few	  as	  examples	  of	  literary	  fictions	  that	  have	  
uncompromising	  stories	  of	  marginalized	  Americans”	  (p.	  11).	  Based	  on	  Morris’	  (2012)	  
textual	  analysis,	  texts	  of	  the	  late	  nineteenth	  century	  mainly	  focused	  on	  the	  life	  experiences	  
of	  European	  immigrants	  in	  New	  York’s	  inner-­‐city	  slums.	  Drawing	  on	  textual	  analysis,	  
Morris	  references	  Stephen	  Crane’s	  A	  Girl	  of	  the	  Streets	  as	  an	  example	  of	  such	  a	  text	  that	  has	  

	  

38	  
many	  parallels	  to	  contemporary	  street	  literature	  texts,	  such	  as	  Sister	  Souljah’s	  The	  Coldest	  
Winter	  Ever.	  Morris	  (2012)	  further	  argues:	  
That	  the	  female	  protagonist,	  Maggie	  in	  Crane’s	  Maggie:	  A	  Girl	  of	  the	  Streets	  and	  the	  
female	  protagonist,	  Winter	  in	  Sister	  Souljah’s	  The	  Coldest	  Winter	  Ever	  were	  both	  
characterized	  as	  melodramatic,	  hard-­‐hearted,	  confused,	  self-­‐centered,	  and	  
depressed.	  The	  men	  in	  Maggie:	  A	  Girl	  of	  the	  Streets	  were	  also	  characterized	  as	  
frustrated,	  demoralized,	  and	  violent.	  (pp.	  11-­‐12)	  
	  
Morris	  (2012)	  further	  highlights	  that:	  
During	  the	  twentieth	  century,	  street	  literature	  was	  primarily	  a	  European	  immigrant	  
story,	  and	  during	  the	  early	  twenty-­‐first	  century,	  it	  is	  primarily	  an	  African	  American	  
and	  Latino	  story,	  with	  novels	  about	  diverse	  African	  American	  characters.	  These	  
European	  immigrant	  stories	  were	  focused	  on	  stories	  of	  low-­‐income	  and	  working	  
class	  city	  dwellers	  who	  happened	  to	  be	  Irish	  (Crane’s	  Maggie),	  Italian	  (Puzo’s	  The	  
Fortunate	  Pilgrim),	  and	  Jewish	  immigrants	  (Cahan’s	  Yekl).	  Hence,	  historicity	  of	  
street	  literature	  isn’t	  chronicling	  cultural	  norms	  or	  stereotypes	  about	  certain	  ethnic	  
groups	  inasmuch	  as	  chronicling	  the	  challenging	  socioeconomic	  realities	  of	  diverse	  
peoples.	  (pp.	  11-­‐12)	  
	  
Despite	  the	  many	  parallels	  between	  both	  types	  of	  texts,	  society	  continues	  to	  
privilege	  some	  forms	  of	  street	  literature	  and	  their	  readers	  while	  not	  privileging	  other	  types	  
and	  their	  readers.	  Such	  an	  approach	  to	  literacy	  continues	  to	  create	  contention	  between	  
urban	  youths’	  in-­‐school	  literacies	  and	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  literacies.	  Haddix	  and	  Price-­‐Dennis’	  
(2013)	  extended	  Morris’	  work	  on	  the	  legitimization	  of	  street	  literature	  and	  multicultural	  
texts.	  Drawing	  upon	  both	  a	  critical	  literacy	  and	  critical	  pedagogy,	  Haddix	  and	  Price-­‐Dennis	  
examined	  how	  secondary	  teacher	  educators	  in	  a	  graduate	  level	  course,	  viewed	  the	  use	  of	  
graphic	  language	  and	  content	  in	  both	  Sapphire’s	  PUSH	  and	  Chbosky’s	  The	  Perks	  of	  Being	  a	  
Wallflower.	  According	  to	  Haddix	  and	  Price-­‐Dennis	  (2013),	  	  
The	  small	  group	  consensus	  was	  that	  Perks	  “deals	  with	  tough	  topics	  but	  language	  and	  
scenes	  are	  not	  overly	  graphic.”	  With	  PUSH,	  the	  same	  small	  group	  consensus	  was	  that	  
the	  plot	  was	  “highly	  graphic,”	  “overly	  explicit	  violence,”	  and	  “very	  mature	  content	  
and	  language.”	  In	  Perks,	  characters	  deal	  with	  date	  rape,	  abortion,	  and	  sexual	  abuse,	  
all	  mature	  topics.	  In	  dealing	  with	  these	  issues	  in	  Perks,	  the	  group	  wrote	  that	  while	  
these	  are	  mature	  topics,	  as	  teachers,	  “you	  have	  to	  know	  your	  class”	  and	  that	  this	  is	  a	  
	  

39	  
positive	  aspect	  of	  teaching	  this	  text	  because	  there	  are	  several	  takeaway	  “life	  lessons.	  
(p.	  263)	  
	  
Haddix	  and	  Price-­‐Dennis	  (2013)	  highlighted	  that	  secondary	  teacher	  educators	  
viewed	  the	  use	  of	  graphic	  language	  and	  content	  in	  PUSH	  differently	  from	  the	  use	  of	  graphic	  
language	  and	  content	  in	  The	  Perks	  of	  Being	  a	  Wallflower.	  Haddix	  and	  Price-­‐Dennis	  (2013)	  
further	  stated	  that	  the	  “secondary	  teachers	  viewed	  these	  themes	  as	  positive	  characteristics	  
of	  The	  Perks	  of	  Being	  a	  Wallflower	  while	  these	  same	  themes	  were	  viewed	  under	  greater	  
scrutiny	  and	  with	  more	  tentativeness	  for	  PUSH”	  (p.	  263).	  Haddix	  and	  Price-­‐Dennis	  (2013)	  
further	  reveal	  that	  	  
The	  responses	  of	  the	  teacher	  educators,	  who	  come	  from	  backgrounds	  different	  from	  
the	  characters	  in	  urban	  fiction	  texts	  they	  read,	  revealed	  how	  literature	  is	  valued	  
differently	  when	  the	  contexts	  changes,	  that	  is,	  a	  middle-­‐class,	  white,	  male	  
background	  versus	  a	  poor,	  urban,	  black,	  female	  background.	  (p.	  265)	  
	  
This	  qualitative	  case	  study	  highlighted	  how	  discourses	  of	  privilege	  around	  literacy	  and	  
language	  are	  often	  embedded	  in	  secondary	  teacher	  educators’	  evaluation	  of	  young	  adult	  
literature	  and	  how	  their	  positionalities	  influence	  their	  perceptions	  of	  students	  from	  
different	  backgrounds	  and	  their	  communities.	  Despite	  the	  scrutiny	  of	  urban	  youths’	  literacy	  
practices,	  urban	  youth	  use	  street	  literature	  texts	  as	  counter	  discourse,	  which	  allow	  them	  to	  
push	  back	  against	  more	  mainstream	  forms	  of	  literacy	  in	  more	  mainstream	  spaces	  allows	  
urban	  youth	  to	  push	  against	  dominant	  discourse	  and	  disrupt	  the	  legitimacy	  of	  the	  
dominant	  discourse	  (Gibson,	  2010).	  
Bridging	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  literacies	  and	  in-­‐school	  literacies.	  According	  to	  Mahiri	  
(1994),	  “a	  better	  link	  must	  be	  made	  between	  what	  schools	  hold	  as	  important	  and	  
meaningful	  and	  what	  .	  .	  .	  youth	  find	  meaningful	  in	  their	  daily	  lives”	  (p.	  144).	  Gainer	  and	  
Lapp	  (2010)	  further	  argue	  that	  “practitioners	  in	  formal	  spaces	  are	  more	  likely	  to	  bridge	  

	  

40	  
out-­‐of-­‐school	  literacies	  and	  in-­‐school	  literacies	  if	  they	  are	  given	  a	  framework	  from	  which	  to	  
begin	  their	  explorations”	  (p.	  19).	  Drawing	  upon	  a	  New	  Literacy	  Framework,	  Morris’	  (2012)	  
new	  framing	  of	  street	  literature	  provides	  public	  and	  school	  librarians	  the	  opportunity	  to	  
take	  a	  culturally	  conscious	  approach	  to	  librarianship	  by	  bridging	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  literacies	  
and	  in	  school	  literacies	  in	  an	  effort	  to	  create	  library	  collections	  and	  library	  programming	  
that	  are	  inclusive	  and	  reflect	  the	  literacy	  practices	  of	  urban	  youth,	  who	  have	  historically	  
been	  marginalized	  within	  and	  excluded	  from	  meaningful	  engagements	  with	  texts	  in	  formal	  
spaces.	  	  
Research	  trends	  on	  street	  literature	  texts.	  There	  has	  been	  a	  dearth	  of	  research	  on	  
the	  sub-­‐genre	  street	  literature.	  Much	  of	  the	  literature	  on	  street	  literature	  texts	  (Guerra,	  
2012;	  Hill	  et	  al.,	  2008;	  Meloni,	  2007;	  Osborne,	  2001;	  Pattee,	  2008;	  Stovall,	  2005)	  is	  non-­‐
empirical	  research	  studies.	  The	  few	  empirical	  studies	  on	  street	  literature	  texts	  (Gibson,	  
2010)	  and	  (Morris	  et	  al.,	  2006)	  have	  been	  situated	  within	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  contexts.	  With	  so	  few	  
empirical	  studies	  on	  street	  literature	  texts,	  there	  has	  been	  limited	  knowledge	  of	  the	  ways	  in	  
which	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  make	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature.	  	  
Much	  of	  the	  literature	  on	  street	  literature	  texts	  has	  originated	  in	  the	  field	  of	  
education	  and	  has	  focused	  on	  the	  implications	  of	  street	  literature	  in	  the	  secondary	  English	  
education	  classroom	  and	  has	  informed	  the	  practice	  of	  secondary	  English	  educators	  
(Gibson,	  2010;	  Haddix	  &	  Price-­‐Dennis,	  2013;	  Marshall	  et	  al.,	  2009;	  Morris	  et	  al.,	  2006;	  
Venable,	  2004).	  Subsequently,	  many	  of	  the	  studies	  on	  street	  literature	  texts	  have	  been	  
published	  predominantly	  in	  education	  journals,	  including	  English	  Education	  (Haddix	  &	  
Price,	  2013),	  The	  Journal	  of	  Adolescent	  and	  Adult	  Literacy	  (Gibson,	  2010;	  Marshall	  et	  al.,	  
2009).	  Luke’s	  Critical	  Literacy	  (Haddix	  &	  Price-­‐Dennis,	  2013;	  Gibson,	  2010)	  and	  

	  

41	  
Rosenblatt’s	  Reader	  Response	  (Gibson,	  2010;	  Morris	  et	  al.,	  2006)	  were	  prominent	  
frameworks	  that	  drove	  several	  of	  the	  empirical	  studies.	  In	  both	  the	  Haddix	  and	  Price-­‐
Dennis	  (2013)	  study	  and	  the	  Gibson	  (2010)	  study,	  Critical	  Literacy	  highlighted	  racialized	  
and	  gendered	  positionalities	  of	  participants	  and	  how	  their	  positionalities	  influenced	  
discourses	  of	  power	  and	  privilege	  embedded	  in	  their	  literary	  responses.	  	  
	  

The	  current	  study	  extends	  beyond	  the	  existing	  body	  of	  literature,	  which	  has	  focused	  

on	  meaning	  making	  of	  street	  literature	  texts	  in	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  settings	  and	  examines	  meaning	  
making	  of	  street	  literature	  texts	  within	  a	  digital	  context.	  In	  doing	  so,	  the	  current	  study	  
focused	  on	  how	  black	  adolescent	  girls’	  meaning	  making	  of	  street	  literature	  re-­‐
conceptualizes	  new	  literacies	  and	  provided	  implications	  of	  bridging	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  
in-­‐school	  and	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  literacies.	  Lastly	  this	  current	  study	  highlighted	  how	  urban	  new	  
literacy	  re-­‐imaged	  the	  role	  of	  information	  professionals	  and	  educators	  and	  has	  the	  
potential	  to	  impact	  the	  library	  services	  they	  provide	  to	  diverse	  populations.	  
	  
Part	  III:	  Adolescent	  Literacy	  Practices	  in	  Book	  Clubs	  
	  

In	  the	  last	  few	  years,	  many	  literacy	  scholars	  have	  explored	  the	  features	  of	  what	  have	  

interchangeably	  been	  termed	  book	  clubs,	  literature	  circles,	  read	  and	  talk	  clubs,	  learning	  
clubs,	  transformative	  spaces,	  and	  literature	  discussion	  groups	  (Appleman,	  2006;	  Casey,	  
2009;	  Daniels,	  2002;	  McMahon	  &	  Raphael,	  1997;	  Polleck,	  2010a,	  2010b).	  These	  groups	  
often	  provide	  students	  with	  a	  sense	  of	  autonomy	  by	  focusing	  on	  student-­‐centered	  
approaches,	  such	  as	  student	  choice	  in	  literature	  selections.	  	  In	  addition,	  these	  groups	  also	  
encourage	  diversity	  and	  inclusion	  by	  focusing	  on	  heterogeneous	  grouping	  (Appleman,	  
2006).	  	  

	  

42	  
According	  to	  Appleman	  (2006),	  several	  studies	  have	  argued	  that	  book	  clubs	  provide	  
“an	  effective	  and	  enjoyable	  learning	  context	  for	  students	  to	  discuss	  literature”	  (p.	  5).	  	  
McMahon	  and	  Raphael’s	  (1997)	  groundbreaking	  study	  on	  book	  clubs	  employed	  a	  
sociocultural	  framing	  of	  literacy	  and	  learning	  and	  found	  the	  following	  (Appleman,	  2006,	  
citing	  McMahon	  &	  Raphael,	  1997):	  
1. Learners	  construct	  meaning	  in	  their	  interaction	  with	  others	  
2. Learning	  is	  best	  facilitated	  as	  more	  knowledgeable	  others	  guide	  the	  learner	  with	  
appropriate	  tasks	  
3. Individuals	  construct	  a	  sense	  of	  self	  as	  they	  participate	  in	  social	  contexts;	  this	  
identity	  includes	  their	  own	  and	  others’	  roles	  in	  the	  group	  
4. Individuals	  construct	  meanings	  for	  language	  within	  their	  experiences	  and	  develop	  
speech	  genres	  particular	  to	  given	  social	  contexts.	  (p.	  6)	  
Book	  clubs	  in	  in-­‐school	  spaces.	  Much	  of	  the	  literature	  on	  book	  clubs	  has	  been	  
situated	  within	  the	  classroom	  context	  (Appleman,	  2006).	  Book	  clubs	  situated	  within	  the	  
classroom	  context	  have	  focused	  predominantly	  on	  elementary	  and	  middle	  school	  
classrooms	  and	  have	  focused	  on	  literacy	  achievement.	  These	  book	  clubs	  have	  focused	  on	  
motivating	  struggling	  adolescent	  readers	  and	  writers	  (Casey,	  2009),	  increasing	  literacy	  
achievement	  among	  students	  through	  the	  use	  of	  comprehension	  skills	  and	  strategies	  
(Raphael,	  Florio-­‐Ruane,	  George,	  Smith,	  &	  Compton-­‐Lilly,	  2001),	  exploring	  students’	  literacy	  
engagement	  and	  group	  interaction	  (McMahon	  &	  Raphael,	  1997),	  examining	  the	  strategies	  
that	  diverse	  students	  use	  to	  draw	  upon	  their	  knowledge	  and	  how	  they	  develop	  
comprehension	  skills	  (Goatley,	  Brock,	  &	  Raphael,	  1995).	  	  These	  in-­‐school	  book	  clubs	  are	  
often	  extensions	  of	  formal	  literature	  instruction	  and	  serve	  as	  academic	  interventions	  

	  

43	  
during	  instruction	  time.	  In	  addition	  to	  much	  of	  the	  book	  club	  research	  focusing	  on	  
elementary	  and	  middle	  school,	  much	  of	  this	  literature	  has	  taken	  a	  mainstream	  approach	  
and	  has	  not	  included	  marginalized	  populations’	  literacy	  practices	  in	  book	  clubs.	  
Book	  clubs	  in	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  spaces.	  Although	  much	  of	  the	  “conversations	  around	  
literacy	  have	  focused	  mostly	  on	  in	  school-­‐based	  contexts,	  studies	  in	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  contexts	  
have	  been	  pivotal	  in	  shaping	  the	  field	  of	  literacy”	  (Hull	  &	  Schultz,	  2002,	  p.	  11).	  A	  broader	  
approach	  to	  literacy	  reveals	  that	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  contexts	  are	  also	  spaces	  for	  literacy.	  Out-­‐of-­‐
school	  contexts	  refer	  to	  settings,	  such	  as	  homes,	  afterschool	  programs,	  public	  libraries,	  and	  
other	  community	  organizations	  (Hull	  &	  Schultz,	  2002).	  Although	  after-­‐school	  literacy	  
programs	  are	  typically	  held	  within	  school	  settings,	  they	  tend	  to	  function	  as	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  
spaces	  and	  are	  not	  extensions	  of	  the	  school	  curricula	  (Hull	  &	  Schultz,	  2002;	  Appleman,	  
2006).	  	  
According	  to	  Appleman	  (2006),	  three	  key	  features	  distinguish	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  book	  
clubs	  from	  in-­‐school	  book	  clubs—book	  club	  setting,	  book	  selection,	  and	  book	  club	  
approach.	  The	  setting	  of	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  book	  clubs	  typically	  takes	  place	  within	  homes,	  
afterschool	  programs,	  public	  libraries,	  and	  other	  community	  organizations.	  The	  setting	  
typically	  is	  often	  a	  precursor	  for	  how	  books	  are	  selected	  and	  who	  selects	  the	  books,	  as	  well	  
as	  members’	  approach	  to	  the	  book	  club	  (Appleman,	  2006;	  Daniels,	  2002).	  Since	  out-­‐of-­‐
school	  book	  clubs	  tend	  to	  be	  book	  club	  member-­‐centered,	  book	  club	  members	  select	  texts.	  
They	  typically	  have	  the	  freedom	  to	  choose	  texts	  that	  seem	  interesting	  to	  them	  and	  are	  
typically	  described	  as	  pleasure	  reading.	  Since	  it	  is	  considered	  pleasure	  reading,	  texts	  
selected	  may	  not	  be	  texts	  typically	  taught	  in	  formal	  education	  settings	  and	  often	  are	  not	  
canonical	  texts.	  Attendance	  and	  participation	  in	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  book	  clubs	  is	  typically	  on	  a	  

	  

44	  
voluntary	  basis	  and	  although	  there	  is	  often	  a	  facilitator	  who	  may	  initiate	  discussion,	  book	  
club	  members	  sustain	  discussions	  (Appleman,	  2006).	  “There	  typically	  is	  no	  formal	  agenda,	  
formal	  evaluation	  or	  assessment,	  which	  allows	  book	  clubbers	  to	  engage	  in	  conversations	  
that	  are	  authentic,	  spontaneous,	  and	  student-­‐driven”	  (Appleman,	  2006,	  p.	  15).	  
Divergent	  goals	  of	  in-­‐school	  and	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  book	  clubs.	  Hull	  and	  Schultz	  
(2002)	  further	  reveal	  that	  the	  goals	  and	  motivation	  for	  in-­‐school	  book	  clubs	  and	  out-­‐of-­‐
school	  book	  clubs	  are	  divergent.	  School-­‐sanctioned	  book	  clubs	  tend	  to	  be	  more	  formal	  and	  
typically	  are	  aligned	  with	  school	  instruction.	  Due	  to	  school	  mandates,	  focus	  on	  increasing	  
student	  achievement	  and	  performance	  on	  standardized	  tests,	  book	  clubs	  in	  school	  spaces	  
focus	  on	  literacy	  achievement	  and	  reading	  comprehension	  (Alvermann,	  Young,	  Green,	  &	  
Wisenbaker,	  1999).	  According	  to	  Appleman	  (2006),	  youth	  understand	  the	  difference	  
between	  reading	  within	  school	  contexts	  and	  reading	  outside	  of	  the	  school	  contexts.	  
Alvermann	  et	  al.	  (1999,	  p.	  226,	  citing	  Dressman,	  1997)	  has	  pointed	  out,	  
Although	  school	  and	  public	  libraries	  share	  a	  common	  goal	  in	  their	  attempts	  to	  
meeting	  the	  reading	  needs	  of	  youth,	  public	  libraries	  represent	  a	  distinct	  culture	  
when	  compared	  to	  libraries	  in	  schools.	  Public	  libraries	  do	  not	  attempt	  to	  regulate	  
what	  adolescents	  read	  at	  least	  not	  to	  the	  extent	  that	  school	  libraries	  do.	  It	  is	  
important	  to	  note	  that	  youth	  literacy	  experiences	  are	  not	  governed	  by	  the	  same	  set	  
of	  experiences	  operating	  in	  formal	  spaces.	  Although	  an	  institution,	  the	  public	  library	  
is	  a	  cultural	  space	  quite	  distinct	  from	  that	  of	  a	  school	  and	  more	  particularly	  the	  
school	  library.	  In	  public	  library	  settings	  socializing	  is	  central	  to	  maintaining	  
participants’	  interests.	  	  
	  
Since	  school	  spaces	  tend	  to	  be	  governed	  by	  school	  mandates	  that	  focus	  heavily	  on	  
scripted	  programs,	  standardized	  tests,	  rote	  memorization,	  and	  individualized	  instruction,	  
school	  libraries	  tend	  to	  not	  place	  as	  much	  emphasis	  on	  curiosity	  and	  discovery	  as	  public	  
libraries.	  Despite	  the	  current	  climate	  in	  school	  spaces,	  school	  librarians	  are	  in	  ideal	  
positions	  to	  bridge	  the	  gap	  between	  youths’	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  and	  in-­‐school	  literacies.	  School	  

	  

45	  
librarians	  can	  structure	  book	  club	  spaces	  in	  schools	  that	  resemble	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  book	  clubs	  
by	  offering	  students	  more	  autonomy	  through	  book	  selections	  and	  student-­‐led	  book	  
discussions	  (Greene,	  2012).	  
Book	  clubs	  in	  public	  libraries.	  Alvermann	  et	  al.’s	  (1999)	  seminal	  qualitative	  case	  
study,	  focused	  on	  adolescents’	  perceptions	  and	  negotiations	  of	  literacy	  practices	  in	  a	  read	  
and	  talk	  club	  situated	  in	  a	  public	  library.	  Alvermann	  et	  al.’s	  (1999)	  study	  placed	  
considerable	  emphasis	  on	  how	  the	  larger	  institutional	  and	  societal	  contexts	  shaped	  literacy	  
practices	  (adolescents’	  perceptions	  and	  negotiations)	  in	  a	  public	  library.	  Drawing	  upon	  a	  
communal	  activity	  framework,	  Alvermann	  et	  al.	  (1999)	  also	  focused	  on	  the	  role	  of	  those	  
clubs	  as	  social	  outlets	  and	  the	  contexts	  in	  which	  adolescents	  play	  out	  a	  variety	  of	  social	  
roles.	  Employing	  Fairclough’s	  Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis	  (CDA),	  Alvermann	  et	  al.	  (1999)	  
found	  “the	  discourse	  in	  the	  book	  clubs	  to	  be	  communal,	  with	  the	  adult	  book	  club	  leaders	  
and	  adolescents	  creating	  and	  negotiating	  literacy,	  institutional,	  and	  societal	  discourse	  
within	  the	  context	  of	  the	  library-­‐situated	  book	  club”	  (p.	  241).	  Alvermann	  et	  al.	  (1999)	  also	  
found	  that	  socializing	  played	  an	  integral	  part	  in	  the	  book	  club	  and	  motivated	  youth	  to	  
participate.	  According	  to	  Alvermann	  et	  al.	  (1999),	  
One	  book	  club	  participant,	  Einstein	  described	  the	  book	  club	  as	  a	  place	  where	  “we	  
talk	  about	  books	  and	  tease	  each	  other.	  .	  .	  .	  It	  means	  a	  fun	  place	  where	  I	  talk	  about	  my	  
favorite	  [reading]	  and	  see	  my	  readings.”	  In	  interviews	  with	  book	  participants’	  
parents,	  researchers	  heard	  the	  adolescents’	  views	  reiterated.	  Einstein’s	  mother	  
stated,	  “I	  think	  she’s	  really	  enjoying	  the	  discussion	  and	  the	  socialization	  of	  the	  
reading	  group.	  .	  .	  .	  She	  looks	  forward	  to	  them	  [the	  meetings]	  and	  enjoys	  the	  social	  
interaction	  with	  other	  avid	  readers.	  .	  .	  .	  It	  seems	  to	  have	  become	  a	  social	  [occasion].	  
(p.	  241)	  
	  
Alvermann	  et	  al.	  (1999)	  also	  found	  that	  club	  members’	  perceptions	  of	  reading	  in	  
public	  libraries	  positively	  impacted	  the	  read	  and	  talk	  discussions.	  According	  to	  Alvermann	  
et	  al.	  (1999),	  one	  book	  club	  participant	  declared	  that	  “the	  R	  &T	  Club	  was	  a	  place	  where	  he	  
	  

46	  
could	  interact	  with	  friends	  who	  liked	  to	  read	  and	  talk	  about	  books,”	  while	  another	  book	  
club	  participant	  noted	  that	  “reading	  in	  the	  public	  library,	  unlike	  in	  school,	  was	  considered	  
“cool”	  (p.	  241).	  Buzz,	  a	  book	  club	  participant	  provided	  insight	  on	  the	  negative	  influence	  of	  
school	  spaces	  on	  reading	  and	  revealed	  that	  reading	  in	  school	  is	  “uncool”	  because	  “the	  only	  
time	  we	  discuss	  books	  in	  school	  is	  to	  get	  what	  we	  need	  to	  know	  for	  the	  test”	  (p.	  241).	  Dr.	  
Funk,	  a	  book	  club	  participant	  discussed	  his	  perception	  of	  school	  by	  shedding	  light	  on	  the	  
influence	  of	  stringent	  school	  rules	  on	  book	  clubs.	  Drawing	  upon	  the	  discourse	  of	  schooling,	  
Dr.	  Funk	  revealed	  schools	  are	  opposed	  to	  “real”	  conversations	  and	  mentioned	  that	  “here	  
we	  can	  interrupt	  each	  other,	  like	  a	  real	  discussion.	  In	  school,	  you	  can’t	  do	  that.	  You	  got	  to	  
raise	  your	  hand	  until	  the	  teacher	  allows	  you	  to	  speak.	  If	  you	  don’t	  you	  get	  lunch	  detention	  
of	  something”	  (p.	  241).	  	  
Although	  the	  book	  club	  was	  comprised	  of	  mixed	  gendered	  groups,	  Alvermann	  et	  al.	  
(1999)	  found	  that	  females	  often	  conformed	  to	  gendered	  norms	  that	  were	  present	  within	  
the	  larger	  societal	  context.	  Throughout	  the	  book	  club	  discussions,	  female	  participants	  
would	  assert	  their	  positions	  and	  engage	  in	  female	  discursive	  resistance	  through	  their	  use	  of	  
“girl	  talk.”	  The	  use	  of	  “girl	  talk”	  provided	  female	  participants	  agency	  within	  the	  book	  club	  
discussions.	  In	  addition,	  one	  of	  the	  male	  book	  club	  facilitators	  (Colin)	  found	  that	  his	  
gendered	  identity	  made	  it	  easier	  for	  him	  to	  build	  rapport	  and	  establish	  relationships	  with	  
boys	  in	  the	  group	  (Alvermann	  et	  al.,	  1999).	  Colin	  also	  found	  that	  his	  role	  in	  the	  book	  club	  
was	  often	  determined	  by	  book	  club	  participants	  and	  his	  ability	  to	  build	  rapport	  and	  
establish	  relationships	  with	  the	  boys	  also	  blurred	  his	  role	  as	  both	  “adult	  leader”	  and	  
“adolescent	  participant	  (Alvermann	  et	  al.,	  1999,	  p.	  244).	  

	  

47	  
	  

Carter	  &	  Kumasi’s	  (2011)	  study	  explored	  how	  black	  adolescents	  develop	  their	  

identities	  in	  a	  community	  literacy	  program	  and	  explored	  ways	  to	  bridge	  black	  adolescents	  
home	  and	  school	  cultures	  to	  facilitate	  academic	  success.	  Drawing	  upon	  Du	  Bois’	  Double	  
Consciousness	  framework,	  Carter	  and	  Kumasi	  (2011)	  analyzed	  how	  black	  adolescents	  
assume	  their	  two-­‐ness	  (that	  of	  a	  American	  and	  that	  of	  a	  Negro)	  and	  how	  this	  two-­‐ness	  
influences	  their	  identity	  development.	  In	  an	  effort	  to	  examine	  literacy	  using	  a	  double	  
consciousness	  lens,	  Carter	  and	  Kumasi	  (2011)	  constructed	  a	  theoretical	  framework	  that	  
explored	  the	  intersection	  of	  literacy	  and	  double	  consciousness—Double	  Reading.	  	  
According	  to	  Carter	  and	  Kumasi,	  (2011),	  Double	  Reading	  explores	  how	  “black	  youth	  
dominant	  White	  and	  Afro-­‐cultural	  ways	  of	  knowing	  and	  speaking	  during	  the	  literacy	  
program”	  (p.	  77).	  Employing	  a	  micro-­‐ethnographic	  discourse	  analysis,	  Carter	  and	  Kumasi	  
(2011)	  found	  that	  tensions	  between	  both	  sets	  of	  norms	  were	  prevalent	  throughout	  the	  
literacy	  discussions.	  Carter	  and	  Kumasi	  (2011)	  found	  that	  “black	  adolescents’	  responses	  
hinted	  at	  a	  larger	  tension	  in	  which	  Whiteness	  informs	  particular	  perceptions	  about	  Black	  
culture	  resulting	  in	  a	  more	  mainstream	  read	  of	  culturally	  conscious	  texts”	  (p.	  6).	  Carter	  and	  
Kumasi	  (2011)	  concluded	  that	  book	  club	  participants	  had	  a	  heightened	  awareness	  of	  how	  
dominant	  White	  society	  perceives	  Black	  people.	  
Book	  clubs	  in	  afterschool	  spaces.	  Brooks,	  Browne,	  and	  Hampton’s	  (2008)	  study	  
examined	  the	  literacy	  practices	  of	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  in	  an	  afterschool	  book	  club	  using	  
the	  young	  adult	  text,	  The	  Skin	  I’m	  In,	  by	  Sharon	  Flake.	  Employing	  a	  Black	  Feminist	  Thought	  
framework,	  Brooks	  et	  al.	  (2008)	  explored	  how	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  responded	  to	  issues	  of	  
race,	  specifically	  colorism	  and	  gender	  based	  on	  their	  read	  of	  the	  text.	  Brooks	  et	  al.	  (2008)	  
found	  that	  many	  of	  the	  participants	  connected	  with	  the	  female	  protagonist	  Maleeka	  to	  

	  

48	  
varying	  degrees	  based	  on	  their	  own	  social	  positioning.	  Brooks	  et	  al.	  (2008)	  also	  found	  that	  
although	  participants	  connected	  with	  the	  protagonist	  and	  several	  themes	  in	  the	  texts,	  their	  
conversations	  around	  colorism,	  a	  main	  theme	  in	  the	  text,	  never	  extended	  beyond	  surface	  
level	  discussions.	  Participants’	  discussions	  provided	  a	  de-­‐historicized	  account	  of	  colorism	  
and	  did	  not	  position	  the	  racialized	  and	  gendered	  identity	  of	  Maleeka	  within	  a	  larger	  social	  
structure.	  	  
Conversely,	  Polleck’s	  (2010a)	  study	  examined	  how	  book	  clubs	  can	  serve	  as	  
transformative	  spaces	  for	  young	  adults	  and	  enhance	  their	  academic,	  social	  and	  emotional	  
development.	  Employing	  both	  Rosenblatt’s	  (1978)	  Reader	  Response	  Theory	  and	  
Vygotsky’s	  Social	  Constructivist	  framework,	  Polleck	  (2010a)	  found	  that	  book	  clubs	  enhance	  
literacy,	  identity	  development,	  and	  personal	  and	  social	  growth.	  	  Polleck	  (2010a)	  found	  that	  
participants	  situated	  themselves	  within	  the	  texts	  and	  discussed	  how	  they	  might	  handle	  
similar	  situations.	  Hence,	  participants	  often	  connected	  their	  own	  personal	  experiences	  with	  
those	  of	  the	  characters	  in	  the	  texts.	  This	  resulted	  in	  participants	  engaging	  in	  discussion	  
about	  their	  personal	  and	  social	  development.	  Polleck	  (2010a)	  also	  found	  that	  the	  
participants	  constructed	  their	  identities	  by	  drawing	  upon	  topics	  that	  addressed	  issues	  that	  
adolescent	  females	  often	  face,	  such	  as	  self-­‐esteem,	  body	  image,	  and	  peer	  pressure.	  Brooks	  
et	  al.’s	  (2008)	  findings	  reveal	  that	  although	  participants	  connected	  with	  elements	  of	  the	  
texts,	  they	  did	  not	  account	  for	  the	  social	  implications	  connected	  to	  blackness	  (colorism)	  
and	  as	  a	  result	  did	  not	  engage	  in	  a	  critical	  read	  of	  certain	  aspects	  of	  the	  text.	  Polleck’s	  
(2010a)	  study	  reveals	  that	  participants’	  engagement	  in	  discussion	  of	  the	  text	  result	  in	  a	  
heightened	  sense	  of	  identity	  and	  social	  growth.	  

	  

49	  
Research	  trends	  on	  adolescent	  literacy	  practices	  in	  book	  clubs.	  Much	  of	  the	  
literature	  on	  book	  clubs	  has	  been	  situated	  predominantly	  within	  school	  settings,	  
specifically	  the	  middle	  school	  and	  high	  school	  contexts	  and	  has	  focused	  predominantly	  on	  
literacy	  achievement	  (Broughton,	  2002;	  Casey,	  2009;	  Goatley	  et	  al.,	  1995;	  McMahon	  &	  
Raphael,	  1997;	  Raphael	  et	  al.,	  2001).	  Since	  much	  of	  the	  literature	  on	  book	  clubs	  has	  focused	  
on	  literacy	  achievement,	  this	  body	  of	  literature	  has	  been	  published	  in	  journals	  within	  the	  
field	  of	  education,	  including	  the	  Journal	  of	  Adolescent	  &	  Adult	  Literacy,	  Journal	  of	  Literacy	  
Research,	  and	  Reading	  Research	  Quarterly.	  Much	  of	  the	  literature	  focusing	  on	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  
book	  clubs	  has	  been	  situated	  within	  public	  libraries	  (Alvermann	  et	  al.,	  1999;	  Carter	  &	  
Kumasi,	  2011),	  and	  after	  school	  spaces	  (Brooks	  et	  al.,	  2008;	  Polleck,	  2010a,	  2010b;	  Smith,	  
2000).	  Aside	  from	  Alvermann	  et	  al.’s	  (1999)	  study,	  there	  have	  been	  very	  few	  studies	  on	  
book	  clubs	  situated	  in	  public	  libraries	  that	  have	  focused	  on	  how	  the	  context	  influence	  
adolescents’	  literacy	  practices.	  Additionally,	  aside	  from	  Carter	  and	  Kumasi’s	  (2011)	  study	  
there	  have	  been	  very	  few	  book	  clubs	  situated	  in	  public	  libraries	  that	  have	  focused	  on	  the	  
literacy	  practices	  of	  marginalized	  populations.	  Although	  Carter	  and	  Kumasi’s	  (2011)	  study	  
and	  Alvermann	  et	  al.’s	  (1999)	  were	  situated	  in	  public	  libraries,	  both	  studies	  were	  published	  
in	  an	  education	  edited	  volume	  (Teachers	  College	  Press)	  and	  an	  education	  journal	  (American	  
Education	  Research	  Journal)	  respectively.	  
	  

Critical	  frameworks,	  including	  Double	  Consciousness/Whiteness	  Theory	  (Carter	  &	  

Kumasi,	  2011),	  and	  Black	  Feminist	  Thought	  (Brooks	  et	  al.,	  2008)	  were	  prominent	  
frameworks	  that	  drove	  several	  of	  the	  empirical	  studies.	  In	  these	  particular	  studies,	  Double	  
Consciousness/Whiteness	  Theory	  and	  Black	  Feminist	  Thought	  accounted	  for	  hegemonic	  
ideologies	  and	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  participants	  negotiated	  their	  racialized	  and	  gendered	  

	  

50	  
identities	  when	  making	  meaning	  of	  texts.	  Reader	  Response	  Theory	  (Polleck,	  2010a;	  Smith,	  
2000)	  and	  Social	  Constructivist	  Theory/Communal	  Activity	  (Alvermann	  et	  al.,	  1999;	  
Polleck,	  2010a)	  were	  also	  prominent	  frameworks	  that	  drove	  several	  of	  the	  empirical	  
studies	  as	  well.	  Reader	  Response	  Theory	  accounted	  for	  participants’	  positionalities	  and	  
their	  personal	  connections	  with	  texts.	  Social	  Constructivist	  Theory/Community	  Activity	  
accounted	  for	  the	  social	  interactions	  of	  participants	  within	  a	  book	  club.	  
	  

The	  current	  study	  extends	  beyond	  the	  existing	  body	  of	  literature,	  which	  has	  focused	  

on	  literacy	  practices	  of	  in-­‐school	  book	  clubs	  and	  examines	  literacy	  practices	  in	  an	  out-­‐of-­‐
school	  book	  club.	  With	  an	  expressed	  aim	  of	  inclusivity,	  the	  current	  study	  was	  also	  situated	  
within	  an	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  digital	  space,	  as	  well	  as	  an	  urban	  public	  library	  within	  an	  
underserved	  community.	  The	  current	  study	  also	  extends	  beyond	  existing	  methodological	  
approaches	  in	  book	  club	  research	  by	  highlighting	  how	  the	  role	  of	  the	  researcher	  influences	  
the	  ways	  in	  which	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  make	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  text	  in	  an	  out-­‐of-­‐
school	  book	  club.	  Such	  a	  methodological	  approach	  encourages	  the	  researcher	  to	  be	  self-­‐
reflexive	  by	  turning	  the	  analytic	  lens	  inward.	  	  
	  
Part	  IV:	  Adolescent	  Literacy	  Practices	  in	  Digital	  Spaces	  
	  

Digital	  natives	  are	  the	  first	  generation	  of	  young	  people	  who	  have	  had	  their	  entire	  

lives	  mediated	  by	  technology	  (Gee,	  2004;	  Howe	  &	  Strauss,	  2000;	  Prensky,	  2010).	  They	  have	  
spent	  their	  entire	  lives	  using	  laptops,	  iPods,	  video	  cams,	  and	  video	  games,	  and	  
communicating	  with	  each	  other	  through	  the	  use	  of	  blogs,	  wikis,	  instant	  messaging,	  and	  
social	  networking	  sites.	  Today’s	  young	  people	  were	  born	  at	  the	  earliest	  1982	  and	  have	  also	  
been	  assigned	  various	  labels	  such	  as	  Generation	  Y,	  the	  Millennial	  Generation,	  Generation	  

	  

51	  
XX,	  Echo	  Boom,	  Generation	  2K,	  Generation	  Next	  and	  the	  Net	  Generation	  (Howe	  &	  Strauss,	  
2000;	  Prensky,	  2010).	  
	  

Digital	  natives	  share	  a	  common	  culture	  that	  is	  defined	  by	  their	  experiences	  growing	  

up	  immersed	  in	  digital	  technology,	  and	  the	  impact	  of	  this	  upon	  how	  they	  interact	  with	  
information	  technologies,	  information	  itself,	  and	  one	  another	  (Presnky,	  2010).	  Due	  to	  their	  
interactions	  with	  digital	  technology,	  their	  learning	  habits	  and	  styles	  have	  been	  heavily	  
influenced	  by	  their	  technology	  experiences.	  Today’s	  young	  people	  think	  and	  process	  
information	  vastly	  different	  from	  their	  predecessors	  (Prensky,	  2001).	  Several	  
characteristics	  distinguish	  digital	  natives	  from	  their	  predecessors.	  Prensky	  (2001)	  argues:	  	  
Digital	  natives	  are	  accustomed	  to	  receiving	  information	  extremely	  fast.	  They	  
also	  prefer	  to	  “parallel	  process	  and	  multi-­‐task,	  to	  receive	  graphics	  before	  
their	  text,	  to	  receive	  random	  access	  through	  the	  use	  of	  hypertext,	  to	  learn	  in	  
social	  environments,	  and	  to	  function	  within	  a	  networked	  environment	  with	  
others.	  They	  thrive	  on	  instant	  gratification	  and	  frequent	  rewards.	  They	  
prefer	  games	  to	  serious	  work.	  (p.	  2)	  
	  
Hence,	  teamwork,	  multitasking,	  collaboration	  are	  keys	  to	  literacy	  and	  learning	  in	  
this	  generation	  (Oblinger,	  2003;	  Howe	  &	  Strauss,	  2000;	  Prensky,	  2001).	  
	  
New	  Literacy	  Studies	  
	  

	  New	  literacy	  studies	  and	  the	  social	  turn.	  A	  large	  number	  of	  disciplines	  have	  been	  

influential	  in	  the	  “social	  turn,”	  which	  entailed	  more	  focus	  on	  social	  and	  cultural	  interactions	  
within	  the	  literacy	  context	  and	  less	  on	  cognitivism	  and	  behaviorism	  (Gee,	  2000).	  The	  
“social	  turn”	  drew	  scholars	  from	  different	  disciplines	  who	  used	  different	  theoretical	  
languages	  and	  used	  different	  dimensions	  to	  frame	  units	  of	  analysis.	  Despite	  the	  differences	  
in	  discipline,	  theoretical,	  and	  analytical	  approaches,	  “they	  shared	  a	  view	  of	  human	  action	  
mediated	  by	  language	  and	  other	  symbol	  systems	  within	  a	  particular	  cultural	  context”	  (Gee,	  
	  

52	  
2000,	  p.	  180).	  Based	  on	  this	  perspective,	  meaning	  in	  language	  is	  never	  neutral	  or	  abstract	  
but	  is	  tied	  to	  individuals’	  social	  and	  cultural	  positioning	  (Gee,	  2001).	  	  
	  

New	  literacy	  studies	  and	  the	  sociocultural	  theory	  of	  literacy.	  Historically,	  

Sociocultural	  Theory	  has	  been	  used	  as	  a	  broad	  term	  to	  represent	  the	  “situatedness”	  or	  the	  
context	  in	  which	  literacy	  occurs.	  The	  sociocultural	  theory	  of	  literacy,	  as	  articulated	  by	  New	  
Literacy	  Studies	  was	  one	  particular	  movement	  among	  many	  that	  took	  part	  in	  the	  social	  
turn.	  Barton	  and	  Hamilton	  (2000)	  laid	  out	  a	  summary	  of	  key	  features	  of	  sociocultural	  
approaches	  to	  literacy:	  
1. Literacy	  is	  best	  understood	  as	  a	  set	  of	  social	  practices	  
2. There	  are	  different	  literacies	  associated	  with	  different	  domains	  
3. Literacy	  practices	  are	  patterned	  by	  social	  institutions	  and	  power	  relationships,	  and	  
some	  literacies	  are	  more	  dominant,	  visible,	  and	  influential	  than	  others	  
4. Literacy	  practices	  are	  purposeful	  and	  embedded	  in	  broader	  social	  goals	  and	  cultural	  
practices	  
5. Literacy	  is	  historically	  situated	  	  
6. Literacy	  practices	  change	  and	  new	  ones	  are	  frequently	  acquired	  through	  processes	  
of	  informal	  learning	  and	  sense	  making.	  (p.8)	  	  
	  

Sociocultural	  approaches	  to	  literacy.	  One	  of	  the	  central	  contributions	  of	  the	  

sociocultural	  approaches	  to	  literacy	  has	  been	  “the	  recognition	  of	  the	  relationships	  between	  
texts	  and	  the	  contexts	  in	  which	  they	  are	  produced	  and	  used”	  (Stone,	  2007,	  p.	  50).	  From	  this	  
perspective,	  literacy	  practices	  are	  deeply	  interrelated	  with	  broader	  social	  relationships,	  
cultural	  traditions,	  economic	  changes,	  and	  ideological	  values	  (Barton	  &	  Hamilton,	  1998;	  
Gee,	  1996;	  Lankshear	  &	  Knobel,	  2007;	  Street,	  1995;	  Stone,	  2007).	  Hence,	  it	  is	  the	  social	  and	  

	  

53	  
cultural	  context	  (e.g.,	  ethnicity,	  gender)	  in	  which	  literacy	  occurs	  that	  contributes	  to	  its	  
meaning.	  Subsequently,	  literacy	  has	  different	  effects	  in	  different	  contexts	  (Gee,	  2012),	  in	  
both	  in	  school	  and	  out-­‐of-­‐school.	  This	  promulgates	  the	  notion	  of	  in-­‐school	  literacies	  as	  the	  
dominant	  literacy,	  devalues	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  literacies,	  and	  disrupts	  the	  notion	  of	  who	  counts	  
as	  a	  reader	  (Hull	  &	  Schultz,	  2002).	  According	  to	  Street	  (1995),	  focusing	  on	  the	  
understanding	  of	  literacies	  solely	  in	  school	  spaces	  offers	  a	  narrow,	  problematic,	  and	  
exclusive	  perspective	  on	  the	  sociocultural	  approaches	  to	  literacy.	  Another	  central	  
contribution	  of	  the	  sociocultural	  approach	  to	  literacy	  has	  been	  the	  recognition	  of	  literacy	  as	  
social	  cultural	  achievement	  and	  ways	  of	  participating	  in	  social	  and	  cultural	  groups.	  Gee	  
(2010)	  posits	  “we	  read	  specific	  sorts	  of	  texts	  and	  engage	  in	  conversations	  around	  
particular	  sorts	  of	  texts	  in	  specific	  ways	  based	  on	  the	  values	  and	  practices	  of	  different	  
social	  and	  cultural	  groups”	  (p.	  168).	  New	  Literacy	  Studies	  has	  little	  to	  say	  about	  the	  
individual	  apart	  from	  the	  individual’s	  membership	  in	  various	  social	  and	  cultural	  groups	  
(Gee,	  2010).	  
	  

New	  literacy	  studies	  and	  discourse.	  New	  Literacy	  Studies	  is	  based	  on	  the	  idea	  that	  

literacy	  is	  always	  situated	  within	  a	  specific	  social	  practice	  within	  specific	  discourses	  (Gee,	  
2000).	  Literacy	  studies	  does	  not	  just	  define	  how	  texts	  are	  made	  in	  this	  new	  “multimodal”	  
age,	  but	  how	  readers	  learn	  how	  to	  make	  sense	  of	  and	  use	  new	  texts	  in	  making	  meaning	  
(Gee,	  2003).	  Gee	  describes	  discourses	  as	  	  
socially	  recognized	  ways	  of	  using	  language	  (reading,	  writing,	  speaking,	  listening),	  
gestures	  and	  other	  semiotics	  (images,	  sounds,	  graphics,	  signs,	  codes),	  as	  well	  as	  
ways	  of	  thinking,	  believing,	  feeling,	  and	  interacting	  in	  relation	  to	  people,	  such	  that	  
we	  can	  be	  identified	  and	  recognized	  as	  being	  a	  member	  of	  a	  socially	  meaningful	  
group	  or	  as	  playing	  a	  socially	  meaningful	  role.	  (Lankshear	  &	  Knobel,	  2007,	  p.	  3,	  
citing	  Gee,	  2000)	  
He	  distinguishes	  primary	  discourse	  from	  various	  secondary	  discourses.	  	  
	  

54	  
	  

Gee	  argues	  that	  our	  primary	  discourse	  is	  how	  we	  learn	  to	  do	  and	  be	  within	  our	  

family	  group	  during	  our	  early	  life.	  He	  posits	  that	  our	  secondary	  discourses	  are	  those	  we	  are	  
part	  of	  through	  participation	  in	  outside	  groups	  and	  institutions,	  such	  as	  schools,	  churches,	  
and	  political	  organizations	  (Gee,	  2000).	  Discourse	  in	  outside	  groups	  and	  institutions	  draw	  
upon	  and	  extend	  resources	  from	  one’s	  primary	  discourse.	  Drawing	  on	  the	  work	  of	  Gee,	  
Lankshear	  and	  Knobel	  (2006)	  define	  literacies	  as	  “socially	  recognized	  ways	  of	  generating,	  
communicating,	  and	  negotiating	  meaningful	  content	  through	  the	  medium	  of	  encoded	  texts	  
within	  contexts	  of	  participation	  in	  discourses”	  (p.	  64).	  Gee	  posits	  that	  groups	  teach	  
participants	  to	  act,	  interact,	  talk,	  know,	  believe,	  and	  value	  in	  certain	  ways	  (Gee,	  2010,	  
p.	  19).	  These	  multiple	  literacies	  are	  why	  New	  Literacy	  Studies	  tend	  to	  not	  study	  literacy	  
itself	  directly	  but	  within	  certain	  social	  spaces	  like	  “communities	  of	  practice”	  (Lave	  &	  
Wenger,	  1991;	  Wenger,	  1998);	  “activity	  systems”	  (Engstrom,	  1987);	  “affinity	  spaces”	  (Gee,	  
2004);	  and	  online	  book	  clubs.	  These	  are	  spaces,	  which	  people	  socio-­‐culturally	  organize	  
themselves	  to	  engage	  in	  activities.	  	  Social	  and	  cultural	  factors	  within	  group	  environments	  
influence	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  people	  engage	  in	  literacy	  practices.	  	  
New	  literacy	  studies	  and	  the	  digital	  turn.	  The	  intersection	  of	  literacy	  and	  digital	  
technologies	  and	  sociocultural	  approaches	  to	  literacy	  expanded	  the	  scope	  of	  New	  Literacy	  
Studies.	  Literacy	  is	  further	  extended	  when	  one	  considers	  the	  social	  practices	  in	  which	  
individuals	  engage	  when	  they	  use	  new	  technologies	  (Gee,	  2000).	  In	  addition	  to	  changing	  
personal	  practices,	  new	  technologies	  have	  changed	  the	  social	  practices,	  which	  include	  new	  
ways	  of	  communicating	  and	  therefore	  new	  ways	  of	  being	  literate	  (Gee,	  2000;	  Lankshear	  &	  
Knobel,	  2007).	  What	  it	  means	  to	  be	  literate	  is	  rapidly	  evolving	  as	  individuals	  master	  new	  
and	  ever-­‐changing	  technologies	  (Gee,	  2000;	  Lankshear	  &	  Knobel,	  2007).	  	  

	  

55	  
The	  most	  significant	  shift	  in	  New	  Literacy	  Studies	  has	  been	  the	  “digital	  turn.”	  The	  
digital	  turn	  accounted	  for	  the	  increase	  in	  globalization	  and	  its	  influence	  on	  literacy	  
practices	  in	  digital	  spaces	  across	  social	  contexts	  (Gee,	  2000).	  In	  response	  to	  the	  influence	  of	  
digital	  technologies	  on	  literacy,	  Leu,	  Kinzer,	  Coiro,	  and	  Cammack	  (2004)	  identified	  central	  
principles	  on	  which	  New	  Literacy	  Studies	  should	  be	  built.	  Leu	  et	  al.	  (2004)	  acknowledged	  
that	  the	  term	  new	  literacies	  holds	  multiple	  definitions	  with	  no	  comprehensive	  theory,	  so	  
they	  outlined	  several	  of	  the	  principles	  of	  New	  Literacy	  Studies	  as	  follows:	  
1. The	  Internet	  and	  other	  ICTs	  are	  central	  technologies	  within	  a	  global	  community	  in	  an	  
information	  age.	  
Historically,	  different	  literacies	  have	  emerged	  that	  have	  stemmed	  from	  the	  
introduction	  of	  the	  printing	  press.	  Today,	  the	  Internet	  and	  its	  technologies	  are	  
defining	  literacy	  within	  a	  more	  global	  context.	  
2. The	  Internet	  and	  other	  ICTS	  require	  new	  literacies	  to	  fully	  access	  their	  potential.	  
Foundational	  literacies,	  including	  reading,	  word	  recognition,	  decoding,	  and	  reading	  
comprehension,	  continue	  to	  be	  important	  but	  are	  insufficient	  to	  acquiring	  new	  
literacies.	  Foundational	  literacies	  are	  transformed	  through	  new	  literacy	  practices.	  
3. New	  literacies	  are	  deictic.	  
Technology	  and	  literacy	  practices	  change	  rapidly	  due	  to	  the	  influx	  of	  new	  
technologies.	  Literacy	  will	  become	  defined	  by	  a	  person’s	  ability	  to	  adapt	  to	  new	  
technologies	  and	  their	  literacies.	  
4. The	  relationship	  between	  literacy	  and	  technology	  is	  transactional.	  
New	  literacy	  practices	  are	  enabled	  by	  new	  technologies	  and	  new	  technologies	  create	  
new	  literacies.	  

	  

56	  
5. New	  literacies	  are	  multiple	  in	  nature.	  
To	  be	  literate	  today	  requires	  multiple	  literacies	  that	  are	  context-­‐specific.	  The	  
multiplicity	  of	  literacy	  is	  also	  dependent	  on	  the	  changing	  nature	  of	  the	  Internet	  and	  
the	  new	  skills	  that	  accompany	  these	  changes.	  
6. Critical	  literacies	  are	  central	  to	  the	  new	  literacies.	  
Due	  to	  the	  ease	  of	  publishing	  information	  on	  the	  Internet,	  users	  must	  be	  even	  more	  
critical	  of	  information’s	  content	  and	  purpose	  
7. New	  forms	  of	  strategic	  knowledge	  are	  central	  to	  the	  new	  literacies.	  
New	  technologies	  are	  complex	  and	  require	  new	  strategies	  for	  effective	  use.	  
8. Speed	  counts	  in	  important	  ways	  with	  new	  literacies.	  
The	  speed	  with	  which	  a	  user	  can	  locate,	  interpret,	  use,	  and	  communicate	  new	  
information	  is	  important	  in	  a	  global	  society.	  
9. Learning	  often	  is	  socially	  constructed	  within	  the	  new	  literacies.	  
Due	  to	  the	  continually	  evolving	  nature	  of	  technology,	  one	  person	  will	  not	  be	  
proficient	  in	  every	  new	  technology,	  so	  the	  teaching	  of	  new	  literacies	  will	  be	  assumed	  
by	  different	  people	  in	  different	  contexts.	  Hence,	  learning	  will	  be	  increasingly	  
dependent	  on	  the	  social	  nature	  of	  new	  literacies.	  
10.Teachers/Librarians	  become	  more	  important,	  although	  their	  roles	  change	  within	  a	  
new	  literacy	  environment.	  
Teachers/Librarians	  are	  no	  longer	  positioned	  as	  the	  most	  literate	  people.	  This	  
change	  has	  ramifications	  for	  their	  roles	  in	  classrooms	  and	  libraries	  and	  the	  
pedagogies	  used	  to	  construct	  learning	  opportunities	  for	  youth.	  (pp.	  1588-­‐1599)	  

	  

57	  
	  

What’s	  “new”	  in	  new	  literacies.	  New	  literacies	  entail	  literate	  practices	  that	  are	  

embedded	  in	  social	  practices,	  social	  contexts,	  and	  social	  identities	  (Street,	  1995).	  The	  “new”	  
in	  new	  literacies	  focuses	  on	  how	  social	  practices,	  social	  contexts,	  and	  social	  identities	  have	  
been	  greatly	  impacted	  by	  these	  “New	  Times”	  (Luke	  &	  Elkins,	  1998),	  often	  converging	  with	  
changes	  associated	  with	  digital	  technologies—particularly	  those	  technologies	  used	  by	  
adolescents	  outside	  of	  school	  (Alvermann,	  Moon,	  &	  Hagood,	  1999).	  Gee	  (2000)	  identifies	  
these	  literacies	  as	  “situated”	  since	  they	  only	  make	  sense	  when	  studied	  within	  a	  particular	  
context.	  As	  digital	  technologies	  evolve,	  they	  will	  continue	  to	  impact	  the	  literacy	  practices	  
and	  result	  in	  literacies	  changing	  and	  become	  new.	  New	  Literacy	  Studies	  not	  only	  pushes	  the	  
socialized	  nature	  of	  learning,	  but	  also	  pushes	  literate	  activities	  outside	  of	  schools,	  as	  well	  as	  
literacy	  practices	  that	  do	  not	  involve	  pen	  and	  paper	  (Alvermann,	  2002).	  	  The	  broadening	  of	  
the	  term	  is	  due	  to	  the	  need	  for	  new	  skills	  necessary	  to	  adapt	  to	  communication	  
technologies	  in	  the	  digital	  age,	  the	  social	  practices	  that	  accompany	  the	  use	  of	  digital	  
technologies,	  the	  ability	  to	  constantly	  evaluate	  information,	  as	  well	  as	  digital	  technologies’	  
constant	  evolution	  of	  what	  it	  means	  to	  be	  literate	  (Leu	  et	  al.,	  2004;	  Tracey,	  Storer,	  &	  
Kazerounian,	  2010).	  
	  

Adolescent	  literacy	  practices	  in	  digital	  spaces.	  Scholars	  continue	  to	  explore	  how	  

youth	  take	  up	  a	  wide	  range	  of	  literacy	  practices	  within	  digital	  spaces.	  These	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  
literacy	  practices	  have	  expanded	  into	  digital	  spaces	  through	  the	  use	  of	  digital	  technologies,	  
such	  as	  gaming,	  blogging,	  and	  instant	  messaging	  to	  name	  a	  few	  (Cowan,	  2010).	  Based	  on
