his	  work	  on	  video	  gaming,	  Gee	  (2007)	  implemented	  several	  learning	  and	  literacy	  principles	  
that	  are	  present	  in	  video	  games,	  yet	  absent	  in	  formal	  learning	  environments.	  Gee	  (2007)	  
argues	  that	  video	  gamers	  learn	  basic	  skills	  that	  are	  not	  in	  isolation	  or	  out	  of	  context,	  unlike	  

	  

58	  
formal	  settings.	  Gee	  (2007)	  further	  argues	  that	  it	  is	  the	  process	  of	  discovery	  that	  takes	  
place	  in	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  digital	  spaces	  that	  allows	  authentic	  learning	  and	  literacy	  to	  occur.	  
Gee’s	  work	  on	  learning	  around	  simulated	  games	  revealed	  that	  children	  acquire	  skills	  in	  
their	  play	  (Ito,	  2010;	  Gee,	  2007).	  Gee	  (2007)	  further	  argues	  that	  new	  media,	  such	  as	  video	  
gaming	  allows	  complex	  learning	  among	  diverse	  learners	  to	  occur,	  while	  formal	  institutions	  
such	  as	  schools	  are	  structured	  to	  fail	  diverse	  learners.	  	  
Steinkuehler’s	  (2007)	  extended	  the	  work	  of	  Gee	  and	  conducted	  an	  ethnographic	  
study	  of	  participants	  in	  Massively	  Multiplayer	  Online	  Games	  (MMOG).	  Steinkuehler	  (cited	  
in	  Warschauer	  &	  Ware,	  2009),	  
Examined	  the	  array	  of	  reading	  and	  writing	  practices	  that	  take	  place	  both	  within	  
games	  (via	  chats,	  narratives,	  and	  letters)	  and	  around	  games	  (vis	  discussion	  boards,	  
blogs,	  and	  fan	  fiction	  sites),	  concluding	  that	  video-­‐games	  are	  actually	  promoting	  
rather	  than	  hindering	  the	  types	  of	  authentic,	  creative,	  and	  wide-­‐ranging	  literacies	  
that	  schools	  purport	  to	  value.	  (pp.	  224-­‐225)	  
	  
Steinkuehler	  (cited	  in	  Warschauer	  &	  Ware,	  2009)	  further	  argues	  that	  reading,	  writing,	  and	  
interacting	  in	  new	  online	  spaces	  provides	  adolescents	  the	  opportunity	  to	  tell	  their	  own	  
stories.	  Gee	  (2007)	  and	  Steinkuehler’s	  (2007)	  work	  reveals	  that	  school	  librarians	  and	  
classroom	  teachers	  should	  re-­‐conceptualize	  their	  pedagogical	  practices	  in	  school	  spaces	  by	  
incorporating	  elements	  of	  play	  in	  youths’	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  digital	  literacy	  practices.	  	  
Literacy	  practices	  of	  adolescent	  girls	  in	  digital	  spaces.	  Drawing	  upon	  a	  New	  
Literacy	  Perspective,	  Chandler-­‐Olcott	  and	  Mahar	  (2003,	  as	  cited	  in	  Hagood,	  2009)	  
conducted	  an	  ethnographic	  study	  documenting	  the	  literacy	  (fan	  fiction	  that	  featured	  
Japanese	  anime)	  and	  social	  practices	  of	  online	  communication	  between	  adolescent	  girls	  
and	  their	  peer	  groups.	  Chandler-­‐Olcott	  and	  Mahar	  (2003,	  as	  cited	  in	  Hagood,	  2009)	  found	  
that	  adolescent	  girls	  used	  their	  identities	  to	  shape	  the	  online	  space.	  The	  adolescent	  girls	  

	  

59	  
used	  their	  everyday	  experiences	  to	  connect	  with	  each	  other	  on	  issues,	  such	  as	  sexuality	  and	  
friendship.	  Extending	  the	  work	  of	  Olcott	  and	  Mahar,	  Black’s	  (2007,	  2008,	  as	  cited	  in	  
Warschauer,	  2009)	  3-­‐year	  ethnographic	  study	  examined	  the	  literacy	  practices	  of	  English-­‐
language	  learners	  who	  had	  a	  common	  interest	  in	  fan	  fiction	  and	  used	  fanfiction.net—“fan	  
fiction	  is	  a	  term	  used	  to	  describe	  stories	  that	  fans	  of	  an	  original	  work	  write	  by	  using	  
settings,	  characters,	  and	  plot	  from	  the	  original	  to	  imagine	  and	  create	  different	  situations	  
that	  sometimes	  include	  curious	  mixes	  across	  genres”	  (p.	  127).	  Black	  (2007,	  2008,	  as	  cited	  
in	  Warschauer,	  2009)	  found	  that	  English-­‐language	  learners	  “used	  the	  online	  community	  to	  
gain	  feedback	  on	  their	  writing	  and	  promote	  their	  literacy	  development	  as	  writers”	  (p.	  127).	  	  
	  

Adolescents’	  self-­‐authoring	  identities	  in	  digital	  spaces.	  In	  Youth	  Online:	  Identity	  

and	  Literacy	  in	  the	  Digital	  Age,	  Thomas	  (2007)	  states	  that	  identity	  is	  comprised	  of	  aspects	  
of	  self,	  others,	  and	  the	  community.	  Thomas	  (2007)	  argues	  that	  virtual	  spaces	  allow	  for	  the	  
creation	  of	  a	  new	  body.	  Embedded	  within	  the	  new	  body	  are	  identity	  markers,	  such	  as	  
gender,	  race,	  age	  that	  are	  present	  within	  social	  and	  discursive	  practices	  (Thomas,	  2007).	  
Buckingham	  (2008)	  argues	  that	  adolescence	  is	  a	  critical	  period—a	  time	  when	  individuals	  
are	  confronted	  with	  the	  task	  of	  defining	  their	  identity.	  “Through	  this	  process	  of	  self-­‐
reflection	  and	  self-­‐definition,	  adolescents	  arrive	  at	  an	  integrated	  coherent	  sense	  of	  their	  
identity”	  (Buckingham,	  2008,	  p.	  2).	  Turkle	  (1995)	  argues	  that	  online	  spaces	  provide	  
opportunities	  to	  experiment	  with	  multiple	  identities	  and	  allow	  individuals	  to	  make	  
meaning	  of	  self	  and	  the	  world	  around	  them.“	  Despite	  adolescents’	  abilities	  experiment	  with	  
multiple	  identities,	  Turkle	  (1995)	  argues	  that	  a	  “real	  world”	  identity	  or	  core	  identity	  exists	  
(p.	  184).	  	  Youths’	  identities	  in	  digital	  spaces	  are	  constantly	  shaped	  and	  re-­‐shaped	  by	  

	  

60	  
internal	  (identity	  markers)	  and	  external	  factors	  (social	  interaction).	  Although	  aspects	  of	  
their	  identities	  are	  ever	  changing,	  their	  core	  identity	  remains	  constant.	  
	  

Participatory	  culture	  and	  new	  literacies.	  Digital	  natives’	  immersion	  in	  digital	  

technologies	  and	  their	  interactions	  with	  information	  technologies	  have	  resulted	  in	  what	  is	  
known	  as	  participatory	  cultures	  (Jenkins,	  2009).	  “A	  participatory	  culture	  is	  a	  culture	  with	  
relatively	  low	  barriers	  to	  artistic	  expression	  and	  civic	  engagement,	  strong	  support	  for	  
creating	  and	  sharing	  creations,	  and	  some	  informal	  mentorship	  whereby	  participants	  pass	  
on	  knowledge	  to	  novices”	  (Jenkins,	  2009,	  pp.	  5-­‐6).	  Jenkins	  (2009)	  further	  identifies	  
“participatory	  culture	  in	  new	  media	  literacy	  as	  a	  way	  of	  communicating	  using	  digital	  
technologies	  in	  a	  collaborative,	  yet	  distributive	  nature”	  (p.	  8).	  Jenkins	  (2009)	  identifies	  
several	  features	  of	  participatory	  culture:	  
Affiliations:	  Memberships,	  formal	  and	  informal,	  in	  online	  communities	  centered	  
around	  various	  media,	  such	  as	  Friendster,	  Facebook,	  Twitter,	  MySpace,	  message	  
boards,	  and	  gaming.	  
Expressions:	  Producing	  new	  creative	  forms,	  such	  as	  fan	  fiction	  and	  zines	  
Collaborative	  problem	  solving:	  Working	  together	  in	  teams,	  formal	  and	  informal,	  to	  
complete	  task	  and	  develop	  new	  knowledge	  
Circulations:	  Shaping	  the	  flow	  of	  media,	  such	  as	  podcasting	  or	  blogging.	  (p.	  9)	  
	  

Ito’s	  (2009)	  research	  further	  extended	  Jenkins’	  (2009)	  work	  around	  participatory	  

cultures.	  Ito’s	  3-­‐year	  longitudinal	  study,	  The	  Digital	  Youth	  Project	  examined	  youth	  informal	  
learning	  through	  the	  use	  digital	  media	  in	  their	  daily	  lives.	  Ito	  (2009)	  found	  that	  “youth	  are	  
coming	  of	  age	  and	  struggle	  for	  autonomy	  and	  identity	  amid	  new	  worlds	  for	  communication,	  
friendship,	  play,	  and	  expression”	  (p.	  1).	  The	  digital	  world	  is	  creating	  new	  opportunities	  for	  

	  

61	  
youth	  “to	  grapple	  with	  social	  norms,	  explore	  interests,	  develop	  technical	  skills,	  and	  
experiment	  with	  new	  forms	  of	  expression”	  (Ito,	  2009,	  pp.	  1-­‐2).	  This	  sense	  of	  ownership	  in	  
their	  learning	  increases	  students’	  levels	  of	  engagement	  and	  motivation	  (Jenkins,	  2006).	  	  
	  

Despite	  digital	  natives’	  immersion	  in	  digital	  technology	  and	  their	  interactions	  with	  

information	  technologies,	  formal	  institutions	  have	  not	  been	  fully	  receptive	  to	  new	  
participatory	  culture.	  Informal	  learning	  communities,	  such	  as	  after	  school	  programs	  and	  
public	  libraries	  have	  been	  more	  receptive	  to	  digital	  natives’	  new	  participatory	  culture.	  
Jenkins	  (2006)	  reveals	  that	  youth	  not	  only	  creates	  original	  works	  but	  they	  seek	  out	  ways	  to	  
remix	  work	  that	  are	  personally	  meaningful	  or	  meaningful	  in	  different	  social	  and	  cultural	  
contexts.	  Jenkins	  reveals	  that	  these	  are	  efforts	  to	  bridge	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  practices	  with	  more	  
formal	  educational	  efforts.	  Hence,	  youth	  are	  bridging	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  and	  in-­‐school	  practices	  
where	  the	  institutions	  are	  not.	  
Literacy	  practices	  of	  Black	  adolescents	  in	  digital	  spaces.	  	  In	  an	  ethnographic	  
study,	  Vasudevan,	  Schultz,	  and	  Bateman	  (2010b),	  drew	  upon	  the	  literacy	  practices	  of	  black	  
adolescents	  in	  digital	  spaces	  by	  exploring	  how	  black	  adolescents	  author	  literate	  identities	  
(authorial	  stances)	  through	  multimodal	  storytelling.	  Drawing	  upon	  the	  sociocultural	  
theories	  of	  identities	  and	  literacies,	  Vasudevan	  et	  al.	  (2010b)	  found	  that	  black	  adolescents	  
demonstrated	  increased	  participation	  and	  engagement	  when	  composing	  was	  situated	  
within	  digital	  spaces.	  Vasudevan	  et	  al.	  (2010b)	  further	  revealed	  that	  extending	  beyond	  
print	  modalities	  create	  opportunities	  for	  black	  adolescents	  to	  tap	  into	  their	  “funds	  of	  
knowledge”	  in	  order	  to	  draw	  upon	  their	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  literacies	  in	  academic	  spaces	  (p.	  
443).	  Funds	  of	  knowledge	  consist	  of	  knowledge	  rooted	  in	  their	  experiences	  and	  passions	  
nurtured	  in	  their	  home	  communities.	  According	  to	  Vasudevan	  et	  al.	  (2010b,	  in	  citing	  

	  

62	  
Schultz,	  2009),	  “through	  these	  new	  composing	  processes,	  youth	  developed	  a	  stronger	  and	  
more	  engaged	  presence,	  by	  shifting	  their	  participation	  and	  sense	  of	  themselves”	  (p.	  443).	  
Additionally,	  historically	  marginalized	  adolescents	  use	  composing	  processes	  through	  the	  
use	  of	  digital	  technologies,	  in	  an	  effort	  to	  provide	  new	  types	  of	  counterstorytelling	  that	  
serve	  as	  a	  source	  of	  empowerment	  (Vasudevan,	  2006;	  Vasudevan	  et	  al.,	  2010b).	  This	  new	  
type	  of	  counterstorytelling	  allows	  historically	  marginalized	  youth	  to	  represent	  themselves	  
in	  ways	  that	  they	  cannot	  in	  their	  school	  life	  (Vasudevan,	  2006).	  In	  addition	  to	  bridging	  out-­‐
of-­‐school	  and	  in-­‐school	  practices,	  historically	  marginalized	  adolescents	  use	  digital	  
technologies	  as	  tools	  of	  resistance	  to	  rewrite	  images	  of	  self.	  
Often	  times,	  “digital	  technologies	  are	  used	  in	  formal	  institutions	  in	  urban	  
communities	  as	  a	  curricular	  tool	  to	  educate	  students	  about	  new	  literacy,	  instead	  of	  being	  
used	  in	  a	  transformative	  and	  emancipatory	  way	  to	  engage	  the	  critical	  capabilities	  that	  
students	  already	  bring	  to	  new	  literacy”	  (Vasudevan	  &	  Hill,	  2008,	  p.	  2,	  as	  cited	  in	  Staples,	  
2005).	  More	  specifically,	  “when	  digital	  technologies	  are	  integrated	  into	  the	  curricula	  in	  
formal	  spaces,	  it	  is	  often	  integrated	  within	  existing	  pedagogical	  frames	  that	  maintain	  
uneven	  dynamics	  of	  power	  and	  authority”	  (Vasudevan	  &	  Hill,	  2008,	  p.	  5).	  Vasudevan,	  
DeJaynes,	  and	  Schmier	  (2010a,	  citing	  Ito,	  2008)	  also	  found	  that	  “when	  youth	  engage	  in	  
spaces	  that	  support	  digital	  technologies	  they	  are	  developing	  a	  range	  of	  social,	  intellectual,	  
and	  technical	  knowledge	  that	  should	  not	  be	  dismissed”	  (p.	  22).	  School	  librarians	  and	  
classroom	  teachers	  must	  account	  for	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  digital	  technologies	  have	  impacted	  
literacy	  and	  learning	  among	  youth.	  These	  literacy	  and	  learning	  practices	  create	  new	  
opportunities	  for	  school	  librarians	  and	  classroom	  teachers	  to	  align	  pedagogical	  approaches	  
in	  school	  spaces	  to	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  youth	  consume	  digital	  technologies.	  

	  

63	  
	  

Online	  book	  clubs	  as	  new	  literacies.	  There	  is	  a	  growing	  consensus	  that	  most	  

engaged	  and	  active	  forms	  of	  literacy	  and	  learning	  with	  digital	  media	  happen	  in	  youth-­‐
driven,	  recreational	  settings	  (Ito,	  2010).	  New	  media	  practices	  are	  most	  often	  situated	  in	  
social	  spaces	  rather	  than	  in	  the	  context	  of	  explicit	  instruction	  (Ito,	  2010).	  Typically	  online	  
book	  clubs	  consist	  of	  forums	  set	  up	  for	  students	  to	  exchange	  ideas	  situated	  within	  a	  social	  
networking	  site.	  Online	  book	  clubs	  in	  the	  21st	  century	  exemplify	  what	  Knobel	  and	  
Lankshear	  (2007)	  call	  “new	  ethos,”	  a	  phenomena	  characterized	  by	  more	  collaborative,	  
fluid,	  and	  distributed	  ways	  of	  using	  literacy	  than	  those	  “typically	  associated	  with	  
established	  literacies”	  (p.	  7).	  According	  to	  Scharber,	  Melrose,	  and	  Wurl	  (2009),	  	  

	  

Participating	  in	  online	  book	  clubs	  is	  considered	  a	  new	  literacy	  practice	  in	  
research,	  where	  book	  clubs	  serve	  as	  forums	  for	  youth	  to	  socialize,	  discuss	  
books,	  and	  utilize	  new	  literacies	  including	  chatting	  and	  posting	  messages	  to	  
discussion	  boards	  while	  being	  grounded	  in	  the	  “old”	  literacy	  of	  reading	  a	  
printed	  text.	  The	  practice	  of	  participating	  in	  online	  book	  clubs	  is	  considered	  
to	  be	  a	  new	  literacies	  practice	  in	  this	  research	  where	  youth	  are	  discussing	  
print	  books	  (traditional	  print	  literacies)	  together	  within	  an	  online,	  
moderated	  synchronous	  and	  asynchronous	  spaces	  (contemporary	  online	  
literacies).	  (p.	  177)	  
	  
Participating	  in	  online	  book	  clubs	  provides	  youth	  with	  opportunities	  to	  merge	  old	  

and	  new	  literacies.	  
	  

Recent	  research	  on	  youth	  and	  new	  media	  has	  called	  attention	  to	  the	  value	  and	  

potential	  for	  literacy	  and	  learning	  by	  allowing	  youth	  opportunities	  to	  pursue	  their	  own	  
interests.	  “Libraries	  are	  key	  components	  in	  facilitating	  such	  opportunities	  and,	  in	  the	  
process	  deepen	  young	  people’s	  engagement	  with	  the	  library,	  foster	  new	  ways	  of	  learning	  
with	  media,	  and	  help	  young	  people	  develop	  and	  practice	  new	  media	  literacy	  skills”	  (Tripp,	  
2011,	  p.	  338).	  Libraries	  have	  a	  stake	  in	  reimagining	  their	  future	  roles	  as	  learning	  
institutions.	  Hence,	  libraries	  are	  in	  positions	  to	  fully	  capitalize	  on	  the	  potential	  of	  new	  

	  

64	  
media	  on	  learning	  and	  literacy	  in	  the	  lives	  of	  youth.	  The	  emergence	  of	  new	  media	  has	  
changed	  the	  face	  of	  libraries,	  librarians,	  and	  library	  services.	  Librarians	  are	  no	  longer	  
gatekeepers	  of	  information,	  but	  have	  evolved	  into	  facilitators	  of	  knowledge	  (Fourie	  &	  
Dowell,	  2009).	  Librarians	  continue	  to	  serve	  as	  facilitators	  of	  a	  wide	  range	  of	  new	  media	  
activities	  including	  anime	  clubs,	  digital	  story	  times,	  and	  book	  blogs	  (Ito,	  2010).	  New	  media	  
activities	  have	  created	  opportunities	  for	  youth	  to	  be	  self-­‐directed	  learners.	  The	  integration	  
of	  such	  new	  media	  activities	  into	  youth	  services	  allows	  libraries	  to	  continue	  to	  be	  relevant	  
spaces	  for	  youth.	  	  
Research	  trends	  on	  adolescent	  literacy	  practices	  in	  digital	  spaces.	  Despite	  the	  
popularity	  of	  new	  literacies,	  specifically	  online	  book	  clubs	  among	  young	  adults,	  there	  is	  a	  
dearth	  of	  empirical	  research	  on	  literacy	  in	  the	  context	  of	  networked	  communities.	  A	  few	  
studies	  on	  adolescent	  literacy	  practices	  in	  digital	  spaces	  have	  been	  situated	  in	  formal	  
school	  space	  (Vasudevan	  et	  al.,	  2010b;	  Chandler-­‐Olcott	  &	  Mahar,	  2003).	  Historically,	  black	  
adolescent	  girls’	  literacy	  practices	  in	  digital	  spaces	  are	  often	  lumped	  together	  with	  
mainstream	  populations.	  This	  has	  resulted	  in	  the	  invisibility	  of	  black	  girls’	  literacy	  
practices	  in	  digital	  spaces.	  “Given	  this	  insight,	  it	  is	  not	  surprising	  that	  the	  sophisticated	  
ways	  in	  which	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  use	  the	  Internet	  to	  express	  their	  everyday	  lives	  and	  
negotiate	  key	  developmental	  tasks	  such	  as	  identity	  formation	  have	  not	  been	  adequately	  
investigated”	  (Stokes,	  2010,	  p.	  46,	  citing	  Stokes,	  2007).	  Technology	  is	  not	  often	  used	  as	  a	  
platform	  for	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  to	  focus	  on	  agency,	  identity	  construction,	  or	  meaning	  
making,	  but	  instead	  used	  to	  solely	  complete	  a	  task	  in	  school	  spaces.	  Literature	  that	  explores	  
the	  intersections	  of	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  literacies	  and	  digital	  technologies	  and	  its	  impact	  on	  the	  
lives	  of	  marginalized	  populations,	  specifically	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  have	  also	  been	  scant.	  

	  

65	  
Much	  of	  the	  literature	  on	  adolescent	  literacy	  practices	  in	  digital	  spaces	  has	  
originated	  in	  the	  field	  of	  education	  (Black,	  2008,	  2007;	  Chandler-­‐Olcott	  &	  Mahar,	  2003;	  Ito,	  
2010;	  Vasudevan	  et	  al.,	  2010a).	  Aside	  from	  Scharber	  et	  al.	  (2009)	  study,	  there	  have	  been	  
few	  studies	  that	  have	  been	  public	  library	  based	  online	  book	  clubs.	  Subsequently,	  many	  of	  
the	  studies	  have	  been	  published	  in	  predominantly	  education	  journals,	  including	  the	  Journal	  
of	  Adolescent	  and	  Adult	  Literacy,	  E-­‐Learning,	  and	  Written	  Communication.	  Sociocultural	  
Theory	  (Ito,	  2010;	  Vasudevan	  et	  al.,	  2010b).	  Scharber	  et	  al.	  (2009)	  and	  New	  Literacies	  
(Scharber	  et	  al.,	  2009;	  Chandler-­‐Olcott	  &	  Mahar,	  2003)	  were	  prominent	  frameworks	  that	  
drove	  several	  of	  the	  studies.	  In	  both	  Ito	  (2010)	  and	  Vasudevan	  et	  al.’s	  (2010b)	  study,	  
Sociocultural	  Theory	  was	  employed	  to	  explore	  adolescents’	  social	  norms,	  whereas	  in	  the	  
Scharber	  et	  al.	  (2009)	  study,	  Sociocultural	  Theory	  was	  employed	  to	  examine	  the	  gendered	  
interactions	  of	  participants	  and	  how	  those	  interactions	  shaped	  their	  gendered	  identities.	  In	  
both	  the	  Chandler-­‐Olcott	  and	  Mahar	  (2003)	  study	  and	  the	  Scharber	  et	  al.	  (2009)	  study,	  
New	  Literacy	  Studies	  was	  employed	  to	  examine	  how	  gendered	  identities	  shaped	  and	  were	  
shaped	  by	  the	  online	  community.	  
	  

The	  current	  study	  addressed	  several	  of	  the	  gaps	  in	  the	  literature	  on	  adolescent	  

literacy	  practices	  in	  digital	  spaces.	  The	  current	  study	  extends	  the	  current	  body	  of	  literature,	  
which	  has	  often	  focused	  on	  digital	  technologies	  as	  a	  tool	  to	  complete	  a	  task	  and	  highlights	  
black	  adolescent	  girls	  use	  of	  digital	  technologies	  to	  make	  meaning	  of	  critical	  social	  issues	  in	  
street	  literature	  texts	  and	  to	  construct	  their	  identities.	  In	  doing	  so,	  the	  current	  study	  
provided	  an	  opportunity	  for	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  to	  re-­‐conceptualize	  new	  literacies.	  
Lastly,	  the	  current	  study	  relocates	  black	  girls	  at	  the	  center	  by	  exploring	  the	  innovative	  and	  

	  

66	  
meaningful	  ways	  in	  which	  black	  girls	  use	  digital	  technologies	  to	  claim	  spaces	  in	  informal	  
institutions.	  
	  
Literature	  Review	  Summary	  
	  

This	  chapter	  drew	  upon	  several	  bodies	  of	  literature	  that	  has	  laid	  the	  foundation	  for	  

the	  current	  qualitative	  study	  including:	  Black	  Girlhood	  (Part	  I),	  Black	  Adolescent	  Girls	  
Literacy	  Practices	  and	  Street	  Literature	  Texts	  (Part	  II),	  Adolescent	  Literacy	  Practices	  in	  Book	  
Clubs	  (Part	  III),	  and	  Adolescent	  Literacy	  Practices	  in	  Digital	  Spaces	  (Part	  IV)	  (Appendix	  B).	  	  
In	  addition	  to	  laying	  the	  foundation	  for	  this	  qualitative	  study,	  it	  has	  also	  highlighted	  the	  
gaps	  within	  the	  body	  of	  literature.	  The	  current	  research	  study	  addressed	  several	  gaps	  in	  
the	  current	  body	  of	  literature	  and	  extended	  research	  in	  the	  fields	  of	  Library	  and	  
Information	  Science,	  Education,	  and	  Rhetorical	  Studies.	  The	  current	  study	  was	  designed	  to	  
create	  safe	  spaces	  for	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  who	  have	  historically	  been	  at	  the	  margins	  both	  
within	  the	  media,	  as	  well	  as	  within	  formal	  spaces.	  In	  doing	  so,	  it	  positioned	  black	  
adolescent	  girls	  at	  the	  forefront	  by	  legitimizing	  their	  literacy	  and	  language	  practices.	  This	  
provided	  opportunities	  for	  them	  to	  voice	  their	  opinions	  on	  a	  range	  of	  critical	  social	  issues	  
prevalent	  among	  black	  girls	  in	  the	  21st	  Century.	  Chapter	  3	  details	  the	  methodology	  that	  
drove	  this	  qualitative	  study.	  
	  

	  

67	  
Chapter	  Three	  
Methodology	  
The	  purpose	  of	  this	  chapter	  is	  to	  describe	  the	  traditions	  in	  which	  this	  study	  is	  
situated	  and	  to	  outline	  the	  data	  sources	  and	  the	  analytic	  procedures	  that	  were	  employed.	  
This	  chapter	  provides	  a	  description	  of	  the	  research	  design,	  the	  research	  methodology,	  and	  
the	  framing	  that	  were	  employed	  in	  this	  study.	  Chapter	  3	  also	  offers	  a	  description	  of	  the	  
selection	  of	  focal	  sites,	  recruitment	  and	  selection	  of	  adolescent	  participants,	  positionality	  of	  
the	  researcher,	  and	  data	  analysis	  for	  this	  study.	  Chapter	  3	  concludes	  with	  an	  overview	  of	  
the	  book	  club	  structures,	  selection	  of	  adolescent	  literature	  texts,	  book	  club	  timeline,	  and	  
details	  of	  reliability,	  validity,	  and	  reflexivity.	  	  
	  
Framing	  the	  Study	  	  
Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis	  is	  a	  cultural	  tool	  that	  stems	  from	  several	  overlapping	  
intellectual	  traditions	  each	  emphasizing	  the	  linguistic	  turn	  in	  the	  social	  sciences.	  These	  
traditions	  are	  discourse	  analysis,	  feminist	  post-­‐structuralism,	  and	  critical	  linguistics	  
(Fairclough,	  1989).	  Fairclough’s	  (1989)	  approach	  to	  critical	  discourse	  analysis	  focuses	  on	  
the	  interplay	  between	  the	  textual	  and	  the	  social	  world.	  Fairclough	  (1989)	  devised	  a	  three-­‐
tiered	  analytical	  framework	  examining	  the	  interplay	  of	  the	  text	  and	  the	  social	  world	  by	  
focusing	  on	  the	  linkages	  between	  talk	  at	  the	  situational,	  institutional,	  and	  societal	  level	  
across	  three	  levels	  of	  interacting	  or	  orders	  of	  discourse:	  genre,	  discourse,	  and	  style.	  
Chouliaraki	  and	  Fairclough	  (1999)	  define	  genre	  as	  the	  forms	  of	  language	  connected	  to	  a	  
particular	  social	  setting.	  Rogers	  and	  Wetzel	  (2014)	  describe	  genres	  as	  “ways	  of	  interacting”	  
and	  define	  genres	  as	  the	  organizational	  threads	  of	  interacting	  (p.	  72).	  Discourses	  are	  “ways	  

	  

68	  
of	  representing”	  and	  describe	  how	  knowledge	  is	  represented	  and	  from	  what	  perspective.	  
Styles	  are	  “ways	  of	  being”	  and	  represents	  interpersonal	  choice,	  specifically	  one’s	  position	  
taking	  (Rogers,	  2003).	  
	  Rogers,	  Malancharuvil-­‐Berkes,	  Mosley,	  and	  Hui	  (2005,	  citing	  Fairclough,	  1989)	  
argue	  that	  critical	  discourse	  analysis	  focuses	  on	  “how	  language	  as	  a	  cultural	  tool	  mediates	  
relationships	  of	  power	  and	  privilege	  in	  social	  interactions,	  institutions,	  and	  bodies	  of	  
knowledge”	  (p.	  367).	  Fairclough	  and	  Wodak	  (1997)	  further	  reveal	  that	  	  
Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis	  sees	  discourse—language	  use	  in	  speech	  and	  
writing—as	  a	  form	  of	  “social	  practice.”	  Describing	  discourse	  as	  a	  social	  
practice	  implies	  a	  dialectical	  relationship	  between	  a	  particular	  discourse	  
event	  and	  the	  situation	  (s),	  institution(s),	  and	  social	  structure(s),	  which	  
frames	  it.	  A	  dialectical	  relationship	  is	  a	  two-­‐way	  relationship:	  the	  discursive	  
event	  is	  shaped	  by	  situations,	  institutions,	  and	  social	  structures,	  but	  it	  also	  
shapes	  them.	  (p.	  55)	  
	  
Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis	  focuses	  on	  how	  “discourses	  are	  constructed	  as	  well	  as	  
how	  they	  enact	  social	  relationships	  and	  social	  identities,	  with	  particular	  attention	  paid	  to	  
patterns	  of	  power,	  such	  as	  dominance/oppression	  and	  liberation/justice”	  (Fairclough,	  
2001;	  Gee,	  2005;	  Rogers	  &	  Wetzel,	  2014	  p.,	  71,	  citing	  Chouliaraki	  &	  Fairclough,	  1999).	  
Hence,	  language	  cannot	  be	  considered	  neutral	  because	  it	  is	  situated	  within	  political	  spheres	  
with	  focuses	  on	  social,	  racial,	  gender,	  economic,	  religious	  influences.	  Critical	  discourse	  
analysis	  is	  often	  used	  in	  working	  with	  historically	  marginalized	  groups	  of	  people	  who	  tend	  
to	  employ	  linguistic	  variations	  and	  discourse	  patterns	  that	  are	  vastly	  different	  than	  
dominant	  linguistic	  and	  discourse	  patterns	  (Rogers	  et	  al.,	  2005).	  Critical	  discourse	  analysis	  
is	  also	  used	  to	  capture	  the	  social	  nature	  of	  literacy	  in	  the	  digital	  world	  among	  youth.	  
Rebecca	  Rogers’	  groundbreaking	  ethnographic	  case	  study	  in	  critical	  discourse	  
research	  laid	  the	  foundation	  for	  the	  current	  study.	  Rebecca	  Rogers	  (2003)	  explored	  family	  

	  

69	  
literacy	  practices	  within	  the	  Treader	  household,	  an	  African-­‐American	  family.	  In	  this	  study,	  
Rogers	  (2003)	  accounted	  for	  issues	  of	  power	  and	  identity	  and	  drew	  on	  the	  connections	  
between	  literacy	  and	  society.	  Drawing	  upon	  New	  Literacy	  Studies	  (NLS)	  and	  Critical	  
Discourse	  Studies,	  Rogers	  (2003)	  highlighted	  discursive	  practices	  that	  the	  Treader	  family	  
engaged	  in	  on	  a	  daily	  basis	  as	  they	  negotiated	  literacy	  and	  language	  in	  both	  their	  home	  and	  
in	  the	  surrounding	  community.	  Rogers	  examined	  the	  raced,	  gendered	  and	  classed	  nature	  of	  
interacting	  with	  texts	  that	  constituted	  a	  family’s	  literacy	  practices.	  Rogers	  (2003)	  found	  
that	  both	  June	  (mother)	  and	  Vicky	  (daughter)	  Treader	  viewed	  themselves	  as	  less	  than	  
proficient	  and	  did	  not	  see	  themselves	  as	  literate.	  Rogers	  further	  asserted	  that	  June	  and	  
Vicky’s	  perceptions	  of	  self	  were	  rooted	  in	  institutional	  discursive	  power	  and	  the	  need	  for	  
validation	  by	  the	  dominant	  social	  institutions.	  	  
Rogers	  further	  highlighted	  June	  and	  Vicky’s	  validation	  and	  consent	  of	  the	  dominant	  
social	  institution	  through	  a	  series	  of	  student	  placement	  meetings.	  Initially,	  June’s	  intention	  
was	  to	  advocate	  to	  have	  her	  daughter	  moved	  out	  of	  the	  special	  education	  class,	  however	  
through	  institutional	  power	  June	  agrees	  to	  have	  her	  daughter,	  Vicky	  placed	  in	  a	  self-­‐
contained	  special	  education	  classroom.	  Although	  it	  was	  June’s	  intention	  to	  advocate	  for	  her	  
daughter	  to	  move	  out	  of	  the	  special	  education	  class,	  she	  agrees	  to	  Vicky	  being	  placed	  in	  the	  
self-­‐contained	  special	  education	  classroom.	  Rogers	  (2003)	  found	  that	  June	  had	  
contradictory	  social	  positions	  that	  made	  the	  work	  of	  institutional	  discursive	  power	  that	  
much	  easier.	  	  Employing	  a	  reflexive	  approach	  to	  research,	  Rogers	  (2003)	  also	  examined	  
how	  her	  own	  role	  of	  power	  complicated	  several	  series	  of	  events	  in	  the	  Treader	  home,	  the	  
student	  placement	  meeting	  in	  the	  school	  setting,	  and	  the	  larger	  community.	  	  

	  

70	  
In	  the	  current	  study,	  Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis	  was	  employed	  to	  interpret	  data	  
(Fairclough,	  1989;	  Gee,	  2005).	  By	  employing	  Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis,	  I	  acknowledge	  
that	  power,	  ideologies,	  and	  positionalities	  are	  embedded	  within	  language	  and	  literacy.	  
Literacy	  and	  language	  cannot	  be	  neutral	  because	  they	  are	  both	  rooted	  within	  social	  
practices,	  social	  institutions,	  and	  the	  lived	  experiences	  of	  my	  participants.	  Critical	  
Discourse	  Analysis	  combined	  genre,	  discourse	  and	  style	  with	  discursive	  theories	  of	  race,	  
gender,	  and	  transactional	  acts	  of	  reading	  to	  guide	  my	  analysis.	  More	  specifically,	  CDA	  
highlighted	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  participants	  draw	  upon	  their	  gendered	  and	  racialized	  
identities	  to	  construct	  critical	  social	  issues	  both	  within	  texts	  as	  well	  as	  within	  the	  larger	  
society.	  	  
Drawing	  upon	  Fairclough’s	  (1989)	  and	  Rogers’	  (2003)	  three-­‐tiered	  CDA	  analytical	  
framework,	  three	  interconnected	  research	  questions	  drive	  this	  study.	  RQ1.	  How	  do	  media	  
representations	  of	  black	  girls	  influence	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  see	  
themselves?	  (Societal).	  RQ2.	  How	  do	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  make	  meaning	  of	  street	  
literature	  texts	  in	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  spaces?	  (Institutional).	  RQ3.	  How	  does	  the	  role	  of	  the	  
researcher	  influence	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  make	  meaning	  of	  street	  
literature	  texts?	  (Situational).	  Critical	  discourse	  analysis	  was	  employed	  in	  this	  study	  to	  
shed	  light	  on	  the	  way	  media	  representations	  shape	  identity	  development	  of	  black	  
adolescent	  girls.	  CDA	  was	  also	  employed	  to	  uncover	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  literacy	  and	  
language	  are	  used	  to	  position	  historically	  marginalized	  populations,	  specifically	  black	  
adolescent	  girls,	  as	  well	  as	  the	  way	  they	  position	  themselves	  within	  a	  variety	  of	  contexts	  
including	  in	  and	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  digital	  environment,	  as	  well	  as	  an	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  
environment.	  CDA	  created	  opportunities	  for	  the	  participants	  to	  speak	  back	  to	  dominant	  

	  

71	  
framings	  of	  literacy	  prevalent	  in	  social	  structures,	  specifically	  school	  spaces.	  Additionally,	  
employing	  a	  critical	  discourse	  analysis	  framing	  allowed	  me	  to	  take	  a	  reflexive	  approach	  and	  
turn	  the	  critical	  lens	  on	  myself	  as	  a	  researcher,	  in	  order	  to	  examine	  how	  my	  position	  of	  
power	  within	  the	  book	  club	  space	  influenced	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  participants	  made	  meaning	  
of	  street	  literature	  texts.	  Although	  CDA	  framed	  this	  study	  and	  served	  as	  the	  analytical	  tool,	  
CDA	  is	  not	  a	  literacy	  framework	  or	  a	  feminist	  framework.	  Since	  this	  study	  was	  grounded	  in	  
both	  literacy	  and	  black	  feminism,	  I	  incorporated	  theoretical	  frameworks	  that	  could	  account	  
for	  literacy	  and	  positionality	  within	  and	  across	  specific	  contexts.	  
	  
Research	  Design	  	  
	  

Qualitative	  research.	  A	  qualitative	  research	  design	  was	  selected	  for	  this	  study.	  

Drawing	  upon	  the	  work	  of	  Bogdan	  and	  Biklen	  (2007),	  a	  qualitative	  research	  design	  was	  
employed	  for	  several	  reasons.	  According	  to	  Bogdan	  and	  Biklen	  (2007),	  “qualitative	  
research	  has	  actual	  settings	  as	  the	  direct	  source	  of	  data	  because	  the	  context	  in	  which	  
events	  occur	  are	  important”	  (pp.	  4-­‐6).	  This	  qualitative	  study	  was	  situated	  within	  the	  
following	  interpretive	  naturalistic	  settings:	  (a)	  an	  urban	  public	  library	  located	  in	  Upstate	  
New	  York,	  and	  (b)	  an	  online	  social	  networking	  site,	  Facebook.	  The	  Facebook	  Book	  Club	  was	  
an	  online	  space	  created	  by	  the	  researcher	  for	  the	  purpose	  of	  this	  study.	  The	  Facebook	  Book	  
Club	  discussions	  provided	  the	  primary	  data	  for	  this	  study.	  Since	  this	  study	  was	  context-­‐
specific	  (out-­‐of-­‐school	  online	  book	  club)	  focusing	  on	  how	  the	  context	  influences	  the	  
occurrence	  of	  meaning	  making,	  a	  qualitative	  research	  design	  was	  most	  appropriate.	  A	  
qualitative	  research	  design	  was	  also	  selected	  to	  examine	  how	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  make	  
meaning	  of	  literacy	  and	  make	  sense	  of	  their	  lives	  and	  the	  world	  around	  them	  (Bogdan	  &	  

	  

72	  
Biklen,	  2007).	  This	  research	  design	  also	  provided	  an	  opportunity	  to	  interpret	  how	  meaning	  
making	  shapes	  their	  representation	  of	  self.	  A	  qualitative	  research	  design	  was	  selected	  to	  
draw	  upon	  two	  traditions:	  the	  advocacy	  and	  participatory	  perspective	  (Creswell,	  2009)	  and	  
the	  critical	  research	  tradition.	  According	  to	  Creswell	  (2009)	  “an	  advocacy	  and	  participatory	  
perspective	  focuses	  on	  the	  needs	  of	  marginalized	  populations	  and	  holds	  that	  research	  
inquiry	  needs	  to	  be	  intertwined	  with	  politics	  and	  a	  political	  agenda”	  (p.	  9).	  Historically,	  
black	  adolescent	  girls’	  voices	  have	  been	  silenced	  and	  their	  narratives	  have	  been	  re-­‐told	  
both	  by	  the	  media	  and	  dominant	  social	  institutions.	  	  
Several	  scholars	  have	  highlighted	  dominant	  academic	  institutions’	  contributions	  to	  
the	  invisibility	  of	  African-­‐American’s	  literacy	  experiences	  spanning	  from	  the	  nineteenth	  
century	  to	  present	  day	  (Heath,	  1983;	  McHenry,	  2002).	  McHenry’s	  (2002)	  groundbreaking	  
historical	  research	  study	  examined	  African-­‐American	  literary	  societies	  in	  the	  nineteenth	  
century	  and	  found	  that	  academic	  institutions,	  such	  as	  libraries	  and	  museums	  excluded	  the	  
literary	  experiences	  of	  African-­‐Americans,	  although	  these	  institutions	  were	  charged	  with	  
the	  responsibility	  of	  preserving	  literary	  and	  cultural	  material	  (McHenry,	  2002).	  McHenry	  
(2002)	  found	  that	  non-­‐traditional	  institutions,	  such	  as	  churches,	  private	  homes,	  and	  beauty	  
salons,	  have	  been	  sites	  of	  literary	  dissemination	  for	  members	  in	  the	  black	  community.	  	  
Shirley	  Brice	  Heath’s	  (1983)	  classic	  ethnographic	  study	  focused	  on	  the	  literacy	  
practices	  in	  various	  sociocultural	  settings.	  Heath	  (1983)	  found	  that	  residents	  of	  two	  
working	  class	  communities	  used	  “a	  variety	  of	  literacy	  traditions”	  that	  were	  “interwoven	  in	  
different	  ways	  with	  oral	  uses	  of	  language”	  which	  challenged	  the	  dominance	  of	  school-­‐based	  
discourse	  (p.	  50).	  Heath’s	  (1983)	  study	  further	  revealed	  how	  working	  class	  children	  
struggled	  to	  assimilate	  within	  school	  settings	  that	  valued	  and	  privilege	  literacies	  other	  than	  

	  

73	  
their	  own	  and	  how	  such	  a	  standard	  became	  a	  source	  of	  communication	  problems	  for	  both	  
teachers	  and	  students.	  Heath	  (1983)	  further	  revealed	  that	  students	  of	  color	  and	  those	  from	  
working	  class	  and	  economically	  marginalized	  backgrounds	  often	  experience	  a	  mismatch	  
between	  cultures	  and	  languages	  at	  home	  and	  in	  their	  community	  and	  the	  academic	  
language	  experienced	  at	  school.	  	  Employing	  an	  advocacy	  and	  participatory	  perspective	  in	  
this	  current	  study	  seeks	  to	  honor	  black	  adolescent	  girls’	  literacy	  and	  language.	  In	  doing	  so,	  
it	  also	  privileges	  black	  adolescent	  girls’	  perspectives	  in	  their	  own	  voices	  and	  provides	  a	  safe	  
space	  to	  address	  social	  issues	  of	  empowerment,	  oppression,	  domination,	  and	  alienation	  
(Creswell,	  2009;	  Collins,	  2000).	  	  	  
An	  advocacy	  and	  participatory	  perspective	  also	  provided	  an	  opportunity	  to	  examine	  
the	  power	  dynamics	  that	  the	  researcher	  inherently	  holds	  and	  the	  ways	  it	  influences	  how	  
black	  adolescent	  girls	  make	  meaning	  of	  texts.	  In	  acknowledging	  the	  existence	  of	  power	  
dynamics,	  an	  advocacy	  and	  participatory	  perspective	  is	  the	  key	  participant	  for	  data	  
collection	  in	  a	  qualitative	  study.	  As	  a	  result,	  literacy	  and	  language	  are	  never	  neutral.	  The	  
goal	  of	  this	  study	  was	  emancipatory	  and	  activist-­‐driven	  creating	  an	  opportunity	  for	  black	  
adolescent	  girls	  to	  disrupt	  dominant	  framings	  of	  how	  they	  see	  themselves,	  who	  they	  are	  as	  
readers	  of	  street	  literature	  texts,	  and	  who	  they	  are	  as	  discussants	  of	  texts	  in	  digital	  spaces.	  	  
The	  current	  qualitative	  study	  is	  informed	  by	  the	  critical	  research	  tradition	  where	  
issues	  of	  power	  and	  privilege	  are	  central.	  Critical	  research	  is	  designed	  to	  engage	  
marginalized	  populations	  by	  confronting	  injustice,	  in	  an	  effort	  to	  empower	  (Carspecken,	  
1996;	  Kincheloe	  &	  McLaren,	  1998;	  McLaren,	  1994).	  Hence,	  critical	  research	  becomes	  an	  
informative	  endeavor	  (Carspecken,	  1996).	  Mayan	  (2009,	  citing	  Thomas,	  1992)	  has	  
depicted	  it	  as	  “analyzing	  and	  opening	  to	  scrutiny	  otherwise	  hidden	  agendas,	  power	  centers,	  

	  

74	  
and	  assumptions	  that	  inhibit,	  repress,	  and	  constrain”	  (p.	  39).	  The	  current	  qualitative	  study	  
was	  situated	  within	  the	  critical	  research	  tradition	  for	  the	  following	  reasons:	  (a)	  to	  provide	  
an	  opportunity	  to	  explore	  how	  a	  broader	  notion	  of	  literacy	  provides	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  
an	  outlet	  that	  encourages	  freedom	  of	  expression	  and	  offers	  autonomy;	  (b)	  to	  examine	  the	  
interplay	  of	  power	  and	  positionality	  in	  both	  an	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  online	  and	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  book	  
club	  setting	  and	  the	  ways	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  negotiate	  in	  both	  spheres;	  (c)	  to	  link	  black	  
adolescent	  girls’	  literacy	  experiences	  to	  broader	  structures	  of	  social	  power	  and	  explain	  how	  
these	  structures	  reinforce	  existing	  social	  images	  of	  them;	  and	  (4)	  to	  create	  	  “safe	  spaces”	  for	  
the	  voices	  of	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  that	  are	  often	  absent	  in	  traditional	  research	  (Collins,	  
2000;	  Morrell,	  2004a).	  	  
	  

Selection	  and	  description	  of	  the	  focal	  site.	  There	  were	  two	  focal	  sites	  employed	  in	  

this	  study:	  Facebook	  and	  an	  urban	  public	  library.	  The	  primary	  focal	  site	  was	  the	  online	  
social	  networking	  site	  Facebook	  and	  the	  secondary	  focal	  site	  was	  an	  urban	  public	  library.	  	  
Facebook	  was	  the	  popular	  online	  social	  networking	  site	  used	  for	  the	  online	  book	  club.	  
Facebook	  was	  a	  focal	  site	  in	  this	  research	  study	  for	  several	  reasons.	  Facebook	  was	  selected	  
as	  the	  most	  appropriate	  online	  tool	  because	  it	  did	  not	  have	  character	  limitations,	  which	  
allowed	  for	  richer	  data.	  Facebook	  was	  also	  selected	  because	  it	  provided	  a	  “closed	  group”	  
environment	  that	  offered	  participants	  a	  sense	  of	  security	  and	  privacy.	  The	  “closed	  group”	  
feature	  also	  ensured	  that	  participants’	  conversations	  specific	  to	  the	  book	  club	  were	  
separate	  from	  their	  other	  conversations	  on	  Facebook.	  
Dunbar	  Public	  Library	  (pseudonym)	  is	  an	  urban	  public	  library	  nestled	  in	  a	  small	  
town	  in	  the	  northeast	  region	  of	  the	  United	  States.	  Dunbar	  Public	  Library	  was	  the	  
community	  space	  used	  to	  conduct	  focus	  group	  interviews	  and	  journal	  writing	  sessions.	  

	  

75	  
Dunbar	  Public	  Library	  is	  located	  within	  a	  predominantly	  black	  community	  on	  the	  south	  
side	  of	  town.	  The	  library	  is	  centrally	  located	  and	  is	  within	  a	  three-­‐block	  radius	  of	  several	  
urban	  middle	  schools.	  The	  building	  is	  situated	  on	  a	  corner	  lot	  adjacent	  to	  the	  main	  street.	  
The	  library	  employs	  a	  young	  adult	  librarian	  and	  recently	  built	  a	  teen	  center	  on	  the	  second	  
floor.	  The	  teen	  space,	  created	  due	  to	  the	  limited	  space	  for	  young	  adults	  on	  the	  main	  floor,	  
was	  designed	  to	  host	  teen,	  art,	  and	  reading	  programs.	  The	  library	  staff	  relies	  heavily	  on	  
outside	  programming	  to	  serve	  patrons.	  	  
Dunbar	  Public	  Library	  was	  selected	  as	  the	  focal	  site	  for	  several	  reasons.	  This	  
research	  study	  is	  part	  of	  a	  larger	  goal	  to	  increase	  library	  programming	  for	  adolescents	  on	  
the	  south	  side	  of	  town,	  in	  the	  hopes	  of	  increasing	  adolescent	  users	  within	  the	  library.	  
Limited	  programming	  and	  an	  underdeveloped	  teen	  collection	  have	  resulted	  in	  a	  steady	  
decrease	  in	  adolescent	  library	  users.	  Of	  the	  few	  library	  programs	  offered	  at	  Dunbar	  Public	  
Library,	  many	  of	  them	  are	  geared	  towards	  children	  and	  not	  teens.	  Many	  of	  the	  black	  
adolescent	  girls	  that	  I	  have	  worked	  with	  on	  previous	  community	  workshops	  and	  programs	  
expressed	  an	  interest	  in	  an	  online	  street	  literature	  book	  club.	  This	  research	  study	  fulfilled	  a	  
community	  need	  and	  provided	  an	  opportunity	  to	  create	  a	  literacy	  program	  that	  was	  teen-­‐
focused,	  girl-­‐centered,	  and	  culturally	  relevant.	  For	  the	  past	  several	  years,	  I	  have	  been	  both	  a	  
community	  organizer	  in	  the	  surrounding	  community	  and	  a	  supporter	  of	  children	  and	  
young	  adult	  programming	  at	  Dunbar	  Public	  Library.	  My	  continuous	  collaboration	  and	  
partnership	  with	  Dunbar	  Public	  Library	  and	  the	  surrounding	  community	  have	  allowed	  me	  
to	  gain	  access	  to	  the	  space	  and	  the	  few	  teens	  that	  frequent	  the	  library.	  	  	  
Recruitment	  and	  selection	  of	  adolescent	  participants.	  Seven	  female	  adolescent	  
participants	  between	  the	  ages	  of	  12-­‐17	  were	  originally	  recruited	  to	  participate	  in	  the	  

	  

76	  
research	  study.	  Of	  the	  seven	  participants,	  one	  participant	  did	  not	  attend	  any	  of	  the	  online	  
or	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  sessions	  and	  another	  participant	  only	  attended	  the	  first	  online	  book	  
discussion.	  A	  total	  of	  five	  adolescent	  female	  participants	  (Tammy,	  Denise,	  Lisa,	  Keisha,	  and	  
Nicki)	  actively	  participated	  over	  the	  course	  of	  the	  research	  study.	  Recruitment	  included	  the	  
distribution	  of	  flyers	  throughout	  the	  community,	  including	  neighboring	  community	  
centers,	  middle	  schools,	  high	  schools,	  barbershops,	  hair	  salons,	  and	  churches.	  The	  
recruitment	  materials	  described	  the	  nature	  of	  the	  research	  project	  and	  the	  nature	  of	  
participants’	  involvement.	  Flyers	  were	  also	  displayed	  at	  the	  Dunbar	  Public	  Library.	  An	  
electronic	  flyer	  was	  also	  posted	  on	  the	  public	  library’s	  official	  Facebook	  page,	  as	  well	  as	  on	  
the	  public	  library	  system’s	  website.	  
This	  qualitative	  study	  employed	  two	  types	  of	  sampling:	  purposive	  sampling	  and	  
convenience	  sampling.	  Employing	  a	  purposive	  sampling	  (Patton,	  1990),	  the	  selection	  
criteria	  included	  the	  following:	  (a)	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  between	  the	  ages	  of	  12-­‐17,	  (b)	  the	  
selection	  of	  participants	  that	  were	  avid	  readers	  of	  street	  literature	  texts,	  (c)	  black	  
adolescent	  girls	  that	  used	  digital	  tools,	  specifically	  the	  social	  networking	  site,	  Facebook,	  
and	  (d)	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  that	  had	  immediate	  access	  to	  technology.	  Employing	  a	  
convenience	  sampling	  entailed	  selecting	  participants	  that	  I	  had	  worked	  with	  on	  previous	  
community	  literacy	  projects.	  	  
After	  the	  recruiting	  process	  was	  completed,	  I	  met	  with	  each	  potential	  participant	  
individually	  and	  informed	  her	  about	  the	  research	  project	  and	  answered	  any	  further	  
questions	  she	  had	  about	  the	  project.	  All	  potential	  participants	  met	  the	  criteria	  to	  
participate	  in	  the	  research	  study.	  Participants	  discussed	  their	  leisure	  activities	  outside	  of	  
school,	  favorite	  books,	  and	  musical	  interests.	  In	  order	  to	  build	  rapport	  both	  individually	  

	  

77	  
and	  collectively,	  participants	  were	  invited	  to	  attend	  a	  meet	  and	  greet	  one	  week	  prior	  to	  the	  
start	  of	  the	  program.	  The	  meet	  and	  greet	  event	  was	  held	  at	  the	  Dunbar	  Public	  Library	  and	  
offered	  participants	  the	  opportunity	  to	  learn	  more	  about	  each	  other	  in	  a	  relaxed	  setting.	  
Participants	  discussed	  their	  musical	  interests	  and	  their	  favorite	  books.	  
	  
Participant	  Profiles	  	  	  
Each	  profile	  (pseudonyms	  have	  been	  used)	  is	  based	  on	  an	  individual	  meeting,	  as	  well	  
as	  a	  meet	  and	  greet	  session	  that	  occurred	  prior	  to	  the	  start	  of	  the	  program.	  The	  profiles	  
provide	  insight	  into	  participants’	  positionalities	  as	  black	  girls,	  as	  readers	  of	  street	  literature	  
texts,	  and	  as	  users	  of	  digital	  technologies.	  Each	  study	  participant	  demonstrated	  similarities	  
and	  differences	  in	  the	  ways	  they	  made	  sense	  of	  the	  world	  around	  them	  and	  the	  ways	  they	  
made	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts	  in	  the	  online	  book	  club.	  
Vanessa.	  Vanessa	  is	  an	  11th	  grader.	  She	  is	  one	  of	  two	  children.	  She	  lives	  with	  her	  
family	  on	  the	  eastside	  of	  town	  in	  a	  house	  that	  is	  situated	  on	  a	  busy	  intersection.	  Vanessa’s	  
house	  is	  situated	  further	  back	  from	  the	  street	  surrounded	  by	  low	  hanging	  trees,	  several	  
flower	  beds,	  and	  a	  well	  manicured	  lawn.	  Vanessa	  is	  an	  active	  member	  of	  the	  community.	  
She	  is	  a	  team	  captain	  for	  her	  younger	  sister’s	  cheerleading	  team	  at	  the	  local	  middle	  school.	  
She	  also	  works	  at	  a	  local	  restaurant	  a	  few	  days	  in	  a	  week.	  She	  would	  like	  to	  be	  an	  Alpha	  
Kappa	  Alpha,	  a	  national	  sorority	  for	  black	  women.	  She	  plans	  to	  attend	  the	  University	  of	  
Rochester	  and	  is	  currently	  preparing	  to	  take	  college	  preparatory	  courses.	  
In	  my	  first	  one-­‐on-­‐one	  meeting	  with	  Vanessa,	  she	  seemed	  reserved.	  She	  mentioned	  
that	  she	  was	  the	  oldest	  of	  two	  and	  her	  mother	  works	  nights,	  so	  she	  helps	  her	  mother	  by	  
taking	  care	  of	  her	  younger	  sister	  and	  tending	  to	  the	  house.	  Vanessa	  stated,	  

	  

78	  
Last	  year	  I	  found	  myself	  trying	  to	  please	  others,	  in	  order	  to	  fit	  in.	  I	  spent	  much	  of	  last	  
school	  year	  hanging	  with	  the	  wrong	  crowds	  even	  though	  I	  knew	  I	  should	  not	  have	  
been	  hanging	  with	  them.	  It	  got	  to	  the	  point	  that	  I	  had	  to	  distance	  myself	  from	  them	  
in	  order	  to	  focus	  and	  stay	  true	  to	  myself.	  (Vanessa,	  personal	  communication,	  August	  
15,	  2013)	  
	  
She	  also	  mentioned	  that	  some	  people	  say	  she	  “sounds	  white	  or	  acts	  white.”	  Vanessa	  further	  
mentioned	  that	  most	  of	  her	  friends	  are	  white	  and	  that	  it	  wasn’t	  for	  any	  particular	  reason	  
but	  that	  “it	  just	  was”	  (Vanessa,	  personal	  communication,	  August	  15,	  2013).	  
	  

Vanessa	  is	  an	  avid	  reader	  and	  enjoys	  reading	  all	  types	  of	  books	  in	  her	  spare	  time.	  

She	  pulled	  out	  her	  cell	  phone	  and	  leaned	  in	  closer	  as	  she	  scrolled	  through	  her	  phone	  
showing	  me	  covers	  of	  adolescent	  texts	  she	  had	  just	  read,	  which	  included	  The	  Watsons	  Go	  to	  
Birmingham	  by	  Christopher	  Paul	  Curtis,	  The	  Skin	  Im	  In	  by	  Sharon	  Flake,	  and	  Monster	  by	  
Walter	  Dean	  Myers.	  In	  a	  conversation	  with	  her	  mother,	  Jackie,	  her	  mother	  stated	  “What	  
books	  will	  they	  be	  reading?”	  I	  showed	  her	  the	  list	  and	  she	  stated	  “this	  would	  be	  good	  for	  
her.	  	  Ya	  gonna	  love	  these	  books.	  I	  want	  Vanessa	  to	  get	  in	  the	  habit	  of	  reading	  more.	  She	  
ALWAYS	  see	  me	  reading.	  I	  buy	  Vanessa	  Kindles,	  Kindle	  Fires,	  and	  iPad	  Minis	  and	  she	  don’t	  
use	  ‘em”	  (Jackie,	  personal	  communication,	  August,	  10,	  2013).	  Vanessa	  then	  chimed	  in	  and	  
stated	  “I	  prefer	  to	  have	  a	  physical	  book	  than	  to	  use	  an	  e-­‐book	  reader”	  (Vanessa,	  personal	  
communication,	  August	  10,	  2013).	  Jackie	  responded	  “Ya	  need	  to	  have	  a	  book	  club	  for	  
parents!”	  as	  she	  leans	  in	  and	  scrolls	  down	  her	  phone	  to	  show	  me	  the	  street	  literature	  texts	  
she	  has	  read	  and	  plans	  to	  read	  (Jackie,	  personal	  communication,	  August	  10,	  2013).	  	  
	  

Denise.	  Denise	  is	  a	  14-­‐year	  old	  freshman	  at	  a	  local	  high	  school.	  Denise	  is	  the	  oldest	  

of	  3	  children.	  She	  lives	  in	  a	  housing	  development	  on	  the	  east	  side	  of	  town.	  She	  is	  an	  active	  
member	  of	  her	  church,	  and	  she	  often	  leads	  praise	  and	  worship	  on	  Youth	  Sunday,	  which	  is	  
usually	  the	  5th	  Sunday	  of	  the	  month.	  Denise	  is	  never	  afraid	  to	  talk	  about	  the	  Bible	  and	  how	  

	  

79	  
much	  she	  loves	  Jesus.	  She	  is	  an	  A	  student	  and	  a	  critical	  and	  analytical	  thinker.	  Denise	  was	  
selected	  as	  one	  of	  two	  academic	  achievers	  in	  the	  city	  by	  a	  local	  sorority.	  She	  is	  a	  social	  
butterfly	  at	  times.	  Denise	  is	  very	  ambitious,	  confident,	  outspoken,	  and	  assertive	  setting	  
high	  goals	  for	  herself.	  	  She	  plans	  to	  attend	  college	  once	  she	  graduates	  from	  high	  school.	  She	  
has	  plans	  to	  be	  a	  lawyer,	  a	  teacher,	  and	  a	  singer.	  Denise	  has	  several	  supports	  in	  the	  
community,	  including	  her	  family,	  her	  church,	  and	  what	  is	  known	  in	  the	  black	  community	  as	  
“other	  mothers”	  (black	  women	  that	  are	  not	  related	  to	  her,	  yet	  serve	  as	  mother	  figures).	  
Denise	  is	  wise	  beyond	  her	  years	  and	  has	  what	  elders	  in	  the	  black	  community	  would	  
describe	  as	  an	  “old	  soul.”	  	  
In	  a	  one-­‐on-­‐one	  meeting	  with	  her	  she	  stated,	  “I’m	  less	  concerned	  with	  peer	  pressure	  
and	  more	  concerned	  with	  making	  the	  best	  possible	  decisions.	  No	  one	  has	  to	  like	  me	  and	  
live	  with	  my	  decisions	  but	  me”	  (Denise,	  personal	  communication,	  August	  16,	  2013).	  She	  is	  
an	  avid	  reader	  and	  reads	  books	  and	  enjoys	  texts	  from	  a	  variety	  of	  genres.	  She	  enjoys	  
romance	  novels	  as	  well.	  At	  the	  time	  that	  the	  research	  project	  began,	  Denise	  had	  just	  
finished	  reading	  The	  Bluest	  Eye	  by	  Toni	  Morrison	  and	  The	  Color	  Purple	  by	  Alice	  Walker.	  
	  

Keisha.	  Keisha	  is	  a	  16-­‐year	  old	  and	  attends	  a	  local	  high	  school	  in	  town.	  Keisha	  is	  an	  

A	  student	  and	  involved	  in	  several	  community	  projects	  across	  the	  city.	  She	  works	  a	  few	  days	  
a	  week	  as	  a	  junior	  peer	  counselor	  at	  the	  local	  community	  center	  in	  her	  neighborhood.	  She	  
lives	  in	  a	  house	  on	  the	  east	  side	  of	  town.	  She	  is	  the	  youngest	  of	  3	  children.	  Keisha	  has	  a	  laid	  
back	  demeanor,	  and	  she	  is	  shy	  when	  she	  first	  meets	  someone.	  She	  likes	  animals	  and	  plans	  
to	  attend	  college	  further	  upstate	  and	  major	  in	  veterinary	  studies.	  	  
Keisha	  mentions	  that	  “my	  school	  schedule	  is	  pretty	  tight,	  so	  I	  don’t	  often	  have	  
leisure	  time”	  (Keisha,	  personal	  communication,	  August	  6,	  2013).	  When	  she	  is	  able	  to	  carve	  

	  

80	  
spare	  time	  out	  of	  her	  busy	  schedule,	  she	  enjoys	  watching	  the	  show	  Criminal	  Minds	  on	  the	  
A&E	  network.	  Keisha	  also	  enjoys	  going	  outside	  to	  hang	  with	  friends	  and	  laugh.	  Keisha	  
describes	  herself	  as	  a	  “fashionista”	  and	  enjoys	  shopping	  both	  online	  and	  in	  stores	  for	  the	  
latest	  fashions.	  Keisha	  also	  enjoys	  reading	  street	  literature	  texts	  and	  prefers	  when	  it	  is	  a	  
series	  and	  not	  a	  single	  book.	  At	  the	  time	  of	  our	  one	  on	  one	  meeting,	  Keisha	  had	  just	  finished	  
reading	  The	  Cartel	  Series	  by	  Ashley	  and	  JaQuavis.	  Keisha	  was	  excited	  about	  the	  list	  of	  books	  
for	  our	  project,	  since	  she	  had	  not	  read	  any	  of	  them,	  except	  PUSH.	  
Lisa.	  Lisa	  is	  15-­‐years	  old	  and	  attends	  a	  local	  high	  school	  in	  the	  city.	  She	  is	  the	  oldest	  
of	  5	  children	  and	  lives	  in	  a	  house	  on	  the	  east	  side	  of	  town	  with	  her	  grandparents.	  Lisa	  is	  
very	  shy	  and	  reserved.	  She	  lives	  a	  block	  away	  from	  a	  local	  community	  center	  that	  she	  
frequents.	  Lisa	  is	  a	  B	  student	  and	  admits	  that	  she	  could	  be	  a	  much	  better	  student	  if	  she	  
focused	  more	  on	  her	  studies	  and	  less	  on	  the	  social	  aspects	  of	  high	  school.	  She	  mentioned	  
that	  she	  is	  “a	  loner”	  at	  times	  and	  “likes	  her	  alone	  time	  and	  personal	  space	  from	  time	  to	  
time”	  (Lisa,	  personal	  communication,	  August	  5,	  2013).	  Lisa	  mentioned	  I	  am	  “pretty	  low	  key	  
until	  you	  really	  get	  to	  know	  me,	  then	  you	  can’t	  shut	  me	  up”	  (Lisa,	  personal	  communication,	  
August	  5,	  2013).	  
She	  plans	  to	  be	  more	  focused	  this	  school	  year	  and	  plans	  to	  do	  better	  academically	  so	  
she	  can	  achieve	  her	  goals.	  Lisa	  plans	  to	  attend	  a	  Historically	  Black	  College,	  like	  Clark	  
Atlanta	  and	  wants	  to	  become	  a	  teacher	  or	  a	  doctor.	  Although	  she	  states,	  “I	  don’t	  do	  nothing	  
in	  my	  spare	  time	  because	  I	  don’t	  have	  any,”	  she	  enjoys	  watching	  movies,	  watching	  popular	  
television	  shows,	  and	  listening	  to	  music	  with	  friends	  (Lisa,	  personal	  communication,	  
August	  5,	  2013).	  	  She	  is	  an	  avid	  reader	  and	  just	  finished	  the	  street	  literature	  texts,	  A	  Piece	  of	  
Cake:	  A	  Memoir	  by	  Cupcake	  Brown.	  	  	  

	  

81	  
	  

Nicki.	  Nicki	  is	  a	  17-­‐year	  old	  queer	  female	  and	  attends	  a	  local	  high	  school	  on	  the	  east	  

side	  of	  town.	  She	  describes	  herself	  as	  “the	  life	  of	  the	  party”	  (Nicki,	  personal	  communication,	  
August	  20,	  2013).	  She	  enjoys	  different	  sports,	  but	  enjoys	  playing	  basketball	  the	  most.	  She	  
plays	  the	  point	  guard	  position	  and	  is	  currently	  on	  her	  school’s	  Varsity	  Team.	  Nicki	  
graduates	  in	  May	  of	  2014.	  Nicki	  lives	  with	  her	  father	  on	  the	  east	  side	  of	  town.	  She	  enjoys	  
watching	  NBA	  and	  College	  games	  with	  her	  father.	  She	  also	  enjoys	  going	  to	  the	  movies,	  
listening	  to	  music,	  and	  hanging	  out	  with	  friends.	  Nicki	  enjoys	  rap	  music	  and	  her	  favorite	  
artists	  are	  Future	  and	  Drake.	  She	  wants	  to	  be	  in	  both	  the	  music	  industry	  as	  a	  sound	  
engineer	  and	  the	  sports	  industry	  as	  a	  point	  guard.	  	  She	  enjoys	  reading,	  but	  hasn’t	  come	  
across	  books	  that	  piqued	  her	  interest	  lately.	  
	  

Tammy.	  Tammy	  is	  a	  16	  year	  old	  and	  attends	  a	  local	  high	  school	  on	  the	  east	  side.	  She	  

has	  two	  brothers	  and	  is	  the	  middle	  child	  in	  her	  family.	  Tammy	  is	  a	  B	  student,	  but	  wants	  to	  
“pull	  her	  grades	  up”	  and	  be	  an	  A	  student.	  Tammy	  states,	  “there	  is	  no	  reason	  why	  I	  can’t	  
becuz	  I	  can	  do	  the	  work.	  No	  Excuse”	  (Tammy,	  personal	  communication,	  August,	  17,	  2013).	  
Tammy	  is	  a	  girl	  scout	  and	  feels	  that	  being	  part	  of	  the	  girl	  scouts	  has	  taught	  her	  life	  skills,	  
like	  “being	  responsible,	  respecting	  your	  peers,	  respecting	  authority,	  and	  being	  a	  leader”	  
(Tammy,	  personal	  communication,	  August	  17,	  2013).	  She	  is	  also	  part	  of	  a	  step	  team	  in	  her	  
community.	  Tammy	  plans	  to	  go	  to	  college.	  She	  is	  undecided	  on	  her	  major	  because	  she	  has	  
“so	  many	  interests,”	  but	  she	  will	  be	  attending	  her	  school’s	  career	  day	  and	  hopes	  that	  she	  
can	  narrow	  down	  her	  interests.	  Tammy	  is	  an	  avid	  reader.	  She	  enjoys	  graphic	  novels,	  
historical	  fiction,	  and	  street	  literature	  texts.	  She	  looked	  forward	  to	  meeting	  new	  people	  and	  
making	  new	  friends	  in	  the	  book	  club.	  
	  

	  

82	  
Pilot	  Study	  
	  

Methodological	  decisions	  in	  the	  current	  qualitative	  study	  were	  heavily	  informed	  by	  

a	  pilot	  study	  conducted	  from	  January	  2010-­‐January	  2011.	  The	  pilot	  study	  examined	  how	  
young	  adults’	  participation	  in	  a	  virtual	  community	  influences	  a	  sense	  of	  community.	  The	  
pilot	  study	  also	  examined	  how	  young	  adults	  engaged	  in	  reading	  in	  a	  virtual	  community.	  
Fledging	  by	  Octavia	  Butler	  was	  the	  young	  adult	  literature	  text	  that	  participants	  discussed	  in	  
the	  virtual	  community.	  
	  

In	  the	  spring	  of	  2010,	  I	  co-­‐taught	  a	  graduate-­‐level	  adolescent	  literature	  course	  in	  the	  

school	  of	  education	  department	  at	  a	  private	  university	  in	  Upstate	  New	  York.	  The	  course	  
consisted	  of	  pre-­‐service	  and	  in-­‐service	  literacy	  teachers.	  The	  course	  was	  designed	  to	  
familiarize	  teachers	  with	  texts	  written	  for	  and	  by	  young	  adults.	  One	  of	  the	  requirements	  for	  
the	  literacy	  teachers	  enrolled	  in	  the	  graduate	  course	  was	  to	  participate	  in	  an	  online	  Twitter	  
Book	  Club	  with	  students	  from	  a	  local	  high	  school.	  Although	  participation	  in	  the	  online	  book	  
club	  was	  a	  requirement	  for	  the	  literacy	  teachers,	  participation	  for	  the	  high	  school	  students	  
was	  not	  part	  of	  classroom	  instruction	  and	  functioned	  as	  an	  afterschool	  online	  book	  club.	  
Thirty	  graduate	  students	  and	  sixty	  high	  school	  students	  participated	  in	  the	  online	  book	  
club.	  The	  school	  librarian	  at	  the	  local	  high	  school	  moderated	  the	  online	  book	  club.	  The	  
school	  librarian	  moderated	  the	  online	  book	  club,	  however,	  participants	  sustained	  book	  club	  
discussions.	  
	  

I	  served	  as	  a	  participant	  observer	  in	  the	  pilot	  study.	  Data	  included	  participants’	  

tweets,	  as	  well	  as	  informal	  conversations	  with	  participants	  about	  their	  experiences	  
participating	  in	  the	  online	  book	  club.	  I	  conducted	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  informal	  interviews	  with	  the	  
literacy	  teachers	  and	  online	  informal	  interviews	  with	  the	  high	  school	  students	  to	  get	  a	  

	  

83	  
sense	  of	  their	  experiences	  as	  members	  of	  a	  virtual	  community,	  their	  reading	  engagements	  
in	  the	  online	  book	  club,	  as	  well	  as	  their	  information	  exchange	  with	  high	  school	  students.	  
Data	  were	  coded	  through	  the	  use	  of	  open	  coding.	  
	  
Conceptual	  Framework	  
Hersberger,	  Murray,	  and	  Rioux	  (2007)	  Conceptual	  Framework	  for	  Examining	  Online	  
Virtual	  Communities	  was	  employed	  in	  this	  exploratory	  research	  study.	  	  The	  conceptual	  
framework	  represented	  a	  four-­‐tier	  pyramid:	  Tier	  1-­‐Foundational	  Building	  Blocks	  
(Membership,	  Influence,	  Integration	  &	  Fulfillment	  of	  Needs,	  and	  Shared	  Emotional	  
Connection),	  Tier	  2—Social	  Networks	  as	  Information	  Networks,	  Tier	  3—Information	  
Exchange,	  Tier	  4—Information	  Acquiring	  and	  Sharing.	  Each	  tier	  of	  the	  conceptual	  
framework	  moved	  from	  a	  focus	  on	  the	  community	  to	  a	  focus	  on	  the	  individual	  (Hersberger	  
et	  al.,	  2007).    
  
Pilot	  Study	  Key	  Findings  
	  

Tier	  1:	  Foundational	  building	  blocks.	  In	  Hersberger’s	  et	  al.	  (2007)	  conceptual	  

framework,	  Tier	  1:	  Foundational	  Building	  Blocks	  is	  based	  on	  McMillan	  and	  Chavis’	  (1986)	  
basic	  building	  blocks	  of	  community.	  These	  building	  blocks	  include:	  
Membership.	  High	  school	  and	  graduate	  participants	  initiated	  several	  forms	  of	  
membership.	  Several	  of	  the	  high	  school	  participants	  were	  users	  of	  Twitter	  prior	  to	  the	  
study,	  while	  very	  few	  graduate	  participants	  were	  users	  of	  Twitter	  prior	  to	  the	  study.	  
Establishing	  membership	  posed	  a	  problem	  for	  a	  few	  of	  the	  graduate	  participants	  because	  
they	  had	  limited	  computer	  skills.	  This	  often	  interfered	  with	  their	  frequency	  in	  posting	  to	  

	  

84	  
the	  online	  discussions.	  However,	  the	  high	  school	  participants	  used	  Twitter	  as	  their	  primary	  
source	  of	  communication,	  so	  they	  established	  membership	  rather	  quickly.	  Despite	  
participants’	  level	  of	  experience	  using	  technology	  and	  Twitter,	  all	  participants	  were	  active	  
throughout	  the	  course	  of	  the	  study.	  This	  might	  have	  stemmed	  mostly	  from	  the	  fact	  that	  a	  
portion	  of	  the	  graduate	  participants’	  grades	  were	  based	  on	  their	  involvement	  in	  the	  online	  
book	  club	  while	  high	  school	  participants’	  involvement	  in	  the	  online	  book	  club	  had	  no	  
academic	  requirement	  associated	  with	  it.	  	  
Although	  all	  participants	  were	  active,	  many	  of	  the	  graduate	  participants	  had	  
difficulty	  participating	  in	  online	  discussions	  for	  several	  reasons:	  (a)	  Because	  of	  the	  
character	  limits	  on	  Twitter,	  many	  of	  the	  high	  school	  participants	  created	  their	  own	  
abbreviation	  system	  and	  seemed	  to	  “communicate	  in	  codes”	  (Graduate	  participant,	  
personal	  communication,	  January	  13,	  2011).	  The	  use	  of	  codes	  seemed	  to	  be	  an	  effective	  
means	  for	  high	  school	  participants	  to	  discuss	  the	  texts	  online,	  however,	  many	  of	  the	  
graduate	  participants	  felt	  alienated	  by	  the	  process.	  This	  resulted	  in	  graduate	  participants	  
engaging	  in	  book	  discussions	  with	  their	  colleagues	  informally	  in	  a	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  setting.	  (b)	  
Throughout	  the	  course	  of	  the	  Twitter	  Book	  Club,	  graduate	  participants	  spoke	  extensively	  
about	  having	  difficulty	  adjusting	  to	  this	  new	  form	  of	  communication	  and	  in	  most	  cases	  felt	  
the	  character	  limits	  hindered	  their	  participation	  in	  the	  online	  book	  club.	  (c)	  Graduate	  
participants	  also	  found	  the	  online	  structure	  of	  Twitter	  “alienating”	  because	  “there	  was	  no	  
main	  thread	  which	  made	  it	  difficult	  to	  follow	  discussions.”	  High	  school	  participants	  seemed	  
to	  navigate	  successfully	  without	  a	  main	  thread	  and	  often	  used	  the	  “follow”	  feature	  to	  
continue	  conversations	  with	  particular	  book	  club	  members.	  The	  “follow”	  featured	  tended	  

	  

85	  
to	  be	  used	  among	  high	  school	  participants	  who	  had	  shared	  personal	  experiences	  with	  other	  
participants.	  
Influence.	  In	  the	  Twitter	  Book	  Club,	  the	  school	  library	  media	  specialist	  was	  the	  
moderator	  and	  influenced	  member	  participation.	  One	  major	  factor	  that	  influenced	  
participation	  was	  the	  fact	  that	  high	  school	  participants	  began	  reading	  the	  text	  a	  few	  days	  
later	  than	  the	  graduate	  participants.	  Graduate	  participants	  were	  encouraged	  to	  not	  
comment	  on	  chapters	  in	  the	  text	  that	  high	  school	  participants	  had	  not	  read	  yet.	  This	  
required	  graduate	  participants	  to	  read	  previous	  posts	  to	  be	  certain	  that	  they	  were	  
responding	  to	  aspects	  of	  the	  texts	  that	  high	  school	  participants	  had	  already	  read.	  To	  ensure	  
that	  high	  school	  participants	  were	  not	  conversing	  solely	  with	  other	  high	  school	  participants	  
in	  the	  online	  book	  club,	  graduate	  participants	  were	  encouraged	  to	  actively	  converse	  with	  
high	  school	  participants.	  	  
Integration	  and	  fulfillment	  of	  needs.	  For	  the	  graduate	  participants,	  integration	  
into	  the	  Twitter	  Book	  Club	  was	  intertwined	  with	  the	  fulfillment	  of	  needs.	  Although	  all	  
graduate	  students	  made	  the	  decision	  to	  become	  members	  of	  the	  virtual	  community,	  their	  
decisions	  were	  based	  on	  their	  need	  to	  successfully	  complete	  the	  course.	  Hence	  
participation	  in	  the	  online	  book	  club	  was	  motivated	  by	  external	  factors.	  Subsequently,	  all	  
graduate	  students	  were	  active	  participants	  in	  the	  Twitter	  Book	  Club	  over	  the	  course	  of	  the	  
sessions.	  	  
Shared	  emotional	  connection.	  Graduate	  participants	  shared	  emotional	  connections	  
as	  members	  of	  the	  same	  graduate	  cohort	  while	  high	  school	  participants	  shared	  emotional	  
connections	  as	  classmates	  and	  members	  of	  previous	  book	  clubs.	  In	  the	  young	  adult	  text	  
Fledgling,	  the	  character	  Shorie,	  an	  African-­‐American	  woman,	  is	  subjected	  to	  a	  great	  deal	  of	  

	  

86	  
racism	  and	  discrimination.	  Both	  graduate	  and	  high	  school	  participants	  shared	  emotional	  
connection	  when	  prompted	  by	  the	  book	  club	  facilitator	  to	  connect	  situations	  in	  the	  text	  
with	  personal	  experiences	  in	  their	  own	  lives.	  In	  one	  example,	  the	  facilitator	  tweeted	  the	  
following	  to	  the	  online	  group:	  “Shorie	  experienced	  racism	  and	  betrayal	  both	  by	  humans	  as	  
well	  as	  by	  Ina.	  How	  might	  you	  relate	  Shorie’s	  experience	  to	  your	  own	  experience?”	  
(Participant,	  personal	  communication,	  January	  12,	  2011).	  Two	  high	  school	  participants	  
spoke	  about	  their	  experiences	  with	  racism	  while	  a	  graduate	  participant	  discussed	  how	  he	  
was	  discriminated	  against	  because	  of	  a	  learning	  disability	  that	  he	  had	  as	  a	  young	  boy.
