Tier	  2:	  Social	  networks	  as	  information	  networks.	  My	  observations	  and	  
discussions	  with	  online	  members	  revealed	  that	  there	  was	  relationship	  building	  between	  
the	  high	  school	  students	  and	  graduate	  students.	  This	  resulted	  in	  many	  of	  the	  high	  school	  
students	  using	  the	  “following”	  feature	  on	  twitter,	  in	  order	  to	  gain	  access	  to	  a	  particular	  
member’s	  twitter	  page	  and	  keep	  in	  touch	  with	  members	  after	  the	  twitter	  book	  club	  ended.	  
In	  many	  instances,	  this	  relationship	  building	  began	  when	  an	  online	  member	  discussed	  
his/her	  personal	  interest	  in	  relation	  to	  events	  in	  the	  text.	  
Tier	  3:	  Information	  exchange.	  Graduate	  and	  high	  school	  participants	  grappled	  
with	  several	  cultural	  and	  racial	  themes	  in	  Octavia	  Butler’s	  text	  The	  Fledging.	  Several	  
participants	  discussed	  their	  viewpoints	  on	  issues	  around	  race	  and	  culture	  drawing	  upon	  
their	  genderd	  and	  racialized	  identities.	  	  
Tier	  4:	  Information	  sharing.	  Participants	  shared	  new	  information	  with	  existing	  
members	  in	  the	  online	  book	  club.	  Although	  norms	  were	  established	  before	  the	  online	  book	  
club	  began,	  time	  constraints	  made	  it	  difficult	  to	  determine	  whether	  members	  of	  the	  twitter	  

	  

87	  
book	  club	  shared	  information	  with	  individuals	  that	  were	  not	  members	  of	  the	  twitter	  book	  
club.	  
	  
Pilot	  Study	  Summary	  
In	  the	  pilot	  study,	  I	  found	  that	  (a)	  Character	  limitations	  greatly	  limited	  the	  richness	  
of	  the	  data;	  (b)	  character	  limitations	  resulted	  in	  participants	  “communicating	  in	  codes”	  or	  
what	  Dr.	  David	  Crystal	  (2004)	  describes	  as	  “netspeak,”	  which	  made	  data	  analysis	  quite	  
difficult	  at	  times;	  (c)	  character	  limitations	  often	  resulted	  in	  adolescents	  providing	  
incomplete	  tweets.	  In	  some	  instances,	  adolescents	  did	  not	  complete	  their	  thought	  in	  
subsequent	  tweets;	  (d)	  participants	  would	  tweet	  about	  book	  club	  related	  matters,	  however	  
they	  would	  forget	  to	  use	  the	  appropriate	  hashtag;	  and	  (e)	  High	  school	  participants	  were	  
reluctant	  to	  participate	  in	  individual	  informal	  interviews	  because	  I	  did	  not	  establish	  
rapport.	  Since	  many	  participants’	  Twitter	  accounts	  were	  used	  for	  both	  the	  book	  club	  as	  
well	  as	  for	  other	  forms	  of	  personal	  use,	  forgetting	  to	  use	  the	  hashtag	  resulted	  in	  having	  to	  
sift	  through	  different	  types	  of	  tweets	  to	  find	  the	  ones	  that	  were	  specific	  to	  the	  book	  club.	  In	  
the	  Spring	  2011,	  this	  same	  Twitter	  Online	  Book	  Club	  migrated	  to	  Facebook	  and	  there	  
appeared	  to	  be	  no	  issues	  or	  concerns	  related	  to	  character	  limitations.	  
	  
Pilot	  Study’s	  Implications	  for	  Current	  Study	  
Methodological	  decisions	  in	  the	  current	  study	  were	  informed	  by	  my	  observations	  
and	  findings	  in	  the	  pilot	  study.	  Facebook	  was	  selected	  as	  the	  online	  platform	  for	  the	  current	  
study	  for	  the	  following	  reasons:	  (a)	  it	  did	  not	  have	  character	  limitations,	  which	  provided	  a	  
richer	  source	  of	  data;	  (b)	  it	  provided	  a	  “closed	  group”	  environment	  that	  offered	  

	  

88	  
participants	  a	  sense	  of	  security	  and	  privacy;	  and	  (c)	  the	  “closed	  group”	  feature	  also	  ensured	  
that	  participants’	  conversations	  specific	  to	  the	  book	  club	  were	  separate	  from	  their	  other	  
conversations	  on	  Facebook.	  The	  current	  study	  also	  consisted	  of	  a	  meet	  and	  greet	  session	  
which	  was	  held	  a	  week	  prior	  to	  the	  start	  of	  the	  program.	  The	  meet	  and	  greet	  provided	  an	  
opportunity	  for	  participants	  and	  the	  facilitator	  to	  build	  rapport,	  in	  order	  to	  ensure	  a	  
comfortable	  and	  safe	  space	  for	  members.	  The	  current	  study	  also	  consisted	  of	  weekly	  
deadlines	  to	  ensure	  that	  all	  participants	  read	  the	  texts	  prior	  to	  the	  start	  of	  a	  discussion	  
week.	  	  
	  
Data	  Collection	  
	  

Data	  collection	  process.	  Data	  were	  collected	  for	  a	  total	  of	  4	  months	  (16	  weeks)	  and	  

began	  in	  September	  2013	  and	  concluded	  at	  the	  end	  of	  December	  2013	  (Appendix	  D).	  The	  
pool	  of	  data	  derived	  from	  a	  range	  of	  data	  collection	  sources	  including	  the	  following:	  	  (a)	  
online	  Facebook	  Book	  Club	  discussion,	  (b)	  semi-­‐structured	  focus	  group	  interviews,	  (c)	  field	  
observations,	  (d)	  participant	  journal	  entries,	  and	  (e)	  researcher	  journal	  (see	  Appendix	  C).	  
Drawing	  upon	  an	  interpretive	  research	  design,	  this	  study	  was	  comprised	  of	  the	  following	  
features:	  (a)	  fieldwork	  was	  gathered	  in	  context	  on	  the	  site,	  (b)	  researcher	  experienced	  the	  
program	  firsthand	  with	  the	  participants,	  (c)	  the	  data	  were	  generally	  in	  narrative,	  
descriptive	  form,	  (d)	  the	  focus	  was	  on	  finding	  participants’	  meaning	  in	  that	  particular	  
context,	  and	  (e)	  data	  were	  analyzed	  inductively	  (see	  Appendix	  C).	  Creswell	  (2009)	  and	  
Fetterman	  (2010)	  state	  that	  employing	  multiple	  data	  methods	  ensures	  trustworthiness	  of	  
research	  validity	  by	  providing	  the	  researcher	  the	  opportunity	  to	  assess	  the	  quality	  of	  the	  

	  

89	  
information,	  as	  well	  as	  capture	  a	  complete	  picture	  of	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  participants	  
interact	  in	  various	  contexts.	  
	  
Data	  Collection	  Instruments	  	  
Facebook	  street	  literature	  book	  club.	  The	  researcher	  facilitated	  an	  online	  street	  
literature	  book	  club	  using	  the	  social	  networking	  site,	  Facebook.	  The	  online	  discussions	  
served	  as	  the	  primary	  source	  of	  data	  in	  this	  qualitative	  study.	  The	  online	  book	  club	  
discussions	  occurred	  over	  a	  12-­‐week	  period	  (see	  Appendix	  D).	  Online	  book	  club	  
discussions	  were	  asynchronous	  and	  occurred	  over	  the	  course	  of	  one	  to	  two	  weeks	  
(depending	  on	  the	  length	  of	  the	  text)	  during	  a	  book	  cycle.	  The	  street	  literature	  book	  club	  
was	  designed	  to	  examine	  the	  way	  participants	  make	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts	  in	  an	  
out-­‐of-­‐school	  digital	  space	  and	  how	  the	  researcher’s	  facilitation	  and	  presence	  influenced	  
their	  meaning	  making	  process.	  Online	  discussion	  prompts	  were	  posed	  during	  each	  online	  
group	  discussion.	  Each	  discussion	  prompt	  focused	  on	  a	  particular	  social	  issue	  (academic	  
success,	  black	  female	  condition,	  sexual	  abuse,	  black	  female	  sexuality)	  based	  on	  the	  
theme(s)	  depicted	  in	  a	  particular	  street	  literature	  text	  (see	  Appendix	  J).	  The	  online	  book	  
club	  discussions	  were	  also	  used	  to	  generate	  field	  notes	  and	  memos	  over	  the	  course	  of	  the	  
study.	  	  
	  

Selection	  of	  street	  literature	  texts.	  Drawing	  upon	  a	  black	  feminist	  epistemology,	  

participants	  were	  provided	  a	  level	  of	  self-­‐expression,	  autonomy,	  and	  choice	  in	  selecting	  
street	  literature	  titles.	  Participants	  selected	  from	  a	  pre-­‐selected	  list	  of	  ten	  street	  literature	  
titles.	  Participants	  were	  given	  a	  brief	  description	  of	  each	  book	  in	  order	  to	  make	  their	  
selections.	  Drawing	  upon	  a	  black	  feminist	  epistemology,	  the	  10	  street	  literature	  titles	  

	  

90	  
included	  black	  female	  protagonist(s)	  who	  confronted	  critical	  social	  issues	  throughout	  the	  
book.	  The	  10	  street	  literature	  texts	  included	  the	  following:	  (a)	  PUSH	  by	  Sapphire,	  (b)	  The	  
Coldest	  Winter	  Ever	  by	  Sister	  Souljah,	  (c)	  True	  to	  the	  Game	  by	  Teri	  Woods,	  (d)	  B-­‐More	  
Careful	  by	  Shannon	  Holmes,	  (e)	  Let	  That	  Be	  the	  Reason	  by	  Vicki	  Stringer,	  (f)	  Midnight	  by	  
Sister	  Souljah	  (sequel	  to	  The	  Coldest	  Winter	  Ever),	  (g)	  Flyy	  Girl	  by	  Omar	  Tyree,	  (h)	  Sweet	  
St.	  Louis:	  An	  Urban	  Love	  Story	  by	  Omar	  Tyree,	  (i)	  A	  Piece	  of	  Cake:	  A	  Memoir	  by	  Cupcake	  
Brown,	  and	  (j)	  Project	  Girl	  by	  Janet	  McDonald.	  The	  four	  street	  literature	  titles	  with	  the	  most	  
participant	  votes	  were	  selected	  for	  this	  study:	  PUSH	  by	  Sapphire,	  Project	  Girl	  by	  Janet	  
McDonald,	  Flyy	  Girl	  by	  Omar	  Tyree,	  and	  Coldest	  Winter	  Ever	  by	  Sister	  Souljah	  (Appendix	  K).	  
The	  final	  selections	  were	  based	  on	  majority	  vote	  for	  4	  of	  the	  10	  texts.	  Since	  this	  research	  
study	  was	  driven	  by	  a	  hip-­‐hop	  feminist	  lens,	  a	  primary	  goal	  of	  this	  study	  was	  to	  examine	  
how	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  draw	  upon	  their	  own	  racialized	  and	  gendered	  identities	  to	  make	  
meaning	  of	  texts	  and	  how	  female	  protagonists	  in	  the	  texts	  helped	  to	  shape	  these	  
experiences.	  	  
Semi-­‐structured	  focus	  group	  interviews.	  Focus	  groups	  are	  a	  collective	  research	  
method	  that	  focuses	  on	  participants’	  attitudes,	  experiences	  and	  beliefs	  (Madriz,	  2000).	  
Semi-­‐structured	  focus	  group	  interviews	  supplemented	  the	  online	  discussions	  and	  served	  as	  
“targeted	  data”	  (Morgan,	  1997;	  Rubin	  &	  Rubin,	  2005)	  providing	  the	  opportunities	  to	  clarify	  
responses,	  for	  follow-­‐up	  questions,	  and	  for	  the	  probing	  of	  responses	  (Stewart	  &	  
Shamdasani,	  1990).	  Focus	  group	  interviews	  provided	  participants	  the	  opportunity	  to	  
expand	  thoughts	  and	  ideas	  discussed	  in	  the	  online	  street	  literature	  book	  club	  (Stewart	  &	  
Shamdasani,	  1990).	  In	  addition	  to	  serving	  as	  a	  form	  of	  “targeted”	  data	  (Morgan,	  1997;	  
Rubin	  &	  Rubin,	  2005;),	  the	  semi-­‐structured	  focus	  group	  interviews	  also	  provided	  an	  

	  

91	  
opportunity	  to	  further	  engage	  in	  intimate	  conversations	  with	  participants	  about	  their	  
online	  experience.	  	  
The	  goal	  of	  employing	  focus	  groups	  in	  this	  study	  was	  to	  allow	  the	  interactive	  
process	  to	  stimulate	  talk	  from	  multiple	  perspectives	  around	  particular	  issues	  (Morgan,	  
1997;	  Stewart	  &	  Shamdasani,	  1990).	  Since	  people	  form	  their	  attitudes	  and	  beliefs	  relative	  
to	  others	  at	  times	  (Mayan,	  2009),	  “group	  dynamics	  may	  provide	  a	  perspective	  on	  particular	  
issues	  that	  may	  not	  have	  been	  anticipated	  by	  the	  researcher	  and	  would	  not	  otherwise	  
emerge	  from	  individual	  interviews”	  (Babbie,	  2010,	  p.	  323).	  Drawing	  upon	  a	  black	  feminist	  
perspective,	  focus	  groups	  provided	  participants	  an	  opportunity	  to	  share	  their	  collective	  
testimonies	  and	  group	  resistance	  narratives	  (Madriz,	  2000).	  Focus	  groups	  were	  used	  as	  “an	  
advancement	  of	  an	  agenda	  of	  social	  justice	  for	  women	  because	  they	  served	  to	  expose	  and	  
validate	  women’s	  everyday	  experiences	  of	  subjugation	  and	  their	  individual	  and	  collective	  
strategies”	  (Madriz,	  2000,	  p.	  836).	  Furthermore,	  the	  multiple	  perspectives	  within	  the	  focus	  
groups	  offered	  participants	  the	  opportunity	  to	  hear	  viewpoints	  vastly	  different	  from	  their	  
own	  on	  a	  range	  of	  topics.	  I	  also	  incorporated	  semi-­‐structured	  focus	  group	  interviews	  into	  
the	  study	  as	  another	  opportunity	  to	  establish	  rapport	  and	  to	  minimize	  the	  power	  that	  I	  
inherently	  assume	  as	  a	  researcher	  and	  as	  a	  facilitator.	  Additionally,	  incorporating	  face-­‐to-­‐
face	  semi-­‐structured	  focus	  group	  interviews	  into	  this	  study	  minimized	  the	  likelihood	  of	  
adolescent	  participants	  assuming	  other	  identities	  that	  are	  vastly	  different	  from	  their	  own	  
(Kivits,	  2005;	  Hine,	  2000;	  Turkle,	  1995).	  	  
Semi-­‐structured	  focus	  group	  interviews	  lasted	  no	  more	  than	  an	  hour.	  Over	  the	  
course	  of	  16	  weeks,	  a	  total	  of	  five	  focus	  group	  interviews	  were	  conducted.	  Two	  methods	  of	  
data	  collection	  were	  used	  for	  the	  semi-­‐structured	  focus	  group	  interviews:	  audio	  recording	  

	  

92	  
and	  fieldnotes.	  I	  used	  two	  audio	  recorders	  with	  MP3	  features	  for	  this	  study.	  The	  second	  
audio	  recorder	  served	  as	  a	  back-­‐up,	  in	  the	  event	  that	  the	  primary	  audio	  recorder	  
malfunctioned.	  Both	  audio	  recorders	  were	  placed	  in	  the	  center	  of	  the	  table	  during	  each	  
session	  to	  ensure	  that	  everyone’s	  voice	  could	  be	  heard	  regardless	  of	  where	  they	  were	  
seated	  around	  the	  table.	  The	  researcher	  transcribed	  all	  focus	  group	  audio	  recordings	  using	  
Express	  Scribe,	  professional	  audio	  player	  software.	  Field	  notes	  were	  used	  to	  capture	  
observations,	  including	  discourse	  and	  multimodal	  resources,	  such	  as	  facial	  expressions	  and	  
body	  language	  (see	  Appendix	  H).	  It	  provided	  another	  lens	  by	  which	  to	  validate	  participants’	  
perceptions	  and	  helped	  paint	  a	  vivid	  picture	  of	  the	  occurrences	  in	  the	  field.	  	  
Journal	  writing.	  	  This	  study	  also	  included	  journal	  writings,	  which	  were	  auto-­‐
ethnographic	  in	  nature	  and	  provided	  intimate,	  private	  spaces	  for	  participants	  to	  express	  
their	  thoughts	  (Chang,	  2008;	  Ellis,	  2004).	  The	  journal	  writings	  were	  also	  used	  to	  determine	  
whether	  there	  was	  alignment	  or	  conflict	  across	  the	  data.	  Each	  journal	  writing	  session	  
lasted	  for	  30	  minutes	  and	  occurred	  immediately	  following	  the	  focus	  group	  interviews.	  The	  
journal	  writing	  sessions	  were	  comprised	  of	  two	  types	  of	  writing:	  prompt-­‐driven	  writes	  and	  
free-­‐writes	  (Appendix	  I).	  The	  prompt-­‐driven	  writes	  focused	  on	  participants’	  perspectives	  
on	  media	  representation	  of	  black	  girls	  and	  personal	  narratives	  on	  black	  girlhood.	  The	  
prompt-­‐driven	  prompts	  provided	  another	  lens	  to	  validate	  perceptions	  and	  helped	  to	  paint	  
a	  vivid	  picture	  of	  the	  occurrences	  in	  the	  field.	  The	  free-­‐writes	  allowed	  participants	  to	  
express	  their	  thoughts	  on	  topics	  of	  their	  choice.	  Participants	  often	  used	  the	  free-­‐write	  
journal	  sessions	  to	  further	  discuss	  their	  views	  on	  conversations	  that	  occurred	  during	  the	  
focus	  group	  interviews,	  as	  well	  as	  their	  experiences	  at	  home,	  at	  school,	  and/or	  in	  the	  larger	  
community.	  Drawing	  upon	  a	  black	  feminist	  methodology,	  participants	  had	  the	  option	  of	  

	  

93	  
private	  free-­‐write	  entries.	  Participants	  indicated	  a	  private	  free-­‐write	  entry	  by	  folding	  the	  
journal	  paper	  inward.	  Over	  the	  course	  of	  the	  study,	  the	  researcher	  responded	  to	  
participants’	  journal	  entries.	  	  
Field	  observations.	  Fieldwork	  is	  a	  key	  component	  of	  qualitative	  research.	  
Fieldwork	  entails	  working	  with	  participants	  for	  an	  extended	  period	  of	  time	  in	  their	  natural	  
environment	  (Fetterman,	  2010).	  Fieldnotes	  were	  recorded	  over	  the	  course	  of	  the	  study.	  As	  
a	  community-­‐based	  and	  activist-­‐driven	  researcher,	  scholarship	  in	  action	  was	  the	  process	  
by	  which	  I	  took	  part	  of	  the	  daily	  activities,	  rituals,	  interactions,	  and	  events	  of	  participants	  
(Dewalt	  &	  Dewalt,	  2002).	  I	  immersed	  myself	  in	  the	  day-­‐to-­‐day	  lives	  of	  participants	  and	  
examined	  their	  behavior,	  literacy,	  language,	  and	  other	  cultural	  practices	  (Creswell,	  1998).	  
Building	  rapport	  with	  participants	  and	  gaining	  access	  to	  community	  spaces	  was	  one	  means	  
of	  learning	  aspects	  of	  who	  they	  were	  and	  their	  culture	  (Dewalt	  &	  Dewalt,	  2002).	  Field	  
observations	  provided	  a	  rich	  source	  of	  data	  in	  virtual	  environments,	  such	  as	  linguistic	  
styles	  and	  literate	  subjectivities.	  Field	  observations	  also	  provided	  a	  rich	  source	  of	  
multimodal	  data	  that	  included	  facial	  expressions,	  vernacular	  voice	  intonations,	  nonverbal	  
cues,	  and	  other	  forms	  of	  body	  language.	  Field	  observations	  were	  also	  used	  to	  create	  
analytical	  memos	  over	  the	  course	  of	  the	  study.	  
Researcher	  journal.	  Over	  the	  course	  of	  this	  study	  I	  kept	  a	  researcher	  journal.	  My	  
dual	  role	  as	  researcher	  and	  participant	  required	  me	  to	  maintain	  a	  professional	  distance	  
with	  participants	  that	  provided	  an	  unbiased	  perspective	  during	  data	  collection	  and	  data	  
analysis	  stages	  (Fetterman,	  2010).	  The	  researcher	  journal	  was	  both	  ethnographic	  and	  auto-­‐
ethnographic	  in	  nature	  and	  was	  used	  as	  a	  self-­‐reflective	  tool	  designed	  to	  balance	  my	  role	  as	  
a	  community-­‐engaged	  researcher,	  book	  club	  participant,	  and	  facilitator.	  The	  researcher	  

	  

94	  
journal	  provided	  “checks	  and	  balances”	  over	  the	  course	  of	  the	  research	  study.	  The	  
researcher	  journal	  also	  provided	  a	  rich	  perspective	  on	  how	  my	  role	  as	  a	  community-­‐based	  
researcher,	  book	  club	  participant,	  and	  facilitator	  influenced	  participants’	  discussions	  in	  the	  
group.	  
	  
Method	  of	  Data	  Analysis	  	  
Unit	  of	  analysis.	  The	  unit	  of	  analysis	  focuses	  on	  the	  construct(s)	  being	  analyzed	  in	  a	  
research	  study	  (Rogers,	  2003).	  The	  unit	  of	  analysis	  in	  this	  study	  was	  the	  orders	  of	  
discourse	  (Gee,	  1996).	  Orders	  of	  discourse	  are	  key	  components	  of	  language	  that	  highlight	  
social	  practices	  	  (Rogers,	  2011).	  	  Hence	  orders	  of	  discourse	  depict	  the	  relationship	  between	  
text	  and	  social	  world.	  According	  to	  Fairclough	  (1995)	  and	  Rogers	  and	  Wetzel	  (2013)	  orders	  
of	  discourse	  consisted	  of	  several	  key	  components,	  including	  ways	  of	  interacting	  (genre),	  
ways	  of	  representing	  (discourse),	  and	  ways	  of	  being	  (style).	  Each	  lens	  individually	  
represented	  a	  specific	  aspect	  of	  talk	  and	  writing	  and	  collectively	  represented	  the	  power	  
and	  positionality	  of	  the	  participants	  in	  the	  current	  study	  (Fairclough,	  1995).	  Chouliaraki	  
and	  Fairclough	  (1999)	  define	  genre	  as	  the	  “sort	  of	  language	  tied	  to	  a	  particular	  social	  
activity.”	  Rogers	  and	  Wetzel	  (2014)	  describe	  genres	  as	  “ways	  of	  interacting”	  and	  represents	  
the	  organizational	  threads	  of	  interacting.	  In	  this	  study,	  genre	  attended	  to	  talk	  by	  analyzing	  
participants	  and	  the	  researcher’s	  conversation	  (turn	  taking,	  overlapping/interruption,	  
topic	  control/maintenance,	  status	  and	  post	  likes).	  Discourses	  are	  “ways	  of	  representing”	  
and	  describe	  how	  knowledge	  is	  represented	  and	  from	  what	  perspective	  (Rogers,	  2003).	  	  
Data	  analysis	  process.	  This	  study	  is	  situated	  within	  the	  area	  of	  critical	  discourse	  
studies	  (Fairclough,	  1999;	  Rogers,	  2003,	  2002).	  The	  data	  in	  this	  research	  study	  described,	  

	  

95	  
interpreted,	  and	  explained	  the	  literacy	  and	  language	  practices	  of	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  in	  
an	  online	  street	  literature	  book	  club.	  Critical	  discourse	  analysis	  accounted	  for	  the	  micro	  
and	  macro	  context	  in	  which	  the	  orders	  of	  discourse	  were	  embedded.	  A	  microanalysis	  of	  the	  
orders	  of	  discourse	  (genre,	  discourse,	  style)	  across	  three	  domains	  (society,	  institution,	  and	  
situation)	  provided	  a	  window	  into	  black	  adolescent	  female	  participants’	  literacy	  and	  
language	  practices	  (see	  Appendix	  G).	  Hence,	  the	  orders	  of	  discourse	  (genre,	  discourse,	  and	  
style)	  were	  examined	  in	  three	  contexts,	  which	  included	  the	  societal,	  institutional,	  and	  
situational	  domains	  (see	  Appendix	  G	  and	  Appendix	  H).	  	  
In	  this	  study,	  I	  drew	  upon	  Luke’s	  definition	  (2000)	  of	  discourse	  as	  “systematic	  
clusters	  of	  themes,	  statements,	  ideas,	  and	  ideologies”	  (p.	  456).	  Discourse	  represented	  
participants’	  ideologies	  around	  critical	  social	  issues.	  Styles	  are	  “ways	  of	  being”	  and	  
represented	  interpersonal	  choice,	  specifically	  one’s	  position	  taking	  (Rogers,	  2003).	  In	  this	  
study,	  I	  attended	  to	  participants’	  styles	  by	  analyzing	  their	  use	  of	  pronouns	  and	  voice	  as	  
members	  of	  the	  group.	  In	  some	  instances,	  participants’	  style	  might	  be	  influenced	  by	  the	  
context	  in	  which	  they	  interacted.	  In	  sum,	  genre	  focused	  on	  interactions	  in	  conversations,	  
style	  focused	  on	  participants’	  positionalities	  through	  the	  use	  of	  language,	  and	  discourse	  
focused	  on	  participants’	  ideologies	  around	  particular	  critical	  social	  issues.	  In	  this	  study,	  the	  
three	  analytical	  lenses	  worked	  in	  tandem	  and	  provided	  a	  microanalysis	  of	  the	  literacy	  and	  
language	  practices	  of	  black	  adolescent	  girls.	  
The	  societal	  context	  represented	  black	  girls	  in	  the	  media	  and	  the	  media’s	  influence	  
on	  how	  black	  girls	  saw	  themselves.	  The	  institutional	  context	  represented	  participants’	  
meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts	  in	  two	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  settings:	  the	  digital	  space	  and	  the	  
face-­‐to-­‐face	  space.	  The	  situational	  context	  represented	  the	  researcher’s	  influence	  on	  

	  

96	  
participants’	  meaning	  making	  of	  street	  literature	  texts.	  In	  the	  current	  study,	  Critical	  
Discourse	  Analysis	  accounted	  for	  the	  ideological	  nature	  of	  talk	  and	  discourse	  around	  
critical	  social	  issues	  that	  impact	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  in	  the	  21st	  Century,	  including	  black	  
female	  sexuality,	  academic	  success,	  black	  motherhood	  to	  name	  a	  few.	  Critical	  discourse	  
analysis	  accounted	  for	  the	  fact	  that	  talk	  and	  discourse	  do	  not	  occur	  in	  a	  vacuum,	  but	  instead	  
occur	  within	  power	  relationships	  and	  social	  institutions.	  
	  
Levels	  of	  Analysis	  
In	  this	  study,	  data	  collection	  and	  analysis	  were	  ongoing	  and	  recursive	  (Strauss	  &	  
Corbin,	  1990).	  I	  developed	  a	  codebook	  to	  standardize	  the	  analysis	  process	  (see	  Appendix	  
H).	  Data	  were	  coded	  manually	  in	  this	  study.	  The	  first	  level	  of	  analysis	  was	  initial	  coding	  and	  
entailed	  multiple	  readings	  of	  the	  data	  sets.	  Employing	  an	  initial	  coding	  approach	  allowed	  
me	  to	  take	  a	  broad	  look	  at	  the	  data.	  This	  consisted	  of	  reading	  field	  notes,	  online	  book	  club	  
discussion	  transcripts,	  focus	  group	  interview	  transcripts,	  multimodal	  resources,	  and	  
journal	  writing	  entries.	  The	  first	  phase	  of	  coding	  served	  as	  a	  starting	  point	  for	  subsequent	  
phases	  of	  analysis	  throughout	  this	  study	  (Saldana,	  2013).	  	  
The	  second	  level	  of	  analysis	  entailed	  coding	  data	  for	  each	  order	  of	  discourse	  (genre,	  
discourse,	  style)	  across	  domains	  (society,	  institution,	  situation).	  The	  orders	  of	  discourse	  
analysis	  was	  comprised	  of	  genre—“ways	  of	  interacting,”	  discourse—“ways	  of	  
representing,”	  and	  style—“ways	  of	  being.”	  Genre	  attended	  to	  talk	  by	  analyzing	  participants’	  
and	  the	  researcher’s	  conversations.	  I	  analyzed	  instances	  of	  genre,	  including	  turn	  taking,	  
interruption,	  status	  and	  post	  likes	  within	  a	  particular	  domain.	  Discourse	  represented	  
participants’	  ideologies	  around	  critical	  social	  issues.	  I	  analyzed	  instances	  of	  discourse	  by	  

	  

97	  
focusing	  on	  the	  ways	  participants	  made	  meaning	  of	  critical	  social	  issues.	  Style	  attended	  to	  
participants’	  positioning	  through	  linguistic	  choices.	  I	  analyzed	  instances	  of	  style,	  including	  
participants’	  rhetorical	  strategies	  (use	  of	  pronouns,	  use	  of	  Standard	  English	  and	  African	  
American	  Vernacular	  English).	  
In	  the	  second	  level	  of	  analysis,	  I	  employed	  Fairclough’s	  Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis	  
(CDA).	  I	  attended	  to	  three	  orders	  of	  discourse:	  the	  societal,	  the	  institutional,	  and	  the	  
situational.	  First,	  I	  analyzed	  the	  selected	  text	  in	  the	  institutional	  domain	  (both	  the	  primary	  
institution—online	  Facebook	  group	  and	  the	  secondary	  institutional	  domain—the	  face-­‐to-­‐
face	  focus	  group	  interviews).	  For	  example,	  I	  examined	  participants’	  perspectives	  across	  the	  
institutional	  domain,	  specifically	  the	  virtual	  and	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  contexts	  and	  analyzed	  
alignment	  and	  conflict	  in	  the	  data	  across	  institutions.	  	  Second,	  I	  analyzed	  the	  selected	  text	  
in	  societal	  domain	  and	  involved	  analysis	  of	  media	  representations	  of	  black	  girls	  and	  its	  
influence	  on	  how	  they	  saw	  themselves.	  Third,	  in	  the	  situational	  domain,	  I	  analyzed	  my	  role	  
as	  the	  facilitator	  and	  my	  influence	  on	  the	  ways	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  make	  meaning	  of	  
street	  literature	  texts.	  	  
I	  coded	  data	  based	  on	  CDA’s	  orders	  of	  discourse	  and	  domains.	  In	  order	  to	  account	  
for	  black	  girls’	  ideologies,	  voice,	  and	  multimodal	  interactions,	  I	  incorporated	  various	  codes	  
into	  the	  Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis	  framework,	  including	  values	  coding,	  emotion	  coding,	  
and	  in	  vivo	  coding.	  This	  provided	  a	  richer	  account	  of	  participants’	  experiences	  and	  
reactions	  surrounding	  a	  wide	  range	  of	  critical	  social	  issues.	  Drawing	  upon	  a	  black	  feminist	  
perspective,	  several	  types	  of	  codes	  were	  employed	  to	  highlight	  participants’	  experiences	  as	  
black	  adolescent	  girls.	  Values	  coding	  represented	  participant’s	  perspectives	  and	  values	  on	  a	  
wide	  range	  of	  issues	  that	  impact	  black	  girls	  in	  the	  21st	  century,	  In	  Vivo	  coding	  represented	  

	  

98	  
participants’	  perspectives	  and	  values	  in	  their	  own	  language,	  while	  Emotion	  coding	  
accounted	  for	  participants’	  emotions	  (Saldana,	  2013).	  I	  also	  recorded	  field	  notes	  to	  
document	  my	  impressions,	  as	  well	  as	  the	  ways	  we	  interacted	  throughout	  the	  course	  of	  this	  
study.	  I	  also	  used	  a	  researcher	  journal	  as	  a	  way	  to	  aid	  in	  the	  emergence	  of	  themes	  and	  as	  a	  
tool	  to	  monitor	  my	  own	  biases	  throughout	  the	  course	  of	  the	  study	  (DeBlase,	  2003).	  	   	  
In	  the	  third	  level	  of	  analysis,	  “I	  looked	  across	  domains	  to	  see	  how	  each	  domain	  
spoke	  to	  one	  another”	  (Rogers,	  2002,	  p.	  258).	  By	  doing	  so,	  I	  accounted	  for	  instances	  of	  
alignment	  and	  conflict	  across	  the	  data.	  I	  compared	  and	  contrasted	  within	  and	  across	  data	  
types	  for	  confirmation	  of	  interpretation,	  as	  well	  as	  to	  reveal	  “cruces”	  or	  critical	  incidents	  or	  
shifts	  in	  the	  data.	  I	  also	  analyzed	  instances	  of	  genre	  across	  domains	  to	  determine	  whether	  
there	  was	  alignment	  or	  conflict	  across	  the	  data.	  According	  to	  Rogers	  (2003,	  in	  citing	  
Chouliaraki	  &	  Fairclough,	  1999)	  “cruces”	  are	  tensions	  or	  inconsistencies	  in	  the	  data.	  I	  
focused	  on	  identifying	  incidents	  of	  overlap,	  as	  well	  as	  incidents	  of	  cruces	  or	  “critical	  
incidents”	  in	  the	  data	  (Rogers,	  2003).	  I	  selected	  examples	  that	  represented	  “cruces”	  and	  
compared	  them	  to	  the	  body	  of	  literature,	  theoretical	  frameworks,	  and	  other	  data	  sets.	  
Critical	  discourse	  analysis	  attended	  to	  the	  ideological	  aspect	  of	  talk	  and	  texts	  and	  
the	  ways	  in	  which	  talk	  and	  texts	  were	  communicated	  within	  social	  settings.	  The	  CDA	  
framework	  provided	  a	  means	  to	  investigate	  the	  linkages	  between	  situational,	  societal	  and	  
institutional	  and	  an	  additional	  three	  levels	  at	  the	  micro-­‐level	  of	  interacting.	  In	  doing	  so,	  
CDA	  allowed	  me	  to	  analyze	  data	  through	  the	  use	  of	  multiple	  lenses	  (Rogers,	  2003).	  This	  
allowed	  me	  to	  weave	  together	  a	  linguistic	  and	  social	  analysis	  that	  links	  together	  the	  
individual	  with	  broader	  social	  structures	  (Rogers,	  2003).	  The	  data	  included	  field	  notes,	  
participant	  journal	  writings,	  online	  book	  discussions,	  focus	  group	  interviews,	  and	  

	  

99	  
multimodal	  resources.	  In	  order	  to	  look	  for	  patterns	  across	  domains	  and	  more	  specifically	  
across	  orders	  of	  discourse	  (genre,	  discourse,	  style)	  for	  each	  domain,	  I	  engaged	  in	  multiple	  
readings	  of	  the	  data	  set.	  	  Multiple	  readings	  of	  the	  data	  set	  across	  several	  data	  sources	  
ensured	  validity	  as	  well	  in	  this	  research	  study	  (Rogers,	  2003).	  	  
Qualitative	  reliability	  and	  validity.	  Qualitative	  validity	  and	  qualitative	  reliability	  
are	  valuable	  components	  in	  qualitative	  research	  used	  to	  determine	  credibility.	  Qualitative	  
reliability	  focuses	  on	  the	  “consistency	  and	  trustworthiness	  of	  the	  research	  findings;	  it	  is	  
often	  treated	  in	  relation	  to	  the	  issue	  of	  consistency	  and	  whether	  the	  finding	  is	  reproducible	  
at	  other	  times	  and	  by	  other	  researchers”	  (Kvale	  &	  Brinkmann,	  2009,	  p.	  245).	  In	  this	  
qualitative	  study,	  I	  employed	  several	  procedures	  to	  ensure	  reliability.	  Over	  the	  course	  of	  
the	  study,	  I	  used	  memos	  as	  a	  means	  to	  constantly	  compare	  and	  contrast	  codes	  and	  to	  
ensure	  that	  there	  were	  no	  drifts	  in	  the	  codebook.	  Drawing	  upon	  the	  work	  of	  Miles	  and	  
Huberman	  (1994),	  I	  cross-­‐checked	  my	  codes	  through	  the	  use	  of	  intercoder	  reliability.	  In	  
addition	  to	  my	  codes	  (researcher),	  two	  independent	  graduate-­‐level	  coders	  coded	  the	  entire	  
data	  set	  in	  this	  study	  to	  ensure	  reliability.	  There	  were	  a	  total	  of	  4	  iterations	  (coding	  rounds)	  
resulting	  in	  82%	  reliability	  in	  this	  study.	  The	  results	  of	  the	  reliability	  test	  were	  high	  and	  
indicate	  that	  the	  coding	  process	  was	  conducted	  in	  a	  reliable,	  consistent	  manner.	  
	  

Qualitative	  validity	  is	  one	  of	  the	  strengths	  of	  qualitative	  research,	  and	  it	  is	  based	  on	  

“determining	  whether	  the	  findings	  are	  accurate	  from	  the	  standpoint	  of	  the	  researcher,	  the	  
participant,	  or	  the	  readers	  of	  an	  account”	  (Creswell,	  2009,	  p.	  191).	  In	  qualitative	  literature	  
the	  term	  trustworthiness,	  authenticity,	  and	  credibility	  are	  often	  synonymous	  with	  validity	  
(Creswell,	  2009).	  In	  this	  study,	  I	  employed	  several	  data	  collection	  methods	  and	  looked	  
across	  different	  data	  sources	  and	  used	  it	  to	  build	  justification	  for	  themes	  and	  codes	  

	  

100	  
(Creswell,	  2009).	  I	  also	  used	  a	  researcher	  journal,	  which	  served	  as	  an	  important	  tool	  for	  
validity.	  The	  researcher	  journal	  was	  used	  to	  clarify	  my	  biases	  and	  reflect	  on	  my	  role	  as	  both	  
a	  researcher	  and	  participant	  in	  the	  study.	  To	  further	  ensure	  qualitative	  validity,	  I	  also	  
conducted	  follow-­‐up	  meetings	  with	  my	  participants	  and	  conducted	  member	  checking	  by	  
giving	  them	  drafts	  of	  my	  narratives	  and	  discussing	  the	  data	  with	  them	  to	  ensure	  that	  it	  was	  
accurate	  from	  their	  standpoint.	  Throughout	  the	  course	  of	  this	  study,	  I	  also	  conducted	  peer	  
briefing	  and	  had	  three	  colleagues	  read	  drafts	  of	  my	  analyses	  and	  give	  feedback,	  in	  order	  for	  
me	  to	  see	  the	  data	  from	  multiple	  perspectives.	  	  
Reflexivity	  and	  the	  role	  and	  social	  location	  of	  the	  researcher.	  According	  to	  
Chouliaraki	  and	  Fairclough	  (1999)	  and	  Hesse-­‐Biber	  and	  Piatelli	  (2012),	  reflexivity	  and	  the	  
role	  of	  the	  researcher	  are	  crucial	  in	  both	  critical	  discourse	  analysis	  research	  and	  feminist	  
research,	  respectively.	  Qualitative	  research	  accounts	  for	  how	  the	  researcher’s	  positionality	  
shapes	  the	  research	  study.	  This	  reflection	  includes	  the	  researcher’s	  acknowledgement	  of	  
biases,	  values,	  and	  interests	  (Creswell,	  2009).	  “Reflexivity	  questions	  the	  authority	  of	  
knowledge	  and	  opens	  up	  the	  possibility	  for	  negotiating	  knowledge	  claims	  as	  well	  as	  holds	  
the	  researcher	  accountable	  to	  those	  with	  whom	  they	  research”	  (Hesse-­‐Biber	  &	  Piatelli,	  
2012,	  p.	  495).	  Hesse-­‐Biber	  and	  Piatelli	  (2012)	  further	  argued	  that	  a	  reflexive	  researcher	  
accounts	  for	  the	  social	  conditions	  and	  social	  location	  of	  the	  researcher	  when	  knowledge	  is	  
produced.	  
Buchotlz	  (2001)	  called	  for	  a	  “heightened	  self-­‐awareness	  in	  critical	  discourse	  
analysis”	  (p.	  166).	  Bulholtz	  called	  for	  a	  reflexivity	  where	  “the	  analyst’s	  choices	  at	  every	  step	  
in	  the	  research	  process	  are	  visible	  as	  a	  part	  of	  the	  discourse	  investigation,	  and	  critique	  does	  
not	  stop	  with	  social	  processes,	  whether	  macro-­‐level	  or	  micro-­‐level,	  but	  rather	  extends	  to	  

	  

101	  
the	  analysis	  itself.”	  “The	  tension	  between	  closeness	  and	  distance	  in	  the	  research	  setting	  is	  
often	  blurred	  in	  research”	  (Rogers	  et	  al.,	  2005,	  p.	  382).	  Bucholtz	  (2001)	  asserted	  that	  it	  is	  
not	  possible	  for	  scholars	  conducting	  critical	  research	  to	  separate	  their	  research	  from	  their	  
political	  positions.	  This	  further	  results	  in	  the	  critical	  discourse	  analysis	  tradition	  rejecting	  
the	  view	  of	  an	  objective	  and	  neutral	  stance.	  
Reflexivity	  in	  the	  Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis	  tradition	  includes	  three	  aspects:	  
participatory	  construction	  of	  the	  research	  design,	  reciprocity,	  and	  turning	  the	  analytic	  
frame	  back	  on	  the	  researcher.	  Throughout	  this	  qualitative	  research	  study	  participants’	  
feedback	  and	  ideas	  were	  incorporated	  in	  this	  study	  and	  served	  as	  key	  components	  of	  the	  
research	  design.	  Participants	  contributed	  to	  the	  research	  design	  of	  this	  study	  by	  selecting	  
and	  finalizing	  the	  street	  literature	  texts	  that	  were	  read.	  Participants	  were	  also	  instrumental	  
in	  determining	  the	  length	  of	  time	  they	  had	  to	  read	  each	  street	  literature	  texts,	  as	  well	  as	  the	  
frequency	  of	  each	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  session.	  Reciprocity	  and	  dialogue	  were	  also	  central	  
components	  of	  this	  qualitative	  study.	  	  Although	  I	  was	  the	  researcher	  in	  this	  study	  with	  
inherent	  authority,	  participants	  were	  the	  authority	  on	  their	  experiences	  as	  street	  literature	  
readers,	  digital	  technology	  users,	  and	  members	  of	  a	  hip-­‐hop	  era	  filled	  with	  
commodification.	  Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis	  allowed	  me	  to	  turn	  the	  framework	  on	  my	  self	  
and	  analyze	  how	  my	  participation	  in	  the	  research	  influenced	  power	  dynamics	  with	  
participants	  (Rogers	  et	  al.,	  2005).	  	  
Drawing	  upon	  an	  interpretive,	  naturalistic	  inquiry,	  within	  the	  ethnographic	  
tradition,	  Denzin	  and	  Lincoln	  (1994)	  argue	  that	  “behind	  these	  terms	  [theory,	  method,	  
analysis,	  ontology,	  and	  epistemology]	  stands	  the	  biography”	  of	  the	  researcher	  who	  speaks	  
from	  a	  particular	  social	  location	  (p.	  1).	  Employing	  a	  black	  feminist	  epistemology,	  my	  social	  

	  

102	  
location	  is	  informed	  by	  the	  intersectionality	  of	  racialized	  and	  gendered	  identities	  situated	  
within	  a	  series	  of	  personal	  and	  professional	  experiences.	  My	  social	  location	  in	  this	  
qualitative	  study	  was	  informed	  by	  my	  personal	  experiences	  as	  a	  black	  adolescent	  girl,	  black	  
woman,	  reader	  of	  street	  literature	  texts,	  member	  of	  the	  hip-­‐hop	  culture,	  and	  a	  speaker	  of	  
African-­‐American	  Vernacular	  English,	  as	  well	  as	  my	  professional	  experiences	  as	  a	  school	  
library	  media	  specialist	  and	  an	  English	  educator	  working	  with	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  in	  
predominantly	  black	  communities.	  	  
As	  a	  black	  adolescent	  girl,	  I	  was	  a	  member	  of	  a	  marginalized	  urban	  youth	  culture	  
that	  read	  street	  literature	  texts	  and	  used	  them	  as	  a	  lens	  to	  view	  the	  world	  and	  make	  
meaning	  of	  self.	  In	  addition	  to	  being	  from	  similar	  communities	  as	  my	  students,	  we	  draw	  
from	  experiences	  of	  marginalization	  due	  to	  both	  our	  femaleness	  and	  blackness.	  As	  a	  black	  
female	  scholar,	  my	  research	  is	  activist-­‐driven	  and	  community-­‐based	  and	  serves	  diverse	  
populations.	  As	  black	  woman	  who	  has	  often	  been	  marginalized	  based	  on	  my	  femaleness	  
and	  blackness,	  my	  scholarship	  serves	  as	  a	  social	  justice	  tool	  allowing	  me	  to	  bring	  visibility	  
to	  the	  injustices	  that	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  experience	  in	  their	  lives,	  as	  well	  as	  create	  a	  safe	  
space	  for	  their	  voices	  to	  be	  heard.	  As	  a	  community	  engaged	  scholar,	  I	  continue	  to	  work	  with	  
libraries	  and	  secondary	  schools	  in	  black	  communities	  and	  engage	  in	  scholarship	  in	  action	  
that	  serves	  diverse	  populations.	  I	  bring	  to	  my	  scholarship	  and	  this	  research	  study	  
intersecting	  identities	  that	  have	  provided	  me	  with	  a	  range	  of	  lenses	  by	  which	  to	  share	  the	  
literate	  lives	  of	  black	  adolescent	  girls.	  Each	  lens	  is	  varied	  and	  provides	  rich	  perspectives	  
and	  intimate	  accounts	  of	  their	  experiences	  (see	  Chapter	  6).	  
My	  social	  location	  in	  this	  qualitative	  study	  was	  informed	  by	  a	  variety	  of	  personal	  
and	  professional	  experiences	  throughout	  my	  life	  that	  shaped	  my	  positionality	  in	  this	  

	  

103	  
qualitative	  study.	  My	  reflexive	  approach	  to	  research	  and	  my	  roles	  as	  a	  researcher,	  book	  
club	  facilitator,	  and	  participant	  were	  key	  components	  in	  this	  qualitative	  research	  study.	  
Throughout	  this	  research	  study	  I	  “worked	  the	  hyphen”	  in	  various	  roles	  as	  a	  researcher,	  
book	  club	  facilitator,	  and	  book	  club	  participant.	  Fine	  (1994)	  describes	  “working	  the	  hyphen	  
as	  a	  self-­‐reflexive	  approach	  to	  the	  many	  ways	  that	  we	  are	  in	  relation	  with	  the	  contexts	  and	  
participants	  in	  our	  studies	  and	  how	  assuming	  multiple	  identities	  are	  in	  relationship	  with	  
each	  other	  and	  offer	  varied	  perspectives	  in	  the	  field”	  (p.	  72).	  	  My	  roles	  in	  this	  study	  were	  
not	  stable	  and	  distinct,	  but	  instead	  were	  fluid	  and	  interconnected.	  Although	  my	  roles	  were	  
fluid,	  each	  role	  (research,	  book	  club	  facilitator,	  and	  book	  club	  participant)	  was	  central	  at	  
various	  points	  throughout	  the	  research	  study.	  My	  positionality	  in	  the	  research	  study	  was	  
also	  heavily	  influenced	  by	  participants’	  conversations.	  Often	  times,	  their	  conversations	  
around	  youth	  hip-­‐hop	  culture,	  their	  use	  of	  linguistic	  practices,	  and	  their	  perspectives	  on	  
particular	  social	  issues	  of	  the	  21st	  Century	  dictated	  my	  positionality,	  contribution,	  and	  level	  
of	  access	  within	  both	  the	  online	  and	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  spaces.	  	  
	  
Methods	  Summary	  
This	  chapter	  highlighted	  the	  methodology	  and	  the	  methods	  of	  inquiry	  in	  this	  study.	  	  
Chapter	  3	  also	  highlighted	  the	  use	  of	  Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis	  to	  frame	  this	  study.	  Two	  
research	  traditions	  also	  informed	  this	  study,	  including	  the	  advocacy	  and	  participatory	  
tradition	  and	  the	  critical	  research	  tradition.	  This	  chapter	  also	  detailed	  the	  pilot	  study	  used	  
to	  inform	  the	  selection	  of	  data	  collection	  instruments	  in	  this	  study.	  The	  unit	  of	  analysis,	  
data	  coding,	  and	  analysis	  process	  were	  also	  detailed	  in	  this	  chapter.	  Reliability	  and	  validity	  
ensured	  credibility	  over	  the	  course	  of	  this	  study.	  Lastly,	  reflexivity,	  the	  role	  and	  social	  

	  

104	  
location	  of	  the	  researcher	  provided	  rich	  perspectives	  on	  my	  positionality	  throughout	  this	  
study.	  Chapters	  4,	  5,	  and	  6	  detail	  the	  findings	  in	  the	  current	  study.	  
	  

	  

105	  
Chapters	  Four,	  Five,	  and	  Six	  
Introduction	  to	  the	  Data	  Chapters	  
	  
Chapters	  4,	  5,	  and	  6	  highlight	  the	  societal,	  institutional,	  and	  situational	  factors	  that	  
influenced	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  make	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts.	  
Chapter	  4	  provides	  black	  adolescent	  girls’	  narratives	  on	  media	  representation	  of	  who	  they	  
are	  and	  how	  they	  seem	  themselves	  (Societal).	  Chapter	  5	  details	  black	  adolescent	  girls’	  
meaning	  making	  of	  street	  literature	  text	  within	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  contexts	  (Institutional).	  In	  
Chapter	  6,	  I	  turn	  the	  analytic	  lens	  on	  myself	  and	  detail	  how	  my	  role	  as	  a	  researcher	  
influences	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  make	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts	  
(Situational).	  	  
Employing	  Fairclough’s	  Critical	  Discourse	  Analytical	  tool,	  each	  data	  chapter	  focuses	  
on	  particular	  levels	  of	  analysis	  (genres,	  discourses,	  styles)	  across	  three	  domains	  (societal,	  
institutional,	  situational).	  Journal	  entries	  were	  used	  to	  examine	  black	  adolescent	  girls’	  
discourse	  on	  media	  representations.	  Both	  online	  Facebook	  discussions	  and	  focus	  group	  
interviews	  provided	  an	  account	  of	  black	  adolescent	  girls’	  meaning	  making	  of	  street	  
literature	  texts.	  Online	  Facebook	  discussions,	  focus	  group	  interviews,	  and	  a	  researcher	  
journal	  were	  used	  to	  depict	  how	  my	  role	  as	  a	  researcher	  influenced	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  
black	  adolescent	  girls	  made	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts.	  Collectively,	  the	  domains	  and	  
orders	  of	  discourses	  highlighted	  the	  alignment	  and	  tensions	  within	  and	  across	  the	  data.	  
Each	  chapter	  is	  organized	  as	  follows:	  a	  narrative	  introduction	  designed	  to	  contextualize	  the	  
chapter,	  a	  detailed	  chapter	  overview,	  a	  summary	  of	  how	  Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis	  

	  

106	  
informed	  the	  data	  chapter,	  analysis	  of	  the	  data	  incorporating	  theoretical	  underpinnings	  
and	  existing	  literature,	  and	  a	  data	  summative	  section	  highlighting	  the	  chapter	  findings.	  	  

	  

107	  
Chapter	  Four	  
Through	  the	  Looking	  Glass:	  
Dominant	  Discourse	  on	  Media	  Representations	  of	  Black	  Girls	  
“These	  images	  in	  the	  media	  discourage	  me	  because	  in	  all	  actuality	  that’s	  not	  how	  we	  are	  or	  
who	  we	  are”—Denise	  (Journal	  data,	  October	  5,	  2013)	  
	  
Introduction	  	  
Denise	  was	  a	  high	  school	  student	  who	  would	  best	  be	  described	  as	  a	  critical	  thinker.	  
She	  was	  outspoken	  about	  her	  worldviews	  on	  a	  wide	  range	  of	  social	  issues	  ranging	  from	  
politics	  to	  racism	  to	  education	  (fieldnotes,	  October	  5,	  2013).	  Despite	  excelling	  academically	  
in	  formal	  spaces,	  Denise	  did	  not	  fully	  attribute	  her	  academic	  years	  to	  grooming	  her	  to	  think	  
critically.	  She	  instead	  credited	  her	  multiple	  identities	  for	  shaping	  her	  perspectives	  on	  many	  
societal	  issues.	  Denise	  drew	  from	  several	  social	  locations	  including	  her	  lived	  experiences	  as	  
a	  black	  adolescent	  girl,	  a	  high-­‐achieving	  student	  in	  a	  failing	  public	  school	  system,	  a	  youth	  
pastor	  at	  her	  local	  church,	  the	  daughter	  of	  a	  politically-­‐engaged	  mother,	  and	  the	  daughter	  
of	  nurturing	  “other	  mothers”	  (black	  women	  that	  are	  not	  related	  to	  her,	  yet	  serve	  as	  mother	  
figures;	  Denise,	  personal	  communication,	  October	  7,	  2013).	  Denise	  credits	  members	  in	  the	  
community	  for	  helping	  to	  shape	  her	  identity,	  as	  well	  as	  her	  worldviews	  on	  critical	  social	  
issues.	  Despite	  Denise’s	  worldviews	  being	  shaped	  in	  part	  by	  her	  family	  and	  members	  in	  the	  
community,	  she	  also	  acknowledged	  that	  her	  awareness	  of	  injustices	  against	  black	  girls	  was	  
rooted	  mainly	  in	  her	  daily	  struggles	  as	  a	  black	  adolescent	  girl	  (Denise,	  personal	  
communication,	  October	  7,	  2013).	  She	  often	  highlighted	  the	  current	  state	  of	  black	  girlhood	  
in	  the	  media	  and	  a	  myriad	  of	  personal	  life	  struggles	  to	  make	  sense	  of	  who	  she	  was	  and	  how	  

	  

108	  
she	  saw	  herself.	  (fieldnotes,	  October,	  10,	  2013).	  Denise	  revealed	  that	  the	  media	  not	  only	  
infiltrated	  damaging	  images	  of	  black	  girls	  on	  television	  and	  the	  radio,	  but	  also	  infiltrated	  
classroom	  spaces.	  She	  revealed	  that	  school	  spaces	  also	  conformed	  to	  negative	  images	  of	  
black	  girls.	  She	  constantly	  negotiated	  her	  identity,	  positionality,	  and	  power	  surrounded	  by	  
dominant	  discourses	  that	  rendered	  her	  voiceless	  or	  deficient.	  
This	  chapter	  examined	  black	  adolescent	  female	  participants’	  discourses	  on	  media	  
representations	  of	  black	  girls	  and	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  these	  representations	  influenced	  how	  
they	  saw	  themselves.	  In	  this	  chapter,	  I	  highlighted	  discourses	  of	  Denise,	  Keisha,	  and	  Lisa,	  
three	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  that	  participated	  in	  the	  journal	  writing	  sessions.	  Through	  a	  
series	  of	  prompt	  and	  free-­‐write	  journal	  sessions,	  Denise,	  Keisha,	  and	  Lisa	  displayed	  and	  
articulated	  their	  constant	  negotiation	  of	  power,	  identity,	  and	  positionality	  as	  black	  
adolescent	  girls	  living	  in	  the	  21st	  century.	  In	  this	  chapter,	  I	  considered	  the	  following	  
questions:	  How	  did	  each	  participant	  position	  herself	  as	  a	  black	  adolescent	  girl	  in	  society?	  
How	  did	  media	  representations	  of	  black	  girlhood	  play	  into	  their	  individual	  identities?	  How	  
did	  media	  representations	  of	  black	  girlhood	  play	  into	  their	  collective	  identities?	  How	  do	  
Denise,	  Keisha,	  and	  Lisa	  address	  media	  representations	  of	  black	  girlhood?	  Do	  they	  conform	  
to	  these	  media	  representations	  or	  did	  they	  challenge	  dominant	  narratives	  of	  black	  girlhood	  
in	  the	  21st	  Century?	  In	  what	  ways	  do	  they	  conform	  and/or	  challenge	  these	  dominant	  
narratives	  of	  black	  girlhood?	  Lastly,	  how	  do	  their	  narratives	  play	  into	  the	  creation	  of	  more	  
critically	  and	  culturally	  conscious	  classrooms	  and	  libraries?	  
	  

	  

109	  
Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis:	  Primary	  and	  Secondary	  Discourses	  
Fairclough’s	  Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis	  draws	  upon	  Gee’s	  definitions	  of	  discourse.	  
According	  to	  Lankshear	  and	  Knobel	  (2007,	  citing	  Gee,	  2000)	  discourses	  are	  
socially	  recognized	  ways	  of	  using	  language	  (reading,	  writing,	  speaking,	  
listening),	  gestures	  and	  other	  semiotics	  (images,	  sounds,	  graphics,	  signs	  
codes),	  as	  well	  as	  ways	  of	  thinking,	  believing,	  feeling,	  and	  interacting	  in	  
relation	  to	  people,	  such	  that	  we	  can	  be	  identified	  and	  recognized	  as	  being	  a	  
member	  of	  a	  socially	  meaningful	  role.	  (p.	  54)	  
	  
Gee	  argues	  that	  primary	  discourse	  is	  rooted	  in	  one’s	  family	  life	  and	  shaped	  by	  an	  
individual’s	  familial	  interactions	  and	  ties	  early	  in	  life.	  In	  this	  current	  study,	  black	  adolescent	  
girls’	  discourses	  around	  their	  sense	  of	  self	  rooted	  in	  their	  home	  and	  community	  
experiences	  served	  as	  their	  primary	  Discourses.	  According	  to	  Gee	  (2000)	  “a	  person	  is	  born	  
or	  initially	  socialized	  into	  their	  primary	  Discourse	  shaping	  new	  members’	  ways	  of	  
speaking,	  ways	  of	  acting,	  views,	  values,	  beliefs,	  experiences,	  and	  their	  first	  social	  identity”	  
(p.	  7).	  One’s	  sense	  of	  self	  is	  rooted	  in	  primary	  Discourse	  and	  provides	  an	  understanding	  of	  
who	  we	  are	  and	  who	  people	  from	  similar	  backgrounds	  are.	  	  
Unlike	  Primary	  Discourses,	  Secondary	  Discourses	  are	  what	  is	  known	  as	  dominant	  
Discourses	  and	  are	  acquired	  within	  public	  spheres.	  They	  are	  acquired	  through	  outside	  
entities	  such	  as	  the	  media,	  religious	  groups,	  and	  school	  spaces	  to	  name	  a	  few.	  In	  this	  
current	  study,	  media	  outlets	  and	  media	  representations	  around	  black	  girls	  served	  as	  
secondary	  Discourses.	  Since	  secondary	  or	  what	  are	  known	  as	  dominant	  Discourses	  are	  
situated	  within	  the	  public	  spheres	  and	  are	  politically	  driven,	  there	  is	  a	  greater	  need	  to	  
conform	  to	  conventions	  (Gee,	  2000).	  	  
In	  the	  following	  section,	  I	  detailed	  Discourse	  by	  employing	  a	  discursive	  theory	  of	  
Hip-­‐hop	  feminism	  that	  represented	  Denise,	  Keisha,	  and	  Lisa’s	  perspectives	  on	  several	  areas	  

	  

110	  
including	  Discourse	  of	  Media	  Representations	  of	  Black	  Girlhood,	  Discourse	  of	  Black	  
Girlhood,	  and	  Discourse	  of	  Self-­‐Identity.	  In	  this	  chapter,	  each	  participant	  was	  presented	  as	  
an	  individual	  case	  to	  highlight	  the	  way	  they	  navigated	  discourses	  of	  power	  within	  the	  
larger	  society	  as	  well	  as	  in	  their	  personal	  lives.	  Both	  prompt	  and	  free-­‐write	  journal	  entries	  
provided	  auto-­‐ethnographic	  accounts	  (Chang,	  2008;	  Ellis,	  2004)	  of	  their	  personal	  
experiences	  as	  black	  girls	  as	  well	  as	  their	  worldviews	  on	  the	  black	  girl	  experience.	  
	  
Denise	  
	  

In	  my	  one-­‐on-­‐one	  introductory	  meeting	  with	  Denise,	  she	  discussed	  her	  experiences	  

as	  a	  black	  girl	  confronting	  racism,	  as	  well	  as	  her	  academic	  experiences	  interacting	  with	  
classroom	  teachers	  who	  had	  low	  expectations	  of	  her.	  She	  spoke	  candidly	  about	  two	  
memorable	  experiences	  that	  shaped	  her	  perspective	  on	  society.	  Denise	  recalled	  an	  incident	  
where	  she	  was	  browsing	  in	  a	  department	  store	  and	  was	  racially	  profiled	  by	  department	  
store	  staff.	  The	  store	  clerks	  and	  the	  security	  guards	  began	  yelling	  derogatory	  comments	  
and	  accused	  her	  of	  stealing	  even	  though	  she	  was	  merely	  browsing	  (Denise,	  personal	  
communication,	  October	  12,	  2013).	  She	  also	  recalled	  a	  second	  incident	  in	  which	  her	  
homeroom	  teacher,	  two	  subject	  teachers,	  and	  a	  guidance	  counselor	  attempted	  to	  
discourage	  her	  from	  applying	  to	  two	  pre-­‐collegiate	  programs	  even	  though	  she	  far	  exceeded	  
both	  institutions’	  academic	  qualifications.	  Denise	  further	  recalled	  that	  the	  situation	  was	  
even	  more	  isolating	  and	  hurtful	  when	  she	  learned	  that	  the	  same	  teachers	  and	  guidance	  
counselor	  encouraged	  her	  white	  counterparts	  to	  apply	  to	  the	  same	  program	  even	  though	  
they	  did	  not	  meet	  all	  of	  the	  academic	  qualifications	  (Denise,	  personal	  communications,	  

	  

111	  
October	  12,	  2013).	  She	  mentioned	  that	  her	  experiences	  were	  examples	  of	  how	  the	  world	  
sees	  black	  girls,	  how	  she	  sees	  the	  world,	  and	  how	  she	  sees	  herself.	  	  
In	  a	  journal	  writing	  session	  on	  media	  representations	  of	  black	  girls,	  Denise	  reflected	  
on	  both	  her	  personal	  experiences	  and	  worldviews.	  In	  response	  to	  the	  journal	  prompt	  “How	  
do	  media	  outlets	  represent	  black	  girls?”	  Denise	  wrote	  in	  big	  bold	  red	  letters	  across	  her	  
notebook	  page,	  “SOCIETY	  CAN’T	  HELP	  WHO	  IT	  WASN’T	  BUILT	  TO	  PROTECT!!!”	  (journal	  
data,	  October	  19,	  2013).	  She	  displayed	  a	  keen	  sense	  of	  awareness	  and	  implicitly	  
acknowledged	  society’s	  intention	  to	  marginalize	  black	  girls	  (fieldnotes,	  October,	  19,	  2013).	  
Although	  Denise	  did	  not	  explicitly	  state	  how	  black	  girls	  are	  represented	  and	  positioned,	  she	  
alluded	  to	  society’s	  role	  in	  the	  negative	  representation	  of	  black	  girls.	  Over	  the	  course	  of	  
several	  journal	  sessions,	  Denise	  transitioned	  from	  implicit	  to	  more	  explicit	  entries	  and	  
from	  prompt-­‐writes	  to	  free-­‐writes	  (fieldnotes,	  November	  9,	  2013).	  She	  often	  attributed	  her	  
level	  of	  openness	  in	  her	  writing	  to	  her	  comfort	  level	  at	  particular	  stages	  in	  the	  literacy	  
program.	  	  
In	  a	  series	  of	  subsequent	  journal	  entries,	  Denise	  journaled	  about	  damaging	  media	  
images	  of	  black	  people	  and	  the	  types	  of	  media	  images	  that	  are	  associated	  with	  black	  girls.	  
She	  referenced	  popular	  television	  reality	  shows	  that	  portrayed	  damaging	  images	  of	  black	  
people,	  specifically	  black	  girls.	  (journal	  data,	  November	  9,	  2013).	  Drawing	  upon	  the	  many	  
damaging	  media	  images	  of	  black	  people,	  Denise	  wrote	  “Society	  really	  isn’t	  concerned	  with	  
the	  black	  race	  and	  it	  sure	  as	  hell	  isn’t	  concerned	  about	  us!!	  The	  media	  stereotypes	  the	  black	  
race	  and	  portrays	  black	  girls	  as	  ghetto,	  pregnant,	  and	  illiterate,	  and	  half-­‐dressed	  video-­‐
vixens”	  (journal	  data,	  November	  16,	  2013).	  Denise	  continued	  to	  display	  a	  heightened	  
consciousness	  by	  challenging	  the	  limited	  value	  society	  places	  on	  black	  lives,	  specifically	  

	  

112	  
black	  female	  lives	  (fieldnotes	  data,	  November	  17,	  2013).	  She	  justified	  her	  position	  by	  
highlighting	  images	  designed	  to	  sexualize	  black	  girls.	  According	  to	  Richardson	  (2013),	  the	  
media	  through	  the	  commodification	  of	  Hip-­‐hop	  culture	  exploits	  black	  girl	  sexuality	  and	  
produces	  degrading	  images	  of	  black	  girls	  to	  ensure	  that	  black	  girls	  are	  represented	  
negatively.	  	  
Denise’s	  journal	  writing	  process	  revealed	  instances	  in	  which	  her	  primary	  discourses	  
and	  secondary	  discourses	  were	  in	  conflict.	  In	  a	  free-­‐write	  journal	  entry,	  Denise	  wrote	  “I	  
find	  the	  images	  of	  black	  girls	  in	  the	  media	  to	  be	  discouraging	  because	  they	  don’t	  show	  who	  
we	  really	  are”	  (journal	  data,	  November	  2013).	  Denise’s	  response	  clearly	  highlights	  that	  
black	  girls’	  primary	  discourses	  around	  self,	  which	  is	  often	  rooted	  in	  their	  home	  life	  tends	  to	  
be	  in	  conflict	  with	  secondary	  discourses	  that	  the	  media	  perpetuates	  (Gee,	  2000).	  In	  
addition	  to	  Denise	  referencing	  disturbing	  images,	  she	  also	  accounts	  for	  the	  pressure	  that	  
black	  girls	  face	  to	  meet	  the	  “gold	  standard”	  of	  femaleness	  in	  order	  to	  be	  viewed	  positively.	  
Denise	  asserts	  that	  this	  “gold	  standard”	  of	  femaleness	  is	  situated	  around	  Eurocentric	  
norms.	  She	  wrote	  “all	  the	  negativity	  around	  us	  is	  discouraging	  because	  girls	  feel	  they	  have	  
to	  be	  the	  opposite	  of	  who	  they	  are	  to	  be	  thought	  of	  positively.	  Basically,	  we	  have	  to	  be	  
skinny	  and	  white	  to	  really	  be	  respected”	  (journal	  data,	  November	  14,	  2013).	  According	  to	  
hip	  hop	  feminist	  scholar	  Ruth	  Nicole	  Brown	  (2009)	  society’s	  dominant	  discourse	  on	  beauty	  
is	  rooted	  in	  standards,	  which	  privileges	  and	  normalizes	  Whiteness.	  This	  results	  in	  the	  
constant	  invisibility	  of	  black	  girls.	  Denise	  argues	  that	  the	  dominant	  discourse	  of	  Whiteness	  
is	  so	  prevalent	  and	  so	  powerful	  in	  society	  that	  she	  feels	  the	  need	  to	  conform	  to	  these	  
standards	  to	  ensure	  visibility.	  She	  argued	  that	  black	  girls	  have	  a	  responsibility	  to	  push	  back	  
against	  dominant	  discourse	  in	  society.	  

	  

113	  
Black	  girls	  have	  a	  responsibility	  to	  be	  strong	  physically	  and	  mentally	  in	  order	  to	  
overcome	  obstacles.	  Proving	  people	  and	  society	  wrong	  that	  we	  aren’t	  what	  we	  are	  
portrayed	  in	  the	  media.	  And	  not	  falling	  into	  the	  trap	  of	  being	  the	  negative	  images	  
society	  claims	  we	  are.	  (journal	  data,	  November	  20,	  2013)	  
	  
Denise	  asserted	  that	  negative	  images	  of	  blackness	  and	  femaleness	  were	  intentional,	  
deeply	  rooted	  in	  society,	  and	  designed	  to	  marginalize	  black	  girls.	  She	  displayed	  a	  
heightened	  sense	  of	  consciousness	  in	  her	  earlier	  journal	  entry;	  however,	  in	  the	  above	  
journal	  entry	  there	  is	  a	  shift	  in	  her	  perspective	  (fieldnotes,	  November	  23,	  2013).	  Denise	  
acknowledges	  that	  black	  girls	  have	  a	  responsibility	  to	  overcome	  obstacles	  and	  she	  
encourages	  them	  to	  be	  “strong	  physically	  and	  mentally.”	  Her	  perspective	  promotes	  two	  
conflicting	  ideologies—one	  ideology	  that	  encourages	  independence	  and	  self-­‐determination	  
(Collins,	  2000)	  and	  the	  other	  ideology	  that	  promotes	  the	  controlling	  image,	  the	  strong	  
black	  woman	  (Richardson,	  2002).	  Denise’s	  perspective	  encourages	  her	  black	  female	  
counterparts	  to	  assume	  the	  role	  of	  a	  controlling	  image—the	  Strong	  Black	  Female.	  
According	  to	  Hip-­‐hop	  feminist	  Joan	  Morgan	  (1999),	  the	  Strong	  Black	  Female	  often	  
possesses	  favorable	  qualities	  such	  as	  self-­‐determination	  and	  independence,	  however	  these	  
qualities	  are	  counterproductive	  when	  black	  girls	  continue	  to	  surge	  forward	  despite	  
physical	  and	  emotional	  hardships.	  Morgan	  (1999)	  further	  argues:	  
When	  you’re	  raised	  to	  believe	  that	  the	  ability	  to	  kick	  adversity’s	  ass	  is	  a	  birthright—
a	  by-­‐product	  of	  gender	  and	  melanin—you	  tend	  to	  tackle	  life’s	  afflictions	  tenaciously.	  
This	  is	  a	  useful	  quality,	  no	  doubt.	  However,	  this	  [stereotype]	  also	  tricks	  you	  into	  
believing	  we	  can	  carry	  the	  weight	  of	  the	  world.	  	  (p.	  104)	  
	  
Morgan	  (1999)	  acknowledges	  the	  slippery	  slope	  that	  exists	  between	  two	  conflicting	  
ideologies	  that	  black	  girls	  are	  confronted	  with.	  Denise	  attempts	  to	  relieve	  society	  of	  the	  
responsibility	  of	  portraying	  black	  girls	  positively	  by	  encouraging	  black	  girls	  to	  assume	  the	  
responsibility	  of	  their	  image	  and	  representation.	  By	  doing	  so,	  she	  disregards	  how	  

	  

114	  
hegemonic	  ideologies	  are	  deeply	  embedded	  within	  society.	  Her	  journal	  entries	  over	  the	  
course	  of	  this	  program	  position	  herself	  and	  black	  girls	  at	  the	  intersection	  of	  two	  conflicting	  
ideologies—one	  ideology	  that	  acknowledges	  the	  media’s	  fervent	  push	  to	  degrade	  black	  
girls	  and	  the	  other	  ideology	  that	  views	  black	  girls’	  control	  over	  the	  way	  in	  which	  they	  are	  
represented.	  She	  offers	  a	  call	  to	  action	  for	  black	  girls—one	  that	  encourages	  black	  girls	  to	  
push	  against	  hegemonic	  ideologies	  by	  not	  conforming	  to	  negative	  images	  of	  black	  girlhood.	  
	  
Keisha	  
	  

During	  a	  one-­‐on-­‐one	  introductory	  meet	  and	  greet	  session,	  Keisha	  discussed	  the	  

cultural	  tensions	  between	  several	  ethnic	  groups	  at	  her	  school.	  Although	  she	  described	  her	  
school	  as	  a	  “melting	  pot”	  comprised	  of	  students	  from	  over	  10	  different	  countries,	  she	  also	  
mentioned	  that	  there	  were	  cultural	  tensions	  that	  existed	  within	  and	  across	  specific	  ethnic	  
groups.	  In	  one	  example,	  Keisha	  recalled	  conflict	  between	  African	  students	  who	  were	  from	  
the	  same	  geographic	  region	  yet	  were	  members	  of	  different	  tribal	  groups	  (Keisha,	  personal	  
communication,	  September	  5,	  2013).	  In	  a	  second	  example,	  she	  spoke	  candidly	  about	  the	  
cultural	  tensions	  between	  African-­‐Americans	  and	  Africans,	  as	  well	  as	  cultural	  tensions	  
between	  Asians	  born	  in	  the	  U.S.	  and	  Asians	  born	  in	  Asia.	  Keisha	  further	  recalled	  that	  the	  
inter-­‐cultural	  and	  intra-­‐cultural	  tensions	  that	  existed	  within	  her	  school	  were	  exacerbated	  
by	  the	  media’s	  representation	  of	  particular	  cultural	  groups,	  specifically	  people	  of	  color	  
(Keisha,	  personal	  communication,	  September	  5,	  2013).	  Keisha	  also	  mentioned	  that	  the	  
media	  misguides	  viewers	  and	  fuels	  already	  existing	  tensions	  across	  cultural	  groups.	  She	  
noted	  that	  these	  tensions	  are	  often	  fueled	  by	  negative	  images	  and	  stereotypes	  that	  they	  see	  
on	  television	  and	  on	  the	  Internet.	  	  

	  

115	  
	  

In	  a	  series	  of	  journal	  writing	  entries	  on	  media	  representations	  of	  black	  girls,	  

Keisha’s	  observations	  of	  cultural	  tensions	  at	  her	  school,	  as	  well	  as	  her	  personal	  experiences	  
shaped	  her	  perspective.	  In	  response	  to	  the	  journal	  prompt	  “How	  do	  media	  outlets	  
represents	  black	  girls?”	  she	  expressed	  her	  disdain	  for	  the	  portrayal	  of	  black	  girls,	  although	  
she	  didn’t	  speak	  explicitly	  about	  how	  they	  are	  represented.	  “I	  really	  don’t	  like	  how	  society	  
portrays	  us!!”	  (journal	  data,	  November	  10,	  2013).	  Similar	  to	  Denise,	  Keisha	  initially	  alluded	  
to	  the	  portrayal	  of	  black	  girls	  in	  the	  media.	  In	  subsequent	  free-­‐write	  journal	  entries,	  Keisha	  
provided	  specific	  examples	  of	  the	  media	  portrayals	  that	  she	  had	  disdain	  for	  including,	  
“images	  of	  black	  girls	  dropping	  out	  of	  school,	  getting	  pregnant	  at	  a	  young	  age	  and	  being	  
uneducated”	  (journal	  data,	  November	  12,	  2013).	  Similar	  to	  Denise,	  Keisha	  displayed	  a	  keen	  
sense	  of	  cultural	  consciousness	  and	  acknowledged	  that	  because	  the	  media	  only	  produces	  
derogatory	  images	  to	  black	  girls	  then	  the	  media’s	  efforts	  are	  intentional	  and	  purposeful	  to	  
marginalize	  black	  girls.	  	  
Over	  the	  course	  of	  the	  writing	  sessions,	  Keisha	  took	  a	  more	  personal	  approach	  to	  
journal	  writing	  and	  discussed	  the	  influence	  media	  representations	  have	  had	  on	  her	  
personal	  life	  (fieldnotes,	  December	  12).	  In	  a	  journal	  free-­‐write,	  Keisha	  mentioned	  that	  her	  
views	  on	  society	  were	  rooted	  in	  her	  lived	  experiences	  as	  a	  black	  girl	  and	  her	  love	  for	  hip-­‐
hop	  music.	  In	  response	  to	  the	  journal	  prompt,	  “How	  do	  these	  images	  make	  you	  see	  
yourself?”	  Keisha	  asserted	  “being	  a	  black	  girl	  is	  hard	  because	  we	  are	  always	  ridiculed	  and	  
stereotyped”	  (journal	  data,	  October	  15,	  2013).	  Similar	  to	  Denise,	  Keisha	  experienced	  the	  
constant	  day-­‐to-­‐day	  struggles	  associated	  with	  both	  her	  blackness	  and	  her	  femaleness.	  She	  
shared	  similar	  worldviews	  and	  perspectives	  with	  Denise	  on	  images	  of	  black	  girls	  and	  self-­‐
identity.	  Keisha	  also	  assumed	  heightened	  cultural	  consciousness	  on	  how	  society	  perceives	  

	  

116	  
her	  and	  positions	  her	  in	  society.	  In	  a	  series	  of	  journal	  free-­‐writes,	  Keisha	  acknowledged	  
that	  the	  images	  were	  “hurtful	  and	  hard	  to	  escape	  because	  they	  are	  everywhere.	  Because	  the	  
negative	  images	  are	  everywhere,	  you	  start	  to	  believe	  them	  even	  if	  you	  are	  living	  a	  positive	  
life—going	  to	  school,	  getting	  good	  grades,	  and	  staying	  out	  of	  trouble”	  (journal	  data,	  October	  
15,	  2013).	  Keisha	  asserts	  that	  the	  media	  is	  saturated	  in	  negative	  images	  and	  the	  constant	  
exposure	  to	  the	  images	  makes	  them	  powerful.	  She	  further	  revealed	  that	  even	  engaging	  in	  
activities	  that	  are	  positive	  does	  not	  ensure	  that	  she	  will	  resist	  believing	  in	  the	  media	  
stereotypes	  of	  black	  girls.	  Her	  response	  is	  a	  clear	  indication	  of	  her	  understanding	  of	  how	  
hegemonic	  ideologies	  function	  in	  society.	  
Keisha	  describes	  the	  power	  of	  negative	  images	  as	  a	  “black	  cloud	  of	  negativity”	  that	  
consumes	  her	  day	  to	  day.	  Despite	  Keisha’s	  feelings	  of	  being	  consumed	  by	  a	  “black	  cloud	  of	  
negativity”	  and	  the	  powerful	  images	  that	  saturate	  the	  media,	  she	  spoke	  quite	  favorably	  
about	  black	  girlhood	  and	  what	  it	  meant	  to	  her.	  She	  described	  herself	  as	  “beautiful,	  smart,	  
and	  successful”	  (journal	  data,	  October	  17,	  2013).	  Keisha	  further	  asserted	  that	  it	  was	  the	  
way	  that	  she	  self-­‐identified	  that	  influenced	  how	  she	  addressed	  negative	  images.	  Keisha	  
positions	  herself	  at	  the	  intersection	  of	  two	  conflicting	  ideologies—one	  ideology	  that	  
acknowledges	  how	  the	  saturation	  of	  negative	  images	  in	  the	  media	  forces	  black	  girls	  to	  buy	  
into	  these	  images	  and	  the	  other	  ideology	  that	  allows	  black	  girls	  to	  still	  see	  themselves	  as	  
having	  positive	  attributes.	  Keisha’s	  twoness	  or	  what	  W.E.B.	  Du	  Bois	  describes	  as	  double	  
consciousness	  is	  an	  indication	  that	  black	  girls	  are	  constantly	  negotiating	  their	  identities	  
and	  how	  they	  see	  themselves	  in	  the	  world	  (fieldnotes,	  November	  1,	  2013).	  As	  she	  
continued	  to	  negotiate	  her	  identity	  over	  the	  course	  of	  the	  journal	  writing	  process,	  Keisha
