displayed	  a	  form	  of	  resistance	  and	  mentioned	  that	  she	  no	  longer	  supports	  media	  outlets	  

	  

117	  
that	  portray	  black	  girls	  negatively,	  including	  magazines,	  music	  videos,	  and	  movies.	  She	  
reveals	  that	  part	  of	  her	  sense	  of	  consciousness	  is	  evident	  in	  her	  buying	  power	  as	  a	  
consumer	  (Keisha,	  personal	  communication,	  October	  30,	  2013).	  As	  a	  lover	  of	  Hip-­‐hop	  
music	  born	  during	  the	  era	  of	  the	  commodification	  Hip-­‐hop,	  Keisha	  reveals	  how	  Hip-­‐hop	  
music	  can	  be	  both	  empowering	  and	  troubling	  for	  black	  girls	  (Pough,	  2004).	  Keisha’s	  activist	  
approach	  to	  Hip-­‐hop	  music	  and	  her	  decision	  to	  no	  longer	  support	  media	  outlets	  that	  
portray	  black	  girls	  negatively	  challenges	  the	  Hip-­‐hop	  feminist	  framework.	  Although	  the	  
Hip-­‐hop	  feminist	  framework	  is	  grounded	  in	  two	  conflicting	  perspectives,	  feminist	  
ideologies	  and	  Hip-­‐hop	  culture,	  Keisha’s	  form	  of	  resistance	  through	  her	  decisions	  to	  no	  
longer	  support	  media	  outlets	  that	  negatively	  portray	  black	  girls	  challenges	  the	  framework.	  	  
	  
Lisa	  
In	  a	  free-­‐write	  journal	  entry,	  Lisa	  recalled	  being	  a	  victim	  of	  cat-­‐calling	  by	  men	  on	  the	  
street.	  Lisa’s	  view	  of	  the	  media	  extends	  beyond	  television	  and	  music	  outlets	  and	  also	  
entails	  everyday	  interactions	  with	  people	  around	  her.	  She	  described	  cat-­‐calling	  as	  “a	  thirsty	  
guy’s	  way	  of	  getting	  a	  girl’s	  attention”	  (Lisa,	  personal	  communication,	  September	  30,	  2013).	  
Her	  experience	  with	  cat-­‐calling	  entailed	  being	  the	  victim	  of	  sexually	  charged	  comments.	  In	  
Lisa’s	  experience	  with	  cat-­‐calling	  occurred	  both	  on	  the	  street	  and	  within	  school	  spaces.	  On	  
one	  occasion	  she	  recalled	  being	  confronted	  by	  several	  teenage	  boys	  after	  ignoring	  their	  
verbal	  advances.	  The	  confrontation	  escalated	  and	  the	  boys	  began	  shouting	  sexually	  charged	  
comments	  at	  Lisa	  and	  throwing	  textbooks	  at	  her	  in	  the	  hallway.	  Lisa	  reported	  the	  incident	  
to	  school	  officials—two	  school	  safety	  officers	  and	  a	  front	  office	  staff	  member	  (Lisa,	  
personal	  communication,	  September	  30,	  2013).	  	  The	  school	  officials	  refused	  to	  address	  the	  

	  

118	  
matter,	  even	  after	  Lisa	  identified	  the	  teenage	  boys.	  In	  response	  to	  her	  complaint	  one	  of	  the	  
male	  safety	  officers	  stated	  “If	  you	  weren’t	  roaming	  the	  hallway,	  none	  of	  that	  would	  have	  
happened	  to	  you!”	  (Lisa,	  personal	  communication,	  September	  30,	  2013).	  Lisa	  
acknowledged	  that	  the	  safety	  officer’s	  response	  to	  her	  complaint	  reveals	  that	  society	  
stigmatizes	  girls,	  specifically	  black	  girls	  even	  when	  they	  have	  been	  victimized.	  Hence,	  the	  
school	  safety	  officers’	  victim	  blaming	  indicates	  that	  black	  girls	  are	  inherently	  guilty.	  Black	  
girls	  are	  characterized	  as	  aggressive	  and	  considered	  agitators	  and	  can	  be	  violated	  without	  
recourse	  (Richardson,	  2002).	  In	  her	  free-­‐write	  journal	  entries,	  Lisa	  details	  her	  experiences	  
as	  a	  victim	  of	  sexual	  harassment.	  
In	  response	  to	  the	  media’s	  portrayal	  of	  black	  girls,	  Lisa	  mentioned	  how	  society	  
views	  black	  female	  sexuality.	  She	  asserted	  that	  “the	  media	  makes	  black	  girls	  look	  like	  
‘smuts’	  and	  ‘thots.’	  I	  feel	  like	  they	  are	  trying	  to	  destroy	  us”	  (journal	  data,	  October	  12,	  2013).	  
Lisa	  described	  the	  terms	  “smuts”	  and	  “thots”	  as	  contemporary	  terms	  “used	  to	  label	  a	  girl	  as	  
a	  ho	  or	  a	  slut.”	  Lisa	  highlights	  the	  stereotypical	  image	  of	  black	  women	  as	  a	  controlling	  
image.	  She	  described	  the	  term	  “thot”	  as	  a	  controlling	  image	  that	  is	  synonymous	  with	  ho,	  
wench	  or	  slut	  and	  was	  used	  to	  refer	  to	  a	  black	  girl	  whose	  sexual	  behavior	  was	  loose	  and	  
immoral	  (Richardson,	  2002).	  According	  to	  hip	  hop	  feminist	  scholar	  Elaine	  Richardson	  
(2002),	  the	  white	  supremacist	  patriarchal	  perspective	  is	  the	  dominant	  discourse	  designed	  
to	  exploit	  black	  female	  sexuality.	  Lisa	  highlighted	  how	  patriarchal	  perspectives	  promote	  a	  
double	  standard	  on	  black	  female	  sexuality	  by	  focusing	  solely	  on	  the	  creation	  of	  negative	  
sexual	  images	  of	  black	  girls	  while	  rewarding	  black	  males	  for	  demonstrating	  their	  sexual	  
prowess	  (fieldnotes,	  October	  20,	  2013).	  Lisa	  spoke	  back	  to	  dominant	  discourse	  by	  
interrogating	  the	  negative	  images	  and	  the	  double	  standards	  that	  the	  media	  perpetuates.	  

	  

119	  
Initially	  Lisa	  was	  hesitant	  to	  participate	  in	  the	  journal	  sessions,	  but	  she	  began	  to	  use	  the	  
journal	  writing	  sessions	  as	  a	  safe	  space	  to	  release	  emotional	  baggage	  that	  she	  had	  
experienced	  in	  her	  life.	  	  
Similar	  to	  Keisha	  and	  Denise,	  Lisa	  asserted	  that	  there	  is	  a	  constant	  assault	  on	  black	  
girl’s	  mere	  existence.	  In	  one	  of	  her	  free-­‐writes,	  Lisa	  wrote:	  	  
	  I	  feel	  like	  we	  are	  judged	  just	  for	  existing.	  I	  never	  judge	  anyone	  until	  I	  get	  to	  know	  
them	  but	  we	  never	  get	  a	  fair	  chance.	  Never	  get	  the	  benefit	  of	  the	  doubt.	  Others	  get	  
the	  benefit	  of	  the	  doubt,	  but	  somehow	  we	  never	  do.	  I	  don’t	  feel	  like	  we	  get	  the	  
respect	  we	  deserve	  as	  black	  girls!	  (journal	  data,	  November	  12,	  2013)	  
	  
Lisa	  acknowledged	  that	  negative	  images	  of	  blackness	  and	  femaleness	  were	  
intentional,	  deeply	  rooted	  in	  society,	  and	  designed	  to	  marginalize	  black	  girls.	  She	  also	  
revealed	  that	  the	  media	  saturation	  with	  negative	  images	  influenced	  how	  she	  saw	  herself.	  In	  
a	  subsequent	  journal	  prompt,	  Lisa	  wrote	  “Sometimes	  I	  believe	  the	  stories	  and	  the	  
stereotypes	  because	  it’s	  everywhere.	  I	  sometimes	  believe	  I’m	  not	  good	  enough”	  (journal	  
data,	  November	  12,	  2013).	  Similar	  to	  Keisha,	  Lisa	  reveals	  that	  media	  images	  are	  so	  
powerful	  that	  she	  sometimes	  believes	  the	  negative	  images	  that	  portray	  who	  she	  is.	  Despite	  
the	  judgment,	  the	  disrespect,	  and	  the	  stereotypes	  that	  she	  has	  been	  confronted	  with,	  she	  
saw	  her	  ability	  to	  create	  change.	  Lisa	  wrote	  “being	  a	  black	  girl	  means	  you	  have	  the	  power	  
to	  make	  changes.	  You	  don’t	  have	  to	  accept	  being	  mistreated	  and	  misrepresented.	  We	  can	  
join	  together	  and	  tell	  our	  own	  stories	  and	  represent	  who	  we	  are”	  (journal	  data,	  October	  12,	  
2013).	  Lisa	  highlights	  the	  importance	  of	  black	  female	  solidarity	  and	  asserting	  the	  power	  in	  
pushing	  back	  against	  dominant	  narratives	  and	  re-­‐telling	  the	  narratives	  of	  the	  black	  girl	  
experience	  (Richardson,	  2002;	  Collins,	  2000).	  	  
	  

	  

120	  
Summative	  Findings	  
Through	  journal	  writing,	  Denise,	  Keisha,	  and	  Lisa’s	  narratives	  highlighted	  how	  black	  
girls	  are	  bombarded	  by	  messages	  in	  the	  media	  and	  how	  these	  messages	  influence	  who	  they	  
are	  and	  how	  they	  see	  themselves	  (Richardson,	  2013).	  The	  media	  images	  are	  often	  socially	  
constructed	  and	  reflect	  hegemonic	  ideologies	  that	  perpetuate	  negative	  images	  of	  black	  
girlhood.	  Although	  these	  hegemonic	  ideologies	  have	  had	  historical	  significance,	  black	  girls	  
continue	  to	  be	  confronted	  with	  media	  representations	  in	  present	  day	  from	  reality	  shows,	  to	  
films,	  to	  the	  Internet	  that	  have	  been	  damaging	  (Richardson,	  2013).	  Over	  the	  course	  of	  the	  
sessions	  Dense,	  Keisha,	  and	  Lisa	  constantly	  negotiated	  their	  identities,	  positionalities,	  and	  
power	  through	  journal	  writing.	  Through	  both	  personal	  narratives	  and	  journal	  writing	  
sessions,	  Denise,	  Keisha,	  and	  Lisa	  documented	  their	  perspectives	  on	  media	  representations	  
of	  black	  girls,	  the	  black	  girlhood	  experience,	  and	  their	  personal	  and	  collective	  identities.	  
Through	  a	  hip-­‐hop	  feminist	  lens	  each	  participant	  shared	  a	  set	  of	  personal	  experiences	  
detailing	  how	  society	  functions,	  the	  interplay	  between	  dominant	  and	  secondary	  discourses,	  
and	  how	  these	  discourses	  influence	  who	  they	  are	  and	  how	  they	  see	  themselves.	  
Media	  representations	  and	  self-­‐identity	  of	  black	  girls.	  Drawing	  upon	  their	  raced	  
and	  gendered	  identities,	  Denise,	  Keisha,	  and	  Lisa	  detailed	  personal	  narratives	  to	  make	  
sense	  of	  their	  place	  in	  the	  world	  and	  articulated	  how	  the	  media	  represents	  black	  girls	  both	  
individually	  and	  collectively.	  Denise	  detailed	  her	  experience	  being	  racially	  targeted	  on	  the	  
street	  and	  within	  the	  classroom	  environment	  while	  Keisha	  detailed	  her	  experience	  and	  the	  
experiences	  of	  her	  classmates	  confronting	  racial	  and	  cultural	  tensions	  fueled	  by	  media	  
images	  of	  particular	  ethnic	  groups.	  Lisa	  depicted	  her	  experience	  being	  sexually	  harassed	  by	  
teenage	  boys	  in	  a	  school	  hallway	  while	  school	  staff	  blamed	  her	  for	  being	  victimized.	  Each	  

	  

121	  
personal	  narrative	  was	  situated	  within	  a	  public,	  yet	  hostile	  environment	  and	  detailed	  how	  
society	  plays	  into	  the	  degradation	  of	  black	  girls.	  
Denise,	  Keisha,	  and	  Lisa	  each	  acknowledged	  that	  they	  were	  constantly	  bombarded	  
with	  images	  from	  various	  spheres	  of	  society	  including	  reality	  shows,	  music,	  and	  magazines	  
that	  aren’t	  affirming,	  uplifting,	  or	  encouraging,	  but	  instead	  were	  designed	  to	  be	  offensive	  
and	  hurtful.	  Participants	  revealed	  that	  negative	  messages	  and	  images	  have	  often	  influenced	  
how	  they	  see	  themselves.	  They	  each	  drew	  upon	  personal	  narratives	  to	  document	  how	  
images	  negatively	  impact	  their	  identity	  development	  and	  also	  revealed	  that	  constant	  
exposure	  to	  negative	  and	  controlling	  images	  of	  black	  girlhood	  influenced	  how	  they	  self-­‐
identified.	  Denise,	  Keisha,	  and	  Lisa’s	  interrogation	  of	  society	  highlighted	  their	  familiarity	  
with	  hegemonic	  ideologies	  and	  the	  perpetuation	  of	  inequities	  against	  black	  girls.	  
Despite	  being	  bombarded	  by	  negative	  images	  of	  blackness	  and	  femaleness	  and	  the	  
constant	  perpetuation	  of	  inequities	  against	  black	  girls	  that	  negatively	  affected	  their	  identity	  
development	  participants	  displayed	  resilience	  against	  media	  representations	  that	  
threatened	  their	  personhood	  (Richardson,	  2013).	  Although	  Denise,	  Keisha,	  and	  Lisa	  each	  
drew	  upon	  different	  personal	  experiences	  to	  make	  sense	  of	  their	  place	  in	  the	  world,	  they	  
each	  assumed	  two	  conflicting	  identities—one	  that	  conformed	  to	  negative	  images	  of	  self	  by	  
the	  media	  and	  the	  other	  that	  displayed	  resilience	  through	  their	  personal	  acknowledgement	  
of	  their	  positive	  attributes.	  Throughout	  the	  journal	  sessions,	  black	  girls	  documented	  the	  
interplay	  between	  dominant	  discourses	  and	  secondary	  discourse	  and	  their	  constant	  
negotiation	  of	  who	  they	  were	  and	  how	  they	  saw	  themselves.	  	  
Clearing	  spaces:	  Free-­‐writes	  as	  tools	  of	  resistance.	  Over	  the	  course	  of	  the	  journal	  
writing	  process,	  Denise,	  Keisha,	  and	  Lisa	  engaged	  in	  both	  prompt-­‐driven	  writes	  and	  free-­‐

	  

122	  
writes.	  Collectively,	  their	  prompt-­‐driven	  writes	  were	  often	  brief	  and	  concise,	  while	  their	  
free-­‐writes	  tended	  to	  be	  lengthier,	  more	  expressive,	  and	  more	  intimate.	  Free-­‐writes	  served	  
participants	  in	  different	  ways	  over	  the	  course	  of	  the	  program.	  Lisa	  was	  very	  shy	  and	  
reserved	  (see	  Participant	  Profiles,	  Chapter	  3),	  and	  although	  she	  participated	  in	  both	  the	  
online	  and	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  group	  discussions,	  she	  was	  most	  comfortable	  using	  the	  free-­‐writes	  
as	  her	  main	  form	  of	  expression.	  Denise	  and	  Keisha	  used	  free-­‐writes	  to	  extend	  their	  initial	  
thoughts	  and	  conversations	  written	  in	  the	  prompt-­‐driven	  writes,	  to	  express	  more	  intimate	  
accounts	  of	  conversations	  that	  occurred	  in	  the	  focus	  group	  interviews	  and	  the	  online	  
discussions.	  Each	  participant	  used	  the	  free-­‐writes	  as	  opportunities	  to	  express	  their	  feelings,	  
expand	  their	  ideas,	  and	  discuss	  issues	  that	  would	  often	  be	  considered	  taboo	  in	  discussions	  
situated	  in	  public	  settings.	  In	  doing	  so,	  free-­‐writing	  provided	  participants	  the	  opportunity	  
to	  clear	  spaces	  in	  order	  to	  re-­‐position	  and	  re-­‐write	  themselves	  into	  society	  and	  validate	  
who	  they	  were	  through	  their	  lived	  experiences.	  Drawing	  upon	  a	  hip-­‐hop	  feminist	  
theoretical	  lens,	  the	  journal	  writing	  sessions	  offered	  participants	  the	  autonomy	  and	  
freedom	  of	  expression	  and	  helped	  to	  create	  affirming	  spaces	  for	  the	  girls	  by	  offering	  them	  
opportunities	  to	  tell	  their	  stories	  in	  their	  own	  words	  on	  their	  own	  terms.	  
Although	  the	  journal	  writing	  sessions	  provided	  participants	  opportunities	  to	  tell	  
their	  stories	  in	  their	  own	  words	  on	  their	  own	  terms	  within	  an	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  setting,	  Denise,	  
Keisha,	  and	  Lisa	  employed	  writing	  conventions	  typically	  found	  in	  school	  spaces.	  Each	  
participant	  used	  writing	  conventions	  that	  conformed	  to	  Standard	  English	  to	  express	  
themselves.	  During	  journal	  sessions	  participants	  were	  consumed	  with	  conforming	  to	  the	  
conventions	  of	  Standard	  English.	  During	  each	  journal	  writing	  session	  Denise,	  Keisha,	  and	  
Lisa	  would	  consult	  with	  each	  other	  to	  ensure	  that	  they	  had	  conformed	  to	  the	  writing	  

	  

123	  
conventions	  for	  Standard	  English.	  They	  each	  made	  conscious	  decisions	  to	  conform	  to	  what	  
would	  be	  described	  as	  the	  dominant	  language.	  Writing	  provided	  each	  of	  the	  girls	  an	  
affirming	  space;	  however,	  their	  association	  with	  writing	  in	  the	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  space	  was	  
based	  on	  writing	  commonly	  endorsed	  in	  school	  spaces.	  Denise,	  Keisha,	  and	  Lisa	  viewed	  
literacy,	  specifically	  writing,	  as	  having	  a	  specific	  form	  and	  function	  that	  was	  affiliated	  with	  
school-­‐based	  writing.	  This	  is	  vastly	  different	  from	  the	  style	  or	  linguistic	  practices	  that	  they	  
employed	  in	  the	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  focus	  group	  interviews	  (see	  Chapter	  5).	  Denise,	  Keisha,	  and	  
Lisa	  each	  incorporated	  features	  of	  African-­‐American	  Vernacular	  English	  (AAVE)	  at	  various	  
points	  in	  their	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  focus	  group	  interviews	  (see	  Chapter	  5).	  	  
Chapter	  4	  focused	  on	  societal	  perspectives	  and	  examined	  Denise,	  Keisha,	  and	  Lisa’s	  
discourses	  on	  the	  media	  representations	  of	  black	  girls	  and	  the	  ways	  these	  representations	  
influenced	  how	  they	  self-­‐identified.	  Each	  participant	  drew	  upon	  their	  personal	  narratives	  
through	  writing	  to	  articulate	  their	  constant	  negotiation	  of	  power,	  identity,	  and	  positionality	  
as	  black	  girls	  confronted	  with	  social	  issues	  in	  the	  21st	  Century.	  Chapter	  5	  focused	  on	  the	  
institutional	  perspective	  and	  detailed	  participants’	  meaning	  making	  of	  street	  literature	  text	  
within	  an	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  digital	  context.	  	  
	  

	  

124	  
Chapter	  Five	  
Black	  Girls’	  Meaning	  Making	  in	  the	  Out-­‐of-­‐School	  Context	  
“I	  wanted	  to	  jump	  in	  the	  book,	  scream,	  and	  try	  and	  save	  her.	  How	  can	  so	  many	  people	  in	  
her	  life,	  in	  society	  watch	  this	  girl	  slip	  through	  the	  cracks.”	  –	  Tammy	  (Journal	  Data,	  
September	  15,	  2013)	  
Introduction	  
Over	  the	  course	  of	  this	  study,	  several	  participants	  sent	  friend	  requests	  to	  my	  
personal	  Facebook	  Page.	  Although	  their	  personal	  Facebook	  Pages	  were	  not	  analyzed	  for	  
this	  study,	  after	  accepting	  their	  friend	  requests,	  I	  observed	  that	  participants’	  linguistic	  
styles	  were	  vastly	  different	  in	  their	  personal	  online	  Facebook	  pages	  from	  our	  online	  
Facebook	  discussion	  group	  (fieldnotes,	  October	  18,	  2013).	  I	  also	  observed	  that	  the	  
participants	  employed	  vastly	  different	  language	  practices	  in	  the	  focus	  group	  interviews	  
(fieldnotes,	  October	  18,	  2013).	  Ensuring	  that	  I	  did	  not	  influence	  their	  literacy	  and	  language	  
practices	  over	  the	  course	  of	  the	  study,	  during	  the	  last	  few	  minutes	  of	  our	  final	  focus	  group	  
interview	  meeting	  I	  asked	  the	  group	  the	  following	  “How	  do	  you	  express	  yourself	  in	  
different	  settings	  both	  online	  and	  face-­‐to-­‐face?”	  Do	  you	  find	  that	  you	  express	  yourself	  in	  the	  
same	  ways	  or	  in	  different	  ways	  across	  different	  settings?”	  Each	  participant	  turned	  left	  and	  
turned	  right	  and	  began	  to	  laugh	  hysterically.	  Lisa	  yelled	  “Sometimes	  I	  do	  and	  sometimes	  I	  
don’t.	  It	  all	  depends	  on	  the	  circumstances”	  (Lisa,	  personal	  communication,	  December	  2,	  
2013).	  Tammy	  chimed	  in	  “	  I	  think	  in	  person	  you	  have	  less	  time	  to	  think	  about	  what	  ya	  
gonna	  say	  and	  how	  you	  say	  it	  so	  it’s	  kinda	  more	  relaxed”	  (Tammy,	  personal	  
communication,	  December	  2,	  2013).	  Lisa	  and	  Tammy’s	  perspectives	  shed	  light	  on	  how	  

	  

125	  
black	  girl’s	  social	  positioning	  and	  level	  of	  consciousness	  influences	  how	  they	  make	  meaning	  
of	  street	  literature	  texts	  across	  contexts	  (Sipe,	  2008;	  Richardson,	  2002,	  2006).	  
	  

In	  this	  chapter,	  I	  examined	  the	  ways	  that	  Denise,	  Keisha,	  Lisa,	  Vanessa	  and	  Tammy	  

made	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  within	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  context.	  I	  highlighted	  the	  orders	  of	  
discourse	  (genre,	  discourse,	  and	  style)	  and	  how	  institutional	  factors	  influenced	  the	  
meaning	  making	  process	  (Fairclough,	  1989;	  Rogers,	  2003).	  In	  this	  study,	  the	  digital	  context	  
was	  the	  primary	  focus	  and	  the	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  context	  was	  primarily	  used	  as	  targeted	  data;	  
however,	  the	  cross-­‐analysis	  approach	  highlighted	  how	  context	  influenced	  the	  ways	  
participants	  made	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts	  and	  constructed	  their	  identities	  across	  
contexts	  (Gee,	  2000).	  A	  cross-­‐analytical	  approach	  accounted	  for	  the	  various	  meaning	  
making	  discourses,	  linguistic	  styles,	  and	  situated	  identities	  across	  contexts.	  Through	  a	  
series	  of	  online	  book	  discussions	  and	  focus	  group	  interviews,	  I	  documented	  how	  black	  
adolescent	  girls	  constantly	  negotiated	  their	  power,	  positionality	  and	  identity	  around	  a	  
series	  of	  critical	  social	  issues	  embedded	  within	  street	  literature	  texts.	  	  
	  
Meaning	  Making	  and	  Identity	  Negotiations	  	  
Historically,	  social	  science	  has	  viewed	  identity	  as	  a	  cognitive	  phenomenon—one	  
that	  focused	  on	  individual	  behavior	  and	  the	  individual	  mind	  (Gee,	  2000).	  More	  recently,	  
research	  in	  the	  social	  sciences	  has	  broadened	  and	  identity	  has	  focused	  on	  the	  social	  and	  
cultural	  interactions.	  Sociocultural	  linguists	  Mary	  Bucholtz	  and	  Kira	  Hall	  (2005)	  describe	  
identity	  as	  the	  social	  positioning	  of	  self	  and	  others.	  Bucholtz	  and	  Hall’s	  framing	  of	  identity	  
accounts	  for	  how	  language,	  culture,	  and	  society	  shape	  identity.	  Bucholtz	  and	  Hall	  (2005)	  
argue	  that	  identity	  is	  a	  relational	  and	  sociocultural	  phenomenon	  that	  occurs	  in	  discourse	  

	  

126	  
across	  contexts	  rather	  than	  a	  fixed	  structure	  that	  occurs	  within	  the	  mind.	  It	  is	  these	  
contexts	  that	  shape	  participants’	  social	  meaning.	  In	  this	  study,	  I	  acknowledged	  that	  
meaning	  making	  does	  not	  occur	  in	  a	  vacuum,	  but	  is	  influenced	  by	  social	  factors	  that	  
ultimately	  shape	  participants’	  identities.	  I	  account	  for	  the	  shifts	  scholar	  Geneva	  
Smitherman	  (2006)	  describes	  as	  the	  “linguistic	  pushing	  and	  pulling”	  that	  marginalized	  
groups	  experience	  around	  their	  use	  of	  linguistic	  styles,	  as	  well	  as	  their	  meaning	  making.	  
Smitherman	  (2006)	  attributes	  “linguistic	  push	  and	  pull”	  to	  marginalized	  groups’	  constant	  
negotiation	  of	  both	  dominant	  and	  local	  ideologies.	  The	  constant	  negotiation	  of	  power	  and	  
positionality	  contributes	  to	  the	  shaping	  and	  re-­‐shaping	  of	  identities.	  
The	  following	  questions	  guided	  my	  analysis:	  How	  might	  black	  adolescent	  girls’	  
positionalities	  (i.e.,	  lived	  experiences,	  marked	  gendered	  and	  raced	  identities)	  influence	  how	  
they	  make	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts	  in	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  context?	  Which	  literacy	  
strategies	  and	  linguistic	  styles	  do	  they	  employ	  in	  the	  meaning	  making	  process?	  What	  new	  
literacies,	  if	  any	  are	  created	  during	  this	  process?	  How	  might	  narration	  (first	  person,	  second	  
person,	  third	  person)	  influence	  how	  participants	  make	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts?	  
How	  do	  Hip-­‐hop	  aesthetics	  embedded	  in	  street	  literature	  texts	  influence	  how	  participants	  
make	  meaning?	  In	  what	  ways	  do	  they	  conform	  or	  challenge	  dominant	  linguistic	  styles?	  In	  
instances	  in	  which	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  neither	  fully	  resist	  nor	  confirm	  to	  any	  particular	  
linguistic	  style,	  how	  do	  multiple	  linguistic	  styles	  co-­‐exist?	  How	  might	  group	  dynamics	  play	  
into	  their	  meaning	  making	  process?	  How	  might	  educators	  support	  diverse	  students’	  
emerging	  literacies	  and	  native	  linguistic	  styles	  within	  school	  spaces?	  
	  
	  

	  

127	  
Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis:	  Orders	  of	  Discourse	  Across	  Institutions	  
	  

In	  this	  chapter,	  I	  draw	  upon	  Fairclough’s	  (1989)	  and	  Rogers’	  (2003)	  three-­‐tiered	  

Critical	  Discourse	  analytical	  framework	  across	  three	  levels	  of	  interacting	  or	  orders	  of	  
discourse:	  genre,	  discourse	  and	  style).	  In	  this	  chapter,	  the	  primary	  institutional	  context	  
represented	  the	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  digital	  space	  and	  the	  secondary	  institutional	  context	  
represented	  the	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  space.	  Although	  this	  study	  was	  not	  designed	  to	  
be	  a	  comparative	  study	  and	  the	  secondary	  institutional	  context	  was	  used	  as	  targeted	  data,	  
the	  analysis	  in	  this	  study	  accounts	  for	  participants’	  meaning	  making	  across	  spheres.	  	  By	  
situating	  this	  study	  primarily	  within	  a	  particular	  institution,	  an	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  digital	  space,	  I	  
acknowledge	  that	  power,	  ideologies,	  positionalities,	  and	  space	  are	  embedded	  within	  
language	  and	  literacy.	  In	  essence,	  meaning	  cannot	  be	  neutral	  because	  it	  is	  rooted	  within	  
social	  practices	  and	  the	  lived	  experiences	  of	  my	  participants	  that	  are	  situated	  in	  particular	  
social	  institutions	  (Gee,	  2000).	  Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis	  accounted	  for	  the	  interconnected	  
and	  ideological	  nature	  of	  talk	  and	  discourse	  across	  critical	  social	  issues	  that	  impact	  black	  
adolescent	  girls	  in	  the	  21st	  Century.	  In	  this	  chapter,	  Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis	  combined	  
genre,	  discourse,	  and	  style	  with	  discursive	  theories	  of	  race,	  gender,	  new	  literacies,	  and	  
transactional	  acts	  of	  reading	  to	  guide	  the	  ways	  participants	  constructed	  critical	  social	  
issues	  within	  texts	  as	  well	  as	  within	  the	  larger	  society.	  	  
Chouliaraki	  and	  Fairclough	  (1999)	  defines	  genre	  as	  the	  “sort	  of	  language	  tied	  to	  a	  
particular	  social	  activity.”	  Rogers	  and	  Wetzel	  (2014)	  describe	  genres	  as	  “ways	  of	  
interacting”	  and	  represents	  the	  organizational	  components	  of	  interacting	  (p.	  72).	  In	  this	  
chapter,	  genre	  attended	  to	  the	  use	  of	  linguistic	  devices	  in	  participants’	  talk	  (Chouliaraki	  &	  
Fairclough,	  1999)	  (turn	  taking,	  overlapping,	  interruption,	  status	  and	  post	  likes).	  Discourses	  

	  

128	  
are	  “ways	  of	  representing”	  and	  describe	  how	  knowledge	  is	  represented	  and	  from	  what	  
perspective	  (Rogers,	  2003).	  In	  this	  chapter,	  I	  drew	  upon	  Luke’s	  definition	  (2000)	  of	  
discourse	  as	  “systematic	  clusters	  of	  themes,	  statements,	  ideas,	  and	  ideologies”	  (p.	  456).	  
Discourse	  represented	  how	  participants’	  ideologies	  (Luke,	  2000),	  stances	  (Sipe,	  2000),	  or	  
social	  positioning	  (Bucholtz	  &	  Hall,	  2005)	  influenced	  how	  they	  made	  meaning	  of	  street	  
literature	  texts.	  Styles	  are	  “ways	  of	  being”	  and	  represent	  interpersonal	  choice,	  specifically	  
one’s	  position	  taking	  (Rogers,	  2003).	  In	  this	  study,	  style	  was	  grounded	  in	  the	  work	  of	  Drs.	  
Geneva	  Smitherman	  and	  Elaine	  Richardson,	  professors	  and	  scholars	  in	  African	  American	  
language	  and	  identity	  politics.	  Style	  represented	  black	  adolescent	  girls’	  use	  linguistic	  styles,	  
specifically	  African	  American	  Vernacular	  English	  (AAVE)	  and	  Standard	  English	  as	  forms	  of	  
position	  taking	  and	  identity	  negotiation.	  	  
In	  this	  chapter,	  the	  orders	  of	  discourse-­‐genre,	  discourse,	  and	  style	  provided	  a	  
microanalysis	  of	  participants’	  meaning	  making	  using	  two	  street	  literature	  texts,	  PUSH	  by	  
Sapphire	  and	  Project	  Girl	  by	  Janet	  MacDonald.	  I	  detailed	  the	  orders	  of	  discourse	  by	  
employing	  discursive	  theories	  of	  Hip	  Hop	  Feminism,	  New	  Literacy	  Studies,	  and	  Reader	  
Response	  Theory	  that	  represented	  Lisa,	  Denise,	  Keisha,	  Vanessa,	  and	  Tammy’s	  
perspectives	  on	  several	  areas	  in	  the	  texts	  PUSH	  and	  Project	  Girl	  including	  Discourse	  on	  the	  
Black	  Female	  Condition,	  Discourse	  on	  Black	  Motherhood,	  and	  Discourse	  on	  Black	  Girl’s	  
Academic	  Success.	  Since	  each	  street	  literature	  text	  selected	  for	  this	  study	  focused	  on	  a	  
particular	  social	  issue	  that	  the	  female	  protagonist	  was	  confronted	  with,	  the	  findings	  in	  this	  
chapter	  are	  organized	  thematically.	  Push	  by	  Sapphire	  details	  participants’	  discourse	  on	  the	  
Black	  female	  condition	  and	  Black	  Motherhood,	  while	  Project	  Girl	  detailed	  participants’	  
Discourse	  on	  Black	  Girl’s	  Academic	  Success.	  

	  

129	  
Discourse	  on	  the	  Black	  Female	  Condition	  	  
As	  double	  minorities	  being	  both	  Black	  and	  female,	  black	  girls	  are	  confronted	  with	  
forms	  of	  oppression	  that	  work	  in	  conjunction	  with	  other	  forms	  of	  oppression	  to	  produce	  
social	  injustice	  and	  marginalization	  (Collins,	  2000).	  PUSH	  by	  Sapphire	  was	  the	  introductory	  
street	  literature	  text	  that	  participants	  read.	  The	  story	  details	  the	  black	  female	  condition	  
through	  the	  experiences	  of	  an	  African-­‐American	  illiterate	  sixteen-­‐year	  old	  name	  Claireece	  
Precious	  Jones	  who	  endures	  hardship	  at	  the	  hands	  of	  society,	  her	  parents,	  and	  the	  school	  
system.	  Precious	  endures	  being	  sexually	  abused	  by	  her	  father	  and	  physically	  abused	  by	  her	  
mother.	  Precious	  has	  two	  children	  by	  her	  father.	  In	  the	  initial	  online	  book	  discussion,	  the	  
participants	  discussed	  their	  perspectives	  on	  Precious’	  life	  experiences.	  The	  storyline	  was	  
graphic	  and	  sensitive,	  so	  I	  asked	  the	  online	  group	  to	  share	  their	  initial	  reactions	  to	  PUSH	  
(What	  was	  your	  initial	  reaction	  after	  reading	  PUSH?”).	  	  The	  conversation	  exchange	  below	  
details	  a	  conversation	  exchange	  between	  Vanessa	  and	  Denise	  in	  response	  to	  my	  online	  
question	  (online	  data,	  September	  7,	  2013).	  
1Vanessa	  When	  reading	  the	  first	  few	  chapters	  in	  my	  head	  I	  was	  just	  watching	  
2Precious	  the	  movie	  but	  then	  it	  got	  more	  in	  depth	  than	  the	  movie	  about	  her	  life	  
3and	  I	  learned	  things	  I	  didn't	  know.	  Although	  I	  haven't	  gone	  through	  the	  same	  
4things	  as	  precious	  I	  felt	  a	  connection	  just	  being	  a	  girl.	  We	  are	  all	  teenagers	  
5and	  go	  through	  obstacles	  at	  school	  and	  we	  still	  face	  rejection	  at	  some	  points.	  
6I	  really	  liked	  the	  book.	  
7Denise I	  Agree	  With	  Vanessa	  ;	  Our	  Experiences	  As	  A	  Young	  Black	  Woman	  In	  

8Society	  Is	  Somewhat	  Similar	  .	  Although	  Our	  Situations	  Are	  Not	  As	  Deep	  As	  
9Precious'	  We	  Still	  Have	  Similar	  Experiences.	  It	  Also	  Shows	  Me	  That	  Being	  

	  

130	  
10Black	  Is	  Somewhat	  Looked	  At	  As	  A	  Downfall	  Or	  A	  Disadvantage.	  That	  We	  
11Cannot	  Really	  Even	  Count	  On	  Society	  &	  Be	  So	  Dependent	  On	  Them	  But	  On	  
12Ourselves	  
Vanessa	  makes	  meaning	  of	  events	  in	  the	  story	  through	  both	  personal	  and	  
intertextual	  connections.	  Vanessa	  engaged	  in	  an	  intertextual	  response	  by	  relating	  the	  text	  
PUSH	  to	  the	  movie	  Precious	  and	  critiquing	  the	  features	  of	  each	  medium.	  Intertextual	  
responses	  are	  those	  in	  which	  the	  text	  is	  linked	  with	  another	  text	  or	  form	  of	  media	  (Sipe,	  
2008).	  By	  analyzing	  the	  book	  PUSH	  through	  the	  use	  of	  media	  imagery	  in	  the	  movie	  
Precious,	  Vanessa	  heightened	  her	  understanding	  of	  Precious’	  experiences	  (lines	  1-­‐3)	  
(fieldnotes,	  September	  10,	  2013).	  She	  also	  made	  personal	  connections	  with	  Precious.	  
Personal	  connections	  entail	  linking	  particular	  characters	  and/or	  storylines	  to	  one’s	  own	  
personal	  life	  or	  the	  life	  of	  someone	  that	  they	  know	  (Sipe,	  2008).	  Vanessa	  established	  her	  
positionality	  by	  employing	  both	  distancing	  language	  (“I	  haven’t	  gone	  through	  the	  same	  
things	  as	  Precious”)	  as	  well	  as	  gendered	  associating	  language	  (“I	  felt	  a	  connection	  just	  being	  
a	  girl”)	  to	  qualify	  her	  personal	  connection	  with	  Precious	  (lines	  3-­‐5)	  (fieldnotes,	  September	  
12,	  2013)	  (fieldnotes,	  September	  10,	  2013).	  In	  addition	  to	  her	  gendered	  connection	  to	  
Precious,	  Vanessa	  further	  established	  a	  personal	  connection	  with	  her	  as	  a	  teenager	  (“We	  
are	  all	  teenagers”)	  and	  co-­‐constructs	  the	  similarities	  in	  their	  experiences	  (“We..	  go	  through	  
obstacles	  at	  school	  and	  we	  still	  face	  rejection	  at	  some	  points”).	  She	  established	  solidarity	  
with	  both	  Precious	  and	  Denise	  through	  her	  use	  of	  the	  pronoun	  “we.”	  	  
Like	  Vanessa,	  Denise	  makes	  personal	  connections	  to	  the	  texts,	  but	  she	  also	  analyzes	  
the	  social	  conditions	  that	  Precious	  is	  confronted	  with	  and	  uses	  Precious’	  experiences	  to	  
make	  meaning	  of	  her	  own	  personal	  life	  (Richardson,	  2008;	  Sipe,	  2008).	  Denise	  highlights	  

	  

131	  
the	  hegemonic	  ideologies	  at	  play	  and	  its	  impact	  on	  black	  girls.	  She	  identifies	  and	  confronts	  
the	  obstacles	  and	  ideologies	  that	  perpetuate	  the	  black	  female	  condition	  in	  society	  
(Richardson,	  2008).	  By	  agreeing	  with	  Vanessa,	  Denise	  responds	  to	  her	  attempt	  at	  solidarity	  
and	  illustrates	  her	  own	  gendered	  positioning	  in	  relation	  to	  Precious’	  gendered	  position	  
(line	  9).	  Denise	  takes	  solidarity	  a	  step	  further	  and	  establishes	  black	  female	  solidarity	  with	  
Vanessa	  through	  her	  use	  of	  the	  pronoun	  “our”	  (“our	  experiences	  as	  a	  young	  black	  woman	  in	  
society	  is	  somewhat	  similar”).	  Denise	  employs	  gendered	  and	  racialized	  associating	  language	  
to	  establish	  her	  personal	  connection	  with	  Precious	  and	  her	  circumstances.	  Despite	  
employing	  distancing	  language	  (“although	  our	  situations	  are	  not	  as	  deep	  as	  precious”),	  she	  
acknowledges	  the	  similarities	  in	  their	  life	  experiences	  (“we	  still	  have	  similar	  experiences”).	  	  
Denise	  uses	  Precious’	  experiences	  as	  a	  lens	  to	  make	  meaning	  of	  the	  texts,	  but	  also	  to	  
assess	  how	  black	  females	  in	  the	  21st	  Century	  are	  treated	  in	  society	  (Richardson,	  2007).	  	  
She	  uses	  her	  experiences	  as	  a	  model	  to	  highlight	  the	  way	  society	  views	  her	  own	  blackness.	  
(“It	  also	  shows	  me	  that	  being	  Black	  is	  somewhat	  looked	  at	  as	  a	  downfall	  or	  a	  disadvantage”)	  
(lines	  8-­‐9).	  Denise	  engages	  in	  a	  critical	  response	  at	  the	  societal	  level	  as	  she	  dismantles	  
hegemonic	  ideologies	  and	  its	  impact	  on	  blackness	  and	  femaleness	  (fieldnotes,	  September,	  
10,	  2013).	  She	  assumes	  black	  female	  solidarity	  through	  the	  use	  of	  the	  pronoun	  “we”	  (line	  
8).	  Denise	  further	  establishes	  how	  her	  personhood,	  one	  of	  being	  black	  and	  female	  dictates	  
the	  ways	  in	  which	  she	  is	  perceived	  in	  society	  (fieldnotes,	  September	  10,	  2013).	  She	  
highlights	  the	  consequences	  that	  come	  with	  being	  both	  black	  and	  female	  and	  declares	  self-­‐
reliance	  as	  her	  act	  of	  resistance	  (“We	  Cannot	  Count	  Really	  even	  on	  society	  &	  Be	  So	  Dependent	  
on	  Them	  But	  On	  Ourselves”;	  lines	  10-­‐11).	  	  

	  

132	  
According	  to	  Black	  Feminist	  scholar	  Patricia	  Collins	  (2000),	  Denise’s	  emerging	  
power	  is	  described	  as	  an	  agent	  of	  knowledge.	  By	  portraying	  Denise	  as	  both	  a	  self-­‐defined	  
and	  self-­‐reliant	  individual	  confronting	  race,	  gender,	  and	  class	  oppression	  that	  knowledge	  
empowers	  herself,	  as	  well	  as	  the	  other	  participants	  on	  the	  online	  thread.	  Through	  social	  
and	  cultural	  awareness,	  Denise	  offers	  a	  counternarrative	  about	  what	  it	  means	  to	  be	  both	  
black	  and	  female	  in	  society.	  Although	  Sipe’s	  (2008)	  work	  has	  greatly	  informed	  my	  
interpretation	  of	  Denise’s	  meaning	  making	  process,	  Denise’s	  responses	  to	  the	  black	  female	  
condition	  on	  the	  societal	  level	  highlights	  the	  limitations	  of	  Sipe’s	  Literacy	  Understanding	  
Theoretical	  Model.	  Denise’s	  positionality	  and	  meaning	  making	  process	  on	  the	  societal	  
highlight	  the	  need	  to	  extend	  Sipe’s	  work	  by	  offering	  a	  more	  culturally	  relevant	  Literacy	  
Understanding	  Theoretical	  Model	  that	  accounts	  for	  critical	  perspectives	  on	  the	  secondary	  
grades	  level.	  
	  
Discourse	  on	  Black	  Motherhood	  	  
On	  the	  same	  discussion	  thread,	  Keisha	  and	  Nicki’s	  initial	  reactions	  focused	  
predominantly	  on	  black	  motherhood.	  Keisha	  and	  Nicki’s	  responses	  consisted	  of	  a	  character	  
analysis	  of	  Mary,	  Precious’	  mother.	  They	  both	  situated	  their	  responses	  around	  dominant	  
perspectives	  on	  black	  motherhood	  and	  the	  mother-­‐daughter	  relationship.	  Tammy’s	  initial	  
reaction	  is	  also	  situated	  around	  black	  motherhood,	  however	  she	  also	  highlights	  society’s	  
neglect	  of	  teen	  mothers.	  The	  exchange	  below	  details	  their	  unique	  perspectives	  on	  black	  
motherhood.	  
1Keisha	  The	  First	  Couple	  Chapters	  Had	  Me	  Riddled	  With	  Emotion.	  Like	  For	  
2Example	  I	  Was	  Angry	  For	  The	  Fact	  That	  Precious's	  Mom	  is	  So	  Cruel	  To	  Her	  

	  

133	  
3And	  Try's	  To	  Break	  Her	  Down	  With	  Everything	  She	  Has.	  Like	  When	  She	  Calls	  
4Precious	  Inappropriate	  Names,	  That	  Can	  Break	  Down	  A	  Persons	  Self-­‐Esteem	  
5Nicki:	  it	  was	  sad	  because	  from	  what	  I’ve	  seen	  precious	  is	  being	  abused	  and	  
6when	  precious	  was	  being	  abused	  I	  didn’t	  like	  it	  at	  all	  when	  she	  was	  getting	  
7hit	  by	  the	  skillet	  and	  other	  things	  
In	  the	  above	  exchange,	  Keisha	  and	  Nicki	  expressed	  their	  emotional	  state	  after	  
reading	  about	  Precious’	  mistreatment	  at	  the	  hands	  of	  her	  mother.	  They	  expressed	  a	  range	  
of	  emotions	  from	  sad	  to	  angry	  to	  frustration.	  Sipe	  (2008)	  describes	  emotional	  responses	  as	  
the	  reader	  having	  a	  transitory	  moment	  to	  the	  point	  of	  entering	  the	  story	  with	  the	  character.	  
Transitory	  moments	  are	  expressed	  through	  emotion	  and	  are	  indications	  of	  meaning	  
making.	  Keisha	  made	  meaning	  of	  the	  texts	  by	  drawing	  upon	  her	  emotional	  state	  (“riddled	  
with	  emotion”;	  lines	  1-­‐2).	  She	  justifies	  her	  feeling	  of	  anger	  by	  explaining	  how	  physically	  and	  
emotionally	  abusive	  Precious’	  mother	  is.	  She	  reveals	  where	  her	  frustration	  lies	  when	  she	  
names	  the	  abuse	  and	  its	  impact	  	  (“like	  when	  she	  calls	  precious	  inappropriate	  names.	  That	  
can	  really	  break	  a	  person	  down.”).	  Keisha	  assumes	  a	  critical	  stance	  by	  analyzing	  the	  power	  
dynamics	  and	  mother-­‐daughter	  relationship	  and	  the	  way	  the	  mother	  minimizes	  Precious’	  
personhood	  through	  verbal	  and	  physical	  abuse	  (fieldnotes,	  September	  15,	  2013).	  	  By	  
linking	  the	  text	  PUSH	  with	  the	  movie	  Precious,	  the	  visual	  images	  and	  graphic	  nature	  of	  the	  
scene,	  Nicki	  connected	  the	  book	  and	  the	  movie	  and	  detailed	  visual	  images	  of	  abuse	  that	  
contributed	  to	  her	  own	  heightened	  emotional	  state	  of	  sadness.	  Both	  Nicki	  and	  Keisha’s	  
emotional	  state	  clearly	  indicates	  that	  their	  views	  on	  mothering	  and	  motherhood	  are	  
contrary	  to	  Mary’s	  acts	  as	  a	  mother	  (Collins,	  2000).	  Based	  on	  their	  responses,	  Nicki	  and	  

	  

134	  
Keisha’s	  dominant	  discourse	  of	  mothering	  is	  loving,	  protective,	  and	  understanding.	  In	  the	  
stanza	  below,	  Tammy	  offers	  a	  unique	  perspective	  on	  black	  motherhood.	  
8Tammy:	  The	  story	  of	  precious	  make	  me	  sad.	  I	  wanted	  to	  jump	  in	  the	  book	  ,	  
9scream	  ,	  and	  try	  and	  save	  her	  .	  How	  can	  so	  many	  people	  in	  her	  life,	  in	  
10society	  watch	  this	  girl	  slip	  through	  the	  cracks.	  The	  question	  again	  I	  asked	  
11why	  does	  society	  put	  on	  blinders	  and	  say	  its	  not	  my	  child.	  But	  its	  our	  
12problem	  and	  it	  becomes	  our	  issue	  when	  tax	  payers	  say	  that	  they	  have	  to	  
13help	  our	  teen	  moms	  who	  have	  to	  get	  on	  public	  assistance.	  So	  I	  Say	  to	  
14society	  it	  takes	  a	  village	  to	  raise	  a	  child	  .	  
Like	  Keisha	  and	  Nicki,	  Tammy	  makes	  meaning	  of	  the	  text	  through	  a	  transitory	  
moment	  through	  an	  expression	  of	  sadness.	  Unlike	  Nicki,	  Tammy	  did	  not	  appear	  to	  draw	  
upon	  visual	  imagery	  to	  make	  meaning	  of	  the	  text.	  In	  fact,	  she	  asserted	  that	  the	  movie	  and	  
the	  book	  were	  “two	  different	  things,”	  however	  she	  does	  compare	  and	  contrast	  both	  forms	  
of	  media.	  Tammy	  offers	  a	  critical	  response	  to	  the	  text	  at	  the	  societal	  level	  and	  she	  reverses	  
roles	  with	  society	  and	  interrogates	  society.	  Tammy’s	  views	  on	  mothering	  are	  in	  relation	  to	  
systematic	  oppression	  that	  exists	  within	  the	  story,	  as	  well	  as	  in	  society	  (fieldnotes,	  
September	  19,	  2013).	  She	  then	  personalizes	  the	  oppression	  and	  details	  how	  systematic	  
oppression	  functions	  on	  critical	  social	  issues	  like	  teen	  pregnancy.	  Tammy’s	  response	  
around	  teen	  mothers	  slipping	  through	  the	  cracks	  reveals	  that	  her	  concept	  of	  motherhood	  
must	  be	  expanded	  to	  the	  whole	  village	  (line	  13-­‐14).	  Tammy	  interrogates	  the	  same	  system	  
that	  vilifies	  teen	  mothers	  and	  demands	  a	  call	  to	  action.	  
In	  our	  first	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  meeting,	  Lisa,	  Tammy,	  and	  Denise	  eagerly	  approached	  the	  
teen	  center	  ready	  to	  discuss	  the	  text	  PUSH.	  Lisa,	  Tammy,	  and	  Denise	  began	  discussing	  the	  

	  

135	  
text	  before	  getting	  settled	  into	  their	  seats.	  Keisha	  stated,	  “Ms.	  we	  need	  to	  talk	  about	  this	  
immediately”	  (personal	  communication,	  Keisha,	  September	  15,	  2013).	  Denise	  then	  chimed	  
in,	  “Yeah,	  that	  book	  was	  beyond	  emotional.	  I	  couldn’t	  believe	  what	  I	  was	  reading	  at	  certain	  
points	  in	  the	  book”	  (Denise,	  personal	  communication,	  September	  15,	  2013).	  Lisa	  with	  her	  
eyes	  lowered	  shook	  her	  head	  in	  agreement	  with	  Tammy	  and	  Denise	  (fieldnotes,	  September,	  
15,	  2013).	  Tammy	  and	  Denise	  began	  discussing	  sensitive	  topics	  in	  the	  book.	  As	  all	  the	  
participants	  settled	  into	  the	  room	  and	  into	  their	  seats	  around	  the	  oval	  table,	  we	  continued	  
our	  conversation	  on	  black	  motherhood.	  By	  posing	  the	  question	  “What	  was	  Precious’	  
relationship	  like	  with	  her	  parents?	  Her	  motha?	  Her	  fatha?”	  I	  focused	  on	  two	  discourses	  that	  
were	  prominent	  among	  the	  participants,	  Black	  Female	  Condition	  and	  Black	  Motherhood	  
(researcher	  journal	  data,	  September	  15,	  2013).	  The	  conversation	  on	  black	  motherhood	  
intersected	  with	  conversation	  on	  the	  black	  female	  condition	  and	  fatherhood.	  In	  the	  
conversation	  exchange	  below	  Denise,	  Lisa,	  and	  Tammy	  each	  discussed	  their	  perspectives.	  
1Denise:	  (sits	  up	  and	  repositions	  herself	  in	  the	  chair)	  She	  was	  born	  into	  
2dysfunction.	  Her	  motha	  was	  sexually	  abusing	  her.	  Her	  motha	  allowed	  her	  
3husband	  to	  abuse	  Precious.	  Her	  motha	  was	  sick	  in	  the	  head!!	  
4Lisa:	  (holds	  head	  down,	  slouches	  in	  the	  chair,	  folds	  arms,	  and	  lowers	  voice)	  It	  
5was	  nasty.	  Her	  father	  abused	  Precious.	  Her	  [Pause]	  well	  at	  the	  end	  her	  father	  
6died.	  It	  was	  like	  the	  relationship	  was	  [Pause]	  it	  was	  not	  like	  people’s	  
7relationship	  with	  dere	  father	  is.	  Precious	  said	  it	  felt	  good!?	  (frowns	  her	  face)	  
8Denise:	  (looks	  at	  Lisa	  and	  repositions	  herself	  in	  the	  chair)	  I	  always	  try	  to	  
9figure	  out	  what	  options	  they	  had	  in	  that	  particular	  situation.	  I	  can’t	  judge	  a	  

	  

136	  
10character	  negatively	  if	  circumstances	  led	  them	  down	  the	  wrong	  path	  and	  
11they	  didn’t	  really	  have	  no	  options	  to	  turn	  their	  life	  around.	  	  
12Tammy:	  (looks	  at	  everyone	  in	  the	  group)	  Her	  mom	  wasn’t	  really	  a	  mother	  
13figure	  to	  her.	  She	  was	  by	  herself.	  Even	  with	  the	  adults	  that	  were	  in	  her	  life.	  
14Becuz	  her	  mom	  didn’t	  play	  the	  mother	  role.	  Her	  mom	  play	  [Pause]	  of.	  I	  
15don’t	  know	  her	  mom	  just	  broke	  her	  down.	  She	  wasn’t	  a	  mother	  that	  help	  
16you	  and	  build	  you	  up	  and	  support	  you.	  	  
Denise’s	  body	  language	  was	  an	  indication	  of	  attentiveness.	  Denise	  attributed	  
Precious’	  circumstances	  to	  a	  cycle	  of	  abuse	  and	  being	  “born	  into	  dysfunction”	  (line	  2).	  
Denise	  positioned	  Mary	  as	  both	  a	  willing	  participant	  and	  an	  accessory,	  associating	  her	  
actions	  to	  mental	  illness	  (fieldnotes,	  September	  15,	  2013).	  In	  her	  pronunciation	  of	  the	  word	  
“motha,”	  she	  code-­‐meshes	  blending	  Standard	  English	  and	  African-­‐American	  Vernacular	  
English	  in	  her	  response	  (Smitherman,	  1977).	  Lisa’s	  physical	  signs	  of	  closed	  body	  language,	  
including	  holding	  her	  head	  down,	  slouching	  in	  the	  chairing,	  folding	  her	  arms,	  and	  lowering	  
her	  voice	  indicate	  that	  she	  is	  uncomfortable	  with	  the	  conversation	  (lines	  4;	  fieldnotes,	  
September	  15,	  2013).	  Lisa	  acknowledged	  that	  Precious	  relationship	  with	  her	  father	  wasn’t	  
normal.	  Despite	  her	  acknowledgement	  of	  the	  uneven	  power	  dynamics	  and	  years	  of	  sexual	  
abuse,	  Lisa	  is	  perplexed	  with	  Precious’	  comment	  that	  it	  became	  normal	  to	  her.	  She	  frowns	  
her	  face	  and	  with	  a	  look	  of	  disgust	  and	  has	  difficulty	  understanding	  how	  Precious	  could	  
find	  pleasure	  in	  the	  sexual	  act	  (fieldnotes,	  September	  15,	  2013).	  Lisa	  employs	  several	  AAVE	  
features	  in	  her	  language.	  She	  code-­‐meshes	  in	  her	  use	  of	  the	  word	  “dere”	  (their).	  Lisa	  also	  
uses	  the	  word	  “be”	  (line	  11).	  (The	  relationship	  was	  not	  how	  people's	  relationship	  be	  with	  

	  

137	  
their	  fathers)	  to	  indicate	  a	  condition	  that	  occurs	  habitually	  (Smitherman,	  1977).	  The	  use	  of	  
the	  “be”	  copula	  is	  a	  prominent	  linguistic	  feature	  in	  African	  American	  Vernacular	  English.	  
In	  response	  to	  Lisa’s	  judgment	  of	  Precious	  saying	  it	  felt	  good,	  Denise	  repositions	  
herself	  in	  her	  chair	  and	  makes	  direct	  eye	  contact	  with	  Lisa	  and	  states	  that	  she	  chooses	  not	  
to	  judge	  decisions	  that	  people	  make	  without	  knowing	  if	  they	  had	  other	  options.	  (I	  always	  
try	  to	  figure	  out	  what	  options	  they	  had	  in	  that	  particular	  situation.	  I	  can’t	  judge	  a	  character	  
negatively	  if	  circumstances	  led	  them	  down	  the	  wrong	  path	  and	  they	  didn’t	  have	  options	  to	  
turn	  their	  life	  around).	  Denise	  acknowledges	  that	  a	  character’s	  circumstances	  may	  be	  a	  
result	  of	  external	  factors	  that	  make	  it	  difficult	  for	  them	  to	  turn	  their	  life	  around	  (lines	  9-­‐12).	  
This	  indicates	  Denise’s	  understanding	  of	  how	  external	  factors	  may	  be	  outside	  of	  a	  
persona’s	  control	  and	  may	  determine	  his	  or	  her	  fate.	  After	  a	  tense	  exchange	  between	  
Denise	  and	  Lisa,	  the	  interactional	  pattern	  shifted	  and	  Lisa	  was	  less	  talkative	  and	  less	  
attentive	  throughout	  the	  rest	  of	  the	  focus	  group	  interviews	  (fieldnotes,	  September	  20,	  
2013).	  	  Looking	  at	  just	  the	  nonverbal	  discourse,	  one	  might	  come	  to	  the	  conclusion	  that	  Lisa	  
is	  disengaged	  from	  the	  focus	  group	  interview.	  However,	  her	  nonverbal	  language	  shows	  that	  
she	  is	  still	  engaged	  through	  a	  series	  of	  multimodal	  movements.	  According	  to	  Richardson	  
(2002),	  Lisa	  is	  engaged	  in	  a	  nonverbal	  literacy	  strategy	  known	  as	  silence.	  According	  to	  
Richardson,	  African	  American	  females	  engage	  in	  verbal	  nonverbal	  communication	  
strategies	  to	  reinforce	  their	  presence	  within	  a	  space.	  Tammy	  turns	  to	  Denise	  in	  agreement	  
and	  stated	  that	  Precious	  had	  no	  choices	  at	  her	  disposal.	  Tammy	  further	  acknowledged	  that	  
”her	  upbringin"	  coupled	  with	  jealousy	  and	  animosity	  sealed	  her	  fate	  and	  resulted	  in	  her	  
mother	  putting	  a	  man	  before	  her	  child	  (lines	  19-­‐22).	  Tammy	  also	  code	  meshes	  throughout	  
her	  speech	  (lines	  18-­‐21).	  

	  

138	  
Both	  within	  the	  digital	  space	  and	  in	  the	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  space	  participants	  discussed	  
their	  perspectives	  on	  the	  black	  female	  condition	  and	  black	  motherhood.	  Participants	  
viewed	  Mary	  as	  falling	  short	  of	  her	  obligations	  as	  a	  mother,	  however	  aside	  from	  
acknowledging	  the	  abuse	  that	  Precious	  experienced	  at	  the	  hands	  of	  her	  father,	  participants	  
did	  not	  hold	  him	  to	  the	  same	  standards	  that	  they	  held	  Mary.	  What	  does	  this	  say	  about	  how	  
participants	  view	  parenting?	  View	  motherhood?	  View	  fatherhood?	  Why	  did	  parenting	  seem	  
to	  fall	  mostly	  on	  Mary	  the	  mother?	  Why	  is	  the	  mother	  seen	  as	  the	  protector?	  Did	  this	  have	  
more	  to	  do	  with	  the	  fact	  that	  the	  author	  Sapphire	  physically	  made	  Mary’s	  character	  present	  
throughout	  the	  story,	  while	  Precious’	  father’s	  actions	  were	  revealed,	  however	  he	  was	  not	  
made	  present	  throughout	  the	  story?	  Or	  does	  black	  motherhood	  hold	  a	  greater	  
responsibility	  to	  be	  a	  protector	  in	  the	  black	  community?	  
	  
Discourse	  on	  Black	  Girl’s	  Academic	  Success	  
Project	  Girl	  is	  the	  powerful	  account	  of	  a	  young	  woman’s	  struggle	  to	  realize	  her	  
dreams	  while	  remaining	  true	  to	  who	  she	  was	  and	  where	  she	  came	  from,	  before	  Ivy	  League	  
schools	  and	  impressive	  diplomas.	  It	  tells	  of	  the	  spectacular	  failures	  and	  unlikely	  comebacks	  
of	  a	  black	  girl	  whose	  academic	  talent	  opens	  doors	  onto	  a	  world	  of	  private	  schools,	  rich	  
classmates,	  and	  plum	  jobs	  but	  who	  back	  home	  confronts	  a	  neighborhood	  of	  growing	  
poverty,	  drug	  abuse,	  and	  crime.	  Project	  Girl	  is	  Janet	  McDonald’s	  memoir	  of	  her	  divided	  life	  
and	  terrible	  battle	  to	  reconcile	  two	  worlds.	  In	  the	  initial	  online	  discussion,	  I	  posted	  an	  
introductory	  status	  inviting	  participants	  to	  respond	  to	  a	  quote	  found	  on	  the	  first	  page	  of	  the	  
text	  Project	  Girl.	  During	  our	  PUSH	  online	  discussion	  participants	  very	  rarely	  revisited	  the	  
online	  group	  to	  respond	  to	  their	  peers	  or	  to	  respond	  to	  other	  questions	  after	  they	  posted	  

	  

139	  
their	  initial	  post,	  so	  I	  made	  certain	  to	  initiate	  the	  conversation	  with	  a	  more	  specific	  
introductory	  question	  (researcher	  journal,	  October	  1,	  2013)	  than	  I	  did	  with	  PUSH	  by	  
Sapphire.	  (In	  the	  opening	  paragraph	  of	  Project	  Girl,	  Janet	  mentions	  “I	  belong	  nowhere	  in	  
particular	  anymore	  and	  feel	  comfortable	  most	  everywhere”	  What	  does	  that	  mean	  to	  you?”)	  
(researcher	  journal,	  October	  1,	  2013).	  The	  conversation	  exchange	  below	  captures	  Keisha	  
and	  Denise’s	  conversation	  about	  the	  quote	  and	  Janet’s	  personal	  and	  professional	  life.	  
1Keisha	  She	  Means	  She	  Is	  Not	  in	  Her	  Usual	  Setting	  But	  She	  Feels	  Comfortable	  
2We're	  She	  is	  Now,	  She's	  Basically	  Living	  A	  Double	  Life	  She	  Involved	  in	  So	  
3Much	  Stuff	  The	  Requires	  You	  To	  Have	  High	  Intelligence	  &	  Back	  Home	  She	  is	  
4Involved	  in	  Poverty	  Stricken	  communities	  she	  use	  too.	  I	  can	  relate	  to	  Janet.	  
5Sometimes	  its	  challenging	  to	  be	  a	  teen.	  There	  are	  so	  many	  circles	  in	  school.	  I

Keisha	  interprets	  the	  quote	  as,	  Janet	  living	  a	  “double	  life”	  (line	  2).	  Keisha	  further	  

explains	  that	  Janet’s	  experiences	  as	  a	  black	  girl	  include	  living	  a	  “double	  life”	  made	  up	  of	  two	  
worlds:	  one	  world	  that	  requires	  her	  to	  have	  “high	  intelligence”	  and	  the	  other	  world	  that	  
involves	  “poverty	  stricken	  communities.”	  Keisha	  acknowledges	  the	  hegemonic	  ideologies	  
embedded	  in	  society	  and	  asserts	  that	  having	  “high	  intelligence”	  is	  associated	  with	  living	  in	  

	  

140	  
a	  more	  affluent	  community	  vastly	  different	  from	  the	  community	  Janet	  grew	  up	  in	  
(fieldnotes,	  October	  15,	  2013).	  Keisha	  also	  makes	  personal	  connections	  with	  the	  texts	  by	  
drawing	  upon	  her	  own	  experiences	  as	  a	  teenager	  living	  a	  double	  life.	  Through	  the	  use	  of	  
associating	  language	  (“Sometimes	  its	  challenging	  being	  a	  teen”),	  Keisha	  explains	  her	  
personal	  challenges	  as	  a	  teen	  and	  the	  pressures	  of	  choosing	  between	  her	  academics	  and	  
her	  social	  life	  (“There	  are	  so	  many	  circles	  in	  school.	  I	  hate	  the	  feeling	  I	  get	  when	  I’m	  forced	  to	  
choose	  between	  going	  to	  class	  and	  making	  good	  grades	  and	  being	  social	  and	  popular”).	  Like	  
Keisha,	  Denise	  acknowledged	  the	  constant	  conflict	  between	  both	  worlds	  and	  the	  isolation	  
that	  Janet	  experienced	  in	  both	  worlds.	  
In	  our	  second	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  meeting,	  Denise	  and	  Nicki	  continued	  the	  conversation	  on	  
black	  girl’s	  academic	  success.	  Drawing	  upon	  dominant	  narratives	  of	  academic	  success	  as	  
whiteness,	  Denise	  and	  Nicki	  named	  the	  racialized	  and	  gendered	  label	  that	  the	  community	  
used	  to	  describe	  Janet—a	  “white	  black	  girl.”	  In	  response	  to	  my	  question	  (“what	  is	  a	  white	  
black	  girl?)	  Denise	  asserted	  that	  Janet	  is	  viewed	  as	  a	  “white-­‐black	  girl”	  because	  she	  existed	  
in	  two	  worlds.	  Janet	  existed	  in	  one	  world	  that	  consisted	  of	  being	  from	  the	  hood	  and	  the	  
other	  world	  that	  consisted	  of	  going	  to	  college	  and	  receiving	  success	  and	  degrees.	  Visibly	  
annoyed	  by	  Janet’s	  circumstances,	  Denise	  shakes	  her	  head	  and	  sucks	  her	  teeth	  as	  she	  
further	  asserts	  that	  existing	  even	  partially	  in	  the	  world	  that	  consisted	  of	  going	  to	  college	  
and	  receiving	  success	  and	  degrees	  makes	  people	  feel	  that	  Janet	  thinks	  she	  is	  better	  than	  
everyone	  else.	  I	  make	  attempts	  to	  include	  both	  Keisha	  and	  Nicki	  in	  the	  conversation	  as	  
Denise	  takes	  and	  holds	  the	  floor	  (fieldnotes,	  October	  25,	  2013).	  Drawing	  upon	  dominant	  
narratives	  of	  whiteness,	  Keisha	  explains	  why	  Janet	  is	  labeled	  a	  “white-­‐black	  girl”	  and	  
mentions	  that	  it	  may	  have	  a	  lot	  to	  do	  with	  the	  way	  she	  speaks	  (lines	  11-­‐14).	  Keisha	  asserts	  

	  

141	  
that	  it	  is	  Janet’s	  use	  of	  Standard	  English,	  the	  dominant	  linguistic	  style	  that	  contributes	  to	  
her	  being	  named	  a	  “white-­‐black	  girl.”	  
	  
Summative	  Findings	  
	  

Meaning	  making	  and	  identity	  negotiation	  across	  contexts.	  Throughout	  this	  

study,	  participants	  constantly	  negotiated	  their	  identity,	  power,	  and	  positionally	  in	  both	  the	  
out-­‐of-­‐school	  digital	  space	  and	  the	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  space.	  The	  following	  details	  how	  school-­‐
based	  social	  norms	  were	  embedded	  in	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  spaces.	  Although	  this	  program	  was	  
situated	  within	  both	  an	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  digital	  space	  and	  a	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  space,	  participants	  
conformed	  to	  school-­‐based	  practices.	  Much	  like	  Janet	  in	  Project	  Girl,	  participants	  navigated	  
two	  sets	  of	  norms	  in	  this	  research	  study:	  dominant	  White	  ways	  of	  being	  and	  Black	  ways	  of	  
being.	  According	  to	  DuBois	  (1903)	  in	  a	  famous	  passage	  in	  The	  Souls	  of	  Black	  Folks,	  Dubois	  
stated:	  	  

	  

It	  is	  a	  peculiar	  sensation,	  this	  double	  consciousness,	  this	  sense	  of	  always	  looking	  at	  
one’s	  self	  through	  the	  eyes	  of	  others,	  of	  measuring	  one’s	  soul	  by	  the	  tape	  of	  a	  world	  
that	  looks	  on	  in	  amused	  contempt	  and	  pity.	  One	  ever	  feels	  his	  two-­‐ness—an	  
American,	  a	  Negro,	  two	  souls,	  two	  thoughts,	  two	  unreconciled	  strivings:	  two	  
warring	  ideals	  in	  one	  dark	  body.	  (p.	  3)	  
Linguistic	  tension	  was	  evident	  in	  participants’	  ways	  of	  being	  throughout	  this	  study.	  

In	  this	  study,	  dominant	  white	  ways	  of	  being	  entailed	  participating	  in	  the	  online	  Facebook	  
discussions	  using	  Standard	  English,	  while	  Afro-­‐cultural	  ways	  of	  being	  entailed	  participating	  
in	  the	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  focus	  group	  interviews	  through	  the	  use	  of	  African	  American	  Vernacular	  
English	  or	  what	  is	  also	  known	  as	  Black	  Dialect.	  Participants	  assumed	  a	  level	  of	  
consciousness	  and	  style	  shifted	  based	  on	  perceived	  norms	  within	  a	  particular	  context.	  In	  
instances	  in	  which	  participants	  employed	  features	  of	  African	  American	  Vernacular	  English	  

	  

142	  
in	  the	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  space,	  they	  often	  code-­‐meshed	  blending	  standard	  English	  
and	  African	  American	  Vernacular	  English.	  	  
According	  to	  Smitherman	  (1977),	  this	  “linguistic	  push	  and	  pull”	  was	  a	  clear	  
indication	  that	  participants	  constantly	  negotiate	  multiple	  identities—a	  dominant	  identity	  
and	  a	  local	  identity	  (p.	  129).	  According	  to	  Smitherman	  (2006),	  instances	  of	  style	  shifting	  
and	  code	  meshing	  are	  political	  and	  are	  indications	  of	  power	  and	  resistance	  among	  
participants.	  Several	  participants	  (Keisha,	  Lisa,	  Denise)	  revealed	  “we	  don’t	  use	  slang	  in	  the	  
online	  discussion	  because	  we	  don’t	  want	  to	  be	  disrespectful.”	  Their	  comment	  reveals	  that	  
they	  assumed	  a	  certain	  consciousness	  and	  took	  up	  certain	  ways	  of	  being	  based	  on	  social	  
norms	  (Richardson,	  2003).	  Hence,	  they	  sacrificed	  what	  Smitherman	  (2006)	  describes	  as	  
their	  mother	  tongue	  or	  their	  native	  dialect	  to	  navigate	  interactions	  in	  various	  contexts.	  
The	  “linguistic	  push	  and	  pull”	  revealed	  that	  participants’	  identities	  were	  without	  
boundaries	  and	  were	  fluid	  across	  and	  within	  particular	  contexts.	  Participants’	  push	  and	  
pull	  in	  this	  study	  can	  also	  be	  contributed	  to	  the	  form	  and	  function	  of	  oral	  speech	  and	  
writing.	  Although	  participants	  assumed	  a	  level	  of	  consciousness	  throughout	  this	  research	  
study,	  it	  is	  important	  to	  note	  that	  features	  of	  writing	  and	  orality	  aided	  in	  their	  ways	  of	  
being.	  	  The	  fact	  that	  participant	  responses	  in	  the	  online	  discussion	  were	  not	  time	  sensitive,	  
provided	  participants	  with	  the	  opportunity	  to	  tend	  to	  attend	  to	  spelling	  and	  grammar	  to	  
ensure	  that	  they	  conformed	  to	  conventions	  of	  Standard	  English.	  	  
The	  use	  of	  popular	  culture	  as	  a	  meaning	  making	  strategy.	  Participants	  made	  
meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts	  by	  drawing	  upon	  multimedia	  with	  a	  popular	  culture	  
focus.	  Participants	  drew	  upon	  the	  visual	  imagery	  in	  media,	  such	  as	  movies,	  song	  lyrics,	  and	  
television	  shows	  to	  make	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts.	  For	  example,	  black	  adolescent	  

	  

143	  
girls	  made	  meaning	  of	  texts	  by	  comparing	  and	  contrasting	  the	  text	  PUSH	  with	  the	  movie	  
Precious.	  The	  visual	  imagery	  in	  the	  movie	  Precious	  heightened	  black	  girls’	  meaning	  making	  
process	  and	  often	  resulted	  in	  a	  more	  transparent	  and	  emotional	  connection	  with	  
characters	  and	  storylines.	  	  
	  

The	  Black	  female	  condition	  as	  a	  reflection	  of	  self	  and	  others.	  Participants	  drew	  

upon	  representations	  of	  black	  girls	  in	  the	  media,	  as	  well	  their	  own	  lived	  experiences.	  
Participants	  often	  made	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts	  by	  analyzing	  the	  black	  female	  
condition	  in	  society	  and	  made	  parallels	  with	  the	  experiences	  of	  female	  protagonists	  in	  the	  
texts.	  They	  often	  made	  personal	  connections	  with	  female	  protagonists	  and	  their	  positions	  
based	  on	  being	  both	  black	  and	  female.	  Participants	  could	  relate	  personally	  to	  the	  
marginalization	  and	  “othering”	  that	  the	  female	  protagonists	  were	  confronted	  with.	  
Participants	  spoke	  back	  to	  hegemonic	  ideologies,	  social	  structures,	  social	  issues,	  and	  
community	  practices	  through	  the	  use	  of	  counternarratives.	  Counternarratives	  were	  used	  to	  
interrogate	  society	  and	  to	  dismantle	  media	  representations	  of	  black	  girls.	  Participants	  
assessed	  female	  protagonists	  and	  their	  circumstances	  based	  on	  the	  options	  available	  to	  
them	  to	  change	  their	  current	  circumstances.	  Participants	  often	  judged	  female	  protagonists	  
favorably	  even	  when	  they	  didn’t	  have	  redeeming	  qualities	  or	  promising	  circumstances.	  
Participants	  often	  drew	  upon	  their	  critical	  literacy	  skills	  and	  their	  feminist	  perspective	  to	  
analyze	  the	  female	  protagonist’s	  interpersonal	  relationships.	  This	  could	  have	  been	  
attributed	  to	  the	  fact	  that	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  associated	  the	  circumstances	  of	  the	  black	  
female	  protagonist	  as	  being	  influenced	  heavily	  by	  society	  and	  social	  structures	  designed	  to	  
mistreat	  and	  disregard	  the	  experiences	  of	  black	  girls	  and	  black	  women.	  This	  study	  revealed	  
that	  participants	  used	  the	  digital	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  space	  as	  an	  emancipatory,	  transformative	  

	  

144	  
space.	  Black	  adolescent	  girls	  used	  literacy	  through	  the	  use	  of	  digital	  technologies	  to	  provide	  
new	  types	  of	  counter	  storytelling	  that	  served	  as	  a	  source	  of	  empowerment.	  	  
Street	  literature	  texts	  and	  socio-­‐emotional	  well-­‐being.	  There	  were	  several	  key	  
features	  of	  street	  literature	  texts	  that	  contributed	  to	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  emotional	  
connection	  to	  the	  sub-­‐genre.	  Participants	  often	  empathized	  with	  female	  protagonists	  and	  
had	  heightened	  emotional	  connections	  to	  them.	  Although	  participants	  connected	  to	  each	  
text	  in	  different	  ways,	  based	  on	  the	  findings	  in	  this	  study,	  participants	  displayed	  a	  
heightened	  emotional	  connection	  to	  PUSH	  by	  Sapphire.	  Several	  participants	  mentioned	  that	  
the	  texts	  captured	  their	  interest	  because	  it	  depicted	  real	  life	  experiences,	  created	  an	  
intimate	  feeling	  for	  the	  reader	  through	  the	  use	  of	  first-­‐person	  female	  narration,	  and	  served	  
as	  narratives	  to	  their	  own	  lived	  experiences	  as	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  confronting	  social	  
issues.	  Many	  participants	  also	  felt	  that	  the	  use	  of	  nonstandard	  English	  made	  it	  seem	  
“realistic.”	  This	  heightened	  emotional	  connection	  often	  resulted	  in	  participants	  using	  the	  
texts	  as	  their	  identity	  by	  merging	  themselves	  with	  the	  texts	  (Sipe,	  2008).	  Their	  emotional	  
connections	  to	  street	  literature	  texts	  also	  served	  as	  therapeutic	  tool,	  aiding	  black	  
adolescent	  girls	  to	  recall	  and	  confront	  the	  challenges	  that	  they	  were	  experiencing	  in	  their	  
own	  lives.	  	  
	  

	  

145	  
Chapter	  Six	  
Looking	  Inward:	  	  
Researcher’s	  Reflexive	  Approach	  to	  Book	  Club	  Facilitation	  
“As	  a	  researcher,	  facilitator,	  and	  participant,	  my	  multiple	  identities	  were	  fluid	  at	  certain	  
points,	  which	  made	  navigating	  a	  challenge	  at	  times”-­‐	  	  
Delicia	  (Researcher	  Journal,	  December	  4,	  2013)	  
	  
Introduction	  
	  

Over	  the	  course	  of	  this	  study,	  I	  served	  as	  a	  book	  club	  researcher,	  facilitator,	  and	  

participant.	  Situated	  within	  both	  an	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  digital	  and	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  space,	  this	  study	  
was	  designed	  to	  create	  an	  inclusive	  literacy	  environment	  for	  participants.	  In	  doing	  so,	  the	  
focus	  was	  to	  create	  an	  environment	  that	  offered	  participants	  freedom	  of	  self-­‐expression	  
and	  autonomy.	  By	  employing	  a	  Critical	  Discourse	  Framework,	  I	  confronted	  my	  power,	  
privilege,	  and	  positionality,	  which	  provided	  the	  opportunity	  to	  examine	  my	  multiple	  roles.	  
My	  social	  location	  was	  informed	  by	  a	  series	  of	  personal	  and	  professional	  experiences.	  
Drawing	  upon	  an	  interpretive,	  naturalistic	  inquiry,	  within	  the	  ethnographic	  tradition,	  
Denzin	  and	  Lincoln	  (1994)	  argue	  that	  “behind	  these	  terms	  [theory,	  method,	  analysis,	  
ontology,	  and	  epistemology]	  stands	  the	  biography”	  of	  the	  researcher	  who	  speaks	  from	  a	  
particular	  social	  location	  (p.1).	  By	  employing	  a	  black	  feminist	  epistemology,	  I	  examined	  
how	  my	  social	  location	  was	  informed	  by	  the	  intersectionality	  of	  raced	  and	  gendered	  
identities.	  	  
My	  social	  location	  in	  this	  qualitative	  study	  was	  informed	  by	  multiple	  identities,	  
including	  my	  personal	  experiences	  as	  a	  black	  adolescent	  girl,	  a	  black	  woman,	  reader	  of	  

	  

146	  
street	  literature	  texts,	  member	  of	  the	  hip-­‐hop	  culture,	  and	  a	  speaker	  of	  African	  American	  
Vernacular	  English	  (AAVE),	  as	  well	  as	  my	  professional	  experiences	  as	  a	  school	  library	  
media	  specialist	  and	  an	  English	  educator	  working	  with	  adolescents	  in	  predominantly	  black	  
communities.	  As	  a	  black	  adolescent	  girl,	  I	  was	  a	  member	  of	  a	  marginalized	  urban	  youth	  
culture	  that	  read	  texts	  by	  a	  number	  of	  authors	  ranging	  from	  Virginia	  Hamilton	  and	  Mildred	  
Taylor	  to	  Donald	  Goines	  and	  Chester	  Himes.	  	  Although	  my	  reading	  interests	  were	  vast,	  I	  
was	  drawn	  to	  texts	  that	  focused	  mostly	  on	  societal	  issues,	  including	  race,	  class,	  and	  culture.	  
Much	  of	  my	  interest	  in	  texts	  that	  focused	  on	  societal	  issues	  stemmed	  from	  my	  personal	  
experiences	  born	  and	  raised	  in	  the	  Bronx,	  New	  York	  and	  witnessing	  injustice	  ranging	  from	  
police	  brutality	  to	  inequities	  within	  schools	  (researcher	  journal,	  November	  15,	  2013).	  Texts	  
that	  focused	  on	  social	  justice	  issues	  helped	  me	  make	  sense	  of	  the	  world	  and	  my	  place	  in	  it	  
as	  a	  black	  girl.	  	  
As	  a	  black	  woman	  who	  has	  often	  been	  marginalized	  based	  on	  my	  femaleness	  and	  
blackness,	  my	  scholarship	  serves	  as	  a	  social	  justice	  tool	  allowing	  me	  to	  bring	  visibility	  to
