the	  injustices	  that	  black	  girls	  experience	  in	  their	  lives,	  as	  well	  as	  create	  a	  safe	  space	  for	  
their	  voices	  to	  be	  heard.	  Although	  I	  was	  born	  and	  raised	  in	  similar	  communities	  as	  my	  
participants,	  we	  draw	  from	  different	  experiences	  of	  marginalization	  due	  to	  generational	  
gaps	  (researcher	  journal,	  November	  15,	  2013).	  My	  social	  location	  was	  also	  informed	  by	  
professional	  experiences	  as	  a	  school	  librarian	  in	  a	  middle	  school,	  young	  adult	  public	  
librarian	  in	  local	  urban	  communities,	  and	  community-­‐engaged	  and	  activist	  driven	  scholar	  
in	  urban	  communities.	  Each	  professional	  experience	  provided	  vastly	  different	  perspectives	  
on	  literacy	  and	  learning	  across	  environments.	  Working	  as	  young	  adult	  librarian,	  there	  were	  
no	  boundaries	  around	  literacy	  and	  student-­‐centered	  environments	  were	  encouraged,	  while	  

	  

147	  
working	  as	  a	  school	  library	  media	  specialist,	  literacy	  had	  clear	  boundaries	  that	  included	  
mandated	  reading	  lists	  that	  consisted	  of	  predominantly	  canonical	  texts	  and	  scripted	  
programs	  that	  encouraged	  teacher-­‐centered	  learning	  environments	  (researcher	  journal,	  
November	  28,	  2013).	  As	  an	  activist	  driven	  scholar,	  I	  continue	  to	  work	  with	  libraries	  and	  
secondary	  schools	  in	  urban	  communities	  and	  engage	  in	  scholarship	  in	  action	  that	  serves	  
diverse	  populations.	  I	  bring	  to	  my	  scholarship	  and	  this	  research	  study	  intersecting	  
identities	  that	  have	  provided	  me	  with	  a	  range	  of	  lenses	  by	  which	  to	  share	  the	  literate	  lives	  
of	  black	  adolescent	  girls.	  Each	  lens	  is	  varied	  and	  provides	  rich	  perspectives	  and	  intimate	  
accounts	  of	  their	  experiences.	  Drawing	  upon	  a	  Critical	  Discourse	  Framework,	  I	  turn	  the	  
lens	  on	  myself	  as	  a	  means	  of	  balancing	  my	  biases	  and	  accounting	  for	  how	  my	  positionality	  
functions	  in	  my	  role	  as	  facilitator,	  participant,	  and	  researcher	  and	  in	  my	  interaction	  with	  
participants	  within	  and	  across	  contexts.	  
This	  chapter	  examines	  my	  role	  as	  a	  researcher,	  facilitator,	  and	  participant	  of	  an	  
online	  street	  literature	  book	  club	  and	  how	  my	  positionality	  influenced	  participants’	  
meaning	  making	  of	  street	  literature	  texts.	  In	  this	  chapter,	  orders	  of	  discourse	  (discourse,	  
style,	  and	  genre)	  were	  embedded	  over	  the	  course	  of	  my	  participation	  in	  this	  study.	  I	  
employed	  an	  auto-­‐ethnographic	  account	  of	  my	  personal	  and	  professional	  experiences	  
through	  the	  use	  of	  a	  researcher	  journal	  and	  validity	  and	  reliability	  approaches	  (see	  Chapter	  
3).	  Through	  the	  use	  of	  a	  researcher	  journal,	  I	  detailed	  how	  I	  negotiated	  my	  power,	  
privilege,	  and	  identity.	  In	  doing	  so	  I	  examined	  my	  role	  as	  a	  researcher	  and	  facilitation	  based	  
on	  my	  type	  of	  talk	  and	  participants’	  responses	  to	  my	  type	  of	  talk	  in	  our	  conversation	  
exchanges.	  In	  this	  chapter	  the	  following	  questions	  guided	  my	  analysis:	  How	  might	  my	  
multiple	  identities	  play	  into	  the	  type	  of	  talk	  I	  employ?	  How	  might	  the	  generational	  

	  

148	  
differences	  on	  critical	  social	  issues	  between	  myself	  and	  my	  participants	  influence	  their	  
meaning	  making	  process?	  In	  what	  ways	  do	  I	  conform	  to	  or	  challenge	  power	  dynamics	  
present	  during	  the	  facilitation/meaning	  making	  process?	  How	  might	  educators	  use	  
facilitation	  and	  type	  of	  talk	  to	  create	  critically	  engaged	  student-­‐centered	  spaces	  within	  
school	  spaces?	  	  
Book	  Club	  Facilitation	  and	  Models	  of	  Education	  
Paulo	  Freire,	  the	  Brazilian	  educationalist	  has	  focused	  his	  work	  on	  progressive	  
education	  practices	  and	  has	  impacted	  the	  development	  of	  liberatory	  education	  practices.	  
Freire	  (2001)	  identifies	  two	  distinct,	  yet	  conflicting	  education	  schools	  of	  thought,	  the	  
banking	  system	  and	  the	  problem-­‐posing	  system.	  In	  the	  banking	  system,	  “education	  
becomes	  an	  act	  of	  depositing,	  in	  which	  students	  are	  the	  depositories	  and	  the	  teacher	  is	  the	  
depositor”	  (p.	  2).	  In	  the	  banking	  system,	  students	  are	  not	  taught	  to	  think	  critically	  but	  
instead	  are	  encouraged	  to	  fit	  into	  the	  world	  as	  it	  exists.	  In	  doing	  so,	  it	  separates	  the	  learner	  
and	  the	  learner’s	  critical	  consciousness	  from	  the	  world,	  which	  contributes	  to	  oppression	  
(Freire,	  2001).	  This	  system	  creates	  a	  “cookie-­‐cutter”	  approach	  to	  learning	  and	  facilitation	  
and	  negates	  the	  individuality	  of	  students	  and	  the	  unique	  learning	  style	  that	  they	  bring	  to	  
learning	  spaces.	  The	  banking	  method	  of	  education	  creates	  a	  binary	  where	  the	  teacher	  has	  
an	  essential	  role	  in	  the	  students’	  education,	  which	  then	  justifies	  the	  position	  of	  the	  teacher.	  
The	  roles	  of	  the	  student	  and	  teacher	  fall	  into	  an	  interplay	  that	  reflects	  the	  colonialistic	  and	  
oppressive	  nature	  of	  our	  society.	  	  Education,	  as	  the	  practice	  of	  freedom,	  must	  begin	  by	  
breaking	  the	  conventional	  roles	  of	  the	  student	  and	  teacher	  (oppressed	  /oppressor)	  (Freire,	  
2001).	  

	  

149	  
Freire’s	  idea	  of	  liberatory	  education	  makes	  both	  learners	  and	  teachers	  of	  us	  all.	  
Liberatory	  education	  works	  against	  the	  alienation	  of	  the	  student	  and	  changes	  the	  role	  of	  
the	  student	  by	  fostering	  a	  setting	  that	  empowers	  participants	  to	  make	  their	  own	  decisions	  
and	  challenge	  the	  world	  (Freire,	  2001).	  	  In	  problem-­‐posing,	  students	  and	  teachers	  carry	  on	  
a	  dialogue	  to	  teach	  one	  another.	  The	  world	  is	  seen	  as	  always	  in	  the	  process	  of	  becoming	  
and	  students	  are	  seen	  as	  part	  of	  that.	  Students	  are	  therefore	  actively	  becoming	  empowered	  
to	  criticize	  the	  world.	  Problem-­‐posing	  thereby	  results	  in	  the	  liberation	  of	  the	  students	  and	  
in	  the	  revolution	  against	  oppressive	  social	  systems.	  “Problem-­‐posing	  education	  allows	  
people	  to	  develop	  their	  human	  natures	  fully	  because	  it	  depends	  on	  dialogue,	  recognizes	  the	  
relationship	  between	  people	  and	  the	  world,	  and	  encourages	  inquiry”	  (Freire,	  2001,	  p.	  2).	  
Freire’s	  problem	  posing	  education	  is	  based	  on	  a	  democratic	  relationship	  between	  teacher	  
and	  student.	  
	  
Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis:	  Orders	  of	  Discourse	  Across	  Situations	  	  
In	  this	  chapter,	  I	  draw	  upon	  Fairclough’s	  (1989)	  and	  Roger’s	  (2003)	  three	  tired	  
Discourse	  analytical	  framework,	  across	  three	  levels	  of	  interacting	  or	  orders	  of	  discourse:	  
genre,	  discourse,	  and	  style).	  In	  this	  chapter,	  the	  situational	  context	  represented	  my	  role	  as	  
a	  researcher	  and	  facilitator	  and	  how	  those	  multiple	  identities	  influenced	  how	  participants	  
made	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts.	  By	  focusing	  on	  the	  situational	  context,	  I	  
acknowledge	  that	  power,	  privilege,	  and	  positionality	  are	  embedded	  in	  each	  interaction,	  
both	  as	  researcher/facilitator	  as	  well	  as	  participant	  to	  participant.	  Employing	  a	  critical	  
discourse	  analysis	  framing	  allowed	  me	  to	  take	  a	  reflexive	  approach	  and	  turn	  the	  critical	  
lens	  on	  myself	  as	  a	  researcher,	  in	  order	  to	  examine	  how	  my	  position	  of	  power	  within	  the	  

	  

150	  
book	  club	  space	  influenced	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  participants	  made	  meaning	  of	  street	  
literature	  texts.	  In	  this	  chapter,	  Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis	  accounts	  for	  the	  inherent	  power	  
that	  I	  assume	  as	  both	  the	  researcher	  and	  the	  facilitator.	  Critical	  Discourse	  combined	  genre,	  
discourse,	  and	  style	  with	  discursive	  theories	  of	  race,	  gender,	  new	  literacies,	  transactional	  
acts	  of	  reading	  to	  guide	  my	  reflexive	  approach	  to	  my	  involvement	  and	  participation	  in	  the	  
book	  club.	  	  
Chouliaraki	  and	  Fairclough	  (1999)	  define	  genre	  as	  the	  “sort	  of	  language	  tied	  to	  a	  
particular	  social	  activity.”	  Roger	  and	  Wetzel	  (2014)	  describe	  genres	  as	  “ways	  of	  
interacting”	  and	  represent	  the	  organizational	  components	  of	  interacting	  (p.	  72).	  In	  this	  
chapter,	  genre	  attended	  to	  the	  use	  of	  linguistic	  devices	  in	  participants’	  talk	  in	  response	  to	  
my	  talk	  (Chouliaraki	  &	  Fairclough,	  1999)	  	  (turn	  taking,	  overlapping,	  interruption,	  status	  &	  
post	  likes).	  Discourse	  are	  “ways	  of	  representing”	  and	  describe	  how	  knowledge	  is	  
represented	  and	  from	  what	  perspective.	  In	  thus	  chapter,	  I	  drew	  upon	  Luke’s	  definition	  
(2000)	  of	  discourse	  “systematic	  clusters	  of	  themes,	  statements,	  ideas,	  and	  ideologies”	  (p.	  
456).	  Discourse	  represented	  how	  my	  ideologies	  (Luke,	  2000;	  Sipe,	  2000),	  or	  social	  
positioning	  (Bucholtz	  and	  Hall,	  2005)	  influenced	  how	  participants	  made	  meaning	  of	  street	  
literature	  texts.	  Styles	  are	  “ways	  of	  being”	  and	  represent	  interpersonal	  choice,	  specifically	  
my	  own	  position	  taking	  (Rogers,	  2003).	  In	  this	  study,	  style	  focused	  predominantly	  on	  my	  
type	  of	  talk	  as	  a	  facilitator,	  researcher,	  and	  participant.	  
	  

In	  this	  chapter,	  order	  of	  discourse—genre,	  discourse,	  and	  style	  provided	  an	  analysis	  

of	  my	  role	  as	  a	  researcher	  and	  facilitator	  and	  its	  influence	  on	  meaning	  making.	  I	  detailed	  
the	  orders	  of	  discourse	  by	  employing	  discursive	  theories	  of	  Hip	  Hop	  Feminism,	  New	  
Literacy	  Studies,	  and	  Reader	  Response	  Theory	  that	  represented	  my	  type	  of	  talk	  using	  two	  

	  

151	  
street	  literature	  text	  Coldest	  Winter	  Ever	  by	  Sister	  Souljah	  and	  Flyy	  Girl	  by	  Omar	  Tyree.	  
Chapter	  6	  turns	  the	  analytic	  lens	  on	  myself	  and	  showcases	  my	  multiple	  identities.	  Since	  
each	  street	  literature	  texts	  selected	  for	  this	  study	  focused	  on	  a	  particular	  social	  issue	  that	  
the	  female	  protagonist	  was	  confronted	  with,	  the	  findings	  are	  organized	  thematically.	  
Coldest	  Winter	  Ever	  detailed	  discourse	  on	  Black	  Girls’	  Sexuality	  while	  Flyy	  Girl	  detailed	  
discourse	  on	  Black	  Masculinity.	  
	  

At	  the	  beginning	  of	  the	  program,	  it	  was	  evident	  that	  participants	  were	  often	  hesitant	  

to	  initiate	  and	  engage	  in	  conversation	  both	  online	  and	  in	  the	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  space.	  
Participants	  often	  relied	  on	  my	  role	  as	  the	  facilitator	  to	  sustain	  the	  book	  club	  discussions	  
(Fieldnotes,	  October	  5,	  2013).	  	  Despite	  the	  fact	  that	  the	  program	  was	  situated	  within	  an	  
out-­‐of-­‐school	  space,	  their	  reactions	  to	  a	  student-­‐centered	  space	  that	  promoted	  self-­‐
expression	  and	  autonomy	  indicated	  that	  they	  were	  accustomed	  to	  literacy	  environments	  
that	  resembled	  those	  in	  school	  spaces.	  Coldest	  Winter	  Ever	  by	  Sister	  Souljah	  and	  Flyy	  Girl	  by	  
Omar	  Tyree	  were	  the	  last	  street	  literature	  texts	  that	  participants	  read	  in	  the	  program.	  
	  
Discourse	  on	  Black	  Masculinity	  
The	  street	  literature	  text	  Flyy	  Girl	  focuses	  on	  the	  life	  of	  Tracy	  Ellison,	  a	  young	  black	  
girl	  who	  enjoys	  living	  life	  in	  the	  fast	  lane.	  Tracy	  was	  driven	  by	  physical	  possessions	  and	  she	  
and	  her	  friends	  would	  do	  anything	  to	  maintain	  their	  lifestyle.	  Tracy	  experiences	  life	  
challenges	  that	  force	  her	  to	  reflect	  on	  her	  life,	  her	  goals,	  her	  sexuality,	  and	  her	  friendships.	  
The	  initial	  online	  Facebook	  prompt	  focused	  on	  black	  female	  relationships	  (“Who	  is	  Tracy	  
Ellison?	  How	  might	  you	  describe	  her	  female	  relationships?	  How	  are	  female	  relationships	  
represented	  in	  this	  story?”).	  The	  initial	  facilitation	  prompt	  was	  designed	  to	  focus	  on	  how	  

	  

152	  
Tracy	  navigated	  and	  found	  her	  place	  in	  the	  world	  through	  the	  support	  of	  her	  female	  
relationships	  with	  Raheema	  and	  Mercedes	  (researcher	  journal,	  November	  30,	  2013).	  The	  
conversation	  exchange	  below	  highlights	  participants’	  topic	  shift	  and	  their	  perspectives	  on	  
black	  masculinity.	  	  
1Delicia:	  Who	  is	  Tracy	  Ellison?	  How	  might	  you	  describe	  her	  female	  
2relationships?	  How	  are	  female	  relationships	  represented	  in	  this	  story?	  
3Denise:	  I	  think	  Omar	  Tyree	  is	  a	  little	  bit	  fruity.	  What	  type	  of	  real	  masculine	  
4man	  would	  write	  a	  book	  about	  a	  lot	  of	  girls	  out	  here	  being	  fast:	  Excuse	  ME	  
5that’s	  my	  personal	  opinion	  
6Nicki:	  Yeah	  I	  agree	  with	  Denise.	  Omar	  Tyree	  is	  type	  fruity	  
Flyy	  Girl	  by	  Omar	  Tyree	  was	  the	  third	  street	  literature	  text	  that	  the	  group	  read.	  In	  
the	  first	  two	  online	  discussions,	  participants	  displayed	  topic	  maintenance	  based	  on	  my	  
initial	  facilitation	  prompt;	  however,	  in	  the	  second	  half	  of	  the	  program	  (last	  two	  street	  
literature	  readings	  and	  discussions),	  participants	  displayed	  topic	  shifts	  and	  more	  instances	  
of	  participant-­‐centered	  conversations.	  	  In	  lines	  1-­‐2,	  the	  facilitation	  prompt	  was	  designed	  to	  
focus	  on	  black	  female	  relationships	  through	  the	  experiences	  of	  Tracy	  and	  Raheema.	  Denise	  
shifts	  the	  focus	  of	  the	  discussion	  from	  black	  female	  relationships	  to	  black	  masculinity	  when	  
she	  critically	  analyzes	  the	  author’s	  masculinity	  based	  on	  his	  development	  of	  female	  
characters	  in	  the	  text	  (researcher	  journal,	  November	  30,	  2013).	  Denise	  describes	  the	  
author	  as	  “fruity,”	  a	  contemporary	  term	  often	  used	  in	  hip-­‐hop	  culture	  among	  youth	  to	  
describe	  a	  man	  that	  is	  gay.	  In	  lines	  3-­‐5,	  Denise	  conforms	  to	  patriarchal	  perspectives	  of	  
masculinity	  by	  qualifying	  masculinity	  through	  her	  use	  of	  the	  term	  “real	  masculine	  man.”	  By	  
doing	  so,	  she	  creates	  a	  hierarchy	  of	  masculinity.	  Denise	  asserts	  that	  Tyree	  focuses	  on	  black	  

	  

153	  
female	  sexuality	  in	  his	  development	  of	  the	  black	  female	  characters	  in	  the	  storyline.	  Denise	  
bases	  her	  assessment	  of	  Tyree’s	  masculinity	  on	  his	  development	  of	  female	  characters	  in	  the	  
text.	  Denise	  asserts	  that	  Tyree	  creates	  life	  experiences	  of	  girls	  being	  “fast,”	  a	  term	  often	  
used	  to	  describe	  girls’	  loose	  and	  immoral	  behavior.	  She	  seemed	  aware	  that	  the	  online	  
discussion	  group	  might	  not	  agree	  with	  her	  perspectives	  on	  Tyree	  and	  she	  re-­‐writes	  her	  
perspective	  into	  the	  conversation	  through	  active	  language	  (“excuse	  ME	  that’s	  my	  personal	  
opinion”).	  By	  agreeing	  with	  Denise,	  Nicki	  establishes	  solidarity	  with	  her.	  
As	  both	  a	  researcher	  and	  facilitator	  of	  the	  online	  discussion	  group,	  my	  focus	  was	  for	  
participants	  to	  take	  ownership	  of	  the	  online	  space	  and	  engage	  in	  discussions	  in	  a	  
participant-­‐driven	  environment.	  My	  initial	  facilitation	  prompt	  was	  designed	  to	  focus	  on	  
black	  female	  relationships;	  however,	  Denise’s	  topic	  shift	  focusing	  on	  Tyree	  and	  black	  
masculinity	  were	  indications	  of	  a	  participant-­‐driven	  environment.	  As	  both	  the	  researcher	  
and	  facilitator,	  I	  chose	  to	  not	  impose	  topic	  maintenance,	  so	  that	  issues	  that	  participants	  
were	  most	  passionate	  about	  and	  most	  connected	  to	  could	  be	  discussed	  in	  the	  online	  group	  
(researcher	  journal,	  November	  30,	  2013).	  By	  doing	  so,	  I	  suppressed	  the	  power	  that	  I	  
inherently	  assumed	  as	  both	  researcher	  and	  facilitator.	  In	  this	  instance,	  the	  topic	  shift	  led	  to	  
one	  of	  the	  most	  fruitful	  and	  organic	  conversations	  among	  participants	  over	  the	  course	  of	  
the	  program	  (researcher	  journal,	  November	  2013).	  
In	  further	  conversation	  exchanges	  on	  the	  same	  thread,	  Keisha	  and	  I	  discussed	  our	  
perspectives	  on	  Tyree’s	  portrayal	  of	  black	  girls	  in	  the	  text	  in	  relation	  to	  his	  masculinity.	  	  
6	  Keisha	  @Denise	  and	  @Nicki	  I	  respect	  everyone’s	  comment,	  but	  I	  don’t	  
7think	  Omar	  is	  fruity.	  Not	  every	  man	  that	  tells	  our	  story	  is	  fruity.	  He	  may	  

	  

154	  
8have	  grown	  up	  with	  a	  lot	  of	  aunts	  and	  sisters	  and	  that	  may	  have	  givin	  him	  a	  
9view	  of	  girls	  that	  he	  writes	  about	  
10Delicia:	  I	  don’t	  think	  Omar	  Tyree	  writing	  about	  girls	  is	  an	  indication	  of	  
11him	  being	  masculine.	  The	  characters	  that	  Omar	  developed	  could	  have	  
12been	  inspired	  by	  people	  he	  knew	  growing	  up	  
Keisha	  directed	  her	  online	  discussion	  post	  to	  both	  Denise	  and	  Nicki	  and	  respectfully	  
disagreed	  with	  their	  perspective.	  Keisha	  and	  I	  agreed	  that	  Tyree’s	  development	  of	  black	  
girls	  in	  his	  story	  was	  not	  an	  indication	  of	  his	  masculinity.	  We	  both	  attributed	  his	  interest	  
and	  creative	  ability	  to	  develop	  black	  girls	  in	  the	  texts	  to	  his	  own	  upbringing	  surrounded	  by	  
aunts,	  sisters,	  and	  other	  mothers	  that	  inspired	  his	  work	  (fieldnotes,	  December	  2,	  2013).	  In	  
lines	  10-­‐12,	  I	  re-­‐position	  myself	  within	  the	  space	  and	  assume	  the	  role	  of	  a	  participant	  and	  
detailed	  my	  own	  personal	  perspectives	  on	  Tyree	  and	  masculinity.	  To	  ensure	  that	  
participants	  expressed	  their	  perspectives	  with	  limited	  influence,	  I	  intentionally	  posted	  to	  
the	  online	  discussion	  last	  (researcher	  journal,	  December	  2,	  2013).	  As	  a	  researcher,	  
facilitator,	  and	  participant,	  my	  multiple	  identities	  were	  fluid	  at	  certain	  points,	  which	  made	  
navigating	  a	  challenge	  at	  times	  (researcher	  journal,	  December	  4,	  2013).	  
In	  the	  face-­‐face	  discussion,	  the	  participants	  continued	  the	  conversation	  we	  started	  
in	  the	  online	  discussion	  about	  black	  male	  author,	  Omar	  Tyree’s	  masculinity,	  and	  his	  ability	  
to	  develop	  black	  female	  characters	  that	  accurately	  depicted	  the	  black	  female	  experience.	  
(“Let’s	  continue	  the	  conversation	  that	  we	  started	  about	  the	  black	  male	  author	  Omar	  Tyree	  
writing	  about	  the	  experiences	  of	  black	  girls”).	  In	  the	  focus	  group,	  Denise,	  Lisa,	  Tammy,	  and	  
Keisha	  discussed	  their	  views	  on	  Omar	  Tyree,	  his	  perspective	  on	  black	  girls,	  and	  life	  

	  

155	  
experiences	  that	  may	  have	  shaped	  the	  development	  of	  female	  characters	  in	  the	  story.	  
Below	  is	  the	  conversation	  exchange	  between	  Denise,	  Lisa,	  Tammy,	  Keisha,	  and	  myself:	  	  
1Delicia:	  Let’s	  continue	  the	  conversation	  that	  we	  started	  about	  the	  black	  
2male	  author	  Omar	  Tyree	  writing	  about	  the	  experiences	  of	  black	  girls	  
3Denise:	  It’s	  probably	  not	  likely	  for	  a	  man	  to	  write	  that	  type	  of	  book.	  I	  just	  
4found	  it	  weird.	  I	  don’t	  think	  he	  accurately	  portrayed	  black	  girls	  in	  the	  book.	  
5He	  did	  to	  an	  extent	  but	  at	  the	  same	  time	  he	  did	  not.	  Well	  they	  did	  live	  in	  a	  
6good	  neighborhood	  and	  they	  wanted	  to	  get	  a	  good	  education.	  But	  sneaking	  
7out	  of	  the	  house	  and	  going	  out	  to	  boys’	  houses.	  Not	  every	  black	  girl	  wants	  
8to	  do	  that	  and	  like	  not	  every	  black	  girl	  has	  their	  priorities	  there,	  but	  I	  think	  
9he	  portrayed	  them	  as	  best	  as	  he	  knew	  how	  but	  not	  necessarily	  portrayed	  
10them	  well	  
In	  support	  of	  her	  original	  position	  in	  the	  online	  discussion,	  Denise	  asserts	  in	  the	  
face-­‐face	  space	  that	  Omar	  did	  not	  accurately	  portray	  black	  women	  in	  the	  text	  Flyy	  Girl.	  	  
Denise’s	  perspectives	  on	  Tyree’s	  development	  of	  female	  characters	  are	  based	  on	  
stereotypical	  images	  of	  black	  girlhood	  (“Like	  sneaking	  out	  of	  the	  house	  and	  going	  out	  to	  
boys’	  houses,	  not	  every	  black	  girl	  wants	  to	  do	  that	  and	  like	  not	  every	  black	  girls	  have	  there	  
priorities	  there”).	  She	  highlights	  the	  positive	  aspects	  in	  the	  text	  to	  justify	  that	  Tyree	  
portrayed	  black	  girls	  and	  the	  black	  family	  accurately	  in	  some	  ways	  (“I	  do	  agree	  with	  how	  he	  
made	  Tracy’s	  motha	  and	  fatha	  together	  becuz	  you	  don’t	  see	  a	  motha	  and	  fatha	  in	  a	  home	  
often”).	  In	  instances	  in	  which	  Tyree’s	  female	  characters	  fall	  short	  of	  portraying	  positive	  
images,	  she	  attributes	  that	  to	  Tyree’s	  limited	  knowledge	  of	  black	  girlhood	  because	  he	  is	  a	  

	  

156	  
black	  male	  (fieldnotes,	  December	  3,	  2013).	  In	  my	  role	  as	  researcher	  and	  facilitator,	  I	  
continued	  the	  conversation	  with	  Denise	  around	  black	  men’s	  representations	  of	  black	  girls.	  
11Delicia:	  (repositions	  in	  seat)	  So	  were	  you	  expecting	  the	  female	  characters	  
12to	  be	  represented	  differently	  since	  Omar	  Tyree	  is	  a	  black	  male	  author?	  
13Denise:	  Umm	  well	  I	  do	  agree	  with	  how	  he	  made	  Tracy’s	  motha	  and	  fatha	  
14together	  becuz	  you	  don’t	  often	  see	  a	  motha	  and	  fatha	  in	  a	  home.	  SO	  I	  think	  
15that	  meant	  black	  marriage	  as	  a	  whole	  is	  still	  possible.	  But	  I	  don’t	  agree	  
16with	  how	  he	  made	  her	  parents	  marriage	  dysfunctional	  
In	  the	  conversation	  exchange	  above,	  I	  posed	  a	  probing	  question	  to	  Denise	  (So	  were	  
you	  expecting	  the	  female	  characters	  to	  be	  represented	  differently	  since	  Omar	  Tyree	  is	  a	  black	  
male	  author?”).	  	  As	  both	  facilitator	  and	  researcher,	  I	  connected	  Denise’s	  response	  to	  
another	  response	  by	  pointing	  out	  how	  it	  extended	  and	  amplified	  what	  she	  had	  previously	  
said	  (Sipe,	  2008).	  By	  posing	  a	  probing	  question,	  I	  attempt	  to	  ensure	  where	  Denise’s	  
perspectives	  lie	  on	  Tyree’s	  representation	  of	  black	  girlhood	  (Sipe,	  2008).	  In	  lines	  13-­‐16,	  
Denise	  doesn’t	  directly	  answer	  my	  question	  but	  attempts	  to	  justify	  her	  initial	  perspective.	  
Denise	  undermines	  her	  own	  authority	  and	  shifts	  her	  perspective	  to	  conform	  to	  my	  line	  of	  
questioning	  (fieldnotes,	  December	  2,	  2013).	  Employing	  African	  American	  Vernacular	  
English	  (AAVE),	  Denise	  highlights	  Tyree’s	  representation	  of	  the	  black	  family	  as	  a	  two-­‐
family	  household,	  however	  disagreed	  with	  elements	  of	  dysfunction	  that	  existed	  in	  his	  
portrayal	  of	  black	  marriage.	  My	  conversation	  exchanges	  with	  Denise	  reveals	  that	  my	  line	  of	  
questioning	  shifted.	  	  
Lisa,	  Tammy,	  and	  Keisha	  disagree	  with	  Denise	  and	  discuss	  their	  own	  perspectives	  
on	  what	  inspired	  Omar	  to	  portray	  the	  black	  girl	  experience.	  Drawing	  upon	  their	  responses	  

	  

157	  
in	  the	  online	  discussion,	  Lisa	  and	  Tammy	  argue	  that	  Tyree’s	  characters	  were	  inspired	  by	  
his	  experiences	  with	  female	  friends	  and	  family	  members.	  Tammy	  challenges	  Denise’s	  
perspective	  and	  argues	  that	  Tyree,	  unlike	  some	  men,	  is	  comfortable	  with	  his	  feminine	  side.	  
Tammy	  associates	  Tyree’s	  ability	  to	  develop	  characters	  as	  being	  part	  of	  his	  “feminine	  side”	  
one	  of	  many	  identities	  that	  make	  him	  who	  he	  is	  as	  a	  black	  male	  author.	  Tammy	  does	  not	  
correlate	  “feminine	  side”	  with	  sexuality,	  but	  instead	  uses	  it	  as	  a	  way	  to	  assert	  that	  Tyree	  is	  
sensitive	  to	  the	  experiences	  of	  black	  girls.	  Keisha	  asserts	  that	  Omar’s	  masculinity	  does	  not	  
influence	  how	  he	  represented	  characters.	  This	  discussion	  prompted	  several	  questions	  on	  
black	  masculinity	  and	  sexuality	  within	  the	  group.	  Is	  it	  possible	  for	  a	  man	  to	  be	  a	  feminist?	  
Can	  a	  black	  man	  take	  up	  the	  issues	  and	  concerns	  of	  black	  females	  without	  his	  masculinity	  
and	  sexuality	  questioned?	  Does	  being	  inspired	  by	  female	  friends	  and	  family	  members	  
provide	  enough	  perspective	  to	  depict	  the	  black	  girl	  experience?	  	  
	  
Discourse	  on	  Black	  Female	  Sexuality	  
Streetwise,	  smart	  and	  tough,	  Winter	  Santiaga	  is	  the	  oldest	  daughter	  of	  a	  Brooklyn	  
drug	  kingpin	  and	  the	  female	  protagonist	  in	  the	  street	  literature	  text	  The	  Coldest	  Winter	  
Ever.	  Her	  father’s	  drug	  empire	  affords	  Winter	  a	  life	  of	  luxury.	  Winter	  is	  used	  to	  the	  most	  
expensive	  name	  brand	  clothes,	  shoes,	  and	  purses.	  Winter	  faces	  a	  bitter	  reality	  when	  her	  
father’s	  thriving	  drug	  business	  comes	  to	  a	  halt	  after	  he	  is	  arrested	  and	  incarcerated.	  The	  
government	  agents	  confiscate	  all	  the	  family’s	  money	  and	  possessions	  leaving	  Winter	  and	  
her	  family	  with	  unanswered	  questions.	  	  In	  my	  role	  as	  both	  researcher	  and	  facilitator,	  I	  
posed	  a	  critical	  question	  to	  the	  group	  regarding	  Winter’s	  position	  in	  the	  storyline	  (“Would	  
you	  consider	  Winter	  Santiago	  a	  victim	  or	  a	  victimizer	  or	  both?”).	  The	  conversation	  exchange	  

	  

158	  
below	  highlights	  my	  facilitation	  and	  details	  Keisha,	  Tammy,	  Denise,	  and	  Nicki’s	  
perspectives	  on	  black	  female	  sexuality.	  
1Delicia	  Would	  you	  consider	  Winter	  a	  victim	  or	  a	  victimizer?	  	  
2Denise:	  Winter	  is	  a	  Victim.	  It	  wasn't	  Necessarily	  Winter's	  Fault.	  She	  was	  Put	  
3In	  The	  Situation.	  She	  Was	  Having	  Sex	  And	  Looking	  For	  Love	  In	  The	  Wrong	  
4Places	  Because	  Her	  Father	  Was	  Incarcerated.	  So	  She	  Was	  Looking	  For	  A	  
5Male	  Figure	  Who	  Reminded	  Her	  Of	  Her	  Father.	  She	  Was	  Trying	  To	  Live	  The	  
6Lifestyle	  She	  Was	  Accustomed	  To	  
In	  line	  1,	  my	  line	  of	  questioning	  as	  a	  facilitator	  is	  close-­‐ended.	  I	  asked	  participants	  to	  
conduct	  a	  character	  analysis	  of	  Winter.	  My	  line	  of	  questioning	  only	  offers	  participants	  two	  
options	  either	  victim	  or	  victimizer.	  I	  posed	  a	  close-­‐ended	  question	  and	  offered	  participants	  
two	  personality	  traits	  to	  describe	  Winter.	  By	  doing	  so,	  I	  exclude	  participants	  who	  might	  not	  
view	  Winter	  as	  a	  victim	  or	  a	  victimizer	  (Fairclough,	  1989).	  This	  approach	  to	  facilitation	  
might	  be	  considered	  a	  teacherly	  move	  prompting	  participants	  to	  analyze	  Winter	  through	  
only	  two	  selected	  lens—victim	  and	  victimizer	  (researcher	  journal,	  December	  10,	  2013).	  In	  
addition,	  the	  labels	  “victim”	  and	  “victimizer”	  precedes	  Winter	  and	  further	  marginalizes	  her	  
and	  her	  experiences	  (Fairclough,	  1989).	  A	  more	  inclusive	  and	  feminist	  line	  of	  questioning	  
would	  be	  “Who	  is	  Winter?	  How	  might	  you	  characterize	  Winter?	  What	  life	  experiences	  and	  
decisions	  support	  your	  response?	  (researcher	  journal,	  December	  20,	  2013)	  Framing	  the	  
facilitation	  question	  in	  this	  way	  creates	  opportunities	  for	  participants	  to	  develop	  their	  own	  
character	  analysis	  without	  being	  bound	  to	  two	  particular	  personality	  traits.	  According	  to	  
Hip-­‐hop	  feminist	  scholar	  Elaine	  Richardson	  (2007),	  framing	  a	  more	  inclusive	  and	  feminist	  
line	  of	  questioning	  would	  position	  Winter	  at	  the	  center.	  

	  

159	  
Through	  my	  initial	  line	  of	  questioning	  (“Would	  you	  consider	  Winter	  a	  victim	  or	  a	  
victimizer?”),	  participants	  described	  Winter	  as	  a	  victim	  and	  understood	  how	  external	  
structures	  functioned	  in	  society.	  Participants	  understood	  that	  it	  was	  these	  external	  
structures	  that	  did	  not	  provide	  Winter	  with	  other	  options	  to	  make	  different	  choices.	  	  
Although	  Denise	  displayed	  a	  heightened	  consciousness	  around	  how	  society	  functions	  (see	  
Chapter	  4),	  she	  identifies	  Winter’s	  material	  possessions	  as	  an	  indication	  of	  living	  a	  good	  
life.	  Denise	  does,	  however,	  acknowledge	  that	  external	  factors,	  specifically	  her	  father’s	  
decisions,	  placed	  her	  in	  the	  situation.	  Denise	  ties	  Winter’s	  need	  for	  love	  with	  her	  need	  to	  fill	  
the	  void	  of	  her	  father’s	  absence.	  	  
1Tammy:	  I	  think	  she's	  a	  victim	  due	  to	  her	  upbringing.	  She	  was	  left	  to	  
2support	  herself	  so	  the	  only	  thing	  she	  knew	  were	  the	  finer	  things	  in	  life	  so	  
3she	  did	  what	  she	  had	  to	  do	  to	  get	  what	  she	  wanted.	  	  
4Nicki:	  She’s	  a	  victim.	  Winter	  needs	  love.	  She’s	  searching	  for	  love	  in	  all	  the	  
5wrong	  places.	  She	  didn’t	  have	  any	  direction	  
6Keisha:	  I	  think	  she's	  both	  because	  she	  grew	  up	  in	  an	  environment	  that	  she	  
7feels	  is	  normal	  
8Delicia:	  Winter	  is	  a	  victim.	  She’s	  not	  able	  to	  create	  another	  plan	  for	  herself	  
9outside	  of	  living	  the	  fast	  life.	  Her	  experience	  reminds	  me	  of	  Sandra’s	  
10experience	  in	  Black	  Girl	  Lost	  
Tammy,	  Nicki,	  and	  Denise	  agree	  that	  Winter	  is	  a	  victim	  because	  she	  had	  no	  support	  
during	  her	  father’s	  absence.	  They	  acknowledge	  that	  Winter	  is	  looking	  for	  love	  in	  all	  the	  
wrong	  places.	  Keisha	  however,	  identifies	  Winter’s	  twoness	  and	  views	  her	  as	  both	  a	  victim	  
and	  a	  victimizer	  based	  on	  her	  surroundings.	  In	  my	  role	  as	  a	  participant,	  I	  also	  identified	  

	  

160	  
Winter	  as	  a	  victim.	  I	  made	  intertextual	  connections	  by	  relating	  Winter’s	  experiences	  with	  
Sandra’s	  experiences	  in	  Black	  Girl	  Lost	  by	  Donald	  Goines,	  a	  text	  from	  a	  different	  time	  period	  
with	  a	  similar	  storyline.	  My	  reference	  to	  a	  text	  written	  several	  decades	  ago	  highlights	  the	  
generation	  gap	  that	  exists	  between	  myself	  and	  my	  participants	  (researcher	  journal,	  
December	  10,	  2013).	  
In	  our	  fourth	  and	  final	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  meeting,	  Keisha	  and	  Denise’s	  conversation	  was	  
based	  on	  a	  larger	  discussion	  that	  occurred	  in	  the	  digital	  space.	  The	  online	  discussion	  
focused	  on	  female	  sexuality	  and	  Winter’s	  need	  to	  fill	  the	  void	  of	  her	  father’s	  absence.	  The	  
face-­‐to-­‐face	  conversation	  was	  driven	  by	  a	  prompt	  that	  focused	  on	  the	  community’s	  
perception	  of	  Winter	  and	  black	  female	  sexuality	  in	  present	  day.	  	  
1Delicia:	  How	  do	  you	  think	  the	  community	  viewed	  Winter?	  
2Keisha:	  Winter	  was	  considered	  a	  gold	  digger	  or	  what	  we	  call	  a	  thot	  
3Delicia:	  (tilts	  head	  to	  one	  side)	  What	  is	  a	  thot?	  
4Keisha:	  (frowns	  face)	  It	  is	  an	  offensive	  term.	  It	  means	  like	  a	  ho	  or	  a	  smut	  
6Denise:	  It’s	  someone	  who	  is	  promiscuous	  and	  she	  has	  bodies.	  
7Delicia:	  (squints	  face	  &	  listens	  intently)	  What	  do	  you	  mean	  by	  bodies?	  
8Denise:	  Like	  her	  body	  count.	  The	  number	  of	  people	  she	  has	  been	  with	  
9Keisha:	  Thot	  can	  also	  refer	  to	  how	  you	  carry	  yourself.	  Like	  how	  you	  dress.	  
10It	  means	  That	  Ho	  Over	  There	  or	  That	  Heffa	  Ova	  There	  
In	  my	  line	  of	  questioning,	  I	  asked	  the	  group	  to	  think	  about	  how	  the	  community	  
views	  Winter	  (“How	  do	  you	  think	  the	  community	  viewed	  Winter?”).	  Keisha	  argues	  that	  the	  
community	  viewed	  Winter	  as	  a	  golddigger	  and	  a	  thot.	  Unfamiliar	  with	  the	  term	  “thot,”	  I	  
tilted	  my	  head	  to	  one	  side	  and	  posed	  a	  probing	  question	  to	  Keisha	  (“What	  is	  a	  thot?”).	  

	  

161	  
Keisha	  states	  that	  the	  term	  “thot”	  is	  a	  derogatory	  term	  and	  she	  offers	  similar	  terms	  to	  make	  
her	  point	  (“smut,”	  “ho”).	  This	  conversation	  exchange	  illustrates	  the	  generational	  gap	  that	  
exists	  between	  myself	  and	  participants	  (researcher	  journal,	  December	  12,	  2013).	  
According	  to	  Richardson	  (2007),	  contemporary	  controlling	  images	  to	  describe	  black	  girls	  
are	  tied	  to	  controlling	  images	  of	  the	  past.	  In	  essence,	  black	  female	  sexuality	  continues	  to	  be	  
associated	  with	  demeaning,	  degrading,	  and	  derogatory	  terms.	  These	  terms	  continue	  to	  
objectify	  and	  marginalize	  black	  female	  bodies.	  Like	  Keisha,	  Denise	  associates	  promiscuity	  
as	  a	  key	  feature	  associated	  with	  the	  term	  thot.	  She	  describes	  a	  thot’s	  number	  of	  sexual	  
encounters	  as	  “bodies”	  (line	  6,	  Line,	  8).	  Keisha	  reveals	  to	  the	  group	  that	  the	  label	  “thot”	  is	  
an	  acronym	  for	  an	  equally	  derogatory	  and	  marginalizing	  reference—‘That	  Ho	  Over	  There.”	  
	  
Summative	  Findings	  
	  

The	  politics	  of	  facilitation:	  My	  role	  across	  contexts.	  The	  goal	  and	  focus	  of	  this	  

program	  was	  to	  offer	  participants	  an	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  space	  that	  provided	  freedom	  of	  
expression	  and	  autonomy,	  two	  features	  that	  are	  often	  noticeably	  absent	  in	  formal	  spaces.	  
The	  following	  details	  how	  school-­‐based	  social	  norms	  were	  embedded	  in	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  
spaces	  and	  influenced	  my	  role	  as	  a	  facilitator.	  In	  the	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  digital	  space,	  
participants	  often	  responded	  to	  my	  initial	  discussion	  prompt	  but	  would	  not	  engage	  with	  
fellow	  participants	  a	  great	  deal.	  	  Participants	  tended	  to	  not	  frequent	  the	  digital	  space	  often	  
to	  read	  posts	  that	  were	  posted	  after	  their	  own	  post.	  This	  resulted	  in	  my	  role	  as	  a	  researcher	  
and	  facilitator	  shifting	  and	  the	  space	  shifting	  from	  a	  participant-­‐centered	  space	  to	  a	  
facilitator-­‐centered	  space	  in	  our	  first	  two	  discussions.	  In	  our	  first	  two	  online	  discussions	  
for	  both	  PUSH	  and	  Project	  Girl,	  I	  would	  often	  post	  probing	  questions	  to	  account	  for	  limited	  

	  

162	  
posts	  and	  extend	  the	  conversation	  in	  the	  online	  space.	  Participants	  would	  often	  make	  and	  
initially	  post	  and	  would	  not	  post	  again;	  my	  follow-­‐up	  questions	  also	  went	  unanswered.	  In	  
the	  first	  two	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  discussions,	  participants	  would	  often	  look	  to	  me	  to	  lead	  and	  
sustain	  discussion.	  My	  role	  as	  a	  facilitator	  was	  constantly	  shifting	  as	  participants	  shifted	  
between	  facilitator-­‐centered	  spaces	  and	  teacher-­‐centered	  spaces.	  As	  the	  program	  
continued,	  participants	  were	  more	  comfortable	  sustaining	  conversations	  both	  in	  the	  online	  
discussion	  and	  in	  the	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  discussion.	  My	  role	  as	  a	  facilitator	  was	  constantly	  in	  flux	  .	  
Facilitation	  and	  types	  of	  talk.	  As	  the	  researcher,	  facilitator,	  and	  participant,	  a	  black	  
woman,	  a	  reader	  of	  street	  literature	  text,	  a	  user	  of	  digital	  technology,	  and	  a	  former	  
educator,	  I	  brought	  several	  identities	  to	  this	  research	  project.	  Over	  the	  course	  of	  this	  study,	  
I	  employed	  particular	  types	  of	  talk,	  including	  participant	  talk	  and	  facilitator	  talk.	  Facilitator	  
talk	  was	  most	  prominent	  followed	  by	  participant	  talk.	  Facilitator	  talk	  consisted	  of	  
employing	  probing	  questions	  and	  resulted	  in	  the	  most	  incidents	  of	  tension	  in	  the	  data.	  The	  
purpose	  of	  probing	  questions	  was	  to	  delve	  deeper	  into	  participant	  responses.	  By	  posing	  
probing	  questions	  in	  the	  online	  and	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  discussion	  group,	  I	  inherently	  assumed	  the	  
power	  and	  privilege	  embedded	  in	  my	  role	  as	  facilitator	  and	  a	  researcher.	  Participants	  often	  
associated	  my	  probing	  questions	  as	  an	  indication	  that	  I	  was	  seeking	  a	  particular	  response	  
as	  oppose	  to	  gaining	  clarity	  on	  their	  perspectives	  (researcher	  journal,	  December	  6,	  2013).	  
This	  resulted	  in	  participants	  undermining	  their	  own	  stance	  and	  position	  on	  issues	  and	  
conforming	  to	  my	  line	  of	  questioning.	  	  
	  
	  
	  

	  

163	  
Data	  Chapter	  Summary	  
	  

Chapters	  4,	  5,	  and	  6	  highlighted	  the	  findings	  in	  this	  study.	  Chapter	  4	  detailed	  how	  

media	  representations	  influenced	  how	  participants	  saw	  themselves.	  Chapter	  5	  detailed	  
participants’	  meaning	  making	  of	  street	  literature	  texts	  within	  a	  digital	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  space,	  
while	  Chapter	  6,	  highlighted	  how	  my	  facilitation	  influenced	  the	  way	  participants	  made	  
meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts.	  Chapter	  7	  provided	  an	  overview	  of	  the	  study	  findings,	  
implications	  for	  research,	  theory,	  and	  teaching,	  methodological	  strengths	  and	  weaknesses,	  
limitations	  of	  the	  study,	  and	  future	  research.	  
	  

	  

164	  
Chapter	  Seven	  
“There	  Needs	  To	  Be	  More	  ‘US’	  In	  Schools!!”	  :	  	  
Bridging	  Out-­‐of-­‐School	  and	  In-­‐School	  Literacy	  and	  Language	  Practices	  
	  
Introduction	  
The	  primary	  focus	  of	  this	  dissertation	  study	  was	  to	  examine	  broadly	  how	  context,	  
specifically	  societal,	  institutional,	  and	  situational	  factors	  influence	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  black	  
adolescent	  girls	  make	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts.	  In	  Chapter	  1,	  I	  highlighted	  the	  
current	  climate	  surrounding	  black	  girls’	  personhood,	  lived	  experiences,	  literacy	  and	  
language	  practices.	  In	  addition	  to	  highlighting	  the	  historical	  positionality	  of	  black	  girls,	  I	  
depicted	  how	  social	  spheres,	  including	  the	  media	  and	  school	  spaces	  have	  worked	  in	  
tandem	  to	  continuously	  oppress	  black	  girls	  in	  various	  ways.	  In	  Chapter	  2,	  I	  provided	  a	  
robust	  review	  of	  the	  literature	  comprised	  of	  four	  strands	  of	  literature	  (black	  girlhood—part	  
1,	  black	  adolescent	  girls’	  literacy	  practices	  &	  street	  literature—part	  II,	  adolescent	  literacy	  
practices	  in	  book	  clubs—part	  III,	  and	  adolescent	  literacy	  practices	  in	  digital	  spaces)	  to	  
highlight	  the	  dearth	  of	  literature	  on	  the	  literacy	  and	  language	  practices	  of	  black	  girls	  across	  
social	  spheres.	  
The	  overarching	  goal	  of	  this	  study	  was	  to	  clear	  spaces	  for	  black	  girls	  and	  re-­‐write	  
their	  literacy	  and	  language	  experiences	  into	  current	  bodies	  of	  literature.	  The	  data	  chapters,	  
Chapters	  4,	  5	  and,	  6	  highlighted	  the	  societal,	  institutional,	  and	  situational	  factors	  that	  
influenced	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  made	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts.	  
Each	  chapter	  highlights	  the	  findings	  of	  a	  4-­‐month	  qualitative	  study	  situated	  in	  two	  out-­‐of-­‐
school	  spaces:	  the	  social	  networking	  site,	  Facebook	  and	  an	  urban	  public	  library	  in	  Upstate	  

	  

165	  
New	  York.	  Each	  chapter	  also	  details	  the	  literacy	  and	  language	  practices	  of	  five	  black	  
adolescent	  girls,	  Vanessa,	  Nicki,	  Lisa,	  Denise,	  and	  Tammy	  individually,	  as	  well	  as	  
collectively.	  	  
Chapter	  4	  examined	  how	  media	  representations	  influenced	  how	  they	  saw	  
themselves	  and	  self-­‐identified.	  Participants’	  experiences	  were	  documented	  through	  free-­‐
write,	  prompt	  write	  journal	  sessions,	  and	  a	  series	  of	  one-­‐on-­‐one	  introductory	  meetings.	  
Employing	  Fairclough’s	  (1989)	  Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis	  Framework,	  I	  drew	  upon	  Gee’s	  
Primary	  and	  Secondary	  Discourses	  and	  highlighted	  the	  tensions	  that	  existed	  between	  
participants’	  primary	  discourses	  and	  secondary	  discourses.	  Participants’	  discourses	  around	  
their	  sense	  of	  self	  rooted	  in	  their	  home	  and	  community	  experiences	  served	  as	  their	  primary	  
Discourses,	  while	  media	  representations	  around	  black	  girls	  served	  as	  their	  secondary	  
Discourses.	  I	  detailed	  discourse	  by	  employing	  discursive	  theories	  of	  Hip-­‐hop	  Feminism	  that	  
represented	  participants’	  perspectives	  on	  several	  issues	  including	  Discourse	  of	  Media	  
Representations	  of	  Black	  Girlhood,	  Discourse	  of	  Black	  Girlhood,	  and	  Discourse	  of	  Self-­‐
Identity.	  In	  Chapter	  4,	  I	  considered	  the	  following	  questions:	  How	  did	  each	  participant	  
position	  herself	  as	  a	  black	  adolescent	  girl	  in	  society?	  How	  did	  media	  representations	  of	  
black	  girlhood	  play	  into	  their	  individual	  identities?	  How	  did	  media	  representations	  of	  black	  
girlhood	  play	  into	  their	  collective	  identities?	  How	  did	  Denise,	  Keisha,	  and	  Lisa	  address	  
media	  representations	  of	  black	  girlhood?	  Did	  they	  conform	  to	  these	  media	  representations	  
or	  did	  they	  challenge	  dominant	  narratives	  of	  black	  girlhood	  in	  the	  21st	  century?	  In	  what	  
ways	  did	  they	  conform	  and/or	  challenge	  these	  dominant	  narratives	  of	  black	  girlhood?	  
Lastly,	  how	  did	  their	  narratives	  play	  into	  the	  creation	  of	  more	  critically	  and	  culturally	  
conscious	  classrooms	  and	  libraries?	  

	  

166	  
This	  study	  found	  that	  participants	  detailed	  personal	  narratives	  to	  make	  sense	  of	  the	  
world	  around	  them.	  Participants	  documented	  how	  negative	  images	  both	  individually	  and	  
collectively	  impacted	  how	  they	  self-­‐identified.	  This	  study	  also	  found	  that	  participants	  
displayed	  resilience	  against	  media	  representations	  that	  threatened	  their	  personhood.	  This	  
was	  evident	  in	  their	  personal	  acknowledgments	  of	  their	  positive	  attributes.	  Participants’	  
identities	  were	  in	  flux	  because	  they	  were	  constantly	  negotiating	  who	  they	  were	  and	  how	  
they	  saw	  themselves	  within	  two	  separate	  and	  conflicting	  worlds.	  This	  study	  also	  found	  that	  
participants’	  journal	  free-­‐writes	  tended	  to	  be	  lengthier,	  more	  expressive,	  and	  more	  
intimate	  than	  writing	  to	  prompts.	  Participants	  used	  free-­‐writes	  to	  discuss	  issues	  
considered	  to	  be	  taboo	  in	  public	  settings.	  Free	  writing	  also	  served	  as	  counter	  narratives	  
that	  re-­‐positioned	  and	  re-­‐wrote	  them	  back	  into	  society	  and	  validated	  their	  experiences.	  
This	  study	  also	  found	  that	  participants	  made	  conscious	  decisions	  around	  their	  use	  of	  
linguistic	  styles	  based	  on	  social	  norms	  and	  conformed	  to	  conventions	  of	  Standard	  English	  
in	  their	  journal	  writing.	  
Chapter	  5	  examined	  how	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  made	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  
texts	  in	  two	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  spaces,	  both	  a	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  and	  an	  online	  space.	  Participants’	  
meaning	  making	  process	  was	  documented	  through	  a	  series	  of	  online	  Facebook	  Street	  
Literature	  Book	  Club	  and	  focus	  group	  interviews.	  I	  detailed	  discourse	  by	  employing	  
discursive	  theories	  of	  Hip-­‐hop	  Feminism,	  New	  Literacy	  Studies,	  and	  Reader	  Response	  
Theory.	  Employing	  Fairclough’s	  (1989)	  Orders	  of	  Discourse,	  I	  highlighted	  how	  institutional	  
factors	  influenced	  the	  meaning	  making	  process.	  More	  specifically,	  I	  analyzed	  how	  black	  
adolescent	  girls	  negotiated	  their	  power,	  positionality,	  and	  identity	  around	  a	  series	  of	  
critical	  social	  issues	  embedded	  within	  street	  literature,	  including	  Discourse	  on	  the	  Black	  

	  

167	  
Female	  Condition,	  Discourse	  on	  Motherhood,	  and	  Discourse	  on	  Black	  Girl’s	  Academic	  
Success.	  In	  Chapter	  5,	  I	  considered	  the	  following	  questions:	  How	  did	  black	  adolescent	  girls’	  
positionalities	  (i.e.,	  lived	  experiences,	  marked	  gendered	  and	  raced	  identities)	  influence	  how	  
they	  make	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts	  in	  digital	  spaces?	  Which	  literacy	  strategies	  and	  
linguistic	  styles	  did	  they	  employ	  in	  the	  meaning	  making	  process	  in	  both	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  
spaces?	  What	  new	  literacies,	  if	  any,	  were	  created	  in	  the	  digital	  space	  during	  this	  process?	  
Did	  narration	  (first	  person,	  second	  person,	  third	  person)	  influence	  how	  participants	  made	  
meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts?	  How	  did	  hip-­‐hop	  aesthetics	  embedded	  in	  street	  
literature	  texts	  influence	  how	  participants	  made	  meaning?	  In	  what	  ways	  did	  they	  conform	  
or	  challenge	  dominant	  linguistic	  styles?	  In	  instances	  in	  which	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  neither	  
fully	  resist	  nor	  confirm	  to	  any	  particular	  linguistic	  style,	  how	  did	  multiple	  linguistic	  styles	  
co-­‐exist?	  How	  did	  group	  dynamics	  play	  into	  their	  meaning	  making	  process?	  How	  might	  
educators	  support	  diverse	  students’	  emerging	  literacies	  and	  native	  linguistic	  styles	  within	  
school	  spaces?	  
This	  study	  found	  that	  participants	  made	  meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts	  in	  the	  out-­‐
of-­‐school	  spaced	  through	  the	  use	  of	  visual	  imagery	  in	  media,	  such	  as	  movies	  and	  music	  
videos.	  This	  resulted	  in	  participants	  engaging	  in	  a	  critical	  analysis	  across	  different	  types	  of	  
media.	  In	  some	  instances,	  visual	  imagery	  heightened	  their	  connection	  to	  text	  and	  resulted	  
in	  emotional	  connections	  to	  characters	  and	  storylines.	  This	  study	  also	  found	  that	  street	  
literature	  created	  opportunities	  for	  participants	  to	  confront	  personal	  experiences	  through	  
critical	  social	  issues	  depicted	  in	  the	  text.	  Participants	  were	  conscious	  of	  how	  the	  world	  
works	  and	  their	  place	  in	  it	  and	  often	  interrogated	  hegemonic	  ideologies	  around	  critical	  
social	  issues	  such	  as	  academic	  success	  perceived	  as	  Whiteness,	  the	  oppression	  of	  the	  black	  

	  

168	  
female	  in	  society,	  and	  verbal	  and	  physical	  abuse.	  In	  these	  instances,	  street	  literature	  served	  
as	  a	  therapeutic	  tool	  and	  tended	  to	  the	  socio-­‐emotional	  well	  being	  of	  participants.	  In	  some	  
instances,	  participants	  conformed	  to	  patriarchal	  ideologies	  to	  make	  sense	  of	  the	  world	  
around	  them.	  Linguistic	  tensions	  were	  evident	  in	  participants’	  ways	  of	  being	  across	  social	  
spheres.	  Participants	  assumed	  heightened	  levels	  of	  awareness	  and	  shifted	  their	  style	  based	  
upon	  perceived	  norms	  within	  a	  particular	  context.	  More	  specifically,	  participants	  employed	  
features	  of	  AAVE	  in	  the	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  space,	  while	  they	  often	  used	  standard	  
English	  in	  the	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  digital	  space.	  Shifting	  linguistic	  styles	  was	  a	  clear	  indication	  
that	  participants	  constantly	  negotiated	  multiple	  identities	  (Smitherman,	  1977,	  2006).	  
Chapter	  6	  examined	  my	  role	  as	  a	  researcher,	  facilitator,	  and	  participant	  in	  an	  online	  
street	  literature	  book	  club	  and	  how	  my	  positionality	  influenced	  participants’	  meaning	  
making	  of	  street	  literature	  texts.	  Drawing	  upon	  a	  Critical	  Discourse	  framework,	  I	  turned	  the	  
lens	  on	  myself	  as	  a	  means	  of	  balancing	  my	  biases	  and	  accounting	  for	  how	  my	  positionality	  
functioned	  in	  my	  interactions	  with	  participants	  within	  and	  across	  contexts.	  I	  employed	  an	  
auto-­‐ethnographic	  account	  of	  my	  personal	  and	  professional	  experiences	  through	  the	  use	  of	  
a	  researcher	  journal.	  Through	  the	  use	  of	  a	  researcher	  journal,	  I	  detailed	  how	  I	  negotiated	  
my	  power,	  privilege,	  and	  identity.	  In	  Chapter	  6,	  I	  considered	  the	  following	  questions:	  How	  
might	  my	  multiple	  identities	  play	  into	  the	  type	  of	  talk	  I	  employed?	  How	  might	  generational	  
differences	  between	  myself	  and	  my	  participants	  on	  critical	  social	  issues	  influence	  their	  
meaning	  making	  process?	  In	  what	  ways	  did	  I	  conform	  or	  challenge	  power	  dynamics	  
present	  during	  the	  facilitation	  process?	  How	  might	  educators	  use	  facilitation	  and	  type	  of	  
talk	  to	  create	  critically	  engaged	  student-­‐centered	  spaces	  within	  libraries	  and	  classrooms?	  

	  

169	  
	  

Chapter	  6	  highlighted	  how	  school-­‐based	  social	  norms	  were	  embedded	  in	  both	  the	  

out-­‐of-­‐school	  digital	  space	  and	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  space	  and	  influenced	  my	  role	  in	  the	  book	  club.	  
In	  the	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  digital	  space	  participants	  often	  responded	  to	  the	  initial	  discussion	  
prompt,	  but	  would	  not	  frequent	  the	  digital	  space	  often	  to	  engage	  with	  fellow	  participants.	  
During	  the	  first	  two	  discussions,	  my	  role	  shifted	  and	  the	  space	  shifted	  from	  a	  participant-­‐
led	  space	  to	  a	  facilitator-­‐led	  space.	  In	  the	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  space,	  participants	  often	  looked	  to	  me	  
to	  lead	  and	  sustain	  discussion.	  Over	  the	  course	  of	  this	  study,	  facilitator	  talk	  was	  most	  
prominent	  followed	  by	  participant	  talk.	  Facilitator	  talk	  consisted	  of	  posing	  probing	  
questions.	  Facilitator	  talk	  revealed	  the	  most	  incidents	  of	  tension	  in	  the	  data.	  As	  a	  facilitator,	  
the	  purpose	  of	  probing	  questions	  was	  to	  delve	  deeper	  into	  participants’	  perspectives.	  In	  the	  
digital	  space,	  probing	  questions	  went	  unanswered	  since	  participants	  did	  not	  frequent	  the	  
online	  space	  after	  their	  initial	  post.	  In	  the	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  space,	  participants	  often	  associated	  
my	  use	  of	  probing	  questions	  as	  an	  indication	  that	  I	  was	  seeking	  a	  particular	  response	  as	  
opposed	  to	  gaining	  clarity	  on	  their	  perspectives.	  This	  resulted	  in	  participants	  often	  
undermining	  their	  own	  stance	  and	  position	  on	  issues	  and	  conforming	  to	  my	  line	  of	  
questioning.	  In	  one	  particular	  instance,	  a	  participant	  asserted	  her	  presence	  in	  the	  online	  
discussion	  and	  shifted	  the	  conversation	  to	  focus	  on	  another	  critical	  social	  issues	  that	  was	  
most	  of	  interest	  to	  her.	  
	  
Implications	  for	  Practice	  
	  

The	  next	  section	  details	  the	  practical	  and	  research	  implications,	  methodological	  

strengths	  and	  weaknesses,	  recommendation	  for	  further	  research,	  and	  limitations	  of	  the	  
study.	  Although	  the	  findings	  for	  this	  study	  are	  not	  generalizable	  in	  the	  traditional	  sense,	  

	  

170	  
this	  study	  offers	  implications	  that	  inform	  the	  work	  of	  school	  and	  public	  librarians,	  
classroom	  teachers,	  social	  workers,	  and	  school	  administrators.	  The	  following	  section,	  
addresses	  the	  implications	  in	  the	  areas	  of	  street	  literature	  texts,	  book	  clubs,	  facilitation,	  
digital	  technologies,	  policy,	  and	  theory.	  
Implications	  for	  the	  use	  of	  digital	  technologies.	  This	  study	  revealed	  that	  
participants	  were	  both	  creators	  and	  consumers	  of	  technology	  in	  their	  spare	  time,	  however	  
they	  did	  not	  appear	  to	  be	  accustomed	  to	  using	  digital	  technologies	  to	  engage	  in	  literacy	  
activities.	  In	  many	  instances,	  participants	  were	  accustomed	  to	  the	  decontextualization	  of	  
literacy	  and	  technology,	  which	  is	  often	  found	  in	  school	  spaces.	  Despite	  not	  being	  
accustomed	  to	  engaging	  in	  literacy	  activities	  through	  the	  use	  of	  digital	  technologies,	  this	  
study	  revealed	  that	  technology	  served	  as	  a	  transformative	  and	  emancipatory	  tool	  that	  
allowed	  them	  to	  engage	  in	  identity	  work.	  One	  prominent	  pedagogical	  strategy	  for	  using	  
digital	  technologies	  is	  to	  create	  opportunities	  for	  students	  to	  use	  it	  regularly	  to	  address	  
critical	  social	  issues	  across	  a	  range	  of	  literary	  genres.	  This	  creates	  opportunities	  for	  
students	  to	  examine	  social	  structures	  and	  the	  ideologies	  of	  both	  the	  privileged	  and	  the	  
oppressed	  in	  genres	  ranging	  from	  classic	  texts	  to	  culturally	  relevant	  texts.	  This	  would	  
provide	  students	  the	  opportunity	  to	  see	  the	  common	  social	  thread	  across	  literary	  genres.	  
Another	  pedagogical	  strategy	  educators	  can	  employ	  is	  to	  work	  across	  content	  areas	  and	  
merge	  language	  arts	  and	  social	  studies	  in	  the	  secondary	  contexts	  (middle	  school	  and	  high	  
school)	  and	  encourage	  students	  to	  use	  technology	  as	  a	  springboard	  to	  address	  social	  justice	  
issues	  that	  affect	  their	  personal	  lives	  and	  their	  communities.	  Students	  can	  use	  social	  
networking	  platforms,	  such	  as	  Twitter	  and	  Facebook	  to	  post	  their	  perspectives	  during	  class	  
time	  on	  a	  wide	  range	  of	  social	  justice	  issues.	  One	  example	  of	  this	  could	  be	  a	  student-­‐led	  and	  

	  

171	  
student-­‐sustained	  online	  discussion	  addressing	  both	  local	  and	  national	  issues	  affecting	  
diverse	  populations.	  This	  would	  provide	  students	  the	  opportunity	  to	  use	  technology	  as	  a	  
tool	  of	  activism	  and	  a	  source	  of	  empowerment.	  
Implications	  for	  the	  use	  of	  street	  literature	  texts.	  This	  study	  revealed	  that	  
participants	  employed	  several	  meaning	  making	  strategies	  in	  their	  readings	  of	  street	  
literature	  texts.	  One	  prominent	  meaning	  making	  strategy,	  entailed	  participants	  engaging	  in	  
critical	  media	  literacy	  by	  merging	  street	  literature	  texts	  with	  other	  forms	  of	  popular	  culture	  
media,	  such	  as	  movies	  and	  television	  shows.	  Another	  pedagogical	  strategy	  could	  entail	  
pairing	  the	  street	  literature	  text	  The	  Coldest	  Winter	  by	  Sister	  Souljah	  with	  supplementary	  
media	  like	  VH1’s	  reality	  show	  Love	  and	  Hip	  Hop	  (Greene,	  2012).	  	  This	  would	  encourage	  
participants	  to	  engage	  in	  critical	  media	  literacy	  and	  compare	  and	  contrast	  the	  cinematic	  
elements	  in	  multimedia	  with	  literary	  elements	  in	  texts.	  This	  provides	  students	  with	  the	  
opportunity	  to	  see	  how	  storylines	  and	  characters	  are	  depicted	  across	  popular	  culture	  
media	  sources.	  Another	  example	  of	  this	  strategy	  would	  be	  to	  offer	  students	  the	  opportunity	  
to	  read	  a	  street	  literature	  text	  and	  pair	  it	  with	  a	  supplementary	  historical	  text.	  An	  example	  
of	  this	  would	  be	  pairing	  the	  text	  PUSH	  by	  Sapphire	  (1996)	  with	  a	  supplementary	  text	  like	  
The	  Color	  Purple	  by	  Alice	  Walker	  (1982).	  	  By	  employing	  a	  critical	  lens,	  students	  can	  conduct	  
a	  comparative	  analysis	  of	  both	  texts	  that	  draws	  upon	  the	  differences	  and	  similarities	  across	  
themes,	  storylines,	  characters,	  and	  time	  periods.	  Using	  a	  critical	  lens,	  students	  can	  also	  
analyze	  both	  the	  oppressors	  and	  the	  oppressed	  in	  both	  texts	  and	  discuss	  how	  power	  
structures	  assist	  in	  perpetuating	  certain	  types	  of	  human	  relationships	  and	  life	  experiences	  
(Greene,	  2012).	  Students	  can	  also	  analyze	  how	  power	  structures	  influence	  characters’	  
gendered,	  racialized,	  and	  classed	  identities	  and	  in	  turn	  influenced	  their	  condition	  in	  the	  

	  

172	  
world.	  In	  another	  example,	  students	  could	  pair	  street	  literature	  text	  with	  a	  supplementary	  
canonical	  text,	  which	  draws	  upon	  similar	  themes	  and	  character	  experiences,	  yet	  focuses	  on	  
a	  different	  time	  period	  and	  includes	  characters	  from	  a	  different	  racial	  group	  (Greene,	  
2012).	  Using	  a	  critical	  lens	  students	  can	  conduct	  a	  comparative	  analysis	  of	  both	  texts	  and	  
discuss	  why	  particular	  texts,	  which	  depict	  the	  experiences	  of	  marginalized	  characters	  set	  in	  
an	  inner	  city	  community,	  are	  often	  devalued	  and	  scrutinized	  by	  society,	  while	  other	  texts	  
that	  depict	  the	  experiences	  of	  mainstream	  characters,	  are	  often	  privileged	  and	  identified	  as	  
canonical	  text.	  
This	  study	  revealed	  that	  participants	  had	  a	  heightened	  connection	  to	  storylines	  and	  
characters	  in	  the	  text.	  This	  resulted	  in	  participants	  making	  emotional	  connections	  with	  the	  
text	  and	  experiences	  in	  their	  own	  lives.	  	  These	  connections	  were	  rooted	  in	  the	  social	  issues	  
that	  plagued	  the	  protagonist,	  as	  well	  as	  several	  of	  the	  participants	  who	  were	  part	  of	  this	  
study.	  Such	  parallel	  experiences	  between	  participants	  and	  female	  protagonists	  and	  the	  
empathy	  that	  participants	  displayed	  based	  on	  the	  protagonist’s	  black	  female	  condition	  
revealed	  the	  potential	  of	  using	  street	  literature	  texts	  as	  bibliotherapy.	  Bibliotherapy	  is	  
broadly	  defined	  as	  the	  use	  of	  literature	  in	  a	  therapeutic	  setting.	  Bibliotherapy	  also	  serves	  as	  
an	  outlet	  for	  students	  confronted	  with	  challenging	  circumstances	  and	  ensures	  the	  socio-­‐
emotional	  well	  being	  of	  students.	  Employing	  street	  literature	  texts	  in	  schools	  develops	  an	  
inclusive	  literacy	  and	  learning	  environment.	  
Implications	  for	  the	  use	  of	  book	  clubs.	  This	  study	  revealed	  that	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  
book	  clubs	  hold	  great	  potential	  for	  participants	  to	  engage	  in	  identity	  work	  as	  they	  make	  
meaning	  of	  street	  literature	  texts.	  Since	  feminism	  values	  diversity	  of	  personal	  experiences	  
and	  community,	  building	  a	  trusting	  environment	  creates	  an	  environment	  that	  black	  girls	  

	  

173	  
feel	  comfortable	  in	  and	  one	  where	  they	  can	  engage	  in	  identity	  work	  safely.	  Books	  clubs	  also	  
create	  opportunities	  to	  construct	  knowledge	  both	  individually,	  as	  well	  as	  collectively.	  It	  
fosters	  multiple	  authorities,	  which	  encourages	  black	  girls	  to	  privilege	  their	  own	  voices	  and	  
respect	  the	  diversity	  of	  their	  peers.	  By	  creating	  a	  safe	  space	  that	  privileges	  all	  voices,	  book	  
clubs	  are	  authoritative	  and	  transformative	  spaces	  that	  democratize	  knowledge.	  	  
One	  pedagogical	  strategy	  that	  educators	  can	  use	  to	  implement	  book	  clubs	  within	  
schools	  spaces	  can	  be	  to	  incorporate	  a	  free-­‐write	  journal	  component	  into	  the	  activity.	  This	  
would	  encourage	  black	  girls	  to	  dialogue	  collectively	  with	  their	  peers	  and	  individually	  with	  
self.	  Another	  pedagogical	  strategy	  that	  educators	  can	  use	  to	  effectively	  implement	  book	  
clubs	  effectively	  in	  school	  spaces	  is	  to	  encourage	  students	  to	  sustain	  discussions	  through	  
the	  creation	  of	  discussion	  questions	  and	  the	  selection	  of	  texts.	  This	  creates	  opportunities	  of	  
self-­‐expression	  and	  autonomy	  and	  promotes	  agency	  and	  encourages	  identity	  development.	  	  
Another	  pedagogical	  strategy	  is	  for	  students	  to	  book	  talk	  the	  book	  they	  have	  selected	  to	  the	  
group	  in	  order	  to	  ensure	  buy-­‐in	  by	  group	  members.	  	  Another	  pedagogical	  strategy	  is	  to	  
have	  students	  engage	  in	  book	  discussions	  within	  the	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  environment	  and	  then	  
continuing	  conversations	  within	  the	  digital	  space.	  	  By	  doing	  so,	  the	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  
environment	  serves	  as	  an	  ice-­‐breaker	  for	  student	  interactions	  in	  the	  digital	  space.	  In	  an	  
effort	  for	  practitioners	  to	  create	  transformative	  spaces	  through	  book	  clubs,	  information	  
professionals	  and	  classroom	  teachers	  should	  provide	  opportunities	  for	  students	  to	  have	  
freedom	  in	  choosing	  texts	  that	  are	  interesting	  to	  them.	  This	  creates	  student-­‐driven,	  
authentic,	  transformative	  book	  club	  discussions	  (Appleman,	  2006).	  	  
	  

Implications	  for	  facilitation.	  The	  findings	  in	  this	  study	  revealed	  that	  participants	  

were	  conditioned	  to	  view	  literacy	  as	  a	  school-­‐based	  activity	  even	  within	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  

	  

174	  
spaces.	  This	  led	  to	  participants	  embedding	  school-­‐based	  social	  norms	  within	  the	  out-­‐of-­‐
school	  space.	  Based	  on	  the	  findings,	  it	  is	  evident	  that	  participants	  were	  accustomed	  to	  
teacher-­‐centered	  and	  teacher-­‐initiated	  instruction.	  According	  to	  Freire	  (2001)	  in	  Pedagogy	  
of	  the	  Oppressed,	  this	  traditional	  model	  of	  education,	  also	  known	  as	  the	  banking	  model	  
positions	  students	  as	  passive	  receivers	  of	  knowledge	  and	  limits	  participants’	  authoritative	  
knowledge.	  This	  study	  also	  revealed	  that	  participant-­‐centered	  discussions	  created	  
opportunities	  for	  participants	  to	  engage	  in	  active,	  reflective	  approaches	  to	  texts.	  There	  are	  
several	  strategies	  that	  information	  professionals	  and	  classroom	  teachers	  can	  employ	  to	  
create	  inclusive	  learning	  environments.	  One	  major	  pedagogical	  strategy	  entails	  creating	  
opportunities	  for	  students	  to	  lead	  and	  sustain	  discussion.	  This	  approach	  creates	  
opportunities	  for	  students	  to	  gain	  the	  tools	  necessary	  to	  be	  active	  learners	  and	  to	  take	  
ownership	  of	  their	  learning.	  In	  order	  to	  effectively	  create	  a	  student-­‐centered	  learning	  
environment,	  it	  is	  also	  important	  that	  there	  is	  a	  decentering	  of	  power	  resulting	  in	  the	  
teacher	  shifting	  into	  a	  less	  authoritative	  role.	  Shifting	  suppresses	  the	  power	  and	  authority	  
that	  the	  teacher	  traditionally	  has	  in	  the	  space,	  which	  encourages	  students	  to	  take	  a	  more	  
active	  role	  in	  the	  learning	  process.	  The	  educator	  should	  also	  share	  his/her	  personal	  
perspectives	  on	  the	  text,	  in	  order	  to	  decenter	  his/her	  power	  in	  the	  educator-­‐student	  
relationship.	  	  
Another	  pedagogical	  approach	  to	  bridging	  in-­‐school	  and	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  literacy	  is	  to	  
encourage	  students	  to	  engage	  in	  critical	  reads	  of	  text	  by	  focusing	  on	  key	  features	  in	  texts	  
including,	  power	  dynamics,	  social	  structures,	  raced,	  classed,	  and	  gendered	  identities.	  This	  
creates	  opportunities	  for	  students	  to	  view	  storylines	  and	  characters	  in	  the	  text	  within	  the	  
social	  world.	  By	  engaging	  in	  critical	  reads	  of	  text,	  students	  are	  encouraged	  to	  assume	  an	  

	  

175	  
active,	  reflexive	  approach	  to	  the	  texts	  by	  highlighting	  inequities	  that	  are	  embedded	  in	  
storylines	  and	  in	  the	  characters’	  life.	  Such	  an	  approach	  positions	  them	  to	  better	  understand	  
the	  power	  structures	  (social,	  political,	  ideological,	  elements)	  that	  are	  at	  play	  in	  the	  text	  and	  
the	  larger	  society.	  The	  above	  strategies	  create	  a	  learning	  environment	  that	  makes	  critical	  
conversation	  a	  part	  of	  the	  everyday	  functioning	  of	  the	  learning	  environment	  and	  gives	  
students	  the	  sense	  that	  probing	  questions	  are	  not	  about	  right	  or	  wrong	  but	  instead	  are	  
about	  engaging	  conversations	  and	  voicing	  multiple	  perspectives.	  If	  information	  
professional	  and	  classroom	  teachers	  expand	  their	  teaching	  repertoires	  to	  include	  critical	  
approaches	  to	  texts,	  students	  would	  be	  able	  to	  make	  meaning	  of	  their	  world	  and,	  in	  doing	  
so,	  make	  meanings	  of	  the	  values,	  attitudes,	  and	  power	  relationships	  that	  are	  being	  
conveyed	  through	  the	  text	  (Greene,	  2012).	  
	  
Implications	  for	  Theory	  	  
Sipe’s	  (2008)	  Reader	  Response	  Theoretical	  Framework	  has	  been	  instrumental	  in	  
examining	  some	  aspects	  of	  meaning	  making	  and	  talk;	  however,	  it	  does	  not	  attend	  to	  key	  
constructs,	  such	  as	  race	  and	  gender.	  Social	  constructs,	  such	  as	  race	  and	  gender,	  have	  been	  
at	  the	  core	  of	  this	  study	  and	  have	  been	  instrumental	  in	  examining	  how	  black	  adolescent	  
girls	  make	  meaning	  of	  the	  world,	  make	  meaning	  of	  the	  text,	  and	  position	  themselves	  within	  
the	  world	  in	  the	  21st	  century.	  Over	  the	  course	  of	  this	  study,	  participants	  have	  assumed	  
critical	  perspectives	  on	  social	  issues	  from	  their	  race,	  gendered,	  and	  classed	  positions	  that	  
this	  framework	  was	  unable	  to	  account	  for.	  Additionally,	  Reader	  Response	  Theory	  was	  only	  
able	  in	  part	  to	  account	  for	  my	  multiple	  identities	  and	  influences	  on	  participants’	  meaning	  
making	  process.	  Reader	  Response	  Theory	  offered	  several	  types	  of	  talk,	  however	  it	  was	  

	  

176	  
unable	  to	  account	  for	  the	  power	  dynamics	  present	  in	  interactions.	  Based	  on	  the	  limitations	  
of	  this	  framework,	  I	  propose	  that	  a	  more	  culturally	  and	  generationally	  relevant	  model	  be	  
developed	  that	  accounts	  for	  the	  raced,	  gendered,	  and	  classed	  identities	  of	  diverse	  
populations,	  the	  racial	  and	  cultural	  themes	  present	  in	  culturally	  relevant	  texts,	  and	  the	  
political	  influences	  embedded	  in	  facilitator-­‐participant	  interactions.	  
	  
Methodological	  Strengths
