One	  of	  the	  most	  prominent	  methodological	  strengths	  in	  this	  research	  study	  was	  the	  

use	  of	  Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis	  to	  frame	  the	  study	  and	  to	  interpret	  the	  data.	  By	  
employing	  a	  Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis,	  I	  was	  able	  to	  conduct	  both	  a	  macro	  and	  micro-­‐
analysis	  focusing	  on	  the	  interplay	  between	  talk	  at	  the	  situational,	  institutional,	  and	  societal	  
level	  across	  three	  levels	  of	  interacting	  or	  order	  of	  discourse—genre,	  discourse,	  and	  style	  
(Fairclough,	  1989).	  Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis	  was	  the	  most	  suitable	  analytical	  framework	  
because	  it	  also	  accounted	  for	  the	  interplay	  of	  literacy	  and	  language	  and	  examined	  the	  
interconnectedness	  of	  talk.	  By	  employing	  a	  Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis	  framework,	  I	  
accounted	  for	  the	  fact	  that	  literacy	  and	  language	  are	  never	  neutral,	  but	  instead	  are	  situated	  
within	  political	  spheres	  around	  a	  series	  of	  social	  constructs	  (race,	  gender,	  class).	  By	  
employing	  a	  three-­‐tired	  approach,	  I	  was	  able	  to	  analyze	  data	  using	  various	  lenses	  within	  
and	  across	  context.	  	  Cross	  analysis	  through	  the	  use	  of	  multiple	  lenses	  provided	  a	  close	  
examination	  of	  both	  data	  alignment	  and	  data	  tensions	  within	  and	  across	  contexts.	  By	  using	  
Critical	  Discourse	  Analysis	  as	  a	  cultural	  tool	  I	  was	  able	  to	  dissect	  relationships	  of	  power	  
and	  privilege	  through	  interactions	  and	  social	  structures.	  It	  was	  these	  instances	  that	  
highlighted	  dominance/oppression	  and	  agency/suppression	  within	  the	  texts	  that	  

	  

177	  
participants	  read,	  within	  their	  own	  personal	  lives	  and	  within	  society	  at	  large.	  Critical	  
Discourse	  Analysis	  was	  also	  the	  most	  suitable	  analytical	  tool	  for	  this	  study	  because	  it	  
accounted	  for	  the	  linguistic	  styles	  of	  historically	  marginalized	  groups	  of	  people	  and	  
captured	  the	  constant	  negotiation	  of	  identities	  that	  existed	  between	  local	  languages	  and	  
dominant	  languages	  (Rogers	  et	  al.,	  2005).	  CDA	  also	  accounted	  for	  how	  social	  norms	  were	  
embedded	  in	  particular	  institutions	  and	  how	  participants	  shifted	  their	  ways	  of	  being	  to	  
conform	  to	  these	  spaces.	  One	  of	  the	  most	  valuable	  components	  of	  Critical	  Discourse	  
Analysis	  was	  the	  reflexive	  feature	  providing	  the	  opportunity	  to	  turn	  the	  analytic	  lens	  on	  
myself	  as	  a	  researcher,	  as	  a	  facilitator,	  and	  as	  a	  participant	  and	  highlight	  my	  inherent	  
power	  and	  privilege	  across	  interactions.	  
	  
Methodological	  Weaknesses	  
Two	  methodological	  weaknesses	  were	  evident	  in	  this	  research	  study.	  Recruiting	  
efforts	  for	  this	  study	  focused	  predominantly	  on	  community	  organizations	  and	  local	  
agencies	  that	  worked	  closely	  with	  children	  and	  teens.	  During	  this	  process,	  several	  instances	  
rooted	  in	  power,	  privilege,	  and	  positionality	  greatly	  impacted	  the	  recruiting	  process.	  The	  
online	  book	  club	  program	  was	  advertised	  as	  the	  “Dark	  Girls	  Online	  Street	  Literature	  Book	  
Club.”	  Shantel,	  a	  parent	  and	  mental	  health	  provider	  in	  the	  community	  expressed	  her	  
frustration	  with	  the	  term	  “Dark	  Girls”	  and	  made	  the	  assumption	  that	  the	  term	  was	  
exclusionary	  and	  used	  to	  promote	  a	  community	  program	  geared	  solely	  for	  darker	  skinned	  
black	  adolescent	  girls	  (Personal	  communication,	  August	  23,	  2013).	  	  In	  an	  email	  to	  me	  
Shantel	  asserted:	  
Both	  my	  daughter	  and	  I	  would	  be	  offended	  sitting	  our	  light	  skin	  in	  on	  your	  book	  
club.	  Are	  all	  your	  members	  of	  dark	  skin?	  Why	  would	  you	  consider	  segregating	  
	  

178	  

	  

“black	  girls”	  like	  this?	  And	  we	  wonder	  why	  we	  STILL	  have	  “issues.”	  	  As	  a	  qualified	  
mental	  health	  provider,	  I	  mentor,	  guide,	  and	  support	  all	  races,	  creeds,	  skin	  colors,	  
nationalities	  etc.,	  of	  girls.	  And	  believe	  me,	  being	  called	  “dark	  skinned”	  or	  “high	  
yellow”	  STILL	  stands	  us	  out!!	  (Personal	  email	  communication,	  August	  23,	  2013)	  
	  
Shantel’s	  reaction	  to	  the	  terms	  used	  to	  describe	  potential	  participants	  in	  the	  

recruiting	  materials	  highlight	  the	  deep-­‐seated	  issues	  around	  colorism	  that	  often	  plagues	  
members	  in	  the	  black	  communities.	  It	  also	  depicts	  the	  way	  language	  is	  associated	  with	  self-­‐
identification	  and	  marginalization.	  Her	  email	  also	  reminds	  me	  that	  self-­‐identification	  and	  
self-­‐representation	  are	  often	  embedded	  in	  personal	  experiences.	  Shantel	  highlights	  her	  
personal	  experiences	  with	  colorism	  as	  a	  light-­‐skinned	  woman.	  Her	  stance	  also	  reminds	  me	  
that	  self-­‐identification	  and	  self-­‐representation	  aren’t	  fixed	  and	  universal,	  but	  instead	  are	  
fluid	  and	  complex.	  The	  way	  members	  in	  the	  black	  community	  self-­‐identify	  varied	  greatly.	  In	  
my	  response	  to	  Shantel	  I	  mentioned	  the	  following:	  
The	  term	  “Dark	  Girls”	  is	  a	  term	  that	  is	  an	  off-­‐shoot	  from	  previous	  community	  project	  
in	  the	  surrounding	  area.	  The	  use	  of	  the	  term	  “Dark	  Girls”	  within	  the	  context	  of	  this	  
program	  is	  used	  to	  represent	  all	  black	  girls	  regardless	  of	  skin	  tone	  or	  complexion.	  
This	  book	  club	  provides	  a	  welcoming	  space	  to	  black	  girls	  of	  all	  colors.	  So	  in	  no	  way	  is	  
this	  book	  club	  designed	  to	  separate,	  segregate	  or	  make	  distinctions	  between	  black	  
girls.	  
	  
As	  a	  light-­‐skinned	  African-­‐American	  woman	  who	  was	  a	  victim	  of	  colorism	  as	  a	  child,	  
I	  am	  very	  much	  aware	  of	  the	  harsh	  realities	  that	  exist	  around	  complexion	  within	  the	  
black	  community	  and	  how	  damaging	  such	  distinctions	  are	  to	  one’s	  identity	  
development	  and	  self-­‐esteem.	  I	  offer	  this	  book	  club	  project	  as	  a	  space	  of	  love,	  
sisterhood,	  and	  empowerment	  to	  celebrate	  black	  girlhood	  regardless	  of	  skin	  
complexion	  and	  to	  celebrate	  their	  love	  of	  street	  literature	  and	  technology.	  (Delicia,	  
Personal	  email	  communication,	  August,	  29,	  2013)	  
	  
My	  email	  conversation	  with	  Shantel	  brought	  about	  several	  thoughts	  around	  race,	  
colorism,	  and	  self-­‐identification.	  How	  do	  participants	  self-­‐identify?	  Does	  the	  term	  “Dark	  
Girls”	  give	  members	  in	  the	  community	  and	  participants	  the	  sense	  that	  the	  book	  club	  is	  
solely	  for	  black	  adolescent	  girls	  that	  are	  darker-­‐skinned?	  What	  identifiers	  represent	  who	  

	  

179	  
they	  are	  and	  how	  they	  see	  themselves?	  How	  do	  they	  construct	  their	  own	  identities	  and	  
what	  language	  do	  they	  use	  to	  represent	  who	  they	  are?	  Did	  the	  advertisement	  for	  the	  book	  
club	  inadvertently	  exclude	  or	  marginalize	  particular	  black	  girls?	  	  
Although	  I	  had	  been	  an	  active	  community	  organizer	  in	  the	  local	  area	  for	  five	  years,	  
during	  the	  recruiting	  process	  for	  the	  Dark	  Girls	  Project,	  my	  positionality	  as	  an	  African-­‐
American	  woman	  who	  was	  not	  a	  native	  of	  the	  area	  and	  a	  researcher	  affiliated	  with	  a	  
private	  university	  was	  often	  challenged	  in	  community	  spaces.	  Over	  the	  course	  of	  the	  
recruiting	  process,	  I	  met	  with	  several	  community	  leaders	  and	  community	  center	  directors,	  
many	  of	  whom	  were	  natives	  of	  the	  area	  and	  had	  strong	  roots	  in	  the	  community.	  	  Although	  
many	  saw	  the	  benefits	  of	  the	  program	  in	  the	  community,	  my	  not	  being	  from	  the	  area	  made	  
it	  difficult	  to	  gain	  buy-­‐in	  from	  the	  surrounding	  community.	  Since	  community	  centers	  
receive	  federal	  funds	  to	  support	  their	  programs,	  many	  alluded	  to	  how	  my	  program	  would	  
negatively	  impact	  student	  attendance	  and	  in	  doing	  so	  “hurt	  their	  numbers”	  that	  determine	  
federal	  funds	  (Personal	  communication,	  August	  15,	  2013).	  Some	  spoke	  about	  the	  
contentious	  relationship	  that	  the	  university	  had	  with	  the	  community	  and	  in	  many	  ways	  my	  
presence	  represented	  that.	  
	  
Limitations	  of	  Study	  
The	  first	  limitation	  was	  based	  on	  the	  timing	  of	  the	  research	  study.	  The	  study	  
occurred	  over	  the	  course	  of	  4	  months	  from	  the	  end	  of	  August	  2013	  to	  the	  end	  of	  December	  
2013.	  During	  this	  time,	  participants	  were	  high	  school	  students	  balancing	  personal,	  social,	  
and	  academic	  obligations.	  At	  the	  time	  of	  this	  study,	  several	  of	  the	  participants	  were	  
preparing	  for	  college	  preparatory	  examinations,	  working	  part-­‐time	  jobs/internships,	  and	  

	  

180	  
participating	  in	  several	  community	  events.	  The	  timing	  of	  this	  research	  study	  coupled	  with	  
their	  personal	  and	  academic	  obligations	  may	  have	  limited	  the	  amount	  of	  time	  they	  were	  
able	  to	  devote	  to	  participating	  in	  the	  project.	  The	  limited	  amount	  of	  time	  they	  were	  able	  to	  
devote	  to	  the	  book	  club	  may	  have	  contributed	  to	  the	  following:	  (a)	  Late	  post	  to	  the	  online	  
discussion	  group,	  (b)	  frequency	  of	  participation	  in	  the	  online	  discussion,	  (c)	  absence	  from	  
the	  face-­‐to-­‐face	  Saturday	  session	  (both	  the	  focus	  group	  interviews	  and	  the	  journal	  sessions).	  
	  

The	  second	  limitation	  was	  based	  on	  frequency	  of	  participation	  in	  the	  online	  

discussion.	  Although	  participants	  were	  not	  required	  to	  post	  a	  minimum	  number	  of	  times	  to	  
the	  online	  discussion	  group,	  participants	  were	  encouraged	  to	  post	  their	  own	  personal	  
responses,	  as	  well	  engage	  in	  discussion	  with	  their	  peers.	  Often	  times,	  participants	  would	  
post	  once,	  but	  would	  not	  revisit	  the	  online	  discussion	  over	  the	  course	  of	  the	  week	  to	  post	  
subsequent	  comments,	  to	  respond	  to	  other	  participants,	  and/or	  respond	  to	  a	  participant	  
that	  commented	  on	  their	  response.	  This	  often	  limited	  online	  conversations	  in	  the	  online	  
book	  club.	  The	  third	  limitation	  was	  based	  on	  participation	  in	  the	  Saturday	  program	  
(includes	  semi-­‐structured	  focus	  group	  interviews).	  Since	  semi-­‐structured	  focused	  group	  
interviews	  were	  time	  and	  space	  sensitive,	  when	  participants	  missed	  a	  focus	  group	  session	  
they	  could	  not	  be	  re-­‐created	  at	  a	  later	  date.	  When	  participants	  missed	  a	  focus	  group	  
session,	  they	  would	  often	  share	  their	  thoughts	  on	  that	  particular	  book	  in	  their	  journal	  
during	  the	  subsequent	  group	  meeting.	  When	  participants	  missed	  sessions,	  it	  also	  limited	  
the	  level	  of	  analysis	  that	  could	  be	  conducted,	  since	  CDA	  was	  grounded	  in	  examining	  
interactions	  across	  context.	  	  
	  

	  

181	  
Recommendations	  for	  Future	  Research	  
	  

In	  Trippin’	  Over	  the	  Color	  Line:	  The	  Invisibility	  of	  Race	  in	  Library	  and	  Information	  

Studies	  (2005),	  Honma	  asserts	  the	  following:	  	  
Despite	  a	  long	  legacy	  of	  race-­‐based	  scholarship	  in	  many	  fields	  of	  the	  academy,	  as	  
well	  as	  the	  ever	  diversifying	  user	  population	  in	  the	  United	  States,	  the	  field	  of	  library	  
and	  information	  science	  (LIS)	  has	  failed	  to	  keep	  up	  with	  the	  ongoing	  discussions	  and	  
debates	  about	  race	  and	  instead	  functions	  in	  a	  race-­‐blind	  vacuum	  while	  failing	  to	  
recognize	  the	  disfiguring	  implications	  such	  blindness	  embodies.	  (p.	  1)	  
	  
Peterson	  (1996)	  further	  asserts,	  “Although	  there	  has	  been	  scholarship	  in	  the	  area	  of	  
race	  and	  racism,	  it	  has	  not	  been	  given	  the	  serious	  attention	  it	  deserves”	  (p.	  172)	  According	  
to	  Honma	  (2005)	  “both	  scholars	  and	  students	  in	  the	  field	  of	  LIS	  have	  echoed	  these	  
sentiments	  numerous	  times,	  yet	  a	  sustained	  critical	  discussion	  surrounding	  the	  issue	  of	  
race	  within	  LIS	  remains	  disturbingly	  absent”	  (p.	  1).	  Based	  on	  the	  findings	  in	  the	  current	  
study	  and	  the	  dearth	  of	  race-­‐based	  scholarship	  in	  LIS	  field,	  I	  recommend	  further	  research	  
on	  the	  information	  and	  literacy	  needs	  of	  diverse	  populations	  and	  the	  information	  
professionals	  and	  educators	  that	  serve	  them.	  One	  recommendation	  for	  future	  research	  
would	  be	  a	  critical	  exploration	  of	  young	  adult	  librarians	  and	  school	  library	  media	  
specialists’	  responses	  to	  multicultural	  texts	  within	  an	  information	  professional	  book	  club.	  I	  
recommend	  that	  this	  research	  study	  examine	  how	  information	  professionals	  make	  
meaning	  of	  multicultural	  texts,	  how	  their	  roles	  as	  information	  professionals	  encourage	  or	  
deter	  the	  censoring	  of	  multicultural	  texts,	  and	  the	  critical	  approaches	  that	  they	  employ,	  in	  
order	  to	  incorporate	  multicultural	  texts	  within	  the	  secondary	  context.	  A	  second	  
recommendation	  for	  future	  research	  would	  be	  a	  critical	  comparative	  study	  of	  the	  literacy	  
practices	  of	  black	  adolescents	  in	  both	  in-­‐school	  spaces	  and	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  spaces.	  This	  

	  

182	  
comparative	  study	  would	  provide	  a	  full	  picture	  of	  the	  ways	  in	  which	  black	  adolescents	  
make	  meaning	  of	  literacy	  across	  formal	  and	  informal	  contexts.	  
	  
Conclusion	  
	  

Concrete	  Roses	  provided	  insight	  into	  societal,	  institutional,	  and	  situational	  influences	  

that	  impacted	  black	  adolescent	  girls’	  meaning	  making	  of	  street	  literature	  texts.	  By	  doing	  so,	  
it	  accounted	  for	  the	  historical	  positioning	  of	  the	  black	  girls	  from	  antebellum	  slavery	  to	  the	  
present	  day	  and	  the	  cultural	  climate	  surrounding	  black	  girlhood	  in	  society	  and	  in	  formal	  
institutions.	  Concrete	  Roses	  has	  re-­‐positioned	  black	  girls	  at	  the	  center	  and	  created	  spaces	  
for	  black	  girls	  to	  re-­‐write	  their	  experiences	  in	  their	  own	  words	  and	  on	  their	  own	  terms.	  By	  
providing	  concrete	  examples	  to	  bridge	  black	  girls’	  out-­‐of-­‐school	  and	  in-­‐school	  literacy	  and	  
language	  practices,	  it	  is	  my	  hope	  that	  this	  study	  informs	  the	  work	  of	  information	  
professionals,	  classroom	  teachers,	  school	  administrators,	  policy	  makers,	  and	  social	  
workers.	  
	  

	  

183	  
Appendix	  A	  
Definitions	  of	  Terms	  
Black	  is	  a	  broad	  racial	  identifier	  used	  to	  describe	  those	  that	  are	  of	  African	  descent.	  The	  
term	  black	  is	  often	  used	  to	  describe	  Africans,	  African	  Americans,	  Caribbean	  Americans,	  and	  
Latin	  Americans.	  
Black	  Girlhood	  is	  the	  “representations,	  memories,	  and	  lived	  experiences	  of	  being	  and	  
becoming	  in	  a	  body	  marked	  as	  youthful,	  Black,	  and	  female”	  (Brown,	  2009,	  x).	  
Critical	  Research	  is	  a	  form	  of	  inquiry	  that	  “confronts	  the	  injustice	  of	  a	  particular	  society	  or	  
public	  sphere	  within	  the	  society”	  Kincheloe	  and	  McLaren	  (1998,	  p.	  291).	  Kincheloe	  (1998)	  
offers	  a	  general	  description	  of	  the	  “essence”	  of	  critical	  research:	  the	  “critical”	  aspect	  of	  the	  
critical	  research	  assumes	  that	  the	  inequalities	  of	  contemporary	  society	  need	  to	  be	  
addressed	  and	  that	  the	  world	  would	  be	  a	  better	  place	  if	  such	  unjust	  realities	  could	  be	  
changed…	  [W]e	  explore	  the	  world…	  for	  the	  purpose	  of	  exposing	  this	  injustice,	  developing	  
practical	  ways	  to	  change	  it,	  and	  identifying	  sites	  and	  strategies	  by	  which	  transformation	  
can	  be	  accomplished	  (p.	  1191).	  
Culturally	  Conscious	  Texts	  are	  texts	  that	  are	  often	  written	  by	  black	  authors	  who	  
recognize	  and	  accurately	  depict	  the	  black	  experience	  (Brooks,	  2006;	  in	  citing	  Bishop,	  
1983).	  These	  texts	  often	  consist	  of	  the	  following	  textual	  features:	  recurring	  themes,	  
systematic	  linguistic	  patterns,	  and	  depictions	  of	  everyday	  ethnic	  group	  practices	  (Brooks,	  
2006,	  p.	  375).	  These	  themes	  that	  depict	  the	  black	  experience	  include	  surviving	  racism,	  
spreading	  African	  American	  heritage	  and	  history,	  living	  in	  the	  city,	  and	  developing	  racial	  
pride	  to	  name	  a	  few.	  

	  

184	  
Digital	  Literacy	  Practices	  are	  culturally	  and	  socially	  shaped	  ways	  of	  using,	  producing,	  and	  
understanding	  information	  in	  multiple	  formats	  from	  a	  range	  of	  sources	  when	  it	  is	  
presented	  via	  electronic	  screens	  of	  digital	  technologies	  (Snyder,	  2009,	  p.	  143).	  
Funds	  of	  Knowledge	  is	  the	  knowledge	  that	  students	  gain	  from	  their	  family	  and	  cultural	  
backgrounds.	  Gonzales,	  Moll,	  Amanti	  (2005)	  state	  the	  importance	  of	  educators	  within	  
formal	  spaces	  tapping	  into	  these	  home	  and	  community	  resources,	  in	  an	  effort	  to	  create	  
inclusive	  learning	  environments.	   	  
Identity	  is	  situational	  and	  shifts	  from	  context	  to	  context.	  Identities	  are	  represented	  and	  
constructed	  through	  literacy	  practices	  in	  which	  participants	  engage.	  How	  one	  thinks	  about	  
herself	  is	  revealed	  and	  shaped	  as	  she	  reads,	  writes,	  and	  engages	  with	  others	  (Sutherland,	  
2005).	  	  
Identity	  Play	  is	  one’s	  ability	  to	  assume	  other	  identities	  in	  digital	  spaces	  that	  are	  vastly	  
different	  than	  their	  own	  (Kivits,	  2005;	  Hine,	  2000;	  Turkle,	  1995).	  
Language	  Practices	  are	  “what	  people	  do	  with	  language.”	  This	  includes	  one’s	  verbal	  
behavior.	  This	  includes	  one’s	  use	  of	  dialects	  as	  forms	  of	  expression,	  use	  of	  pronouns	  to	  
represent	  solidarity	  or	  individualism,	  modification	  of	  words	  to	  express	  mood,	  
apprehension	  in	  speech	  or	  break	  in	  thought,	  and	  interruption,	  overlap,	  or	  simultaneous	  
talk	  in	  a	  group	  setting	  (Fairclough,	  1989;	  Rogers,	  2003).	  
Literacy	  is	  defined	  as	  ideological	  practices,	  rather	  than	  a	  set	  of	  neutral	  or	  technical	  skills	  
traditionally	  found	  in	  formal	  institutions	  (Street,	  2003).	  “Literacy	  is	  never	  an	  autonomous	  
skill,	  but	  always	  embedded	  in	  institutions	  and	  are	  implicated	  in	  social,	  political,	  and	  
cultural	  processes	  (Street,	  2003;	  Mahiri,	  2004).	  Hence,	  literacy	  is	  never	  neutral.	  Ideological	  
model	  offers	  a	  more	  culturally	  sensitive	  view	  of	  literacy	  (Street,	  2009,	  p.	  23).	  

	  

185	  
Literacy	  Practices	  are	  “what	  people	  do	  with	  literacy.”	  This	  includes	  one’s	  awareness	  of	  
literacy,	  constructions	  of	  literacy	  and	  discourses	  of	  literacy,	  and	  the	  cultural	  and	  political	  
forces	  that	  influence	  how	  people	  talk	  about	  and	  make	  sense	  of	  literacy.”	  These	  processes	  
are	  internal	  to	  the	  individual;	  at	  the	  same	  time,	  practices	  are	  the	  social	  processes,	  which	  
connect	  people	  with	  one	  another,	  and	  they	  include	  shared	  cognition	  represented	  in	  
ideologies	  and	  social	  identities”	  (Barton	  &	  Hamilton,	  1998,	  p.	  6-­‐8).	  “Literacy	  practices	  are	  
socially	  situated	  practices	  that	  only	  make	  sense	  when	  studied	  in	  the	  context	  of	  social	  and	  
cultural	  practices	  which	  they	  are	  part	  of”	  (Rogers,	  2003,	  p.	  26	  in	  citing	  Barton	  &	  Hamilton,	  
1998;	  Heath,	  1983;	  Street,	  1995).	  
Street	  Literature	  Street	  literature	  is	  a	  collection	  of	  culturally	  conscious,	  coming	  of	  age	  
texts	  that	  graphically	  depict	  the	  ills	  of	  inner	  city	  life	  and	  vividly	  illustrate	  the	  impact	  of	  
African	  Americans	  living	  in	  marginalized	  societies	  (Hill	  et	  al.,	  2008;	  Morris,	  2007).	  The	  term	  
street	  literature	  has	  often	  been	  used	  interchangeably	  to	  refer	  to	  urban	  fiction	  texts	  (Morris	  
et	  al.,	  2006),	  hip-­‐hop	  literature	  (Meloni,	  2007;	  Osborne,	  2001),	  Black	  pulp	  fiction	  (Osborne,	  
2001),	  ghetto	  lit	  (Stovall,	  2005),	  urban	  fiction	  (Marshall	  et	  al.,	  2009),	  gangsta	  lit	  (Venable,	  
2004),	  and	  street	  fiction	  (Hill	  et	  al.,	  2008).	  
Voice	  is	  a	  concept	  rooted	  in	  Black	  Feminist	  Perspective.	  Black	  females	  who	  are	  historically	  
marginalized	  populations	  are	  often	  left	  “voiceless”	  by	  social	  structures	  that	  cater	  to	  
populations	  that	  assume	  the	  dominant	  discourse	  in	  traditional	  learning	  environments.	  
McElroy-­‐Johnson	  (1993)	  defines	  voice	  as	  “a	  strong	  sense	  of	  identity	  within	  an	  individual,	  
an	  ability	  to	  express	  a	  personal	  point	  of	  view,	  and	  a	  sense	  of	  personal	  well-­‐being	  that	  
allows	  students	  to	  respond	  to	  and	  become	  engaged	  with	  material	  by	  other	  students	  in	  the	  
classroom	  and	  the	  teacher”	  (p.	  85-­‐86).	  

	  

186

Appendix	  F	  
Linguistic	  Symbols	  
Linguistic	  Symbols	  Chart	  
Symbol	  

Summary	  

:	  

Extends	   vowel	   or	   consonants	   in	   a	   word.	  

	  

Colon	   positioned	   immediately	   after	   the	  

(i.e.	  li:ke)	  

vowel	   or	   consonant	   that	   is	   being	   extended.	  
The	  use	  of	  more	  than	  one	  colon	  denotes	  the	  
extent	   to	   which	   the	   vowel	   or	   consonant	   is	  
being	  extended	  

(italics)	  

Describes	   a	   physical	   action	   (i.e.	   looks	   at	  	  	  	  
another	   participant,	   frowns	   face,	   directs	  
question	  or	  comment	  to	  a	  specific	  person(s)	  	  	  
in	  the	  group).	  

Italics	  

Describes	  the	  modification	  of	  a	  word	  to	  
express	  categories	  such	  as	  mood,	  voice	  

[Pause]	  

Describes	  apprehension	  or	  a	  break	  in	  
thought	  

{bold	  letters}	  

Interruption,	  overlap,	  or	  simultaneous	  talk	  
	  
	  
	  
	  

	  

	  

203	  
1

204	  

Appendix	  H	  
Coding	  Categories	  
Orders	  of	  Discourse:	  Coding	  Categories	  (adapted	  from	  Smitherman,	  2006,	  1997;	  
Rogers,	  2003;	  Richardson,	  2003)	  
GENRE:	  Ways	  of	  interacting;	  the	  organizational	  components	  of	  interacting;	  use	  of	  
linguistic	  devices	  in	  interactions	  (Chouliaraki	  &	  Fairclough,	  1999)	  
Participant	  structures	  (turn-­‐taking;	  overlapping;	  interruption)	  
Piggybacking	  
Affirmation	  and	  Disagreements	  
Cohesion	  devices	  (parallel	  structure,	  repetition)	  
Topic	  Control/Topic	  Maintenance	  
DISCOURSE:	  Ways	  of	  representing;	  “the	  representation	  of	  knowledge	  and	  from	  what	  
perspective”	  (Chouliaraki	  &	  Fairclough,	  1999,	  p.	  63);	  “systematic	  clusters	  of	  themes,	  
statements,	  ideas,	  ideologies”	  (Luke,	  2000,	  p.	  456).	  	  
Black	  girl	  identity	  
Media	  representations	  
Black	  male	  gaze	  on	  the	  black	  girl	  experience	  
Black	  female	  relationships/Sisterhood/Mothering	  
Black	  girls’	  academic	  Success	  
Black	  female	  condition	  
Black	  female	  sexuality	  
Incest/Sexual	  Abuse/Physical	  Abuse	  

	  

205	  

STYLE:	  Ways	  of	  being;	  linguistic	  processes	  that	  are	  drawn	  from	  a	  particular	  
positionality	  (Smitherman,	  2006,	  1997;	  Rogers,	  2003).	  
Pronouns	  
Affect	  
Word	  choice	  
Active	  passive	  voice	  
Rhetorical	  Strategies-­‐Code-­‐switching	  	  &	  code-­‐meshing	  (African-­‐American	  Vernacular	  
English-­‐AAVE	  &	  Standard	  English)	  
Type	  of	  Talk	  (Teacher	  Talk,	  Black	  Girl	  Talk)	  
MULTIMODAL	  RESOURCES:	  
Facial	  Expressions	  (frown	  face,	  smile)	  
Eye	  Contact	  (eye	  rolling,	  side	  eye,	  stare,	  glance)	  
Body	  Expressions/Gestures	  (holds	  head	  down,	  nods	  head,	  tilts	  head	  to	  the	  side,	  hands	  
folded);	  Posture	  (Open,	  Closed)	  
Speech	  (intonation)	  

	  

206	  

Appendix	  I	  
Journal	  Protocol	  
Positionality	  
1. How	  would	  you	  describe	  yourself?	  
2. What	  does	  it	  mean	  to	  you	  to	  be	  a	  black	  girl?	  
3. How	  might	  you	  describe	  black	  girlhood?	  
4. Are	  there	  particular	  life	  experiences	  that	  have	  greatly	  contributed	  to	  who	  you	  are?	  If	  
so,	  please	  describe.	  
Media	  Portrayals	  
5. What	  would	  you	  describe	  as	  media?	  Give	  an	  example	  
6. How	  do	  the	  particular	  media	  outlet(s)	  you	  described	  represent	  black	  girls?	  
7. What	  aspects	  of	  the	  media	  (television	  shows,	  movies,	  music	  videos)	  do	  you	  like	  
most?	  Least	  enjoy?	  Please	  describe	  
8. Would	  you	  describe	  these	  images	  of	  black	  girls	  as	  positive	  or	  negative?	  What	  makes	  
them	  positive?	  What	  makes	  them	  negative?	  	  
	  Self-­‐Identity	  
9. Why	  do	  you	  think	  these	  images	  are	  the	  images	  that	  are	  used	  to	  represent	  black	  
girls?	  
10. How	  do	  these	  images	  influence	  how	  society	  views	  black	  girls?	  	  
11. What	  are	  your	  reactions	  to	  these	  images?	  How	  do	  these	  images	  make	  you	  feel?	  
12. Do	  these	  images	  influence	  who	  you	  are?	  If	  so,	  how?

ways?	  
3.	  Who	  was	  Precious?	  How	  might	  you	  describe	  her?	  
4.	  What	  was	  Precious’	  relationship	  with	  her	  mother?	  
5.	  What	  type	  of	  mother	  did	  Precious	  have?	  How	  might	  you	  describe	  her	  mother?	  
6.	  What	  was	  Precious’	  relationship	  like	  with	  her	  father?	  
7.	  How	  would	  you	  describe	  the	  relationship	  between	  Precious’	  mother	  and	  her	  father?	  
8.	  Would	  you	  consider	  Precious’	  mother	  an	  abuser?	  A	  victim	  of	  abuse?	  Or	  both?	  Please	  
explain	  
Semi-­‐Structured	  Focus	  Group	  Interviews:	  PUSH	  by	  Sapphire	  
1. What	  was	  your	  initial	  reaction	  after	  reading	  PUSH	  by	  Sapphire?	  
2. Were	  there	  any	  parts	  of	  PUSH	  that	  made	  you	  feel	  comfortable?	  
3. Did	   you	   relate	   personally	   to	   any	   of	   the	   characters	   and/or	   events	   in	   PUSH?	   	   If	   so,	   in	   what	  
ways?	  
4. How	   might	   you	   describe	   Precious?	   How	   might	   you	   describe	   her	   in	   the	   earlier	   part	   of	  
PUSH	  and	  moving	  towards	  the	  middle	  and	  the	  end	  of	  PUSH?	  
5. Why	  do	  you	  feel	  that	  Precious	  was	  treated	  so	  badly	  by	  her	  mother?	  
6. How	  do	  you	  feel	  the	  relationship	  Precious	  had	  with	  her	  mother	  affected	  her?	  

	  

208	  

7. Why	   do	   you	   believe	   the	   responsibility	   of	   protecting	   a	   child	   falls	   mostly	   on	   the	   mother	  
and	  not	  the	  father?	  
8. How	   do	   you	   feel	   the	   relationship	   Precious	   had	   with	   her	   father	   affected	   her?	   Why	   do	   you	  
think	  she	  mentioned	  that	  sex	  with	  her	  father	  “felt	  good?”	  Why	  was	  that	  normal	  for	  her?	  
9. What	   were	   Precious’	   experiences	   like	   at	   her	   first	   school?	   How	   was	   it	   different	   at	   the	  
alternative	  school?	  What	  role	  did	  Ms.	  Rain	  play	  in	  Precious’	  school	  experiences?	  
10. What	   images	   did	   Precious	   feel	   represented	   physical	   beauty?	   Why	   did	   being	   white	  
represent	  beauty	  in	  her	  eyes?	  	  
Online	  Facebook	  Book	  Discussions:	  Project	  Girl	  by	  Janet	  MacDonald	  
1.	  What	  was	  your	  initial	  reaction	  after	  reading	  Project	  Girl	  by	  Janet	  MacDonald?	  
2.	  Did	  you	  relate	  personally	  to	  Janet	  or	  any	  experiences	  in	  her	  life?	  	  If	  so,	  in	  what	  ways?	  
3.	  Who	  is	  Janet?	  How	  might	  you	  describe	  her?	  
4.	  In	  the	  first	  paragraph	  of	  the	  text,	  the	  author	  mentions	  the	  following	  “I	  belong	  nowhere	  in	  
particular	  anymore	  and	  feel	  comfortable	  most	  everywhere.”	  What	  does	  that	  mean?	  	  
5.	  	  What	  does	  that	  mean	  in	  your	  own	  personal	  life?	  Please	  explain	  
6.	  Who	  is	  Janet	  in	  her	  hometown?	  Who	  is	  she	  in	  her	  household?	  Who	  is	  she	  within	  the	  
university?	  Who	  is	  she	  in	  the	  corporate	  world?	  	  
7.	  How	  does	  she	  manage	  her	  many	  identities	  across	  different	  settings?	  
Semi-­‐Structured	  Focus	  Group	  Interviews:	  Project	  Girl	  by	  Janet	  MacDonald	  
1.	  How	  might	  you	  describe	  Janet?	  How	  might	  you	  describe	  her	  in	  the	  earlier	  part	  of	  Project	  
Girl	  and	  moving	  towards	  the	  middle	  and	  the	  end	  of	  Project	  Girl?	  
2.	  Why	  do	  you	  think	  Janet	  faces	  so	  many	  personal	  struggles	  at	  different	  points	  in	  her	  life?	  
3.	  Why	  did	  you	  think	  Janet	  was	  ridiculed	  and	  alienated	  by	  people	  in	  her	  community?	  

	  

209	  

4.	  Why	  was	  Janet’s	  success	  in	  school	  label	  as	  a	  ‘white	  thing”	  by	  members	  in	  the	  community?	  
4.	  What	  is	  a	  white-­‐black	  girl?	  Why	  do	  you	  think	  Janet	  was	  labeled	  a	  white-­‐black	  girl?	  
5.	  Have	  you	  ever	  been	  labeled	  because	  of	  a	  decision	  you	  made?	  Please	  explain.	  
6.	  Why	  is	  academic	  success	  often	  associated	  with	  ’acting	  white?’	  	  What	  then	  is	  associated	  
with	  ‘acting	  black?’	  
7.	  You	  mentioned	  that	  Janet	  did	  some	  ‘black	  things’	  and	  some	  ‘white	  things.’	  What	  would	  be	  
considered	  ‘black	  things’	  and	  what	  	  would	  be	  considered	  ‘white	  things?’	  	  
8.	  What	  are	  your	  thoughts	  when	  there	  are	  differences	  made	  based	  on	  race	  and	  gender?	  
9.For	  example,	  do	  you	  consider	  playing	  certain	  sports	  as	  being	  a	  ‘white	  sport’	  or	  a	  ‘black	  
sport?’

2. Do	  you	  think	  the	  characters	  and	  storyline	  are	  realistic?	  Can	  you	  identify	  with	  any	  of	  
them?	  If	  so,	  in	  what	  ways?	  
3. What	  did	  you	  find	  disturbing	  about	  Winter’s	  relationship	  with	  her	  parents?	  
4. How	  does	  her	  images	  of	  fatherhood	  and	  motherhood	  impact	  her	  relationships	  in	  the	  
street?	  
5. Winter	  uses	  sex	  as	  a	  means	  to	  gain	  the	  affections	  of	  young	  men	  and	  to	  gain	  material	  
possessions.	  How	  does	  her	  behavior	  tie	  into	  how	  she	  sees	  herself?	  Her	  self	  worth?	  Her	  
self	  respect?	  
6. Why	  would	  Winter	  be	  labeled	  a	  thot?	  What	  qualities	  does	  Winter	  have	  that	  makes	  her	  a	  
thot?	  
7. Do	  you	  think	  these	  same	  rules	  around	  sexual	  behavior	  would	  still	  apply	  if	  winter	  were	  a	  
male?	  Please	  explain.	  
8. At	  the	  end	  of	  the	  text,	  Winter	  encounters	  her	  younger	  sisters.	  Despite	  what	  Winter	  
experienced,	  she	  chose	  not	  to	  offer	  words	  of	  advice	  to	  her	  younger	  sisters,	  although	  they	  
seemed	  to	  be	  going	  down	  the	  same	  path.	  Why	  do	  you	  think	  Winter	  chose	  to	  silence	  her	  
own	  voice?	  
	  

	  

212	  

Appendix	  K	  
Street	  Literature	  Text	  Summary	  
Brief	  Citation	  

Street	  Literature	  Text	  Summary	  

McDonald,	  J	  (1999).	  Project	  Girl	  

This	  is	  a	  memoir	  of	  the	  life	  of	  a	  young	  woman	  
Janet	  McDonald,	  who	  struggles	  to	  realize	  her	  
dreams	  while	  trying	  to	  remain	  true	  to	  who	  
she	  was	  and	  where	  she	  came	  from.	  Janet	  is	  
caught	  between	  two	  worlds:	  One	  world	  of	  Ivy	  
League	  schools	  and	  impressive	  diplomas	  and	  
the	  other	  world	  growing	  up	  in	  a	  
neighborhood	  filled	  with	  poverty,	  drug	  
abuse,	  and	  crime.	  This	  story	  depicts	  Janet’s	  
divided	  life	  and	  her	  constant	  battle	  
reconciling	  opposing	  worlds.	  

Souljah,	  S.	  (1999).	  The	  Coldest	  Winter	  Ever	  

In	  this	  novel,	  Winter	  Santiago	  is	  born	  to	  a	  
fourteen-­‐year-­‐old	  mother	  and	  drug	  kingpin	  
father,	  Ricky	  Santiaga.	  Winter	  grows	  up	  in	  
the	  projects	  in	  Brooklyn	  along	  with	  three	  
younger	  siblings.	  Although	  Winter’s	  father	  
keeps	  her	  away	  from	  the	  “business,”	  she	  
indulges	  in	  the	  finer	  things	  in	  life	  as	  the	  
daughter	  of	  a	  drug	  kingpin.	  Winter	  came	  into	  
adolescence	  shallow	  and	  self-­‐centered	  and	  
motivated	  solely	  by	  material	  possessions,	  
physical	  attractiveness,	  and	  the	  desire	  to	  
attract	  men.	  This	  story	  also	  depicts	  the	  rise	  
and	  fall	  of	  her	  father’s	  drug	  empire	  and	  the	  
family’s	  need	  to	  fend	  for	  themselves.	  	  
	  

	  

213	  

Brief	  Citation	  

Street	  Literature	  Text	  Summary	  

Tyree,	  O.	  (1996).	  Flyy	  Girl	  

Tracy	  Ellison	  is	  a	  young	  knockout	  with	  tall	  
hair	  and	  attitude,	  is	  living	  life	  in	  the	  fast	  lane.	  
Tracy	  is	  motivated	  by	  material	  possessions	  
and	  she	  enjoys	  looking	  for	  the	  affections	  of	  
men.	  It	  is	  only	  after	  a	  series	  of	  unfortunate	  
events	  in	  her	  life	  and	  the	  life	  of	  people	  close	  
to	  her	  that	  she	  re-­‐evaluates	  her	  life,	  her	  
goals,	  and	  her	  sexuality.	  	  

Sapphire	  (1996).	  PUSH	  

Precious	  Jones,	  an	  illiterate	  sixteen-­‐year-­‐old,	  
has	  been	  raped	  by	  her	  father	  and	  battered	  by	  
her	  mother.	  Precious	  is	  pregnant	  with	  a	  
second	  child	  by	  her	  father.	  Her	  mother	  
despises	  her	  and	  verbally	  abuses	  Precious.	  
Precious	  meets	  a	  determined	  and	  nurturing	  
teacher	  at	  a	  local	  community	  education	  
center	  that	  supports	  Precious	  on	  her	  path	  to	  
education	  and	  enlightenment.	  Through	  a	  
series	  of	  trials	  and	  tribulations	  and	  with	  the	  
help	  of	  her	  support	  system,	  Precious	  learns	  
to	  persevere	  and	  create	  a	  better	  life	  for	  
herself	  and	  her	  children.
